**Deals Final Outline:**

# 1: Introduction

- Not learning a body of law. Instead about fundamental problems in transactions  
- Paranoia of a deal: See potential of a deal and recognize the pitfalls and steer your client around them  
- Although deals are different in many ways and encounter many problems, there are similar fundamental problems and potential solutions and a toolkit to solve them in each setting  
  * Understand problems  
  * Address them with solutions

- **2 Themes in Class ‚Äì Where do lawyers add value in a deal:**  
  * **1\) Overcoming a familiar set of problems and incentives**  
  * **2\) Regulatory arbitrage**  
    * **You can do transactional things that are similar but receive different treatment; Steer client direction with better treatment**  
    * Tax planning  
- Similarities across different areas of practice  
  * Non-profits and VC are similar

- **Information and Incentive problems exist in every transaction and can prevent parties from engaging in transactions**  
- **Some tactics can manage these incentive problems and bridge information asymmetry so that parties can proceed with value-maximizing deals**  
  * Lawyers help navigate these problems

1\. I offer to sell you a used car for $5,000.  You need a car and the price is within your budget.

1. Is there anything else you need to know or think about before you agree to purchase the car?

- Usage: Mileage  
- Damage  
- Repairs  
- Legality  
- Why you are selling it

- Need assurances in writing because seller knows more about the quality of the car  
- The fact that you are selling alone may question the car‚Äôs value  
  * If the seller knows more and wants to sell it, signals bad news  
- **The fact that the seller knows more combines with the fact that the seller wants to sell should raise questions**

2. Please define the term ‚Äúasymmetric information.‚Äù How does it apply to the purchase of a used car?  
- **Asymmetric information \= Seller knows more than buyer**  
  * Arises in almost every transaction because sellers know more than buyers  
  * Combined with fact that they are willing to sell ü°™ Raises questions

3. In principle, could the problem of asymmetric information persuade a potential buyer not to buy a used car? Could this be true even if the used car is as good as the seller says it is?  
- It could be that the seller is telling the truth  
- **Seller also needs to communicate properly to buyer the truth. Otherwise, asymmetric information can persuade buyer to walk**

- Paranoia of asymmetric information can persuade buyers not to buy  
- Identify the ambiguity and figure out what to do about it

4. As a buyer, would you be more comfortable if you can ask me questions?  What if I am not honest in my answers? How can you improve the odds that I will answer honestly (or, at least, prove later that my answers were false)?  
- Questions make buyer more confident  
- **Transaction potential that buyer is asking information about \= Due diligence process**  
  * **DD can serve as a potential response to asymmetric information**

5. Would you be more comfortable if we bring in a mechanic to look at the car?  
- Yes. Part of the due diligence process  
- Or could be apart from DD ‚Äì bring in third party expert  
  * **Bringing in third party experts is another solution to asymmetric information**

6. In part ‚ÄúE,‚Äù what if the mechanic is my brother-in-law? Please define the term ‚Äúagency cost.‚Äù  
- Incentives are wrong  
- **Agency Cost \= Sometimes you are supposed to do something in best interest of principal but agent actually acts for their personal interest instead**  
  * Can interfere with a transaction

- **1\) Start with information problem: Buyer doesn‚Äôt know what seller knows**  
- **2\) Look for various solutions: Only work if they do not create a different kind of problem (an incentive problem/agency cost problem)**

7. Would you be more comfortable if I offer to let you pay only $2,000 now, and to pay $3,000 in a year if the car is still running well?  
- **Contingent Purchase Price: This is a common solution to an asymmetric information problem**  
  * **Maybe this is what the buyer would pay for a badly used car; gives possibility of upgrading payment to a good used car**

8. In Part ‚ÄúG,‚Äù does it matter how we define whether the car is ‚Äúrunning well‚Äù?  
- Yes. This could give rise to a dispute in the future  
- Layers of this problem:  
  * **Even if both parties act in good faith, it is hard to measure ‚Äúrunning well‚Äù**  
  * **Each party has an incentive to define ‚Äúrunning well‚Äù in a particular way**  
    * **New information problem. How does seller prove that he was right and it really was running well?**

9. If we agree to the idea in ‚ÄúG,‚Äù would it affect how carefully you take care of the car? Please define the term ‚Äúmoral hazard.‚Äù  
- **The buyer has an incentive to treat the car poorly to inflict enough damage on the car (that seller can‚Äôt prove wasn‚Äôt their fault) so that they avoid paying the upgrade in price**

- **Contingent purchase price is hard to measure in unanticipated situations but when it is possible it can eliminate asymmetric information problem because it can give assurance to uncertain buyer**  
    
- **Moral Hazard: A type of incentive problem especially focused on risk**  
  * Another version of agency cost  
  * **Protection against risk makes it more likely that you take more risk**  
    * **You will be more reckless when someone else bearing the loss**

10. Instead of a car, assume I offer to sell you a house for $500,000.  You need a house and the price, again, is within your budget. Is there asymmetric information in this situation? As you consider whether to buy the house, could variations of the steps discussed above for buying a car be helpful?  
- Asymmetric information problem: Seller knows more about the house  
- If the seller were going to move down the street, this raises questions because it is more likely that the problem is with the house

- Assurances for home buyers:  
  * Due Diligence (Inspection)  
  * Third party experts (Inspectors)  
  * Contingent purchase price (Reduce the purchase price unless the pipes stop working)  
- Problems and solutions for buying a home are similar to buying a car even though the setting is different

11. What if you want to buy a business? Is there asymmetric information in this situation? Are variations of the steps discussed above potentially helpful?  
- Yes. You will have asymmetric information that could prevent you from doing a deal  
- These mechanisms give confidence to buyers to do a deal that they otherwise might not want to do

- You want to protect yourself when doing a deal but also that you don‚Äôt walk away from one that could be beneficial

12. Do you agree with the following statement? ‚ÄúBecause the seller always knows more than the buyer, I should never buy anything ever again?‚Äù  
- No.

13. Do you agree with the following statement? **‚ÄúAlthough transactions vary in their details, every transaction involves problems of information and incentives that can discourage parties from entering into what otherwise would be value-maximizing arrangements.‚Äù**  
- Correct.

14. Do you agree with the following statement? **‚ÄúA central responsibility of transactional lawyers is to help overcome information and incentive problems.  They do so with a range of contractual and organizational responses, such as due diligence, contingent purchase price, staged financing, and vouching by third parties.‚Äù**

\- Correct.

2\. Stores that sell clothes online often allow people to return them at no charge. But it costs them time and money to let people return them.  

1. Why give people a ‚Äúmoney-back guarantee‚Äù of this sort?

- Creates incentive for buyers to buy more  
- 2 Benefits for Sellers:  
  * 1\) Sellers can make sales to people who would not otherwise buy. The buyer may be lazy or forget to return it  
  * 2\) Buying clothes online has more asymmetric information problem than brick and mortar store  
    * **Money back guarantee helps buyer overcome asymmetric information problem**

2. If you sell clothes online, and someone who buys them spills wine on them, would you accept the clothes back?  
- No. Because **the function of the guarantee is to allow the buyer to conduct due diligence on the product ‚Äì not alter the product**

3. You are the CEO of an online retail store. You decide to offer a new option to buyers: if they pay an extra amount, they can return clothes in the first year for any reason, including the fact that they stained them. You learn that under the old system‚Äîno returns for stains‚Äîone in ten items of clothing are stained during the first year. As a result, if you charge an extra 10%, would that be enough to fund the return policy? (Hint: If you sell people the right to return clothes they have stained, do you think it will affect how careful they are with the clothes?)  
- No. Because the protection of having the guarantee creates a moral hazard to damage the clothes more

1) **Asymmetric information problem**  
2) **Solution to it**  
3) **Trying to make a solution that goes beyond the asymmetric information problem (providing effective insurance) creates a moral hazard problem**

3\. You want to go into business organizing outdoor concerts.  A challenge, though, is that it sometimes rains, so the concert has to be cancelled. 

1. Do you consider this to be an issue of asymmetric information or an issue of uncertainty? What is the difference?

- **Uncertainty because neither party knows what the weather will be**  
  * **Uncertainty \= Neither party knows**  
  * **Asymmetric information \= One party knows more than the other**

2. If a concert is cancelled because of rain, who should bear this risk‚Äîthe performer, the ticket holders, or the owner of the arena?  
- **Bargaining Power in a transaction exists but it is not the only explanation for who should bear a particular risk (because other parts of the transaction can be used to accommodate risk-bearer)**  
  * **Bargaining Power helps you, but you must be strategic in deciding how to use it**

- Performer: Could play at an indoor arena so does not want to bear the risk  
  * Easy to avoid risk by not doing the transaction  
- Ticket Holders: Could go to the indoor arena so does not want to bear the risk  
  * Easy to avoid risk by not doing the transaction  
- Owner: Usually bears the risk  
  * **Cheaper for owner to bear the risk because he participates in the outdoor arena every night; on average he has the same number of likely rainy days a year and can plan for these**  
    * **If there is someone in a transaction who can bear the risk more efficiently, they should do it**  
    * **Can be comfortable with the transaction based on the average in a way that the other parties cannot**  
      * **Set the price of the 365 concerts at a point where you factor in 60 rained out concerts a year**  
    * **Benefits from diversification across days**  
      * **Performer and ticket holder are not positioned to benefit from diversification**

- **When you allocate any risk:**  
  * **The person that knows more about the product (asymmetric information) should bear the risk because they can price it better**  
  *  **Even if no one knows about the product (uncertainty), there is still a party that is going to have a comparative advantage**  
    * **In this case, as in others, diversification benefits one party which allows them to bear the risk**

4\. You are considering buying stock in either Autoco or Carco.  They make similar products (automobiles) and are otherwise similar, except for this difference: The executives in Autoco all own stock in Autoco, but the executives in Carco do not own stock in Carco.  Why might this difference be significant?

- **Agency Cost: Risk for people managing the company to not run the business as best as they can if they do not own the business**  
  * **Equity compensation aligns incentives (VC), dealing with incentive problem**

5\. You are the chief executive officer (‚ÄúCEO‚Äù) of Bigco, a publicly-traded firm.  You are thinking about buying a corporate jet.  The business of the company does not require it, but you think ‚ÄúI‚Äôm the boss, I deserve it.‚Äù  The jet costs the company $10 million, and you will derive pleasure worth 1% of that, or $100,000.  

1. Will shareholders want you to buy the jet? If not, how much influence to public shareholders have over this sort of issue? Who else might be in a position to stop you?

- No. You are taking $10M to get $100K satisfaction. This is inefficient  
- Public shareholders do not have influence over the hiring of CEO‚Äôs  
- The BoD might have influence to police CEO

2. If you are not a shareholder, is it beneficial to you personally to buy the jet? What is this type of incentive problem called? (See the excerpt from Gilson & Baums above.)  
- Yes. $100K of value you didn‚Äôt have before  
- Agency cost

3. If you own 2% of the company‚Äôs outstanding stock, is it beneficial to you personally to buy the jet?  
- No. The CEO bears 2% of $10M \= $200K  
  * Spending $200K of CEO‚Äôs own money to get $100K of value is not efficient

4. Can equity compensation be a response to agency costs?

- **Equity compensation addresses agency costs by turning company expenses into personal expenses and potential company profits into personal potential profits**

6\. You are the chief executive officer of Bigco, whose stock price currently is $100 per share. You have worked there your entire career, and you are well respected in the business world for your successes.  As a result, you constantly receive other offers to work elsewhere \-- something you appreciate \-- but you have turned them down.  

A. One of your senior managers brings you the following proposal.  If you invest all of Bigco‚Äôs assets in a new technology (the ‚ÄúVenture‚Äù), it could pay off very well.  Specifically, there is a 50% chance that the company‚Äôs stock price will be worth $500.  On the other hand, if Venture does not succeed, the company will become bankrupt (so the stock will be worth $0). If you proceed with Venture, what would your stock price be worth to risk neutral investors? How does that compare with the stock‚Äôs current value of $100?

- A shareholder will want the company to do this because 50% chance of $500 \= $250; While 50% chance of $0 \= $0  
- The stock price should jump from $100 to $250 after this deal  
  * Because the stock price is basically a lottery ticket for $500 or $0 \= $250 value  
  * From the perspective of the diversified shareholder, this is appealing

  C. If the company enters bankruptcy, what would the CEO lose? Do you think the CEO is likely to be more or less enthusiastic about making the risky new investment? Is there a potential agency cost here?

- But the CEO is less likely to do this because their professional reputation is at stake and their income (job) is at stake ‚Äì they are less diversified than the shareholder  
- **The shareholder‚Äôs perspective should matter (executive is just the agent of the owner), but their incentives are not aligned**  
  * This is an agency cost

  D. Assume that CEO has been receiving $3 million in cash each year, but the compensation package is changed to $2.5 million in cash and 10,000 shares of Bigco stock (currently worth $1 million if Bigco). If Bigco proceeds with Venture, how much would the stock be worth if the investment succeeds? What if it fails? Under the new compensation package, will the risky investment seem more or less attractive to the CEO?

- CEO is more willing to take extra risk because the upside is a concrete number to them  
- **Equity compensation encourages risk taking from executives**  
  * **This can align shareholders‚Äô risk tolerance with executives‚Äô risk tolerance**

7\. Assume that the stock of Company is currently trading at $60. Assume also that the S\&P 500 Stock Index is trading at $6,000 (to pick a round number). Assume that Company grants Executive 100,000 options to buy Company stock at $60 any time during the next ten years. 

1. Assume that ten years later, Company‚Äôs stock price has increased by just over 10% to $66. How much does Executive earn from these options?

- $600K

2. Assume also that ten years later, the S\&P 500 has increased by 50% to $9,000. How happy do you think shareholders are with Company‚Äôs performance?  
- Not happy because the market as a whole did better than the increase in Company‚Äôs stock price  
- Even though the performance was disappointing, executive will still get $600K

3. Assume that ten years later, the S\&P 500 has declined by 50%, and is $3,000. Company‚Äôs stock price is still $60. How happy do you think shareholders are with Company‚Äôs performance?  
- Very happy shareholders because Company beat the market by 50%, but Executive earned $0 from the options

4. Do you think the options given to Executive are structured in the right way to create the incentives shareholders want Executive to have? Are there any problems you would like to fix?

- **Options are not well structured because it does not accurately reward/penalize Executive for Company‚Äôs performance in comparison to the rest of the market**

8\. Like in the question above, assume that the stock of Company is currently trading at $60. Assume also that the S\&P 500 Stock Index is trading at $6,000. But instead of granting Executive 100,000 options to buy Company stock for $60 (a ‚Äúconventional option‚Äù), Company offers an ‚Äú**Indexed Option**‚Äù instead. Under this ‚ÄúIndexed Option,‚Äù Executive has the right to buy Company stock at any time during the next 10 years \-- not for the fixed price of $60--but for 1/100 of the value of the S\&P 500 Stock Index on the date of the purchase. \[**Price Executive can exercise his option at \= (Price in 10 years of S\&P)/100**\]

1. Assume that ten years later, Company‚Äôs stock price has increased by 10% to $66 and the S\&P 500 has increased by 50% to $9,000. How much money (if any) would Executive make in exercising his 100,000 options? Does this result seem appropriate?

- Option price \= $90  
  * Executive is not going to exercise his options because he bought them for $60. He is not going to pay $90 for something worth $60  
- **There is no payout in a situation where Company got outperformed by the market**

2. Assume that ten years later, Company‚Äôs stock price is still $60 and the S\&P 500 has declined by 50% to $3,000. How much money (if any) would Executive make in exercising his 100,000 options? Does this result seem appropriate?  
- Option price \= $30  
  * Executive will exercise his options because he will pay $30 for something worth $60  
- **Indexed option rewards Company for outperforming market**

3. If you served on a board of directors and were named the chair of the compensation committee, would you favor granting conventional options or indexed options?

- Favor indexed options because they more properly align incentives  
- Rare for companies to use them because they are complicated  
- **Compensation, properly structured, is a standard effort to solve incentive problems**

# 2: Tax Planning

- 2 Similar Situations treated differently is common but not a good thing ü°™ Regulatory Arbitrage  
  * **Tax planning: Find economically similar situations taxed differently**  
    * **Variations of this:**  
      * **Exclusions ‚Äì Sources of economic that are not taxed at all**  
      * **Valuation ‚Äì Sources of economic value that should be valued (and taxed) lower**  
      * **Conversion ‚Äì Different kinds of income taxed differently (rather have capital gain than ordinary income)**  
      * **Deferral ‚Äì If you have to pay the same amount, it is better to pay that later instead of now so you can hold onto your money, invest it, and make more money**  
        * **Effectively an interest-free loan from government**  
      * **Shifting ‚Äì Move income from higher paying taxpayer to lower**  
        * Works with progressive rates  
        * Can work with multinational corporations  
          * US incorporated parent and Irish subsidiary  
          * Earning money by selling to US consumers but still pay tax in Ireland because you take loans from Irish subsidiaries, pay them royalties  
          * Shifting profit from US to Ireland  
    * Remember, frictions and all parties too

- Information and incentive problems arise in every transaction and it is lawyers‚Äô role to act so they don‚Äôt prevent transactions from happening

1. Our last class ended with a discussion of executive compensation. Let‚Äôs continue with that topic, but we now turn to the other theme of this class: **regulatory arbitrage**. **Economically similar arrangements are sometimes treated differently**. **This can happen because regulatory regimes are poorly drafted, not perfectly conceptualized, or require compromises to be administrable**. This class uses U.S. tax law as a recurring case study of this more general point. 

   As an illustrative example, assume that Emma Employee works for Barbara Boss. Assume also that the tax rate for each of them is 50% in order to keep the numbers round. (The top federal rate for individuals in the U.S. actually is 37%.)

   1. When Barbara pays Emma a salary of $100,000, does Emma pay tax on it?  
- Yes. $50K in tax

  2. Someone who receives a gift is not taxed under U.S. law, so Emma makes the following suggestion to Barbara. ‚ÄúFrom now on, instead of paying me $100,000 in salary, why don‚Äôt you pay me $80,000? And on my birthday, you can give me a $20,000 gift.‚Äù Would this reduce her tax bill? (There is a special rules under U.S. law treating gifts from employees as taxable, but please ignore this rule in answering this question.)  
- Yes, she would pay $40K in tax  
- **This is an economically similar situation (same total salary amount) with different tax treatment**  
- But in reality gifts from your employer are always taxable

  3. Do you agree with the following statement? ‚Äú**When economically similar arrangements are treated differently, a change in the way the transaction is structured can yield dramatically better (or worse) treatment**.‚Äù  
- True.

2. Let‚Äôs now view this situation from Barbara‚Äôs perspective, since Emma is not the only one who might be affected by her idea.  
   1. Assume that Barbara brings in $300,000 in revenue for the work that she and Emma do together. Does Barbara pay tax on the entire $300,000? What does the term ‚Äúdeduction‚Äù mean? Can an employer deduct salary payments?  
- She does not pay tax on the entire $300K  
- She can deduct the salary payments of $100K and other expenses she incurs in generating the revenue. She is taxed on $200K  
- **A deduction is an amount taken out of your gross income which you will be taxed on**  
  * **You can subtract the cost of earning income so you are only taxed on profit**

  2. Can Barbara deduct the $20,000 gift? (By analogy, do you expect to be able to deduct the cost of a wedding present that you give your friend? Or a birthday present you give your mother?)  
- Gifts are not deductible  
  * Money deducted must be for the generation of income

  3. How, if at all, would Barbara be affected if she agrees to Emma‚Äôs request to receive an $80,000 salary and a $20,000 gift, instead of a $100,000 salary? Would Barbara change her own tax bill?  
- Barbara would pay more in taxes because she cannot deduct the $20K gift in the new proposal  
  * She pays an extra $10K in taxes  
  * Emma saves $10K in taxes  
    * **The tax savings to Emma is matched by an $10K increase in tax to Barbara**  
    * **Benefit to one perfectly offsets cost to another**

  4. Do you agree with the following statement? ‚Äú**In deciding whether a tax strategy should be implemented, tax planners cannot focus on only one party to the transaction. Instead, they need to think about the tax implications for *all* parties**.‚Äù  
- True.

  5. Assume that Barbara lives in another country, which allows employers to deduct the cost of gifts to employees. Would this affect Barbara‚Äôs willingness to accept Emma‚Äôs proposal to earn only $80,000 in salary and to receive a $20,000 gift?  
- Yes, Barabara would be more willing to pay Emma with the part salary part gift transaction because it doesn‚Äôt cost her anything  
- Different rules change the result

  6. The same facts as in ‚Äúe.‚Äù What if Barbara suggested $80,000 of salary and a $15,000 gift? Would Emma prefer that to $100,000 of salary? Why would Barbara prefer this?  
- In this situation, Emma‚Äôs after-tax income is $55K. This is better than the $50K of after-tax income she would have if she accepted $100K of pure salary  
- In this situation, Barbara can deduct $80K from her taxes and pays Emma $95K. This is better than deducting $100K and paying Emma $100K  
  * By switching to an approach where you are giving part salary part gift, you are reducing Emma‚Äôs taxes  
  * Barbara is sharing the tax savings (reduction of $5K in salary in exchange for $5K in tax savings)  
    * **If only one party in the transaction collects the tax benefit, they may not keep the entire benefit. The other party, if needed to cooperate to do it, will share the surplus**

  7. Do you agree with the following statement? ‚Äú**When parties to a transaction can restructure it to generate more favorable tax treatment for one of the parties, they are likely to share the tax benefit. So even if only one of the parties can claim the benefit, they are likely to adjust the pre-tax cash flows to share this benefit with others, whose cooperation is needed to generate the tax benefit**.‚Äù

- Yes.

3. **Exclusion / Valuation:** The planning discussed in questions 1 & 2 takes advantage of **exclusions**. Taxpayers strive to improve their economic situation in ways that are not included in ‚Äúgross income‚Äù for tax purposes (e.g., a gift). Planners also take advantage of **opportunities to understate the value of what a taxpayer has received, effectively excluding a portion of this value**.  
   1. Continuing with the saga of Emma, assume she works for ABC Co and her salary is $100k. Emma needs a car, and a new one costs $40k. If Emma asks ABC Co to cut her salary to $60k, and to buy her a $40k new car, do you think her tax treatment changes? In other words, if someone is paid with goods or services, instead of cash, are they taxed on the value of the goods and services?  
- Emma‚Äôs tax treatment will not change. She is still taxed on $100K because goods and services as salary are taxable  
  * **The fact that you aren‚Äôt paid in cash does not mean you do not pay tax. You are taxed on the value of these goods and services**  
- **Exclusion \= A source of value that you do not pay tax on**

  2. Emma reads an article, which says that new cars lose 20% of their value as soon as they are driven off the lot (because they become used cars, so their quality becomes more suspect). So Emma changes the request in ‚Äúa.‚Äù: ‚ÄúPlease pay me $60k in cash and a car. But instead of letting me pick it up from the dealer, ABC should buy it, keep it for a week (without using it), and then give it to me.‚Äù What is Emma‚Äôs goal in making this suggestion?  
- **Emma is trying to reduce her tax bill by lowering the value of the car when she receives it**  
- She will be taxed on $60K cash \+ the value of the used car (instead of the new one) which will be around $32K (20% of $40K new car is lost) 

  3. Let‚Äôs consider the effect on ABC. If the car is worth $30k when they transfer it to Emma, how much is their compensation deduction?  
- $30K  
- This situation is equally bad for ABC as it is good for Emma

  4. Is there an additional deduction that ABC might claim here? (Hint: Did they lose money on the car?)  
- ABC can claim $10K of depreciation on loss on car (Paid $40K and gave to her with $30K \= $10K loss)  
  * Employer gets a $100K total deduction, while employee gets $90K salary to be taxed on  
- **Valuation \= Manage valuation decreases tax bill**

4. **Conversion:** Another type of tax planning strives to **convert income that the tax system classifies unfavorably to income that is classified more favorably**.

   1. In the U.S., there are two types of income. One is ‚Äúordinary income,‚Äù which includes salary income. The maximum rate is 37% under current law. The other is ‚Äúcapital gain,‚Äù which generally is a taxpayer‚Äôs profit from an investment. (This is oversimplified, but it is approximately right). The maximum rate for capital gain is 23.8%. Which income would a taxpayer usually rather receive?

- Capital gain

  2. Emma (from the questions above) thinks the stock of ABC Co. is going to appreciate in value. She spends $10k of her $100k salary to buy stock (100 shares at $100 per share). The stock appreciates to $15k, so she sells it for a $5k profit. Is her profit taxed as capital gain or ordinary income?  
- $5K capital gain if she held it for a year or more

  3. Instead of receiving cash and using it to buy stock, Emma asks ABC to reduce her cash salary by $10k (to $90k) and to give her 100 shares of ABC (worth $10k). ABC agrees, but with a condition: Emma keeps this stock only if she continues to work for the company for 12 months. After 12 months, the stock is worth $15k. How is the stock taxed? (Hint: When employees receive stock or other property subject to ‚Äúvesting‚Äù‚Äîthat is, they don‚Äôt keep it unless they satisfy the condition, which often is about staying in their jobs‚Äîthey don‚Äôt pay tax until the property vests, and then they pay tax based on its value at the time it vests.)  
- Without the vesting component, this is all treated as cash salary because what matters is how you earned it  
  * **If you get property from your employer in exchange for services, you are taxed as if you earned cash ‚Äì ordinary**  
  * **If that property appreciates, you are taxed as if you invested in that property ‚Äì capital gain**

- **If you get something subject to vesting, you do not pay tax until the condition is satisfied. The amount of tax is determined by the value at the time the condition is satisfied**  
  * When you get property in your job (stock) which is subject to vesting, you pay tax later when it vests  
  * Retention device  
- **$90K ordinary initially; $15K ordinary after one year when it vests (treated at vesting as if she just got all the stock now for services)**  
  * **This is bad for Emma because the $5K increase in the stock is treated as ordinary income (because condition has not yet been satisfied), but if it could have been treated as capital gain if she didn‚Äôt have the vesting condition because it is property that increased in value**  
- Increases her tax bill by converting $5K from capital gain to ordinary income

  4. U.S. tax law offers someone in Emma‚Äôs position a choice. If she receives property subject to a vesting requirement, she can make a so-called ‚Äú**83(b) election,‚Äù which allows her to pay tax on the value of the property when she receives it‚Äînot when it vests. Any gain (or loss) after that is treated as capital gain (or loss)**. If Emma expects the stock to increase in value (e.g., from $100 to $150 per share), should she make an 83(b) election? Is this a conversion strategy?  
- Emma should make an 83(b) election to convert a portion of her salary to capital gain  
  * She pays her tax early based on the value of what it is initially worth before she has received it  
  * All gain later is capital gain  
  * This places her back in the position as if there was no vesting condition

- If stock decreases, this is bad because you will have capital losses (where you would rather have ordinary)  
- If you use the 83(b) election and quit so you don‚Äôt have the vested stock, you paid ordinary income tax on the stock‚Äôs value, and now you have a capital loss that is the amount of the stock that you lost

- To take advantage of the 83(b) election, taxpayer needs to elect, have the stock vest, and have the stock go up

  5. An employer deducts the same amount that an employee includes. So what is ABC‚Äôs deduction in ‚Äúc‚Äù (i.e., when Emma does not make an 83(b) election)? What about in ‚Äúe‚Äù (when Emma does make this election)? Which is better for the employer?  
- No 83(b) election \= $105K deduction ($15K for stock)  
- 83(b) election \= $100K deduction ($10K for stock)  
- **If the stock increases, 83(b) elections are bad for the employer  (reduces deduction) and good for the employee (reduces ordinary income)**  
  * **All parties aspect ‚Äì It is up to the employee (which binds the employer) if the employer chooses to include the vesting condition**

- **One of the ways to improve tax treatment is by reclassifying income into a favored category (conversion ‚Äì capital gain over ordinary)**  
    
- **Tax planning \= Look for economically similar situations that are taxed differently**  
  * **Exclusion/Valuation**  
  * **Conversion ‚Äì Changing the kind of income that it is**  
  * **Deferral**  
  * **Shifting**

5. **Deferral:** Another type of tax planning defers a tax. Let‚Äôs consider why is deferral valuable and how it is achieved.  
   1. Assume that your neighbor needs $10,000 to pay a bill. She is expecting a $10,000 payment to come in one year, but until then she is short of money. You, on the other hand, have $10,000 on hand. She asks to borrow the money from you.  
      1. What concerns, if any, might you have in making this loan?  
- Credit risk \= Might not get it back  
- Liquidity risk \= Might need the cash  
- Interest

  2. If you make this loan, will you do it for free?  
- No.  
- The use of money that is not yours is going to cost you something

  2. In general, why do taxpayers prefer to pay a tax next year, instead of this year (assuming the relevant tax rate does not change)?  
- **Tax deferral is an interest-free loan from the government, reducing the present value of the tax. Impact of deferral depends on:**  
  * **1\) Interest rates: Higher interest rates \= Lower Present Value of the tax**  
    * **This is the payment, the interest, you are avoiding**  
  * **2\) Time: Longer period \= Lower Present Value of the tax**  
    * **More time to invest without paying lender**

  3. You anticipate having to pay a $10,000 bill in one year. For simplicity‚Äôs sake, assume that your marginal tax rate is 50% and (quite unrealistically) that interest rates are 10%.  
     1. You decide to invest money today in order to fund this $10,000 obligation in one year Do you need to invest $10,000 today? More? Less?  
- Less. It will grow

  2. If you invest $9,524, what will your pretax return be after one year (assuming you earn 10%)? What will you have left after paying a 50% tax on this interest income?  
- Interest income \= $952.4  
- After-tax interest income \= $476.2  
- Total Investment \= $10,000.2  
  * 10% interest rate and 50% tax \= 5% after-tax interest rate

    3. What is the present value of $10,000 in one year, assuming a discount rate of 5%?  
- $9,524

  4. What if the obligation to pay is in two years, instead of in one year? Do you still have to set aside $9,524? If not, would you have to set aside more or less?  
- Less.

  5. How much will you have in two years if you set aside $9070 today?  
- $9,999.68

  4. Do you agree or disagree with the following statement: ‚Äú**Deferring a tax reduces its present value**.‚Äù  
- Correct.

  5. Is tax deferral more or less powerful as the deferral period grows? In other words, from the taxpayer‚Äôs perspective, is deferring a tax for two years better or worse than deferring it for one year (assuming the nominal amount of the tax does not change)?  
- Deferral is more powerful as the deferral period grows

- The longer the time, the lower the present value  
- The higher the interest rate, the lower the present value

- Deferral lowers the present value of the tax. Its impact depends on:  
  * Time ‚Äì Longer period \= Lower present value  
  * Interest Rate ‚Äì Higher interest rate \= Lower present value

  6. The same problem as above, but interest rates are 2%, instead of 10%. After paying the 50% tax on your interest income, how much will you have in one year if you set aside $9,901 today?  
- Interest income \= $198  
- After-tax interest income \= $99  
- Total Investment \= $10,000

  7. What is the present value of $10,000 in one year, assuming a discount rate of 1%?  
- $9,901

  8. Is tax deferral more or less powerful as interest rates rise?  
- Deferral is more powerful as interest rates rise  
  * You can invest deferred money that would otherwise be for taxes and make more with higher interest rates

  9. Fran is an independent contractor, who is doing some freelance editing for George. As a ‚Äúcash method‚Äù taxpayer, Fran owes tax on income only after she receives it. Fran has worked quite a number of hours, and is going to receive a significant payment. In late November, Fran mentions to you that she is about to send an invoice for this payment. What tax advice can you offer about the timing of when she should send this invoice?  
- Fran should send the invoice in January so that she can defer tax on this income until the following year

6. **Shifting:** Another type of tax planning shifts income from one taxpayer to another.  
   1. Diane is an investment banker, whose marginal income is taxed in the 37% bracket. Diane‚Äôs son, Eric, is a high school teacher, whose marginal income is taxed in the 22% bracket. Diane owns a portfolio of bonds, which pays $10,000 of interest each year. She wants to use this money to help Eric. What is her after-tax income from these bonds?  
- Diane after-tax interest income from bonds \= $6,300

  2. Instead of giving Eric her after-tax interest on the bonds, she considers giving him the bonds. When he receives the $10,000 interest payment, how much tax would Eric pay? How much would he have after paying this tax?  
- Eric tax on interest income \= $2,200  
- Eric after-tax interest income from bonds \= $7,800  
  * **By shifting the income to Eric, the collective tax bill between the two parties is reduced**  
  * **Shifting only exists in a system that uses different progressive rates**

  3. Either way, Diane is dedicating the return from this bond portfolio to Eric. Which of these alternatives reduces their combined tax liability and gets Eric the most money?  
- Giving the bonds to Eric reduces the combined tax liability and gets Eric the most money

  4. Are there reasons (having nothing to do with tax) why Diane might hesitate to adopt this tax planning strategy?  
- Yes. Diane will have to relinquish control over the bonds and might be nervous Eric will sell the bonds or do something irresponsible with them  
  * Additionally, if Diane needs the money from the bonds in the future she cannot access them

- **In tax planning, there are frictions (non-tax costs like giving control over) that would make taxpayers not choose the most tax efficient result**

7. **Shifting: Multinationals**. Parent, a U.S. company, sells phones to U.S. consumers, charging $500 per phone. The phones cost $100 to manufacture and another $100 in marketing and distribution costs, netting a $300 profit.

- **Businesses desire to earn money in districts with a lower tax rate**  
  * **This is a form of shifting**

  1. Absent any tax planning, the $300 will be taxed currently in the U.S. How much tax must Parent pay, assuming the U.S. corporate tax rate is 21%.  
- Tax per phone \= $63

  2. Parent incorporates a wholly owned subsidiary in Ireland, called IrishSub. The corporate tax in Ireland is 12.5%. (It may increase to 15% under a recent agreement, but the timing and details of this change are unclear.). Is Parent better off shifting income to IrishSub?  
- Yes. Parent will pay $37.5 in tax per phone if IrishSub reports this income

  3. Parent borrows funds from IrishSub and pays $100 of tax deductible interest per phone to IrishSub. How much taxable income does Parent now have in the U.S.? How much does it have in Ireland? By how much has Parent reduced its overall tax bill? (Assume that the U.S. does not tax IrishSub‚Äôs income, and that this tax would be triggered only if IrishSub pays a dividend to Parent. This used to be the rule in the U.S.)  
- Profit on phones \= $300 per phone  
- Taxable Income in US after $100 per phone loan \= $200 per phone  
- Taxable Income in Ireland \= $100 per phone  
- If $100 was taxable in US ü°™ Parent pays $21 per phone  
- But $100 is taxable in Ireland ü°™ Parent pays $12.5 per phone  
- Overall reduction in tax bill \= $8.5 per phone

- **Companies will take loans from their subsidiaries in low tax jurisdictions to lower their tax rates, shifting the taxable value of the loan to the lower tax jurisdiction**

  4. When Parent developed the current version of its phones, it included a number of technological innovations, which are patented. Although the engineers developing these innovations work in California, they work for IrishSub, which is responsible for coordinating Parent‚Äôs research. Assume Parent starts paying a $150 royalty to IrishSub for each phone it sells in the U.S. (Assume that Parent is also paying the interest described in part ‚Äúc.‚Äù) How much taxable income does Parent have in the U.S.? How much taxable income does IrishSub have in Ireland? Through these strategies, how much is the multinational reducing its total tax bill (including both Irish & U.S. taxes)?  
- Taxable Income in US \= $50 income per phone  
- Taxable Income in Ireland \= $250 income per phone  
- If $250 was taxable in US ü°™ Parent pays $52.5 per phone  
- But $250 is taxable in Ireland ü°™ Parent pays $31.25 per phone  
- Overall reduction in tax bill \= $21.25 per phone

- **Multinationals shift income from higher portions of the corporate structure to lower portions of their business without changing business structure**

  5. How do changes in the U.S. corporate tax rate affect a multinational‚Äôs incentive to engage in this shifting? For example, the U.S. corporate rate used to be 35%, and Congress reduced it to 21% in 2017\. Do you think that change reduced or increased the incentive of multinationals to shift income?  
- As the U.S. corporate tax rate becomes higher, multinational‚Äôs incentives to engage in shifting increase  
- This 2017 change reduced the incentive of multinationals to shift income

8. **Frictions: In analyzing whether tax planning is effective, we have to consider non-tax costs associated with the planning strategy, which economists call ‚Äúfrictions.‚Äù If those costs exceed the tax savings, a taxpayer will not pursue the strategy.**   
1. Recall the example of Fran, the independent contractor. What nontax costs might persuade her not to take your tax advice?  
- She needs the money now  
  * Maybe she needs to pay off credit card interest which would be higher than the tax savings  
    * Cost of the tax planning is too high because she had to pay the credit card interest

- If the goal is to minimize taxes ü°™ Wait to get the invoice in next year  
- If the goal is to maximize after-tax returns ü°™ Get the money now, forgo the tax planning, and pay off the credit card

- **Frictions: Cannot just look at tax in isolation**  
  * **The point is to have the most money at the end of the day considering all other goals**  
    * **Frictions are the other goals**

2. Recall the example of Diane, who is deciding whether to give the bond portfolio to her son, Eric. What is the most tax efficient step for her to take? Again, why might she not take this step (for nontax reasons)?  
- Most tax efficient step would be to give the portfolio to Eric, but she might not do this because she does not want to relinquish control of it  
  * There is a tax savings, but the non-tax cost (relinquishing control) dominates. Her goal is not to minimize taxes but to get to the lowest tax paid in light of all her goals

3. Recall the multinational that manufactures phones. Under current law, Parent‚Äôs engineers live in California, but Parent can shift the value from their work to Ireland, where it is taxed at a lower rate. What if Congress changed the law, requiring the engineers to work in Ireland in order for profits from their work to be taxed there? Would that matter?  
- That would matter because engineers might not want to move there  
  * The company might have to pay to move employees relocation  
  * The company might also lose talent  
  * These non-tax goals offset the cost

4. Do you agree or disagree with the following statement? ‚Äú**The goal of tax planning is not to minimize taxes, but to maximize after-tax returns**.‚Äù  
- True.


9. **All Parties:** As review, remember that **when analyzing whether tax planning is effective, we have to consider all parties to the transaction**.  
   1. Recall the example of Fran, the independent contractor. For tax reasons, she cares when she receives the payment. What about George, who is the one *making* this payment. Assume that he is also a cash method taxpayer, and that the payment is a business expense that he can deduct. Does he have a preference about timing? Is it the same as Fran‚Äôs preference?  
- George has a timing preference to pay in the current year because he can take an immediate deduction, reducing the present value of his tax  
- This is the opposite of Fran‚Äôs preference

- **The tax strategy that works for one party in the transaction may not work for all parties in the transaction**

  2. If Fran and George are in the same tax bracket, what tax advantage, if any, is there for them to adjust the timing of the payment?  
- There is no tax advantage to adjusting the timing of the payment

  3. What if Fran is doing this work for a nonprofit (e.g., a university)? Does that change the analysis?  
- If Fran is working for a nonprofit that would change the analysis because the nonprofit does not pay income tax so they have no preference when to deduct. Therefore, it would benefit Fran to include the income in the following year and make no difference to the nonprofit when it is included

  4. Have you heard the phrase ‚Äútax accommodation party‚Äù or ‚Äútax indifferent party‚Äù? Can you think of an example of one?  
- Tax Accommodation Party/Tax Indifferent Party (TIP) \= Timing of Inclusions/Deductions does not matter  
  * Native American Tribes  
  * Dutch banks  
  * Nonprofits

# 3: Residential Real Property (DD, Allocating Costs, Tax)

- Different aspects to a residential purchase. But fundamental problem is asymmetric information  
  * Seller knows more than buyer about the house  
- Can get third party monitor  
  * Home inspection  
- Due diligence process  
  * You ask questions, they answer  
  * **Sometimes what you learn is not the answer itself but lack of confidence from inconsistencies in other party‚Äôs answers which signals concerns**  
- Who should bear the costs of due diligence?  
  * **Need a principle to justify why the other party should bear the cost**  
    * Buyer should bear: This is a unique thing Buyer wants so they need to bear the cost  
      * Key is to ask, is this something anyone would ask for?  
        * Tank Unhealthy: Seller should bear it because all buyers will notice it and all buyers care  
        * Personal color preference: Buyer should bear. This is what it means to buy something. Then you can do whatever you want with it  
      * Other key question is, will others know?  
        * Can seller mask this to other buyers? If so, buyer more likely to bear the cost  
      * **Two components to getting seller to bear the cost:**  
        * **Future buyers need to know**  
        * **Future buyers need to care**  
    * Seller should bear: This is under seller‚Äôs control. Seller can do it at lower cost  
- What if there is information that is not available yet?  
  * This creates a challenge when one party is looking to close sooner rather than later  
  * Easiest action is to delay the closing  
  * **But if one party wants to close earlier, this is a source of negotiating power for the other side that doesn‚Äôt have the time pressure**  
  * Well, can the seller find another buyer?  
    * Yes, but this takes weeks. Have to restart due diligence process  
  * So buyer can:  
    * Assume the system doesn‚Äôt work and get credit for it  
    * But seller will want a contingent purchase price ($15K less at closing but $15K more when the systems can be tested and confirmed are working)  
      * Challenge: Will seller actually bring a lawsuit to collect the $15K? Probably not.  
        * Solution: Escrow account. Lawyers make judgment to release money to buyer or seller depending on if the system works or not  
      * Other challenge: The money is to fix the problems with the system but not aesthetic. Appearance is responsibility of the buyer. This tracks the question, ‚Äúwill another future buyer care‚Äù. **Need to be clear on language. Also, need to look ahead to situations to see what situations would arise where each side might have an argument as to saying it is functional/aesthetic so it can be drafted/agreed upon ahead of time**  
        * Functional ü°™ Seller pays  
        * Aesthetic ü°™ Buyer pays

- Real estate has:  
  * Asymmetric information, incentive problems  
  * Regulatory arbitrage tax planning opportunities

1. Bob Buyer wants to buy a weekend house. You are Bob‚Äôs lawyer. He sees a property that he really likes in Pretty County, which is 50 miles away from his year-round home. Susan Seller‚Äôs asking price is $1.1 million. Bob offers $950,000 and they settle on a price of $1,030,000. Susan is represented by Linda Lawyer.

   Before signing a contract, Bob needs your help with due diligence on the house, which was built in 1957\. An important issue is that most houses built then were heated by oil and had oil tanks buried underground. In newer houses, oil tanks are no longer buried underground because of concerns about contaminating soil and groundwater. Buyers looking at older houses sometimes hire experts, who use metal detectors to look for underground tanks. This inspection costs approximately $1,000. If a tank is found, the market practice is for sellers to bear the expense of removing it, which costs about $5,000, but can be significantly more if the tank has leaked and the soil needs to be remediated.

   

   In the property disclosure form, which sellers provide to anyone looking at a house, Susan answered ‚Äúno‚Äù to the question, ‚ÄúIs there an underground oil tank?‚Äù

   

   Before signing a contract to buy the house, Bob hires Ira Inspector to look for potential defects in it. In his inspection report, Ira observes that the oil tank currently is in the garage. But he sees pipes going from the garage into the ground, which is a sign that there could be an old oil tank there. ‚ÄúI‚Äôm pretty sure there was an underground tank,‚Äù Ira tells Bob. ‚ÄúOtherwise, what are those pipes for?‚Äù

   Bob relays this question to Susan, who answers: ‚ÄúThere aren‚Äôt any pipes.‚Äù When Bob sends a picture, Susan answers: ‚ÄúI don‚Äôt know, but maybe those pipes lead to a propane tank. We use propane to heat the pool.‚Äù Ira says that this doesn‚Äôt make sense, since the pool is quite a distance from the garage. 

   

   Susan follows up with another email: ‚ÄúSpoke to the oil company that has supplied oil to the house since I bought it. They re-used the tank that was there and relocated to existing location. Neither I nor they have any knowledge of an underground tank. Further, during construction, we excavated around the entire house and none was found.‚Äù

   Bob wonders where the tank was before it was moved. Was it underground, so that it might have left contaminants in the soil? Through his broker, Bob asks Susan whether the tank that was relocated used to be underground. Susan replies: ‚ÄúWhen we bought the house in 1992, the oil tank was in the original garage and was above ground. When we expanded the house, we knocked down the garage and built the new one and relocated the tank to where it is today.‚Äù

   

   Bob asks Susan whether she would put a representation in the contract, saying that there is no oil tank and if one is later found, she will pay to clean up any environmental damage. Susan says that all she would say in the contract is, ‚ÄúTo the best of my knowledge, there is no underground tank,‚Äù and that this representation would ‚Äúnot survive the closing.‚Äù This means that if one is found after closing, she is not responsible for any harm it might have caused.

   1. Based on these responses, do you think Bob should conduct a scan for an old underground oil tank?  
- Yes, Susan‚Äôs explanation for the no oil tank is changing. She is slippery, making excuses  
  * **People are smart enough not to admit something they do not want you to know, but if you keep asking the same question in different ways and you are getting inconsistent answers, then this makes it look questionable**  
- **You can‚Äôt investigate every issue but you can investigate some. Therefore, to figure out which ones to look into, you want to see if there are inconsistent answers regarding one issue**  
  * **Also, Susan is not willing to take responsibility for the possibility of the tank**

  2. Do you think it is reasonable for Bob to ask Susan to bear some of the cost of the scan? As Bob‚Äôs lawyer, please offer arguments explaining why Susan should share in the cost.  
- Yes. Susan should bear some of the cost because it is an important component to understanding Bob‚Äôs liability and thus a pre-requisite for him buying the house. Additionally, Susan will not give the representation that Bob asked for  
- This might protect both parties‚Äô interests

- **If you are so sure that there is no tank, give a real representation**  
  * **If you say ‚Äúbest of your knowledge‚Äù that looks like you are not sure**  
- **The answers Susan has given are flawed, inconsistent**  
  * **Expose Susan for not having full information (she said there are no pipes, but I found pipes)**  
- **Eventually, could compromise. Bob could cover the cost of the scan and if he finds a tank, Susan bears the cost of removing it and the scan**  
  * **You should agree to this because you said there is no tank**  
    * **Their comfort with paying for the tank if there is one will tell you how confident they are there isn‚Äôt one**

- **You can learn just by the effort to get information the comfort level the other party has (if their story keeps changing, inconsistent replies)**  
- **During negotiation, the positions the other party takes can shed light on things too (why are you only willing to represent ‚Äúto the best of your knowledge‚Äù if you actually know)**

  3. Assume that Bob does a scan and finds an oil tank, and Susan agrees to remove it. How might this interaction affect Bob‚Äôs dealings with Susan on other issues?  
- Bob will trust Susan less in future dealings when Susan makes claims about the property  
  * **One party misleading will create distrust on what else there is in terms of other problems with the deal** ü°™ Bob should get a significant discount or walk from the transaction

2. The same facts as in problem 1\. While inspecting the property, Ira notices another issue: the current oil tank (which, as noted above, is in the garage) is not installed properly. Exhaust fumes are not vented out of the garage. Since the garage is attached to the house, Ira says there is a risk that exhaust fumes will vent into the house, something he says is unhealthy and potentially dangerous.  
   1. What should Bob‚Äôs position be about the venting issue with the current oil tank?  
- Bob must ask Susan to fix the tank before move-in at her expense or at least compensate him after closing for costs to fix the tank‚Äôs ventilation himself  
  * Why should Susan pay?  
    * Health matter ‚Äì Regulations will require it  
    * Bob will walk without this fix  
    * **Not just Bob but all future buyers will want this fixed. Therefore, Susan will have to fix this to sell to anyone**  
      * Not just I want this but everyone who is in my position will want this

  2. Assume that Bob does not like the color and appearance of the oil tank. Is it likely that Susan will bear the expense of replacing it? Is the analysis the same as the request in ‚Äúa‚Äù about venting the tank?  
- No. Venting of the tank is a safety hazard that is necessary to purchasing a home. Color and appearance of the tank are less important  
  * Color and appearance are specific to Bob‚Äôs aesthetic preference. Unlike health where the preferences are general, the preferences here are unique to this one party

- **Unique preferences (color) ü°™ Seller does not have to do it or discount because future buyers will not require this**  
- **General preferences (health) ü°™ Seller will have to do it or discount because future buyers will require this so to sell at all, seller must fix**  
    
3. The same facts as in problem 1\. Ira also finds mold growing in the crawl space under the house. This means there are likely to be mold spores in the air inside the house, something that is not healthy for anyone but is especially an issue for Bob and his family, who have allergies and various respiratory problems.   
     
   Ira outlines two steps to eliminate the mold. First, the area must be cleaned thoroughly by professionals who specialize in remediating mold. This step costs approximately $5,000. Second, to prevent growth of mold in the future, a pump must be installed to remove water from below the house and a dehumidifier must be installed to remove moisture in the air. This step costs $2,000.  
     
   In response, Susan says, ‚ÄúThat‚Äôs not mold. It‚Äôs just a discoloration, which has been there since we bought the house thirty years ago. If it bothers you, I will ask my housekeeper to scrub it thoroughly with bleach and paint over it.‚Äù   
     
1. If Bob walks away from this deal, will other buyers care as much about the mold as Bob?  
- They will not care as much about the mold because they do not have the same health pre-dispositions, but they still should care  
  * Suggests that Bob can get Susan to pay for some but not all of it

2. If Bob walks away, is another buyer likely to detect the mold?  
- This is important because to have Susan do it, Bob needs to show Susan that other buyers will know about the issue, and they will also ask for the same thing  
- Susan will have a chance to paint over it, making it for another buyer to detect the mold  
  * Susan‚Äôs ability to paint over it in the future will undercut Bob‚Äôs position in the negotiation because future buyers will not be able to detect and be placed in Bob‚Äôs position  
- **The issue that, once identified, is easier to conceal from the next buyer harms the current buyer‚Äôs position**

- Note: Poor ventilation, mold is uninteresting if you are a buyer who wants to tear down the house and build something new. Susan might want to hold out for someone who won‚Äôt care about any of these issues and is going to tear down  
  * But then again someone who is looking to tear down the house, is buying just the land so the price will probably be lower than the person who wants to keep the house

3. Assuming that Susan does the same analysis of ‚Äúa‚Äù and ‚Äúb,‚Äù what do you expect her position to be? Will she be willing to address this issue?  
- Susan should address this issue by bearing some but not all of the cost. Clearly, the mold does not bother her as much as it would bother Bob because she has been living with it for 30 years. However, she has a willing buyer who would be willing to walk away over this issue 

4. If Susan agrees to address the issue, why might Bob worry that she won‚Äôt address it as carefully as he would? If he has this concern, what alternative can he suggest to resolve the issue? (Hint: With this alternative, he would be the one deciding the specifics of what to do.)  
- Bob might be concerned that she won‚Äôt address the issue thoroughly and might just try to cover it up because:  
  * 1\) she has incentive to hide the flaw as the seller with the least amount of cost  
  * 2\) she suggested trying to cover it up by scrubbing it out  
- Bob might want to seek an alternative where he hires someone to resolve the problem by treating the mold and installing the pump, and he has Susan compensate him for conducting this

- Bob may want a top notch solution because of his health problems. Susan will want to just do a good-enough treatment that most other buyers would be happy about  
  * Bob should then propose to get the cost of the good-enough treatment in a discount of the purchase price and then do the top-notch treatment himself  
    * **Should the seller complete the task or should the purchase price be adjusted?:**  
      * **Adjust the price where the buyer has a unique thing that this buyer wants and others would want a generic response (but the generic response is not good enough for the buyer). Then after the purchase price is lowered by the amount of the generic treatment, buyer will complete the unique treatment at his cost**


4. The same facts as in problem 2\. The house has a pool--a nice feature for a weekend house. Bob wants the pool to be attractive. Resurfacing the pool costs approximately $10,000.  
     
   Bob also wants to be sure that the pool‚Äôs systems are in working order. For example, the heater should work, the drainage pipes should not be clogged or leak, and the motorized filter, which cleans the pool, should also work. Repairing all of these systems would cost approximately $15,000.  
     
   In addition, the pool needs all the necessary safety features. For example, the pool needs to have a fence, so no one accidentally falls in and so no uninvited guests use the pool and are injured. Indeed, to get home owners insurance, Bob needs to show the insurance company that there is a fence. It costs $2,000 to install a fence.  
     
   The pool also should have two drains, instead of one, in order to reduce the suction from each drain. Otherwise, the suction might be strong enough to pull swimmers down to the drain, causing them to drown. Installing a second drain costs $8,000.  
     
   Bob is doing his due diligence in late January. The pool has been covered for the winter, and there is snow on the ground, so Bob is limited in the due diligence he can do. For example, although he can have the cover removed, the pool systems are not designed to work in freezing temperatures. Turning the systems on when the temperature is less than 50 degrees Fahrenheit--as almost certainly will be the case in late January--can cause the systems to break. Bob is willing to delay the closing until the weather is warmer, but Susan wants the closing to take place no later than March 1\.  
     
1. Which issues can Bob explore before March 1? Which ones cannot be investigated before March 1?  
- Can explore:  
  * Attractiveness  
  * Safety features (fence)  
  * Amount of drains  
- Cannot explore:  
  * Working order of systems

2. If Susan insists on closing before March 1, what should Bob suggest as a way to address his concerns about the pool and, specifically, about issues that cannot be investigated before March 1?  
- Bob could just push the closing until the spring when he can check the working order of the systems (this is the lowest cost to both parties in this specific transaction)  
  * **By having the flexibility of time that Susan doesn‚Äôt have, Bob has additional leverage. He can just wait. Burden is on Susan to solve the problem**

- If Susan insists on closing now, then Bob is going to require that Susan bear the cost of the systems not working. Bob could agree to do the deal now and have it lowered by $15K, basically assuming that the systems are not working

- He could get Susan to provide a representation guaranteeing the pool systems are in good working order that survives closing and ensures that she will be responsible for any costs associated with repairment if they are not  
  * **$15K is put in escrow and if the system works, all of that money is released to Susan. If the repairs will cost $10K, Bob gets $10K, etc.**  
  * **This is better for Bob than disputing the charge later on and chasing her down**

- **Bob‚Äôs Options:**  
  * **1\) Just wait (knowing she cannot)**  
  * **2\) Knock down the price because I cannot test this (and you are the one rushing this so I will assume that the systems do not work)**  
  * **3\) Contingent purchase price ($15K less and if it works I get the money back) and Escrow account**  
    * **And if Susan says I don‚Äôt want to do escrow, Bob can go back and say let‚Äôs wait (this is a solution he can keep coming back to)**

3. Susan is willing to assure Bob that the pool systems work and are safe. ‚ÄúBut if you want the pool to look different, that‚Äôs your responsibility,‚Äù she says. ‚ÄúYou can make any changes you want to the appearance of the pool once you buy the house, but that expense is your responsibility.‚Äù Does this distinction seem reasonable? If so, how can Bob and Susan operationalize it?  
- Yes. **Every buyer wants the pool system to work. Not every buyer will have the same aesthetic preferences for how the pool looks**

- Include that language in the purchase agreement: ‚ÄúBuyer is responsible for all aesthetic changes‚Äù and Make sure that ‚Äúescrow account is just used for repairs and not aesthetic changes‚Äù

4. If Susan and Bob proceed with the idea in ‚Äúc,‚Äù can you predict any scenarios in which they disagree about whether an expense is addressing safety or aesthetic issues?  What language would you suggest to deal with this?  
- **On one hand, they may both agree to this in principle before the fact**  
  * **But Susan has incentive to say that all changes are aesthetic**  
  * **Bob has incentive to say that all changes are repairs/functionality**  
    * **They will disagree on how to classify expenses**  
    * **Lawyers must anticipate these pressure points and see where something might be unclear**

- Filtration systems can be behind the walls of the pool. What if that this system is not working, then you have to break and fix the walls?  
  * Seller: The wall of the pool was fixed and we replaced it with pure cement  
  * Buyer: The wall of the pool was fixed and we put tiles with diamonds in them  
  * **In fixing what is functional, you impact the aesthetic**  
    * **Solution: Not simply aesthetic is paid by buyer and functional is paid by seller. Instead, add ‚ÄúAny cost of aesthetic repairs necessary to restore the appearance of the pool to where it was aesthetically before the repair is the responsibility of the seller.‚Äù**


5. Same facts as above. In asking $1.1 million for the house, Susan has offered to include outdoor furniture, art, exercise equipment, beds, a dining room table, and other personal property. This personal property was worth approximately $50,000. Since this is a second home for Bob, he liked the idea of buying this personal property as well. When they settled on a price of $1,030,000 (as noted above), this included the personal property.   
     
   Before signing a contract, Bob reaches out to you. As a real estate lawyer, you know that New York (where Pretty County is located) has a ‚Äúmansion‚Äù tax. Under this tax, a buyer pays 1% of the value of any home with a purchase price of $1 million or more. This tax does not apply to a home with a purchase price of less than $1 million.  
- **Cliff Effect:** If the purchase price is under the edge of $1M, tax is $0 ($999,999 \= $0 tax)  
  * If the purchase price is over the edge of $1M, tax is 10% ($1,000,000 \= $10,000 tax)


  New York also charges an 8 percent sales tax on sales of personal property. While this obviously applies to purchases in a store, it also applies at garage sales and in other circumstances when one individual buys personal property from another, as long as the total price of all the property is more than a minimum amount (which you should assume here is $500).


  The town where the house is located also has an annual property tax of 3% of a house‚Äôs appraised value. The town‚Äôs last appraisal three years ago valued Susan‚Äôs house at $800,000, so she has been paying property taxes of $24,000 per year. The town is likely to use Bob‚Äôs purchase price as the valuation for his property taxes.


1. Susan‚Äôs lawyer sends over a contract, which includes the following language: ‚ÄúThe parties hereto acknowledge that there is no consideration attributable to the personal property included hereunder.‚Äù In light of this language, what taxes will Bob owe? Which will he avoid?  
- Owes:  
  * Mansion tax (1 percent) because $1.03M purchase price \= $10,300  
  * Property tax (3 percent of $1.03M) because of the new valuation \= $30,900  
- Avoids:  
  * Sales tax (8 percent) because the personal property is included in the purchase price of the home \= would have been $4K

2. What suggestion can you make that will reduce Bob‚Äôs tax bill in the coming years? Please note that Bob will still have to pay Susan a total of $1.03 million--that‚Äôs not negotiable--and that Bob is very conservative about tax planning. He will only do something that is clearly legal.  
- Bob should pay for the personal property separately than including it in the purchase price of the house  
  * This will increase his sales tax bill currently to $4,000, but it will avoid the mansion tax of $10,300 entirely and lower his property taxes for coming years by decreasing the valuation of the home at the time of sale to $980K (making the taxes in the following years $29,400 instead of $30,900)

- **Allocate the purchase price in different ways can help you avoid certain taxes**

- If mansion tax was gone, **are you better off allocating the price to the personal property or the real property**?  
  * **The answer has to do with how long you plan to hold the house**:  
    * The rate on the **sales tax is higher but it is only paid once**  
      * If living in the house for short time ü°™ Minimize the personal property value ü°™ Lowers total tax by lowering one-time higher sales tax  
    * The rate on the **property tax is lower but is paid multiple times**  
      * If living in the house for long time ü°™ Maximize the real property value ü°™ Lowers total tax by lowering repeat lower property tax

- OH:  
  * Home buyers are usually going to want to allocate personal property to the purchase price so they don‚Äôt pay sales tax on that as long as doing so would not put them over the amount of paying some other tax like the mansion tax. Real property contracts allocate $0 to personalty but make specific requests for what needs to stay in the purchase agreement. Isn‚Äôt this a way that the government tax collector could police how much to allocate to personal property?  
    * Yes. But the valuation you show the government has to be plausible (although you might be able to say that the seller just left the stuff for you)  
    * But also remember the other tax (mansion maybe) of property tax  
      * Here, it depends on if the one-time upfront higher sales tax is better than allocating that money to purchase price and paying more in the recurring lower property tax several times  
      * You will know the answer to this by thinking about both the percentages of each tax and the number of years you live in the house (the number of times the recurring property tax is paid)

3. Before the closing, there is some damage to the house in a storm. Susan knows that the damage is her responsibility but she has moved away and is not in a position to supervise the repairs herself. She also realizes that Bob will want the opportunity to ‚Äúdo it his way.‚Äù She offers to give Bob a $2,000 credit. ‚ÄúThe purchase price will be the same,‚Äù she says, ‚Äúbut we‚Äôll treat the amount you will pay your contractors as part of the purchase price.‚Äù Bob mentions this to you and you say, ‚Äúwe should do it differently as a way to reduce your taxes.‚Äù What is the better alternative?  
- Bob should get Susan to knock $2,000 off of the purchase price  
  * This lowers the mansion tax and property taxes in subsequent years by lowering the valuation at the time of sale


6. The same facts as in above. Susan‚Äôs lawyer sends over the attached draft, which (among other things) is supposed to address the following issues. How does the draft treat these issues. What changes (if any) would you suggest for each?  
   1. Ira‚Äôs inspection yields a list of minor repairs that are needed for the house to be in working order. Susan commits to do them before the closing, and Bob wants her to use a licensed contractor for these repairs (i.e., and not to do them herself or to ask someone who is not an expert to do them). Bob wonders how he can confirm that all the work was done, especially since some of it involves electrical work inside the walls of the house. What does the draft provide? What change, if any, would you suggest? (Hint: Focus on two clauses in the rider.)  
- Rider 4: ‚ÄúThe Seller represents that the appliances and systems are in working order and will be in working order at closing.‚Äù  
- Rider 5: ‚ÄúPrior to the closing of title, the Seller will complete the work on the punch list attached to this Agreement as Appendix B at the Seller‚Äôs sole cost and expense.‚Äù

- Bob might want to add that all work must be completed by a licensed contractor that he approves of AND that he maintains a warranty that the work continues to operate normally 6 months after closing  
  * He can also add a clause that allows him to inspect the work after it is complete  
    * Need to add inspection contingency into form contract **(align form contract with rider)**

  2. Susan built a guest apartment over the garage and applied for a permit from the town, which would allow her (and any subsequent owner) to rent out the apartment to tenants. Bob wants to make sure that the town has, in fact, issued a permit (or a so-called ‚Äúcertificate of occupancy‚Äù) for the apartment. What does the draft provide? What change, if any, would you suggest? (Hint: Focus on the rider.)  
- Rider 1: ‚ÄúAt the time of closing, the final Certificates of Occupancy shall have been issued for all work performed at the premises **for which building permits have been issued**.‚Äù  
  * Problem: ‚ÄúI have a permit when I have a permit.‚Äù But what we want to know is: do we have a permit when I need a permit?  
    * **Watch for things that sound like things that sound like but don‚Äôt mean what they are supposed to say**  
- Bob might want to take out the building permits part and get a representation that says that ‚ÄúAll work performed at the premises maintains a certificate of occupancy which survives closing‚Äù  
  * ‚ÄúI have permits for all improvements and changes for which a permit has been required including the shed.‚Äù

  3. Bob is borrowing money to buy the house. He wants to be excused from the contract if after reasonable efforts he is unable to secure a mortgage. What does the draft provide? What change, if any, would you suggest? (Hint: Focus on the main contract.)  
- The draft does not provide for a mortgage contingency, but Bob will want to get one to ensure that if he does not get financing he can get his downpayment back  
- **Downpayment: Allows seller to keep downpayment without lawsuit (form of liquidated damages)**  
  * Does not apply with mortgage contingency. Without funding after best efforts, you get downpayment back  
  * Cash buyers do not need mortgage contingencies. So their downpayment is firm  
    * In exchange, cash buyer will want a lower the price

  4. Bob‚Äôs lawyer does a ‚Äútitle search,‚Äù in which a company searches public records to confirm that Susan actually is the owner of the property and that the town‚Äôs building department has not found any building code violations. The search reveals that Susan does not have a permit for the shed next to the house. In light of the above violation, Bob still wants to buy the house, but he wants Susan to get this permit before closing. What remedy does the draft provide? What change, if any, would you suggest? (Hint: Focus on the main contract‚Äôs Sections 6 and 10a.)  
- Section 6: The draft provides for a termination clause if the buyer‚Äôs searches do not contain ‚Äúreasonably satisfactory title‚Äù  
- Bob will want to keep the provision that allows the Seller to perfect title but in case she cannot, Bob‚Äôs remedy should be a re-negotiation of the purchase price subject to the additional liability  
  * **The remedy the client wants needs to be in the section**

  5. Bob wants to do a ‚Äúwalk through‚Äù the day before the closing to make sure all the work that Susan promised to do has, in fact, been done and that there are no new problems with the house. If he finds any issues, he wants to delay the closing until Susan has addressed them. What does the draft provide? What change, if any, would you suggest? (Hint: Focus on the main contract‚Äôs Sections 7 and 15).  
- Section 7: The draft does not allow for an inspection contingency  
- Section 15: Daily penalty for delayed closing  
  * **Daily amount for delayed damages is good, but he needs to make it clearer that the seller in this case must pay the damages for a delay if she does not deliver something by closing**  
  * ‚ÄúDelays are the responsibility of the seller if seller has not done \_\_\_\_.‚Äù  
- Bob will want to add an inspection contingency and carve out an exception to the delayed closing penalty in case he finds something the day of that he justifiably believes needs to be remedied where the seller is responsible for delay damages

# 4: M\&A (DD solving asymmetric information)

- **Why acquire a business?**  
  * **Can‚Äôt do everything with a small business, many contracts because there are information/incentive problems that contracting doesn‚Äôt solve but owning a business might solve these**  
    * **Hard to specify in a K what the business wants in detail. And then we don‚Äôt know if the K has properly been honored**  
    * **Owning not only enforces quality but also enforces proprietary nature of the work**  
- **Alternatives to K:**  
  * **Repeat Relationship and Reputation: Evolving understanding between the parties can work**  
  * **Instead Make instead of Buy: Have employees instead of independent vendors with K‚Äôs**  
- **If you want to buy (do it yourself), acquisition is solution to information/incentive problems of contracting, but it is in itself a host to information/incentive problems**  
  * The fact that the seller is willing to sell should create skepticism because they know more than you  
  * **Due diligence: Key mechanism to address information problems so you can continue to do deals**  
    * **DD differs from stock to asset deals**

  

- DD in M\&A  
  * It is a cooperative process ü°™ Gilson  
    * Good news increases the price so seller shares  
    * Bad news can be in seller‚Äôs interest to share so that buyer does not assume the worst if seller is visibly hiding something which would lower price (or buyer walk away)  
    * Mutual interests pursued  
  * It is a contentious process ü°™ Critique Gilson
