    * If there are ways seller can avoid sharing bad news without seeming like they are hiding something (using a neutral reason to avoid sharing information), then that is best for seller  
      * This creates a challenge for the buyer. Then buyer will need to evaluate if seller is trying to make an excuse or is seller is actually cautious about sharing that information for a good reason  
    * Buyer then makes counter-proposal that would be appealing if seller is being honest about motivations for sharing information, but not appealing if seller is concealing for bad reason  
      * Third party to look at files: If seller says no, still could be that they are hyper-cautious but more likely that they are hiding something  
  * Seller should pay to get the information  
    * Adjust purchase price to cover it  
      * This is unusual in reality unless it is a major item  
    * If seller is trying to hide something about the litigation, they will allow buyer to look at their mounds of files which buyer will not be able to do  
- Reputation for being honest and forthcoming is important because you will be a repeat player, but these strategies might be used by others so need to be aware of them

1\. 	Steve runs a company called Orange, which makes computers, phones, and other consumer electronics products. Steve‚Äôs goal is to distinguish the company by offering unique features and reliable, high-quality products. He wants to make sure the company‚Äôs products look, feel, and really are different. In addition to the hardware, he believes they need to have an operating system and other software that are different from what other companies use.

1. You are Steve‚Äôs lawyer. He is considering forming a contract with a software developer, asking them to develop an innovative operating system. He asks you to draft a contract. You ask him to define what he‚Äôs looking for and he says, ‚ÄúIt has to be unique and innovative.‚Äù You ask him to be more specific, and he says, ‚ÄúIt has to be really good, creative, and different.‚Äù‚Äô What advice do you give him? Is a contract going to be effective in policing the quality standards Steve has in mind?

- Steve needs to add specificity to the contract to ensure that there are explicit obligations that can be carried out and he can have recourse for them if they are not  
- **Limits on K‚Äôs: In order to depend on a K, you need to be able to be specific about what performance requires**  
  * Need businesspeople to create more specificity that can be incorporated into a K  
  * Steve might resist because he is a creative genius. He doesn‚Äôt reduce his vision to specific words

2. Aside from a contract, do software developers have other (not legally- enforceable) reasons to want to impress Steve?  
- Yes, the developers‚Äô future relationship relies on Steve to think they are doing a good job  
- **Contract is very important, but it plays only a partial role in ensuring that commercial actors fulfill each other‚Äôs expectations**  
  * **Repeat Players**: Powerful role in markets  
    * You don‚Äôt need to fully specify quality if you are in repeat relationship because if the vendor disappoints, they will not be offered work with the buyer in the future  
  * **Reputation**: The reputation of the vendor to other sellers will guarantee them future business

3. If Steve can‚Äôt rely on a contract or on non-legal reasons to ensure that the software is innovative and high quality, what else can Steve do?  Do you see a reason why the business of making software might be paired with another business, so that the same owners own both?  
- **Make or Buy Decision: In principle, you could rely on K‚Äôs/reputation/repeat relationships with other businesses for all of the things you need, but you can also do everything yourself**  
  * **You can be a small company and you outsource everything (BUY)**  
    * **Other companies‚Äô employees do the functions**  
  * **You can be a big company and do everything yourself (MAKE)**  
    * **Your employees do the functions**

- **What types of factors determine the make or buy decision?**  
  * **If what the business needs is easy to specify with detail in a K ü°™ Strengthens the case to buy, rely on outsiders**  
  * **Where repeat relationships and K is not enough ü°™ Strengthens the case to make yourself**  
    * **The decision to make or buy often involves addressing asymmetric information and incentive problems**  
  * In a world where there is perfect information (no information/incentive problems), then you would not have big companies because everyone would transact with through K‚Äôs

4. What is ‚Äúvertical integration‚Äù?  What economic problems is it designed to solve? Have you heard the phrase ‚Äúmake or buy decision‚Äù?

- **MAKE: Vertical Integration \= A business strategy for a company to own multiple stages of production/supply chain**  
  * **Vertical Integration solves the problem of diverging incentives and aligns them by creating the same goals/track for different companies**  
  * **Not necessarily serving more customers (like horizontal) but when we serve customers, we are doing it all ourselves**

- **Horizontal integration \= We have one stage of production and buy another business that is in the same stage of production (bigger market share in the niche we are in)**

- **Why do people do M\&A? To solve information and incentive problems**  
  * **But when they do it, they create other information and incentive problems**

2\.     Assume that Jim runs a law firm. He has 40 associates, but he has more work than they can do.  Carla approaches Jim with the following idea: she runs an agency that can provide Jim with lawyers who take ‚Äúfreelance‚Äù assignments, which means they will come work temporarily for Jim (e.g., for a day or a few hours) to help with a specific assignment.  They can help Jim draft a specific contract or motion or a will.  Once they are done, they no longer work for Jim.

1. What might Jim like about this idea?

- He can complete additional work and make more revenue without extensive fees  
  * Flexibility tailored to his exact needs, reducing overhead costs  
  * Buy strategy

2. Why will Jim hesitate to make by contracting for labor instead of buy company?  
- **Information problem: Jim depends on people he didn‚Äôt vet or hire. He doesn‚Äôt know how good they will be at the job**  
- **Incentive Problem: The lack of long-term employment or commitment gives the employees less incentive to do good work**  
  * **Jim loses the repeat relationship with the individuals**

3. What else might Jim do if he has too much work?  What are the benefits and costs of this alternative, compared with the idea described above?

- If Jim hires more attorneys this may cost him more but these employees are more deeply tied to Jim and have incentive to do better work  
  * Make strategy

3\.     What is ‚Äúdue diligence‚Äù?  What problem is it supposed to solve? (Hint: It‚Äôs a fundamental theme of this course.) How does a buyer know that a seller is telling the truth when the seller provides information?

\-	Due diligence solves information asymmetry

\-	A buyer needs to verify the information a seller is giving him

4\.     Due diligence‚Äîand, more fundamentally, the information a buyer wants‚Äîcan vary with the deal structure. Assume that a company called ‚ÄúBuyer‚Äù wants to acquire a business called ‚ÄúSeller‚Äù for cash. Buyer can either buy the assets of Seller (an ‚Äúasset deal‚Äù) or Buyer can by the stock of Seller (a ‚Äústock deal‚Äù).

1. As a logistical matter, what is the difference between an asset and a stock deal? What, specifically, is Buyer buying in each case?

- Asset Deal \= Buyer transacts with the corporation that owns the assets; Purchasing physical components of the company; Seller remains the legal owner of the company  
- Stock Deal \= Buyer transacts with the shareholders who own the corporation; Purchasing the value of the company through equity from shareholders; Buyer becomes the legal owner of the company

2. Assume Seller did not properly file a patent.  Does Buyer need to worry about this in an asset deal? What about in a stock deal?

- Asset Deal \= Worried about this because the buyer is buying the patent as an asset  
- Stock Deal \= Worried about this because the value of the company can decrease, and buyer will be on the hook

- **The quality of the assets that come over to you matter in both the Asset and Stock deal**  
  * **In asset deal you buy the asset directly; In stock deal you are buying the corporate shell that holds the assets (so you will own it indirectly)**  
  * Quality of assets is relevant to both kinds of deals and DD must focus on quality of assets for both kinds of deals

3. Assume Seller has a cheap lease, but if Seller is no longer the counterparty on the lease, it expires. Does Buyer need to worry about this in an asset deal? What about in a stock deal?  
- Asset Deal \= Worried because seller will remain the counterparty as legal owner so to switch the lease counterparty you need to re-negotiate  
  * **The company the buyer did not buy is still the counterparty on these leases (likely you will have to re-negotiate. You don‚Äôt inherit the leases)**

- **Stock Deal \= Not worried because nothing is changing about the lease. The lease is still between seller and corporation. It is just that corporation has a new owner**  
  * **Lease is legally still in place**  
    * Sometimes a lease will have a change in control provision with a new ownership control where landlord requires re-negotiation for any change of control. This makes the stock deal and asset deal equivalent  
  * Stock deal is better here because you will keep the below market lease

4. Assume Seller has not paid its taxes for three years.  Does Buyer need to worry about this in an asset deal? What about in a stock deal?  
- Asset Deal \= Not worried about this  
  * Buyer will not take on Seller‚Äôs tax liabilities  
- Stock Deal \= Worried about this because seller‚Äôs liability can impact buyer‚Äôs current company

- **Key Reason people prefer Asset deals instead of stock:**  
  * **Asset deal \= Buyer gets assets of company without liabilities of company**  
    * **Easier to DD than a stock deal**  
  * **Stock deal \= Buyer gets assets of company with liabilities of company**  
    * **More DD required**

- One wrinkle: Tax liens give government interest in the buyer‚Äôs property after transfer. When assets are sold, government collects beforehand

- What if buyer has not paid its taxes for three years? Does seller need to worry about this in an asset deal? In a stock deal?  
  * **The legal situation of the buyer does not matter to the seller as long as the buyer pays**  
    * **DD is mostly the buyer understanding the seller and problems the seller presents**  
  * **BUT in a stock deal the seller cares about the buyer‚Äôs liabilities**  
    * **In a stock for stock deal, both parties conduct DD as if they are the buyer**  
      * **Actual buyer buying the business**  
      * **Seller is a buyer needing to understand the value of the stock they are purchasing in the new company**

5. Assume that one of Seller‚Äôs employees made inappropriate jokes in the workplace, and Seller just terminated his employment.  Does Buyer need to worry about this in an asset deal? What about in a stock deal?  
- Asset Deal \= Not worried about this  
- Stock Deal \= Worried about this because seller‚Äôs liability can impact buyer‚Äôs current company

- **If you buy the assets, the liabilities do not come with the assets in asset deal**  
  * **Past liabilities are not an issue in asset deal but future liabilities are**  
    * **Liabilities that arise after you bought the business are yours**  
    * So if the victim employee is continuing with the company and then decides to bring litigation after purchase, that would be an issue in an asset deal as well as stock deal

6. Assume Buyer is buying a drug patent from Seller.  There is uncertainty about whether the drug will work, so Buyer is paying a first installment now, and will pay another amount in two years.  The amount of this payment will depend on whether the drug works.  Assume also that Buyer has not paid its taxes for three years.  Does Seller need to worry about this in an asset deal? What about in a stock deal?

- **Since seller is not getting paid all at once, seller must do DD on buyer to make sure that buyer will have the ability to pay at the time the money is owed**  
  * **If seller is getting paid all at once, seller does not need to do a lot of DD on buyer. This changes with staged payment arrangement**  
  * **This is the case in both stock and asset deals** 

5\.  Seller is negotiating to sell a theme park to Buyer. It used to be very popular, but lately it has not been drawing the same crowds. Seller has built a new ride, based on a popular videogame, which will be the subject of a movie that will be released after the sale closes. Seller has produced the game for many years and also is producing the movie. The ride won‚Äôt open until after the movie opens‚Äîand, therefore, until after the sale.

A.  Buyer asks for information about the game, including the number of users over the past several years. Why do you think Buyer wants this information? Do you think Seller should provide it?

- Buyer wants this information because it will inform the buyer of the potential popularity of the ride and inform the price of the theme park  
- **Seller should provide this information to help buyer find a proper valuation for the ride and hopefully its popularity will convince buyer to pay more. Also, if seller hides this information or gives misleading answers, buyer will interpret that as the information is unfavorable to seller**  
  * Refusal to answer, ambiguous answers, misleading answers are not workable strategies for seller  
  * Seller‚Äôs information will make buyer more confident in paying a larger price for the theme park  
- Flat out lying in writing is not a successful strategy for seller because they become liable. But a more common strategy is to give a non-answer or exaggeration (which in turn is interpreted poorly by buyer)

B. Ron Gilson has argued that due diligence should be a cooperative process, since the parties have shared interests. Does the example in ‚ÄúA‚Äù support his view?

- Yes. Both buyer and seller have interest in sharing information because this builds the trust with the buyer that helps the seller get a better price

C. Buyer asks for information about the movie (which, again, has not been released), including information about the reaction of focus groups who have seen it. Why do you think Buyer wants this information? Why might Seller be more concerned about addressing Buyer‚Äôs questions about the movie? (Hint: is there a difference between offering facts, on the one hand, and predictions, on the other?)

- Offering predications are less reliable than offering facts  
  * If focus groups love the movie, seller will share information. Get buyer to pay more  
  * If focus groups hate the movie, seller will not want to share  
  * If focus groups are mixed, seller will want to share to build trust with the buyer to quell the buyer‚Äôs bad feeling about not having information (buyer will assume that the reaction from the focus group was awful) despite the fact that it was mixed and does not add to the case that it proactively helps  
- **There is reason for the seller to share fairly bad information because by not sharing it, buyer will think it is worse**

D.  Does ‚ÄúC‚Äù support or undercut Gilson‚Äôs claim that due diligence should be a cooperative process?

- **Gilson: Seller has an interest in buyer not assuming the worst about a situation. With good news, there is a shared interest in seller providing information. With bad news, there is still a shared interest in seller providing information because seller prevents buyer from assuming the worst**

- Gilson critique: If there is unknown information then seller bears the cost if it provides the rep. because seller doesn‚Äôt know what the outcome would be, and they could be liable in the future for providing that rep.  
  * At the same time, the seller bears the cost if they don‚Äôt provide that rep. because, like Gilson says, the buyer will walk or think the worst of the situation which is that something is wrong by them not providing it  
  * So, either way the seller bears the cost with unknown information  
    * Chess moves ‚Äì think ahead of what the other party will say when you ask for the rep. and how can you contradict that

6\. Carol is an entrepreneur who is developing a cure for the common cold.  She would like to sell her company, Seller, to Buyer, a pharmaceutical company.  Doug, who works for Buyer, wants to see the clinical trials for the new drug.  Carol knows that the results are mixed.  They suggest that the drug does cure colds, but they also suggest that the drug may have some serious side-effects. 

1. Without any legal constraints, what would Carol (or, indeed, an unethical version of Carol) want to say about the trials? Does she have an incentive to share good news? What about bad news?

- Carol would want to say that the drug cures colds and accomplishes its goal without sharing the news about negative side-effects  
  * She has incentive to share good news without bad news

2. Can Carol simply share the good news (‚Äúthe drug cures colds‚Äù) but lie about or omit the bad news (serious side-effects)?

- No. Carol must show the results of the clinical trials which will yield both the good and bad

3. Does Carol face any legal liability if she simply refuses to share any information about the trials? If Carol refuses, what will Doug assume about the trials?

- Carol does not face legal liability if she just does not share information about the trials at all  
- In this case, Doug will assume the trials show bad results

- **You are liable for what you say but not for what you refuse to say**  
  * **But then Buyer will assume that the lack of information is the worst scenario (cost will be the better price)**

4. Given your answer to C, do sellers have an incentive to share bad news, as well as good news?

- **Sellers have incentive to share bad news as well as good news to show buyers that they are not hiding anything. More information is better**

5. In principle, would your answer to ‚ÄúD‚Äù change if a seller is able to offer a justification for not to share information, which has nothing to do with the fact that this information might not be flattering?

- Yes. If the seller can justify why to not give the bad information, then the buyer will be less skeptical of the information omission

6. Building on ‚ÄúE,‚Äù can you think of a reason why Carol might regret sharing clinical trials with Buyer (even if the trials are glowingly positive) if the deal does not go through?  (Hint: Assume that Carol‚Äôs company and Buyer are competitors and that the trials contain valuable information.) If so, does this offer Carol a strategy for refusing to disclose the information?

- Carol might claim that the trials give Buyer a competitive advantage by seeing material non-public information  
  * **This is a strategy for Carol to justify non-disclosure**

7. If Carol has more than one reason not to disclose the trials, can Buyer tell what the real reason is?  What, if anything, can Buyer do in response?

- Buyer cannot tell what the real reason is  
- **Gilson critique: Seller does not have incentive to share bad information all the time when she can mask the reason for not sharing it justifiably**

- **Buyer could institute a confidentiality agreement or non-compete. Here, this agreement would allow Buyer to see trials but not produce the drug based on research Buyer does**  
  * **If Seller disagreed to confidentiality agreement, Buyer will be more inclined to believe that seller is actually concerned about the bad news**  
    * Buyer could have the results with MNPI redacted or investigate by a third party (expert can be picked by seller from a list provided by buyer and they can only tell high level about what the results are)  
      * If Seller says no to this, then she is probably covering bad news

- **When there are 2 reasons for buyer to be skeptical (one is a real worry of the seller and the other is something she is trying to hide), Buyer should make proposals which reveal if the stated objection is still plausible. And if they refuse these proposals protecting the real worry, then seller has answered you indirectly that the problem is with the thing they are trying to hide**

7\.  Gilson says parties have the incentive to be truthful in due diligence?  Do you agree?

- **Not all the time. They have an incentive to seem truthful. Seller does not always have the incentive to give the information if they can seem truthful without providing information**

- NOTE on Good Faith: You cannot lie but you can non-answer questions  
  * For the most part under U.S. law you can decide not to share certain information (but some jurisdictions require good faith in dealings which you can be liable for hiding something). From your silence, other party can draw their answers

- **Gilson says that it is in the Seller‚Äôs interest to share unflattering information so that Buyer doesn‚Äôt think it is worse**  
  * **Schizer Criticism of Gilson on Seller‚Äôs interest to share information: Can be true but be careful of situations where Seller can give an alternative explanation for why they won‚Äôt share. Seller will find an alternative explanation that is plausible to not tell you something without admitting to not telling you something**

8\. Let‚Äôs say Buyer wants to know about any litigation pending for Carol‚Äôs company.  Carol can ask her lawyers, who know the details of the litigation, to write up a description, but the time they will spend will cost Carol $10,000.  Alternatively, Carol can invite Buyer‚Äôs lawyers to come and read the litigation files (which are quite large).  If she does this, Buyer is likely to have to pay their lawyers $50,000.  What should Carol do?

- Carol should charge Buyer $10K to get the information herself and split the surplus in profits with Buyer

- **Gilson says the most efficient, lowest cost provider to get the information should provide the information (Carol here). The surplus of $40K (what it would have cost buyer to the DD more than Seller) should be saved and be factored into the purchase price (so the price should increase by $20K ‚Äì they cut the buyer in)**

- **Schizer Criticism of Gilson on Who Should Bear the Cost of Producing Information:**  
  * **1\) Institutional Factors Interfere: Deal prices are set by senior officials for big deals who cannot factor in all modest DD costs and do not get factored in after the prices are already set. Instead, just make the other party bear it ‚Äì price won‚Äôt get re-adjusted**

  * **2\) Instead of blatant refusal to share litigation of information, Carol can get away with not sharing it by placing the burden on the Buyer to investigate and pay the cost themselves even if it is more efficient for her to bear the cost of producing information**  
    * **Alternative explanation of something plausible defeats assumption of Buyer of worst case scenario**

10\. Third Party Experts

1. What role do accountants play in acquisitions?

- Figure out financial status of the target

- **Third parties come in and vouch for effects**  
  * **Bridges parties‚Äô mistrust of one another**  
- Don‚Äôt want accountant to be biased

2. What about investment bankers? Do you know what a ‚Äúfairness opinion‚Äù is?  
- **Fairness Opinion \= Evaluation of if a price is fair from a buyer‚Äôs perspective**  
  * **Schizer: Fairness opinions are not effective (they aren‚Äôt for reassurance but for a litigation insurance device). Doesn‚Äôt really vouch for the parties‚Äô trust**

3. Do lawyers ever vouch for aspects of a deal?  
- Yes, on legal issues lawyers issue opinions to vouch for parties

4. When these experts are asked to reassure the parties on particular issues, why (if at all) might they be tempted to overstate their case? What (if anything) stops them from doing so?

- Things preventing them from overstating their case might be:  
  * Reputational risk (lose credibility)  
    * **You have a temptation to overstate but this may come at the long-term cost of your work in the future (less people will trust your opinion)**  
  * Regulatory liability

- Law firms have functions attempting to deal with asymmetric information and incentive problems  
  * If each individual partner is allowed to make an opinion, then each partner may be tempted to overstate hoping that her overstatement will be balanced by more cautious colleagues and she will make her clients happy

  10\. Ben Buyer owns a plot of land. He is considering buying a neighboring plot from Sue Seller. Ben wants to combine the two plots and build a skyscraper. He is concerned, though, that he won‚Äôt be able to get approval from the zoning board. If Ben can combine the two plots, he‚Äôs willing to pay $10 million for Sue‚Äôs plot. If he can‚Äôt, he‚Äôs only willing to pay $7 million. It‚Äôs very likely that the zoning board will approve Ben‚Äôs request, but the process takes time.

1. Assume that Sue is not in a hurry. What do you think is the cleanest way for the parties to proceed?

- Wait and see approach: Wait to see if Ben gets approval from the zoning board

2. Assume that Sue actually is in a hurry and is flexible on the price. You represent her. What suggestion do you have to help her get the deal done?

- Contingent deal: Price fluctuates depending on zoning decision  
  * Put money in escrow in meantime

  12\. Seller, a start-up company, is developing a new type of bus, which is supposed to be more fuel efficient.  Seller has developed a design, and has made a few of the new buses in order to test them, but has not yet begun delivering buses to customers.  New York City has ordered 10,000 of these buses, but no buses have been delivered yet.  Buyer is a big company that makes buses, and wants the New York City contract. 

  Seller has begun testing the buses, and has discovered a problem with the wheels, which may be expensive to fix.  Seller has not yet finished testing the buses.

  The agreement has the following language: ‚ÄúBuyer has made a lengthy, detailed, and independent investigation regarding the design and specifications of the new bus,‚Äù and "Seller has not finished testing the new bus.‚Äù  The agreement also says that ‚ÄúSeller shall continue to give Buyer access to inspect the new bus‚Äù and that "neither party is relying upon any warranty or representation of the other not fully set forth in this agreement." The agreement does not have any other representations about possible defects in the bus or about the tests Seller has been conducting.

  After the deal closes and Buyer discovers the defect in the new bus, Buyer sues, claiming that Seller should have told Buyer about the defect. 

1. If you are the judge, and you have to decide the case based only on the contract language quoted above, who do you think should win the lawsuit?

- Seller wins argument: Buyer never asked. We had this contractual language. We never lied or misrepresented anything  
- Buyer wins argument: Seller cheated us. They knew their buses were defective and didn‚Äôt tell us


2. If you decide that Seller wins, are you rewarding sneakiness?  
- They didn‚Äôt ask. We had the language in the contract  
  * You are rewarding sneakiness

3. If you decide that Buyer wins, are you rewarding carelessness?  
- You are rewarding carelessness

4. What language should Buyer have asked for in the agreement in order to avoid this problem?

- **‚ÄúTo the best of the seller‚Äôs knowledge, there are no defects in the design or manufacture of the buses‚Äù**

- In some jurisdictions, a duty of good faith would be required  
- **But in the U.S., it is a buyer beware approach**

- **Benefits of U.S. approach (seller friendly):**  
  * Low quality is priced into the K  
  * Encourages sellers to sell at discount where they wouldn‚Äôt in other approaches  
  * Court is not there to re-negotiate the deal for a party  
  * We don‚Äôt really know what the terms were. It could be that when you said you were not asking for representations, the seller gave you a cheaper price. We shouldn‚Äôt intervene  
  * Better for parties to negotiate for themselves and have people trust the language of the K‚Äôs because this creates predictability for parties doing future deals to similarly trust the language of the K and do a deal  
- **Benefits of other approaches (buyer friendly):**  
  * Economizes DD (don‚Äôt need to expend the resources and ask/research every question)  
  * Built in duty of good faith

# 5: M\&A (Covenants and Conditions)

- MAC Clause is a way for buyers to get out of a transactions even after they have signed and said they are going to go through with the deal  
- Other clauses can be used to get out of a transaction:  
  * Representations (true as of signing date), Covenants (what happens between signing and closing date), Conditions  
    * Not ordinary course of business ‚Äì This is a covenant relevant in buyers re-negotiating deals signed pre-pandemic even without using the MAC Clause  
    * Debt covenants not fulfilled ‚Äì This is a covenant relevant in buyers re-negotiating deals signed pre-pandemic even without using the MAC Clause  
- 2 Reasons to Include a MAC Clause (to let a buyer out of a signed deal):  
  * 1\) Symmetry: Sellers want to drum up more interest with buyers by giving them something to be willing to get into an auction  
    * Evens the playing field between buyer (back-out protection) and seller (shareholder approval vote) if market changes and the deal becomes better for one party after signing  
      * This only is an advantage once you have the deal but is an obstacle for getting the deal you want  
      * Buyer will not bid on fringe deals because seller can back out. Buyer is at disadvantage  
      * Sellers then have fewer buyers in the market to bid against each other and push the price up  
      * Irony: Seller doesn‚Äôt want corporate law‚Äôs unilateral advantage. I want to offset it by giving Buyer a similar ability to walk away  
  * 2\) Investment: Motivates seller management to maintain state of the business between signing and closing  
    * More about moral hazard, agency problem between signing and closing  
    * Buyer depends on seller to take care of the company between signing and closing, but selling management is not motivated to do that because they are going to lose their jobs  
    * MAC Clause allows buyer to walk if there are company-specific problems  
    * Incentivizes selling management to do as well as peer companies  
      * 3 Parts to MAC Clause:  
        * 1\) If bad thing happens, buyer can cancel  
        * 2\) If bad thing impacts whole industry generally, buyer cannot cancel  
        * 3\) If bad thing impacts whole industry but has a disproportionate impact on you, buyer can cancel  
          * Company specific problems allow buyer to walk which motivates selling management  
- Why have these clauses?  
  * Symmetry rationale \= Common situation for things to change between signing and closing  
    * Buyer/Seller can get better deal if market moves  
  * Investment rationale  
- Seller also has protection of BoD (can back out after signing)  
  * Shareholders need to approve transaction through vote  
  * Buyer shareholders do not have that ability to cancel the deal if the market moves against them  
    * There is a difference

1. Acquisition agreements contain representations, covenants, and conditions? What is the difference?  
- **Representations \= Statement of fact in signing/purchase agreement (past or current state)**  
  * This fact is true as of the signing  
  * Can change over time without giving rise to breach  
  * Then in a closing agreement, you get a Tru-Up Statement (stating that everything you said since signing is still true)  
- **Covenants \= Party‚Äôs promise to perform/not perform an action (after signing, before closing future promise)**  
  * Build in governance over the course of the transaction  
  * Many covenants (and some representations) end at closing but some survive  
- **Conditions (Warranties) \= Term where something must happen/not happen or else the deal is not going to close (future promise)**  
  * A super-covenant

- Signing \= Contract is made  
- Closing \= Fruition of the contract. When transfer of the business takes place  
  * Can be days-months after signing

2. Is the following language a representation, a covenant, or a condition? 

   1. ‚ÄúTarget and the Shareholder jointly and severally represent, warrant and agree as follows: Target is a corporation duly organized, validly existing and in good standing under the laws of the State of Delaware, with the power to own its property, to carry on its business as now being conducted, and to enter into and carry out the terms of this Agreement.‚Äù  
- Representation

  2. ‚ÄúEach balance sheet delivered to Acquiring pursuant to this Agreement shall reflect all claims, debts or liabilities of Target which should be reflected thereon in accordance with generally accepted accounting principles.‚Äú  
- Covenant  
  * A condition would say that if this didn‚Äôt happen then the deal is off  
  * This is a promise from a party between signing and closing  
  * ‚ÄúShall reflect‚Äù is covenant language

  3. ‚ÄúDuring the period from the date hereof to and including the Closing, Target will conduct its business solely in the usual and ordinary manner and will refrain from any transaction not in the ordinary course of business or except as otherwise permitted herein unless the prior written consent of Acquiring to such transaction has been obtained.‚Äù  
- Covenant  
  * Between signing and closing

  4. ‚ÄúNo action or proceeding before a court or any other governmental agency or body shall have been instituted or threatened to restrain or prohibit the Merger contemplated hereby.‚Äù

- Condition  
  * We will buy you unless authorities tell us we can‚Äôt. If they tell us we can‚Äôt, the deal is off

3. You represent the buyer of a company.  You send over the following representation: ‚ÄúTarget is not subject to any material liability by reason of a violation of any order, rule, or regulation of any Federal, state, municipal or other governmental agency.‚Äù  In response, the seller changes the language.  Find the change.  What do you learn from the change they made in the language, and what follow up questions do you want to ask? 

   5. ‚ÄúTarget is not subject to any material liability by reason of a criminal violation of any order, rule, or regulation of any Federal, state, municipal or other governmental agency.‚Äù

- Added ‚ÄúCriminal violation‚Äù  
- If seller cannot give a representation about all violations, they are probably hiding a non-criminal violation

- If you are given the opportunity to do the first draft of the agreement, take it so that you can shape it in a way favorable to you  
- Always pay close attention to what is changed because there is information in that and it will show you what to follow up about  
  * You want to ask for a comprehensive list of any civil violations they are subject to

  6. ‚ÄúTarget is not subject to any material liability by reason of a violation of any order, rule, or regulation of any national governmental agency.‚Äù

- Change to ‚ÄúNational governmental agency‚Äù  
- If seller can only give a representation about national governmental agency violations, they are probably hiding state/municipal agency violations  
  * I would ask if they are subject to any violations from state/municipal agencies

- **The changes are potential sources of information**  
- **You want a balanced question:**  
  * **You want a broad enough question to learn information**  
  * **You don‚Äôt want the question to be overly broad or else you will annoy them and give more opportunity to skirt the narrowing information you want to know** 

4. Materiality & Knowledge Qualifiers

1. You represent the seller. Your client is asked to make the following representation: ‚ÄúTarget has paid all taxes and assessments (including interest or penalties) owed by it to the extent that such taxes and assessments are due.‚Äù  Why might you want to add the word ‚Äúmaterial,‚Äù so that the rep reads ‚ÄúTarget has paid all material taxes . . . .‚Äù

- 3 Reasons to Add ‚ÄúMaterial‚Äù:  
  * 1\) Avoid tedious and costly task of gathering all taxes and assessments or conceal potentially bad information about non-material taxes and assessments  
  * 2\) Do not furnish purchaser with an out over a minor misrepresentation, in the event that purchaser turns luke-warm on the deal and needs an excuse to call it off  
  * 3\) Guard against purchaser going after his client for indemnification down the road

- **Seller wants to add ‚Äúmaterial‚Äù because they do not want to be on the hook for small fees and it will take a lot of work/resources to track down every last penny. But if it is important, we paid it**  
- **Should buyer agree to materiality clause?**  
  * **You would always rather not have it and know more but you need to be reasonable**

2. Let‚Äôs say we define ‚Äúmaterial‚Äù as an issue involving at least $500.  Do you see a problem with defining materiality in this way?  Hint: does it matter how many nonmaterial facts are left out?  How can we deal with this problem?  
- **Problem with defining material as involving at least $500 is that, in the aggregate, nonmaterial taxes and assessments can add up to a lot**  
- **We can deal with this by putting a cap on the non-material total costs or amount of times that, if exceeded, construes a material breach**  
  * Material means that it shall not exceed $500 unless there are multiple instances that exceed $10K

3. Buyer wants to buy a plot of land, and asks Seller to give assurance that Seller actually owns the land.  Seller sends over the following representation: ‚ÄúTo the best of my knowledge, I am the legal owner of this land.‚Äù You represent Buyer, who asks whether he should accept this representation.  Are you comfortable with it?  
- No. The knowledge qualification is worrisome and provides Buyer with less recourse in case Seller actually does not own the land

4. Buyer wants to buy a business, and asks Seller to give assurance that no one has already sued the business, and that no one is threatening to sue the business in the future.  Seller offers the following representation: ‚ÄúNo one has sued our business and, to the best of the knowledge, no one is threatening to sue the business.‚Äù  You represent Buyer.  Are you comfortable with this representation?

- I would be comfortable with this because Seller only knows if someone is threatening to sue the company to the best of his knowledge  
  * **Sometimes knowledge qualifiers are reasonable and sometimes they are not**  
    * **Need to be aware of what you can reasonably expect someone to know**  
      * **Third parties views/actions that are not under your control ü°™ Knowledge qualifier makes sense (future lawsuits)**  
      * **Views/Actions/Events that have taken place ü°™ Knowledge qualifier is unnecessary (selling land)**

5. False Representations

1. What happens if a seller gives a representation that turns out not to be true? Can you think of circumstances in which a buyer does not get any meaningful protection from a representation, even if he can easily show it was false?

- If a seller‚Äôs representation turns out not to be true, a buyer can sue them for damages  
- A buyer would not get any meaningful protection from a false representation if the seller subsequently was insolvent

- **2 Reasons Buyer Might Not Get Recourse for False Representations:**  
  * **1\) The K gives you protection but sometimes the protection comes with a lawsuit and it isn‚Äôt worth the hassle**  
    * **Lawsuits are expensive and time consuming**  
  * **2\) Seller is insolvent**

2. How can a buyer plan in advance to prevent this sort of problem?  
- Buyer can require part of the purchase price to be held in escrow away from seller‚Äôs control until a certain amount of time has passed and the buyer is more certain that the seller‚Äôs representations were true. If a representation that the seller gave turns out to be false, the escrow money is released back to the buyer to compensate him for the false representation

3. Explain why a buyer might demand the following language in an acquisition agreement: ‚ÄúSeller hereby agrees that at the Closing, upon receipt of the Merger Shares, he will immediately deposit 15,000 of such Merger Shares into escrow for a period ending no later than 42 months after the Closing at which time the escrowed Merger Shares then remaining in escrow will be returned to him.‚Äù  
- This is to protect the buyer in case one of seller‚Äôs representations turns out to be false. Then, the buyer has recourse

6. Ann owned a shopping mall during the pandemic. One of her tenants, Ben, owned a restaurant. Ben‚Äôs lease ran through 2023\. Because of the pandemic, Ben had to close his restaurant on April 1, 2020, and did not send Ann any rent for several months, starting in April. The lease had no exceptions for pandemics (or for anything else), so Ben was clearly breaching his contractual commitment. Why might Ann have decided not to evict Ben? Why might she have been willing instead to forgive some rent if he agreed (eventually) to pay the rest later when his business revived? What does this fact pattern tell us about the limits of the influence of contracts?

- Ann might have forgiven Ben because she could not find a suitable replacement. Therefore, it would make more financial sense for her to forgive some rent and receive payment later than to risk having no rental income by not getting a new tenant to move in  
- Contracts are limited by the contextual circumstances they are agreed in

- You can have a contractual right that you do not want to exercise because there is no upside  
- You can make deals outside of the contractual relationship  
  * Look for the best way to make some money on a transaction down the line

- **Context of business environment is key**  
  * **Lawyers who understand this context and client‚Äôs needs can add more value to a transaction**

7. Carl is the CEO and largest shareholder of a public company, C Brands, which owns a chain of stores and a popular brand of women‚Äôs apparel called ‚ÄúD‚Äôs Secret.‚Äù Sales have been flat or declining for several years. In January 2020, Carl signs a deal in which a private equity firm, Evergreen, agrees to buy 55% of the stock of C Brands. The deal has a ‚ÄúMAC‚Äù clause, which specifically carves out ‚Äúthe Covid-19 pandemic,‚Äù so Evergreen cannot invoke the Pandemic as a reason to terminate the deal. In the contract, C Brands makes the following commitment: ‚ÄúThe seller will continue to operate the business in the ordinary course and will not take any major actions without the prior consent of the buyer.‚Äù  
   1. In response to the Pandemic, most of C Brands‚Äô stores were legally required to close. Carl decides to furlough 1,600 employees, and also stops paying rent on most of the stores. Carl also slashes employee compensation. As you read the language above, do any of these steps give Evergreen the legal right to cancel the deal?  
- Even though Evergreen cannot cancel the deal through the MAC clause, they may be able to terminate through the ordinary course of business clause. This is because C Brands had to close stores and furlough employees ‚Äì this is not operating a normal course of business (to stop paying rent, laying off thousands of workers)  
  * But as the buyer, you might expect the company to take these steps. These are reasonable, universal steps for retail businesses. It is likely that if the deal closed, the buyer would have done the same steps  
  * The clause though requires the company to consult the buyer if ordinary course changes, creating a potential lever for buyer to get out of the deal

  2. Evergreen brings a lawsuit to terminate the deal. Among other reasons, Evergreen alleges that D-Brands has ‚Äú"drastically \[reduced\] new merchandise receipts which, when coupled with D-Brands' failure to dispose of existing out-of-season, obsolete and excess merchandise, has saddled the D's Secret Business with a stock of merchandise of greatly diminished value." Based on the contractual language above, do you think this is a winning argument?  
- Buyer would have a similar claim that buyer wanted to be consulted before a major action  
- But this was not ordinary course of business (acquiring an inventory but not selling it)

- **If seller had warned buyer about the impending steps, then the deal would not have been terminated**  
  * All seller needed to do was tell buyer that this is what they planned to do and then run the business by buyer‚Äôs recommendation

  3. In bringing a lawsuit, what other motive might Evergreen have aside from trying to terminate the deal?  
- Evergreen did not just fundamentally disapprove of the company‚Äôs changes  
  * **They want to terminate the deal**  
  * **They want to lower the purchase price by threatening termination**

  4. Assuming the MAC clause carves out the Covid pandemic, what other clauses are there in a typical deal that Evergreen can try to use to get out of this deal? (Hint: What is a debt covenant?)

- **Debt Covenant \= If the earnings compared to debt changes, then buyer can terminate**  
  * When seeking a loan, the fact that the business is doing well today is relevant. But what is key is how the business is going to do in the future  
    * Build in triggers which alert the bank that the loan might have become riskier than they initially thought  
    * **Use ratios to ensure loan will be paid back: Cash flow should be 3x the interest obligation on the loan**  
    * **If the business is in trouble debt covenant requires:**  
      * **You have to tell the bank**  
      * **The loan comes due**

- **So buyer will rely on the third party to understand the health of the business: seller‚Äôs debt covenant breach gives buyer a termination option**  
  * Deal is off if you breach your debt covenants and can‚Äôt correct them

8. ‚ÄúSymmetry‚Äù Rationale for MAC clauses: When a seller and a buyer sign a contract, they presumably expect to close the deal in a few weeks or months, but sometimes things change.  
   1. Why might a seller regret selling and want to cancel the deal?  
- The business starts doing better than when they agreed to the deal initially, and they can get a higher purchase price  
  * Could be because of better market conditions or a product innovation that happen in between signing and closing

  2. Assume the seller is a public company, and the relevant jurisdiction requires shareholders to vote to approve a deal before the deal can close. If the seller‚Äôs business is doing well in the weeks before the shareholder vote, how confident can the buyer be that the deal will close? What if the seller‚Äôs business is doing badly?  
- Buyer will be more confident that the deal will close if the seller‚Äôs business is doing badly because shareholders will be more okay with getting rid of it  
  * **When the market changes after signing to improve value of business, the shareholders can stop it**  
  * **When the market changes after signing to hurt value of business, the shareholders can approve it**  
  * **Buying shareholders do not need approval either way**  
    * **This gives the seller an edge of protection on the deal between signing and closing. It gives the seller a backstop if the deal seems bad for them between signing and closing**

- Process:  
  * Board signs the merger  
  * Shareholders ratify the merger through vote

  3. The buyer‚Äôs stockholders do not have to vote to approve a deal. Does this put buyers at a disadvantage if conditions change after a deal is signed?  
- Yes, because a seller‚Äôs stockholder approval will reflect the condition of the current state of the market and business. It serves as a protection against bad deals. The buyer does not have this advantage  
- Buyers are at disadvantage

  4. Assume that corporate law gives sellers an advantage over buyers, in affording sellers (but not buyers) ways to back out of a deal if market conditions change. Is there a reason why sellers might want to give up this advantage and ‚Äúlevel the playing field‚Äù?  
- Sellers may want to level the playing field to get buyers to be more confident in their purchase and therefore raise the purchase price  
- Increase in purchase price  
  * More buyers is better for seller (auction) creates competition  
  * Buyers only want to bid on the best situations  
  * So sellers will want to level the playing field to get more bidders  
- Built-in seller advantage of having back-out protection means that there are fewer buyers to express  
  * Sellers cannot waive DE law  
  * **So you can give the buyers a back-out advantage too. This levels the playing field. They do this by giving buyer a MAC Clause**

  5. How could sellers make the playing field more level? What kind of clause could they use? Note that some commentators call this a ‚Äúsymmetry‚Äù explanation for the relevant contractual term.  
- **Symmetry explanation for MAC Clause: Give buyers that same back-out provision that the seller has if the market changes after deal is signed**  
  * **Not perfectly symmetrical**  
- Schizer: Doesn‚Äôt buy this explanation totally because it is not perfectly symmetrical

  6. Once again, we see the need for contractual (or other) responses to incentive and information problems. What does the term ‚Äúmoral hazard‚Äù mean and how does it apply to this issue?

- Moral Hazard: Parties who are protected (insured) against risk are more likely to make risky decisions  
- This applies because the seller‚Äôs stockholder approval of the deal for a public company protects them against risk of making a bad deal to begin with. So, sellers may try to make aggressive deals, knowing that if they make a bad one, the stockholders will not approve it

9. Investment Rationale for MAC Clauses: Fran is the CEO of Seller, which owns residential real estate. She signs a deal on July 1 to sell the company to Buyer, which also owns real estate. The deal is scheduled to close on December 31\.  
   1. Fran knows that she is unlikely to stay on after the merger closes. What issue does that create for Buyer?  
- Fran has no incentive to run the business well or work hard, knowing that in six months she will be fired. This is a problem for Buyer who wants to buy the business in its current state  
- This is an incentive problem after a deal is signed. What if between signing and closing the buildings burn down. We need a mechanism to make sure the selling management cares and still feels like the owner

  2. How important is it to continue to maintain the properties and to renew the relevant leases? How motivated is Seller‚Äôs management to pursue these goals?  
- Maintaining the properties and renewing the relevant leases is extremely important to Buyer because this is what they are purchasing  
- Seller is not very motivated to pursue these goals because they will not benefit from accomplishing them ‚Äì only Buyer will because Seller‚Äôs price is locked in

  3. What kind of incentive problem are we describing? How could a MAC clause play a role in addressing it? Note that some commentators call this an ‚Äúinvestment‚Äù explanation for a MAC clause.  
- Moral Hazard: Now that seller has been paid, they don‚Äôt care about what happens to the company ‚Äì risk is on Buyer  
- Agency Costs: Seller is running Buyer‚Äôs company without incentive to run it well  
- Investment Rationale for MAC Clause: MAC clauses can solve this problem because if Seller does not conduct its normal jobs in its regular course of business and its business starts to falter, Buyer can terminate the deal through the MAC clause and Seller will be left with a failing business

10. Assume that an acquisition agreement includes the following definition of a ‚Äúmaterial adverse change‚Äù or ‚ÄúMAC‚Äù:

    *‚ÄúMaterial Adverse Change‚Äù shall mean any event change that is materially adverse to the business, assets, or operations of the Company; provided, however, that none of the following shall constitute a Material Adverse Change: changes in the national or world economy or financial markets as a whole or changes in general economic conditions that affect the industries in which the Company conducts business or any change in applicable law, so long as such changes or conditions do not adversely affect the Company in a materially disproportionate manner relative to other similarly situated participants in the industries or markets in which they operate.*

    1. Like other MAC clauses, this one has three components. First, it says that a deal can be cancelled if there is a ‚Äúmaterial adverse change.‚Äù Please quote the relevant language.

- ‚Äú*Material Adverse Change‚Äù shall mean any event change that is materially adverse to the business, assets, or operations of the Company‚Äù*  
  * Very broad out from deal

  2. The second component of MAC clauses is a list of exceptions or carve outs, which do not justify a change. Please quote the relevant language.  
- ‚Äú*none of the following shall constitute a Material Adverse Change: changes in the national or world economy or financial markets as a whole or changes in general economic conditions that affect the industries in which the Company conducts business or any change in applicable law‚Äù*  
  * Narrowing the out with a broad exception

  3. The third component creates ‚Äúa carve out from the carve out.‚Äù Something that otherwise would be excluded can still justify termination of the deal in some circumstances.  What are those circumstances? Please quote the relevant language.  
- *‚Äúso long as such changes or conditions do not adversely affect the Company in a materially disproportionate manner relative to other similarly situated participants in the industries or markets in which they operate.‚Äù*

  4. In the clause above, does the buyer or the seller bear the risk of economic downturns and other general market conditions? Why do you think the risk is allocated in that way?  
- Buyer bears the risk of general market conditions and economic downturns  
  * The carve-out applies  
- This risk is allocated to the buyer because these are foreseeable because they happen commonly and should be baked into the purchase price of the deal  
  * This is inherent in the idea of being a buyer  
    * You will reap benefits of future upturn  
    * You will bear risk of future downturn

  5. In the clause above, does the buyer or the seller bear the risk of firm-specific problems? Why? Does this allocation of risk make sense?  
- Seller bears the risk of firm-specific problems  
  * Buyer can still get out if bad thing in the world is having a worse effect on the seller than others  
- This risk is allocated to the seller because it imposes the risk on the seller of bad seller management. To keep the deal and not have buyer back-out, seller must do as well as everyone else is doing  
  * This deals with the incentive problem (not about symmetry)

  6. Assume that the language above was used in a deal that closed in October of 2019\. Assume the seller is an online retailer that delivers groceries and other products to people‚Äôs homes. Assume that the pandemic has increased demand for the seller‚Äôs services, but reduced its profitability (because its costs are higher). Under this MAC clause, is the buyer able to back out?  
- No. Buyer cannot back out because these are conditions that are impacting all businesses ‚Äì not just the one the buyer is purchasing  
  * Clause 1: Bad thing happened in the world. Buyer out  
  * Clause 2: General market conditions caused change. Buyer in  
  * Clause 3: This retailer is not doing worse than other retailers. Buyer in  
    * MAC Clause does not apply

  7. The same as ‚Äúf,‚Äù but the seller is an airline that has no local routes and runs only international flights. These routes, connecting the U.S. to Europe and Asia, have all been cancelled. Under this MAC clause, is the buyer able to back out?  
- Ambiguity here  
  * Seller: If this airline is compared to every airline in the world, you look worse than other airlines. Buyer out  
  * Buyer: If this airline is compared to every airline doing international flights, you aren‚Äôt worse than other airlines. Buyer in  
    * Key: Is this buyer similarly situated?

- Yes. Buyer should be able to back out because the pandemic conditions impacted this airline disproportionately in comparison to other airlines because of its specific routes  
  * Clause 1: Bad thing happened in the world. Buyer out  
  * Clause 2: General market conditions caused change. Buyer in  
  * Clause 3: This seller is doing worse than other sellers. Buyer out

  8. Assume the seller owns a chain of restaurants across the United States, and assume there is an act of terrorism in Metropolis, a major U.S. city where half of seller‚Äôs restaurants are located. In response to the act of terrorism, consumers in Metropolis are more cautious and many stop going to restaurants, but consumers in other cities do not change their behavior. Can the buyer invoke this to get out of the deal?

- Ambiguity here  
  * What does it mean to be similarly situated?  
    * Compared to other chains with 50 percent of their restaurants in Metropolis ü°™ Seller is not doing worse than the general market ü°™ Buyer in  
    * Compared to other chains generally ü°™ Buyer is doing worse than the general market ü°™ Buyer out  
  * **Schizer: Given the purpose of the clause, which is to control seller management, you should limit the clause‚Äôs application to things that the seller can manage/control. Buyer in, deal closes, MAC should not apply**  
    * It is not seller‚Äôs responsibility between signing and closing to change where restaurants should be or what airline routes are best  
      * This is seller‚Äôs responsibility but not between signing and closing

- Buyer will probably still need to execute the deal because the carve-out calls for MAC-based termination when there is impact that is ‚Äúdisproportionate manner relative to other similarly situated participants in the industries or markets in which they operate.‚Äù This business was not disproportionately impacted in comparison with other businesses in the market where it operates, Metropolis  
  * Clause 1: Bad thing happened in the world. Buyer out  
  * Clause 2: General market conditions. Buyer in  
  * Clause 3: This seller is doing worse than other sellers

# 6: Spin-offs (Cooperation to claim tax losses)

- Regulatory arbitrage: Look for situations where you can get an advantage by doing the same economic thing: Buying and selling tax benefits  
- To claim tax differences, parties need to trust each other  
  * Problem of cooperation

- NOL: Hold onto deductions to avoid paying tax in another year  
  * Carryback: Applied to earlier year  
  * Carryforward: Applied to later year  
    * Carryback is better:  
      * 1\) Timing advantage (lower present value of the tax; get the money now)  
      * 2\) Rate advantage (for every $1 deduction, it was worth 14 cents more)  
- Congress hoping to give US companies cash tax refunds during pandemic  
- Spin-off tax problem:  
  * Spin-off can amend a prior year tax return (use the carryback provision in CARES Act to get the advantages above) only with the cooperation of the parent company to carryback spin-off‚Äôs losses on the parent‚Äôs prior return. Then the parent gives spin-off the refund money  
    * The tax matters agreement governs the cooperation between the parent and subsidiary  
  * Note: Many of the shareholders in the spin-off are also the shareholders of the parent so they want the companies to cooperate because either way they will reap the benefit of the $14M no matter how it is divided

1. The **economic value of a tax deduction is the amount of tax it allows you to avoid**. 

   1. Is the ability to deduct $1 of expense worth $1?

- No. It is worth the amount you would have had to pay in tax on $1

  2. Assume that a corporation‚Äôs tax rate is 21%. If the corporation earns $1, how much tax must the corporation pay?

- 21 cents

  3. Assume now that the corporation is able to deduct a $1 expense. How much tax does the corporation avoid? What is the economic value of this $1 deduction?

- 21 cents avoided/deducted

  4. **The economic value of a deduction depends on a taxpayer‚Äôs tax rate.**

2. In response to the Covid-19 pandemic in 2020, the U.S. Congress passed the  Coronavirus Aid, Relief, and Economic Security (CARES) Act. One of its provisions enabled taxpayers to ‚Äúcarry back‚Äù net operating losses (‚ÄúNOLs‚Äù). As background, let‚Äôs cover a few basics of how NOLs are taxed in the U.S.  
   1. Assume that Corporation (a U.S. taxpayer) has $9 million of revenue in 2020 and $10 million of expenses. Do you expect Corporation to pay any tax in 2020?  
- No tax because losses exceed revenue and therefore the corporation has no income  
  * NOL: The deduction alone does not generate income. Instead, save the deduction and use it in another year  
  * NOL cannot be used as a refund  
  * NOL cannot be used in current year

  2. How is its ‚Äúnet operating loss‚Äù (‚ÄúNOL‚Äù) of $1 million treated? The U.S. corporate tax rate is 21%. Do you expect the NOL to generate a $210,000 tax refund in 2020?  
- The NOL is carried forward to a following year to be used as a deduction  
- No, the NOL does not generate a $210K refund. Instead, 80 percent of the NOL ($800K) can be deducted from income in a future year where the company has income (more revenue than expenses)

  3. Starting in 2018, U.S. corporations with NOLs could only ‚Äúcarry forward‚Äù tax losses. ‚ÄúCarrybacks‚Äù were no longer allowed. What is the difference between a carryforward and a carryback?  
- Carryforward \= Current policy; Allows corporation to use NOLs on future years where they have income  
- Carryback \= Allowed under the old policy; Allows corporation to use NOLs by amending a return of a prior year to which it had income  
  * Carrybacks were eliminated in 2018 when Congress reduced the corporate tax rate. Eliminating carrybacks was a way to pay for the reduced rate

  4. In the above example, Corporation generates a $1 million net operating loss in 2020\. Assume that Corporation had more than $1 million of net income in 2019, and expects to have more than $1 million of net income in 2021\. If only carryforwards are allowed, in which year would Corporation deduct its NOL? What if carrybacks are allowed?

- If only carryforwards, Corporation‚Äôs only option is to deduct $1M in 2021 if they have income  
- If carrybacks are allowed, Corporation will want to deduct $1M in 2020 because it is guaranteed that they will use the NOL (the income is already generated) and also using a carryback lowers the present value of the tax (you always want to use deductions immediately rather than wait)  
  * Schizer: Guarantee is not really an advantage (just advantage of higher, more valuable rate and time value of taking deductions as soon as you can)

3. In the CARES Act, Congress authorized taxpayers once again to carry back losses (as they used to be able to do before 2018), but only losses generated in three years: 2018, 2019, and 2020\. Assume that Businessco had $5 million of taxable income in 2017, $5 million of taxable income in 2018, and $2 million of NOLs in 2019\. Because of the pandemic, Businessco expects to have more losses in 2020\.   
   1. Do you agree or disagree with the following statement: ‚Äú**The CARES Act allows Businessco to get a tax refund for its 2019 NOL \-- and thus to access more cash \-- sooner than it otherwise would**.‚Äù In light of your answer, what policy do you think Congress was pursuing?  
- Yes. Through the CARES Act, Businessco can use its losses to prior years where it had income instead of waiting for future year income. This helps businesses because they do not have to pay as much in tax in the present year. Businesses can instead use that money for rent or payroll  
- Congress was trying to stimulate the economy by helping businesses keep more cash in years they are struggling and get more cash into the economy. Get corporations to take tax refunds now so that there is more cash in their pockets

  2. The U.S. corporate tax rate was 35% in 2017 (and in years before then), and this tax rate was reduced to 21% in 2018\. What was the value of a $1 tax deduction in 2017? What about in 2018? In which year would you prefer to take a deduction?  
- 2017 $1 Deduction \= 35 cents  
- 2018 $1 Deduction \= 21 cents  
- You would rather take the deduction in 2017

- **2 Things Government Is Doing through CARES to be generous to corporations:**  
  * **1\) Allow carrybacks (get money sooner which is better for struggling corporations who need cash but also better because of time value of money)**  
  * **2\) Deduct in 2017 and you get more money, more deduction because of the higher rate**

  3. Do you agree or disagree with the following statement: ‚Äú**Through the CARES Act, the U.S. government used the tax system to get cash in the hands of corporations by making tax refunds more generous**.‚Äù   

- Yes.

4. Hotelco, a publicly-traded U.S. company, owns a chain of U.S. hotels catering to business travelers. In the 1990s, Hotelco branched out, opening a number of theme parks and casinos throughout the U.S.. In 2017, Hotelco hired a new CEO, Carla, who decided that casinos and theme parks are very different from hotels, so they should not be operated together. So she put all of Hotelco‚Äôs theme parks and casinos into a subsidiary, and on January 1, 2018, Hotelco ‚Äúspun off‚Äù this subsidiary, ‚ÄúRecreationco,‚Äù to Hotelco‚Äôs shareholders as an independent publicly-traded company.   
   1. What is a ‚Äúspin off‚Äù?  
- A parent company creates a spin-off division as an independent entity  
  * A spin-off is initiated when the company¬†[expects the spin-off may be worth more](https://www.investopedia.com/ask/answers/050415/what-are-some-more-common-reasons-divestiture-occurs.asp)¬†independently than as part of the parent company

- **Opposite of an acquisition**  
  * **When a group of businesses that have been operating together split up**

  2. Assume a shareholder owned one share of Hotelco before the spin. Would she still have this share after the spin? What else would she have?

- Yes. This shareholder would have the share of Hotelco and a share of the newly created company, Recreationco  
  * In a spin-off, existing shareholders of the parent company receive shares in the newly created "spin-off" company, essentially getting a share of the new company, distributed on a pro-rata basis based on their existing shareholdings in the parent company

5. Continuing with the facts of the prior problem, assume that Hotelco used to be very profitable, but the company had very large losses in 2018, and less significant losses in 2019 (as it started to make a comeback). Although 2020 started out better, the pandemic changed that overnight, so Hotelco expects large losses again in 2020\. Specifically, the results are as follows:

   2013: $100 million profit

   2014:  $100 million profit

   2015:  $100 million profit

   2016:  $100 million profit

   	2017:  $100 million profit

   	2018:  $200 million loss (Hotelco)

   	2019:  $50 million loss (Hotelco).

1. Under the CARES Act, Hotelco can carry back losses five years (e.g., from 2018 to 2013). If it does not have enough income to use those losses, it can carry the remainder back four years (from 2018 to 2014), and then three years (to 2015), and then two years (to 2016), and then one year (to 2017). Assuming that Hotelco carries back its $200 million loss from 2018, in what years does it deduct it?

- 2013 and 2014  
  * $100M each year

2. In what years would Hotelco use its $50 million loss from 2019?  
- 2015 as a partial deduction of $50M

3. The CARES Act allows a company to waive its right to carryback losses, and to carry them forward instead. You are Hotelco‚Äôs tax advisor. Should Hotelco carry back its 2018 and 2019 losses to earlier years? Why or why not?  
- Hotelco should carry the losses back for two reasons:  
  * 1\) Carrying back losses is better because it allows Hotelco to use the deduction immediately which lowers the present value of the tax and Hotelco expects to have losses in 2020 as well so using these NOLs is not guaranteed going forward (because they might not have income to offset). So, Hotelco should use the NOLs now to make sure they use them at all  
  * 2\) If Hotelco can carry back losses to get a higher rate (35 percent) on their deduction in a previous year. But in the future years, deductions will be worth less at a lower tax rate (21 percent)

4. If Hotelco has $100 million of losses in 2020, could it carry all of that back?  
- Yes. It could carry those losses back to 2015 ($50M) and 2016 ($50M) because those are within the five-year carryback period

5. What if Hotelco has $300 million of losses in 2020\. Could it carry all of these losses back?

- No. Hotelco can only carryback $250M of the $300M losses to prior years because it does not have enough income to offset $300M of loss

6. Continuing with the same facts, business has not been good for Recreationco either since the spin. Recreationco lost $50 million in both 2018 and 2019\. Recreationco also expects losses in 2020 because its theme parks and casinos all had to close in mid-March because of the pandemic.  
   1. Do you think Recreationco would want to carry back its 2018 and 2019 losses back to earlier years? Why or why not?  
- Yes, if it was possible, Recreationco would rather carryback to get the two advantages above

  2. Before January 1, 2018, Recreationco was wholly owned by Hotelco. As a result, for the tax years before 2018, Recreationco did not file its own tax return. Instead, as part of Hotelco, its results were reflected in Hotelco‚Äôs tax return. Compared with Hotelco, is it easier or harder for Recreationco to carry back losses?  
- It is harder for Recreationco to carry back losses because it had less prior years of making income  
  * They didn‚Äôt have their own tax return in the prior year to amend

  3. Do you know whether the CARES Act provides Recreationco with a way to carry back its losses to the years before the spin off?

- It allows Recreationco to attribute its losses to its parent company‚Äôs income in years before the spin-off if they agreed on this in the tax matters agreement. They needed to work through its former parent company by having parent claim a refund on its return and giving the spin off the money

7. In a spinoff, two businesses that have been affiliated \-- and thus have filed a single tax return together \-- become independent businesses, which each file their own return. To prepare for the spinoff, these businesses have to come to an agreement, usually called a ‚Äútax matters agreement,‚Äù about how they will handle this transition. The following are relevant portions of the tax matters agreement for the spinoff of Recreationco. 

   *1.01: Hotelco has the right to amend any Hotelco tax return with respect to 2017 or any earlier years.* 

   *1.02 Recreationco shall not carry back any U.S.federal income tax Item to 2017 or an earlier tax year.*

   *1.03 Any refund of U.S. federal income Tax received from a Taxing Authority by Hotelco or Recreationco with respect to a Hotelco return shall be the property of the Hotelco, regardless of whether all or any portion of such refund is attributable to any credit or deduction of Recreationco.*  

1. What financial benefit would there be for Recreationco to carry back its $100 million of losses (from 2018 and 2019\) to 2017 or earlier? What is the financial value of this benefit?

- Recreationco can get a cash refund in the present year from amending a prior return of $35M (because the corporate tax rate in 2017 and earlier was 35 percent). It would be $21M if amending a year after 2017  
- This is a $14M benefit

2. If Recreationco wants to carry back these losses, does the tax matters agreement give it the ability to do so? What (if anything) can Recreationco do to tap this financial benefit?  
- The tax matters agreement does not give them the ability to carry back losses  
- Recreationco instead should try to attribute the losses to Hotelco so Hotelco can claim them or it can carry the losses forward to a future year

- **2 Elements Give Rise to the ability to claim the $14M tax deduction:**  
  * **1\) Carryback ‚Äì Only Hotelco amend its previous returns**  
  * **2\) Current tax losses ‚Äì Only Recreationco has the losses**  
    * **Companies to work together to generate the benefit**

3. Under the tax matters agreement, which company has more leverage in any negotiation?  
- Hotelco has more leverage in negotiations because Recreationco needs them to do the return, and they are the party who is losing money  
  * But they do not have that much leverage because Hotelco needs Recreationco to use the current losses

4. Richard, the CEO of Recreationco, asks Carla, the CEO of Holdco, to help Recreationco carry back its losses. You are Carla‚Äôs mentor, and she calls to ask you for advice. Should she agree? What should she ask for in return?

- Carla should agree because Hotelco can share in the $14M tax benefit  
  * Cannot be done as a favor because Carla has an obligation to her own shareholders  
- Carla could ask for a piece of the profit from the tax avoidance. She should also make the deal contingent on if Hotelco has more losses in future years that they can no longer carryback to prior years because Hotelco used the income to help Recreationco carryback its losses, then Recreationco must pay Hotelco that amount

8. Grace is the general counsel of Hotelco. Carla calls Grace and says, ‚ÄúWe‚Äôre thinking about partnering with Recreationco to carry back their losses. If we do this, I want to be protected from all risks. Can you please identify the relevant risks and figure out how to protect us?‚Äù You are Hotelco‚Äôs outside counsel, and Grace calls you with the following questions:  
   1. Recreationco has $100 million of losses from 2018 and 2019\. To carry back this amount, which company is going to file for this refund \-- Recreationco or Hotelco? Assume that Recreationco‚Äôs tax return for 2018 and 2019 has not been audited yet, so it‚Äôs possible that the I.R.S. (the tax authority in the U.S.) will challenge parts of it. Does this possibility create a risk for Hotelco? If so, what should Hotelco do?  
- **Hotelco will file for this refund because it is their tax return to amend**  
- Yes, this creates credit risk for Hotelco because the government hasn‚Äôt confirmed Recreationco‚Äôs $100M of losses. If Recreationco‚Äôs claim that the $100M losses is inaccurate, then Hotelco is on the hook for these because they are amending Hotelco‚Äôs return  
  * Hotelco is depending on the accuracy of Recreationco‚Äôs claim  
- Hotelco should stipulate in the deal with Recreationco that Recreationco is financially responsible for the cost of any additional charges from audits  
  * This risk is Recreationco‚Äôs risk because you are going to know if these losses are correct  
- If Recreatioco doesn‚Äôt have the money, then Hotelco is out of luck  
  * **DD: So Hotelco should audit Recreationco themselves**  
    * Vet the quality of the losses themselves  
- **Indemnity: Hotelco could also take the part of the $100M worth of losses (the part that Recreationco‚Äôs tax audit thinks is uncertain) and put it in escrow until after the audit**  
  * Allow the money to generate interest for Recreationco in treasury bonds  
  * Recreationco gets the money back if the audit is clean  
- **This is a combination of DD and indemnity to address this risk**

  2. Hotelco has $500 million of income from 2013 through 2017\. Hotelco has $250 million of losses in 2018 and 2019\. Remember that Hotelco is allowed also to carry back losses from 2020, and does not know yet how much its losses will be in 2020\. Does this create a risk for Hotelco in partnering with Recreationco to carry back losses? If so, what can Hotelco do about this?  
- Yes, this creates risk for Hotelco because if Hotelco‚Äôs 2020 (or thereafter) losses are greater than the remaining $250M of income they have from the previous years, they will not be able to use their losses  
  * If Hotelco takes Recreatioco‚Äôs losses, they risk not using their own future losses  
- Hotelco should stipulate in the deal with Recreatoinco that Recreationco is financially responsible for the cost of any future NOLs that go unused due to Recreationco‚Äôs use of Hotelco‚Äôs prior income  
  * Hotelco can also just wait and see to know what their 2020 losses are and then decide if they want to cooperate with Recreationco  
    * Hotelco might only agree to this if they have capacity

  3. Grace says the following to you: ‚ÄúThe CARES Act was very generous, giving us an unexpected tax benefit. Do you think the U.S. government might pass more legislation like it?‚Äù Does this possibility create any risks for Hotelco in partnering with Recreationco to carry back losses? If so, what can Hotelco do?  
- Yes, this creates risk for Hotelco because the benefits of more legislation like the CARES Act might go unused by Hotelco if they allow Recreationco to use their prior income  
- Hotelco should stipulate in the deal with Recreationco that Recreationco is financially responsible for the cost of any unused benefits from tax forgiveness legislation passed in the following 2 years due to Recreationco‚Äôs use of Hotelco‚Äôs prior income

  4. Recreationco has been losing a lot of money, and business continues to be very bad. What risk (if any) does this create for Hotelco in partnering with Recreationco to carry back losses?

- This creates a credit risk for Hotelco (explained in 8a)

# 7: VC (Staged Financing, Incentive Compensation)

- VC is interesting for K problems because:  
  * Every transaction has some asymmetric information and uncertainty but there is extreme in VC start-ups (founder knows more; no idea if this technology will work)  
    * These problems can prevent transactions unless creative solutions develop to build trust and motivate people to them  
  * There is an important advantage in studying VC ‚Äì Management has extra autonomy (can be used to prove its own interests instead of the best of the company)  
    * There are few parties involved  
    * Easy to monitor management (unlike public companies)  
    * No coordination problems  
    * When VC‚Äôs get involved, the only investors are probably angel investors  
- These make VC like a lab: How can experts address these extreme problems with full incentives and ability to do it?  
  * Expert investors: When information is not equal, that discourages the transaction. But the expert investors lower the asymmetric information imbalance  
  * Staged Financing: Rounds of funding structure
