    * Gives expert investors the advantage of time to bridge the asymmetric problem  
    * Also solution to incentive problems: Creates a structure for founder to demonstrate success early on to justify new round of investing  
    * Also signaling: The very fact that founder would accept only a preliminary investment gives you information about their confidence in the company  
    * This only works because there are expert investors  
- But solutions themselves have problems:  
  * Founder puts a lot of power in hands of VC  
  * If VC doesn‚Äôt give second round of funding, that signals to other investors to not invest either  
    * So why wouldn‚Äôt VC pretend to not want to renew the deal until they re-negotiate?  
      * Reputation, repeat players. VC can only do that once or twice  
        * Reputation is a powerful asset to overcome information and incentive problems

1\. Assume that Ellen has an idea for a new business.  She wants to hire professors to teach courses online, and to charge people for taking these courses.  But Ellen needs money to start ‚ÄúLearn-online, Inc.‚Äù  She has two potential investors.  One, Schoolco, is an educational institution.  The other, Pensionco, invests money for the police department in order to be able to pay pensions to retired policemen. 

1. Which of these investors has potentially greater expertise about the subject of Ellen‚Äôs business?  In what ways can investors potentially add value beyond their investment?

- Schoolco has greater expertise about Ellen‚Äôs business because it is an educational institution like Schoolco  
- Investors can add value of:  
  * Networking/Industry connections/Relationships  
  * Credit/Reputation  
  * Business practices/Advice  
  * Building initial customer base  
- VC is for start-ups. The non-financial benefits are especially valuable for start-ups

2. If Ellen was also asking you to invest, would you care who the other investors are?  Would you be more likely to invest if you heard that the other investor was Schoolco or Pensionco?  
- Investors care about who the other investors are because they can influence how well the business is going to do  
  * Also want to understand how much control the other investors have (day to day business involvement? Passive?)  
- I would rather see Schoolco as a co-investor  
  * Seal of Approval: Schoolco knows this industry and the fact that they are willing to invest is reassuring to other investors  
  * When Buffet puts money into a company, the stock price goes up  
    * The mere fact that he is investing provides a seal of approval

3. Ellen plans to be the CEO of this business.  In addition to wanting the investment, she also wants to make sure she will not be replaced as CEO.  Review \-- what is this sort of conflict of interest called? In pursuing this second goal (of keeping her job), does she have a reason to prefer one employer over the other?

- This is an agency problem. Her main goal is to stay on as CEO but someone else could run it better  
  * Person running business is more interested in her own professional prospects than the success of the business

- Ellen probably will prefer Pensionco because pension funds are more passive and have less expertise so they will not bother her and will probably not know if she is doing a good job  
  * The advantages an experienced investor provides might not be advantages for CEO to the same extent as other investors want (investors want another investor can add the most non-financial value but CEO doesn‚Äôt want new investor to meddle in her work or replace her because the more experienced investor knows more)

2\. On January 1, Ellen will need $200,000 to hire technology consultants for six months to prepare the website.  The effectiveness of the website is going to be quite important in determining the success of the business. Six months later (on July 1), she will need $200,000 to hire faculty to teach the courses, which will begin on September 1\. The quality of the faculty will obviously also be important.  

In response, Pensionco offers to buy 500,000 shares of Learn-online, Inc. ‚Äì representing half of the company‚Äôs 1 million shares ‚Äì for $400,000.  They would pay $200,000 on January 1, and $200,000 on July 1\.

But Schoolco‚Äôs offer is different.  They are offering to pay $250,000 on January 1 for two things: first, ¬º of the shares of the company (250,000 shares); and second, an option to buy another ¬º of the company (i.e., another 250,000 shares) on June 30 for $250,000.  Although Schoolco would have the right to buy these additional shares, they are not obligated to do so. 

1. Does each offer ensure that Ellen has the money she needs to launch the business?  
- Only Pensionco‚Äôs offer guarantees her the ability to launch the business. This is a firm commitment ‚Äì certainty  
- Schoolco is willing to pay more per share but does not give all the money up front

  2. Why might Ellen be tempted to take Schoolco‚Äôs offer?  Why might she be worried about doing so?  
- She might be tempted to take Schoolco‚Äôs offer because they are in the same industry, better non-financial reasons, but also they can offer her more money per share  
- She might be worried to take this offer because it doesn‚Äôt actually lock in financing to get this launched

  3. Why do you think Schoolco is willing to pay more?  What are they getting in return?  Why do you think they value the extra business term they are seeking?  
- Schoolco is not willing to commit everything up front and wants to make another decision in a few months because they will be able to get in and evaluate the business  
  * **Time can give you more information: Schoolco is willing to pay more for the privilege of deciding whether to stick with the investment later**  
- **Staged Financing: Fund you now and see how it goes and then we will decide whether to fund you again**  
  * Critical feature of the VC industry  
- Pensionco might not value the time to make a future decision as much as Schoolco because they do not have the expertise to evaluate it  
  * **Time is only useful if you have the expertise to evaluate how the investment is going**  
  * Expert investors can make informed judgments on how it‚Äôs going, but Pensionco is not an expert  
    * Pensionco won‚Äôt know much more in a few months than they know now

  4. Let‚Äôs say Ellen makes a counteroffer to Schoolco, saying she will agree to their terms if they agree to decide whether to make their second investment ‚Äì not on June 30 (after six months) ‚Äì but on January 6 (after six days).  If you were advising Schoolco, would you think this change in timing is significant?  
- Yes. Because the **more amount of time gives you more opportunity for evaluation** (to make an updated judgment)

  5. If Ellen is unwilling to take Schoolco‚Äôs offer, does that tell us something about her prediction for the business?  
- Yes. **Ellen‚Äôs unwillingness to take the higher but more uncertain offer will signal to the investor that she doesn‚Äôt believe in the idea**  
  * Truly confident CEO would take Schoolco‚Äôs offer and less confident CEO would take Pensionco‚Äôs offer

- Value of Staged Financing:  
  * 1\) Time for Expert Funder to evaluate investment, update the valuation, and decide if they want to continue  
  * 2\) Signaling how confident the owner is that the company will succeed

- **High tech start-ups have a lot of uncertainty. Addressed by:**  
  * **Expert investors who know enough about technology to evaluate the initial investment (but they still don‚Äôt have as much information as the Founder ‚Äì still have information asymmetry) so:**  
  * **Expert investors use staged financing to pay to evaluate their decision at a later point and gain information from the Founder about her confidence in the idea**  
- **These mechanisms also addresses an incentive issue for the Founder to lie about how good their idea is**

3\.   Assume that Ellen takes Schoolco‚Äôs offer.  Assume also that six months later, Schoolco decides not to buy the additional 250,000 shares.  At that point, Ellen goes back to Pensionco to ask if they want to invest.  If you are advising Pensionco, would it matter to you that Schoolco had decided not to invest?

- Yes. If another investor, with more expertise than they had and worked with the Founder, decided to pass after a few months of working with the Founder, then they will likely pass too because the initial investor‚Äôs pass signals that this is not a good company

- **Problem for Ellen: Once she agrees to this structure, she is putting herself at Schoolco‚Äôs mercy**  
  * If it is going badly ü°™ Schoolco is out  
  * If it is going well ü°™ Schoolco can pretend to be out and then re-negotiate the terms of their deal more favorably because other investors will not work with her after seeing this Signal  
- **So, Ellen‚Äôs rejection of Schoolco‚Äôs initial offer could be a signal of lack of confidence or a signal that she doesn‚Äôt trust Schoolco**  
  * **So it is hard for Schoolco to know at the outset if her rejection of their offer is due to lack of confidence in her company or due to her lack of trust in them**  
    * **Repeat Players: This vulnerability doesn‚Äôt actually come to fruition for the CEO because there are not many players so a VC‚Äôs poor reputation can be problematic for it to get the next deal**

- **Imbalances of information**  
  * **Contractual mechanisms meant to bridge the imbalances**  
  * **This also aligns incentives**  
- **Incentive Problem: Capital provider can try to manipulate or re-negotiate**  
  * **But they don‚Äôt want to do that because if their reputation takes a hit then they won‚Äôt get to work with the other players**

- VC Idea: 1 in 10 will succeed, 2 in 10 will survive, the rest will fail ü°™ Failure is not embarrassing  
  * Ellen can fail and come back and get funding in the new venture  
  * Firms can make poor investments and get new great venture deals

4\.  What is a ‚Äúright of first refusal‚Äù?  Why is it costly to grant one?  If it is, why would anyone offer one?

- Right of first refusal: Agreement where you give someone the option to invest in something first, and they have the option to turn it down before any other investors can come in  
- It is costly because:  
  * You lock in a price and forgo possible auction bidding  
  * If the investor says no, depending upon how informed they are, their answer may be a negative signal to the rest of the industry which would make it hard for you to sell someone else  
- Grant one anyway because:  
  * You can get more upfront money  
  * It signals confidence (especially given the reputational constraints on investors noted above)

5\.  Do you agree or disagree with the following statements?: 

a.  	As long as the underlying business makes sense, it doesn‚Äôt matter who owns it or how the ownership is divided.

- False.  
- Owners matter (expertise)  
- Structure matters (staged financing, incentive alignment)  
- Can‚Äôt just say if the product will work I will invest. A lot goes into if the product will work: owners, capital, incentives created

  b. 	In structuring a new deal, we should use the structure from a past deal as a model (or ‚Äúprecedent‚Äô).

- True to some extent.  
- Want to model a deal on another deal makes sense because you don‚Äôt need to reinvent the wheel. However, you cannot hold onto the precedent too closely because there is new context

  c.  	**We need to structure a deal to reflect what each party brings to the table**.

- True.  
- Schoolco and Pensionco want different deal terms because one of them brings expertise which makes staged financing valuable and the other doesn‚Äôt so they do not want to pay for that extra time

  d. 	**Current structuring can affect future bargaining**.

- True.  
- As soon as Ellen consents to the staged financing, she is opening herself up to a strategic re-negotiation by the investor  
  * But she can be confident in the fact that the industry monitors poor behavior and penalizes it

5. **When we structure a deal, we need to focus on the information each party has (or does not have)**.   
- True.

6\.  What is venture capital?  What type of businesses do venture capitalists (‚ÄúVC‚Äôs‚Äù) finance?

- **VC is start-up, high-technology investing** (new technologies incubated by small companies)  
  * Need both components  
- **Asymmetric Information and uncertainty exist in every transaction, but exist here more than in other industries** 

7\.  A Columbia law professor named Adolph Berle (writing with Gardiner Means) identified a problem in public corporations, which they called ‚Äúseparation of ownership and control.‚Äù The problem was that there were so many owners that individual owners did not have an incentive to monitor the business, so managers had too much power, which they could use in self-interested ways.  Does this problem arise when venture-capitalists make investments?

- This problem does not arise in VC because there are small number of investors and employees so it is easier to monitor  
  * **VC has extreme uncertainty/information (both in the company and the Founder)/incentive problems but lacks other common problems like transactions costs and monitoring costs (like public company)**

8\.  Venture capitalists often specialize in a particular sector.  Why?

- Specialization even within types of technology because investors get more expertise which lowers the information asymmetry  
- They can gain the expertise and make the relationships by focusing their time on one particular sector more deeply than if they invested in many different sectors  
  * This makes them more attractive to founders and more equipped to evaluate the value of good investments

9\.  Do venture capitalists usually invest all at once or invest in stages?  Why?

- **Staged financing is an important component of VC because it gives:**  
  * **1\) Opportunity for evaluation of the investment (not all in right away)**  
  * **2\) Signaling of founder confidence**  
- **Staged financing and expert investors solve information asymmetry**  
  * **Expert investors: People know enough about the particular sector that they have a better chance than most to understand the viability of a certain idea or certain Founder**  
  * **Because the investors are experts, they want staged financing because it allows them to re-evaluate the company down the line. The additional time helps to bridge the asymmetric information problem**  
    * **Although they know less now, they will know more in the future when they need to decide whether to allocate more capital**  
- **Staged financing also helps align incentives because to get another round of funding, the Founder must demonstrate success early on**  
- **Staged financing also signals to investors the confidence of the Founder by demonstrating that Founder is willing to accept preliminary funding because he thinks he will be successful enough to earn a future round**  
- **Staged financing also creates a problem by putting a great deal of power in the hands of the VC (see re-negotiation issue)**  
  * **But this can be minimized by repeat players/reputation**

10\. Beth offers you the following proposal: If you invest $1,000, she will use it to develop a cure for the common cold.  She is close to a cure, but there are no guarantees.  If she is successful, your $1,000 will be worth $100,000.  But she could fail, leaving you with nothing.  What risks do you see in this proposal?

a.  	In dealing with these risks, are you comforted or are you more nervous if this investment is one of several that you make? Does diversification help with uncertainty? Or asymmetric information? Or agency costs?

- Diversification of investment helps with uncertainty because it is more likely that one will succeed and outweigh the costs of the failures (we will be right once)  
- **Diversification does not help with asymmetric information and agency costs because all the VC investments present the same asymmetric information and agency cost (incentive problems) at every company**  
  * These are systemic issues that could be true at every company so diversification won‚Äôt help this  
  * 10 founders can cheat you just as easily as 1

  b. 	Does it matter if you personally have expertise about developing drugs? Can this expertise help with uncertainty? Or asymmetric information? Or agency costs?

- **My personal expertise developing drugs makes the investment less risky**  
- **This expertise lowers the problems associated with uncertainty, asymmetric information, and agency costs because:**  
  * **Uncertainty is lessened if I have some knowledge of what is going on**  
  * **Asymmetric information is lessened because I can evaluate the company**  
  * **Agency costs are lessened because Founder knows I can evaluate what he is doing effectively**

- This makes me more confident in the investment (better able to assess), limits the information the owner can keep from me because they know I can evaluate it, and empowers me to assert my views  
  * For agency costs, the person you are dealing with is less likely to do what is good for them (and not you) if they think you are less likely to notice. Expertise empowers me to notice and lets founder know I can notice so they are less likely to keep things from me

  c.  	Let‚Äôs say that Beth is very wealthy and can finance the investment herself.  Is that fact relevant to your decision about whether to invest?

- Yes. I would be less likely to invest because this is a signal that Beth does not trust her own company because she does not want the risk of investing her own money

  d. 	Let‚Äôs say you learn that Beth is going to do some of the relevant research as part of graduate work she is doing in biochemistry.  Is that relevant to your decision? How might it be helpful? How might it be unhelpful?

- This might be helpful because the university credentials, validates her (they trusted her so I should too)  
- This might be unhelpful because she is going to prioritize research findings over a commercially viable product  
  * Mixed motivations (is her heart in the business or the degree?)

  e.  	Let‚Äôs say you learn that Beth plays a lot of golf.  Is that relevant to your decision?

- Yes. This would mean that Beth is less invested in making the product work

6. Can we use a contract to address the risks you have identified?  If this is hard to do, why is it hard?  
- This is hard to do because these are life decisions Beth is making outside the scope of the employment of the product. It is difficult to control her life decisions within a business contract

7. Do you agree with the following statement and, if so, why? ‚Äú**An organization can substitute for a contract**.‚Äù  
- We do not rely on detail specific contracts to specify how we go from idea to product  
- **Instead, we have to create the conditions in which information is exchanged as needed to build trust and in which people have incentives to do what is in the interest of the business and not themselves**

11\. Would it help if Beth in the above problem is paid a small cash salary, and her main compensation is shares in the new venture? When an entrepreneur and a venture capitalist strike a deal, how is the entrepreneur usually paid?  Why?

- Yes. The entrepreneur is usually paid in equity with a small cash salary. This incentivizes the entrepreneur to work hard at making the best possible product because their wealth is directly tied to the success of the company

- **Key Pillar of VC: Incentive compensation**  
  * Note that this is different from staged financing  
  * People doing the work will prioritize the things you want them to because there is a lot at stake for them personally  
    * **You don‚Äôt need to contract exactly what needs to happen if you can motivate the founder to want what you want**

12\.  Steve founds a computer company. He takes a modest salary and owns all the equity in the startup. He comes up with a new type of personal computer, and needs capital to produce and sell them. Venture Capital invests $2 million in exchange for convertible preferred shares, which can be converted into common shares representing ‚Öì of the company. The preferred stock has extra voting rights, entitling the VC to four of the company‚Äôs six board seats.

1. Is it typical for VCs to control the company before it goes public?

- Yes. VC‚Äôs do not own a majority of the business but they have majority voting control (This is control beyond their economic investment)

2.  Why or why not?

- Founders have so much inherent control (your idea, your operations)  
  * Founder has so much asymmetric information that it‚Äôs a problem for VC  
  * Founder has control on day to day basis  
  * VC has legal control

- VC‚Äôs need this voting (legal) control to compensate for the day to day control the Founder has  
  * They want say in when business is sold etc.

3. What risk does an entrepreneur take in giving venture capitalists control rights?

- Risk of being replaced  
  * Jobs fired from Apple early on  
- Repeat players ü°™ Reputational Constraint  
  * **VC firms do not replace founders often because they don‚Äôt want to have that stain on their reputation or they will not get the best Founders**

4. When do venture capitalists lose their control rights?  What incentive does this create for an entrepreneur?

- **VC‚Äôs have preferred stock with extra voting rights that turns into common stock without extra voting rights control at IPO**

- **Incentive Mechanism (like incentive compensation): Founders want to IPO so that they can undo the control**  
  * **At IPO, Founder gets money but also job security**  
  * **This aligns incentive with VC‚Äôs wanting an IPO so that they can sell their stock, cash out, and invest in a new venture**

5. Are there reasons why a venture capitalist might hesitate to fire an entrepreneur, even if she has the legal right to do so?

- Reputational Constraints

13\.  So far, we have focused on the relationship between start-ups and their venture capitalist investors. But there is another relevant relationship: Venture capitalists often have partners in their funds, like universities and pension funds. Let‚Äôs turn to the issues that can arise in that relationship and the ways VCs address them.

1. We have discussed incentive and information problems that arise when VCs invest in startups. But are there **incentive and information problems that arise when universities, pension funds, and other investors invest in VCs**? How do the latter issues compare with the issues the VCs themselves face in deciding whether to invest with an entrepreneur?

- Less uncertainty with a VC than with a new technology. VC‚Äôs have a track record that the investors can look to  
- But similarly to a VC-Founder investment, there are large gaps in understanding what the VC investments look like  
  * Asymmetric Information ‚Äì The VC‚Äôs have the expertise in the portfolio that the university investor doesn‚Äôt have  
  * Incentive Problem ‚Äì The VC has reason to tell the university that they will do a great job  
- **Same problems that exist at portco level, exist at the fund level**  
  * **Experts say they can generate value but hard for other investors to understand due to asymmetric information and incentive problems**

2. How do universities, pension funds, and other investors compensate venture capitalists?  Why do you think the pay takes that form?

- Incentive Based Compensation:  
  * Small management fee ‚Äì 2%  
  * **They don‚Äôt make any additional money unless they earn profit**  
    * The profits are shared between VC and Founder  
      * Usually VC takes 20 percent of profits and investors get the rest

- **They themselves get compensated in the same way that they compensate the founders**  
- **At one level, the VC‚Äôs have an interest that are perfectly aligned with their investors ü°™ They both want profits**  
- **However, there is incentive misalignment because the compensation structure promotes risk:**  
  * **The VC gets 20 percent of profits but bears none of the losses (these are on the investor)**  
    * Challenge of Incentive Compensation: Aligns incentives but if taken too far it can cause a party to take unnecessary risk

3. If a university invests in a venture capital fund, is there a particular date on which it is supposed to get its money back?  Why are VC funds structured in this way?

- VC‚Äôs in effect use staged financing also for the outside investors  
  * You come into this fund with us. Give us money for a particular period of time, and we will return it back to you  
  * Then the VC‚Äôs hope is that investor will come back for Fund 2 and Fund 3  
  * They will always do this, but it is up to the investor whether they want to keep investing  
- Investors can try it once and decide later if they want to keep going. They will do this only invest again if it worked the first time

- **2 Critical Elements Used at Both the Portco and VC level:**  
  * **1\) Staged Financing**  
  * **2\) Incentive Compensation**

14\. Assume that University invests $1 million with VC. University agrees to pay a 2% annual fee ($20k), and also to share 20% of **profits above** a 10% **annual return (called a ‚Äúcarried interest‚Äù)**. VC uses University‚Äôs money to invest $500k in each of two ventures, A and B.

1. After two years, VC sells its position in A for $1 million, doubling University‚Äôs money. A 10% return on $500k over two years is approximately $100,000. The profit above that level, therefore, is $400,000. How much should VC receive?

- VC receives: $40K (2 years of 2% annual fees) \+ $80K (20% of profits above $100K) \= $120K

2. What if B goes bankrupt in the following year, so that University loses all $500,000 invested in it. Obviously, VC gets no carried interest for B, since there is no profit. But is there a problem with the carried interest that VC has already claimed for the investment in A.

- Yes, because now University has paid VC too much because the deal was for total profits (not for profits in A only). Total profit is now $500K  
  * The carry of $80K is wrong because the first investment generated it, but the second investment failed  
  * 10% annual return for 2 years of the whole investment \= $200K so profit above 10% annual return \= $300K. Therefore, $300(20%) \= $60K  
  * Clawback \= $60K

3. What is a clawback, and why are they included in VC funds?

- Clawback \= VCs (general partners) take back some of the carried interest given to the other investors (limited partners) when the initial payout was incorrect

4. Under U.S. law, does the ‚Äúcarried interest‚Äù earned by the VC seem more like salary or an investment? Do you know whether it is taxed as ordinary income or capital gain?

- **Carried interest is a salary bonus for investing the other investors‚Äô money, but it is taxed as capital gain**  
  * Too generous treatment because it is a bonus for a job well done but it is taxed as capital gain when it vests

15\. Which of the following terms do you think are likely to appear in University‚Äôs contract with the VC.

1. University generally will have its capital returned by a set date, with a limited exception for exceptional circumstances.

- Yes.  
- This is the staged financing. There will be a time limit

2. The VC cannot sell or transfer its general partnership in the fund to anyone without University‚Äôs consent.

- Yes.  
- When you invest with a VC, you invest in the team that is there. Limited partners do not trust a new regime

3. The VC cannot invest in businesses owned by individual partners in the VC firm or their family members without University‚Äôs consent. 

- Yes. Greater agency costs and more asymmetric information so VC can do something for their own best interest  
- Why allow the consent possibility?  
  * Maybe the family members will be prominent tech entrepreneurs. Don‚Äôt want to shut the possibility out entirely but just shut it out if it done for the wrong reasons

4. The VC cannot operate any other funds.

- **No. Staged financing model means that investors can re-up into new funds**  
- **But this creates a risk for investor: Because then VC can find a great investment that investor is not a part of**  
  * **VC would say that to ensure you are in everything we do, you just need to re-up into a new fund (part of the stage financing model)**  
    * Usually if you are in one fund, VC allows you to be in another automatically  
  * **Or VC will say that they will try to include as many of their funds as possible in each opportunity. So the fact that you are in one of these funds means that you will get a slice of what they do overall**

# 8: VC (Capital Structure, Incentive Compensation, Tax)

- VC is unique area because it has unusually concentrated:  
  * Uncertainty  
  * Information asymmetry  
  * Can generate incentive problems  
  * No free riding because of small amount of repeat players (focused allocation of capital)  
- Incentive misalignment

- 4 Qualities of VC Investors:  
1) Subject matter Experts  
   * Bridges asymmetric information  
2) Staged Financing ‚Äì Addresses asymmetric information and incentive problem  
3) Incentive compensation ‚Äì Monitoring entrepreneur by having the founder monitor herself by financially rewarding her for company‚Äôs success  
4) Control Rights: Right kind of success (IPO) means Founder gets control of company back

- VC‚Äôs confront similar problems with their funders and use same solutions:  
  * 1\) Staged Financing  
  * 2\) Incentive compensation

- Tax planning structure in VC super-charges the incentive compensation (by taxing it at capital gains rate):  
  * Debt: Paid first, no upside  
    * Not a lot of debt in VC because there aren‚Äôt many hard assets  
  * There are different tiers of ownership interests:  
    * Plain Vanilla Preferred Stock: Paid first fixed amount, larger dividend, little upside  
      * Functions like debt when there isn‚Äôt any  
    * Common Stock: Residual claimant, paid last after preferred stock  
    * Convertible Preferred: Preferred stock that converts to common stock if you choose  
      * Usually will be discounted (worth 9/10 of common stock share)

- When to use Common/Preferred?  
  * Mature company with a lot of assets that is acquiring a boutique company  
    * Market reaction is mixed: Issue plain vanilla preferred stock (we have more assets than debt so you will get paid no matter what even if acquisition is bad)  
      * Could be good because new company could refresh the brands of acquirer  
      * Could be bad because companies don‚Äôt fit  
    * Issue common stock where market reaction is that this is very good  
- **Note: In VC, shareholders are forced to convert at IPO**

- Tax treatment of VC  
  * Incentive compensation plays an important role in the VC industry to solve information asymmetry and incentive problems  
    * Don‚Äôt need to monitor founder anymore ‚Äì she will monitor herself  
    * Industry has also developed to use US tax law to supercharge that incentive by taxing it at the capital gain rate  
      * General Rule: When you get paid in property, you include FMV in income  
      * But in VC: Stock you get must vest before you keep it  
        * **Default Tax Rule: You don‚Äôt pay tax until stock vests. Pay at ordinary rates when it vests (and then from there the changes after vesting are capital gains/losses)**  
        * **83(b) Election: Pay value of stock today at time received. Choose to pay FMV on stock at time you get it before it vests and pay capital gain on changes thereafter**  
          * It is as if you got cash and used the cash to buy the stock  
          * Value your stock as low as possible at time received  
            * **Incentive to offer low valuation (when you are reporting at ordinary rate)**  
          * Can‚Äôt low ball valuation when you have stock in public company  
          * But with private company, the deals are structured so that it isn‚Äôt obvious to the government what the valuation is  
            * **Convertible preferred stock to VC investor gives Founder discretion to say that they are getting something different (their common stock) than the VC investor so they can report a low valuation and pay less in ordinary, more in capital gains**  
      * Cost to this strategy:  
        * Company loses deduction  
        * **Companies don‚Äôt actually mind because they don‚Äôt have offsetting income yet**

**1\.** Background: Two Types of income

A. What are the two types of income in the U.S. tax system? Which type do taxpayers usually prefer?

- Capital gain is preferred to ordinary income

B. How is income from performing services usually classified?

- Ordinary

C. What about gains from selling a profitable investment?

- Capital gain

D. Ann starts her own business and owns 100% of the stock. Through her hard work, the value of the stock doubles, so she sells it to a private equity firm. Is her profit taxed as ordinary income or as capital gain?

- Capital gain  
- The difference between capital gain and ordinary income is not always clear  
  * **The reason the investment did well is because of her personal work, measured in an increase in the stock price so taxed as capital gain**

E. Ann runs Barbara‚Äôs business, and makes it much more profitable. Barbara rewards Ann with a generous bonus. Is this bonus ordinary income or capital gain?

- Ordinary income  
- Conceptually, these are similar situations, taxed differently  
- We want to convert our labor/work to look like an investment gain (capital gain)

2\. Please compare the following three types of equity securities \-- common stock, ‚Äúplain vanilla‚Äù preferred stock, and convertible preferred stock \-- using the following criteria:

- Convertible preferred stock is a hybrid  
  * Convertible preferred acts like preferred stock as long as you do not convert  
    * Business failing: Acts like preferred stock and you are paid fixed amount before common stock holders  
    * Business successful: You can convert to common stock and become a residual claimant  
      * Usually not a full share (9/10th) which makes it slightly worse than common stock if business succeeding

1. Which would you rather have if the company‚Äôs business is doing really well?

- Business successful: Common stock because you have unlimited upside (not capped at the fixed amount like plain vanilla preferred)  
  * You are residual claimant (if all other claimants like debt and preferred stock holders have been paid, everything else is yours)

2. What if the business is doing badly?  
- Business failing: Plain vanilla preferred stock because you are first in line to collect if company is insolvent, and you will collect a fixed amount (behind debt which is rare in VC-backed companies because start-ups don‚Äôt have hard assets)

3. Do you think they will have the same voting and control rights?

- They all have different voting power and control rights

3\. Before considering the significance of these differences in a VC-backed start-up, let‚Äôs begin with another setting: a consumer products company. Proctor & Scramble (‚ÄúP\&S‚Äù) manufactures and sells a large portfolio of ‚Äúname brands,‚Äù which are popular with consumers, including detergent, hand soup, paper towels, toothpaste, and similar products. The company has $1 billion of debt outstanding, and the value of its outstanding equity is $2 billion (suggesting that its assets are worth $3 billion).  

P\&S has just hired a new CEO, Alice, who comes from the ‚Äúedgier‚Äù world of marketing to young people. P\&S‚Äôs board is concerned that the company does not appeal enough to the younger generation, and that its image is a bit old-fashioned. In her first six months, Alice decides to acquire a company \-- Millennial Mania (‚ÄúM\&M‚Äù) \-- which markets ‚Äúhipper‚Äù products to younger people. 

Alice‚Äôs believes that the two companies will complement each other. M\&M will help to ‚Äúrefresh‚Äù P\&S‚Äôs brands, and will provide a pipeline of new consumers. But some market analysts worry that the two companies are not compatible at all, and that the acquisition might end badly. Alice needs $500 million in cash to buy M\&M, and she reaches out to Bill, her investment banker, to consider options for raising this cash. 

1. Alice asks Bill about issuing $500 M of common stock. Before answering her, Bill reads a negative story about the deal in the financial press. ‚ÄúM\&M is a niche firm for flakey young people,‚Äù a prominent market analyst is quoted as saying, ‚Äúand its employees have complete contempt for a business like P\&S. If these two companies are brought together, they will be looking to part company soon.‚Äù Given this coverage, why might Bill hesitate to recommend common stock?

- **If you sell common stock, you need people to believe in the transaction**  
- If the market is unsure about the transaction, you should not get common stock

2. Instead, Alice asks Bill if P\&S should issue $500M of ‚Äúplain vanilla‚Äù preferred stock to fund the cash acquisition. It will pay a 3% dividend (compared with the 1% dividend yield on P\&S common) and has a liquidation preference. If the acquisition does not go well, how will the preferred shareholders do? How does their situation compare with that of common stockholders?  
- Preferred stockholders will do better than the common stockholders because they have a liquidation preference (and have a higher dividend payout)  
  * Preferred stockholders still get paid even if the business fails (as long as they have some assets to pay you with)  
    * There are $3B in assets here and $1B of debt that claims above you. But then there is $2B left. You come in ahead of everyone else to claim that $2B  
- **Preferred stockholders do not need to believe in the acquisition**

3. What if the acquisition goes well (as Alice hopes)? How will the return on the preferred compare with the return on the common?  
- Return on the common stock would be better (preferred stockholders only get their 3% return)  
  * Preferred stockholders will not participate in any upside

4. Instead of ‚Äúplain vanilla‚Äù preferred, Bill recommends convertible preferred, which pays only a 1.5% dividend, has a liquidation preference (only if shareholders don‚Äôt convert), and can be converted at the shareholder‚Äôs option at any time for five years; each preferred share can be converted into .9 shares of common. Why do you think he makes this recommendation?

- Convertible preferred  
  * Bigger dividend than common, smaller than plain vanilla preferred

- **Market loves deal ü°™ Sell common**  
- **Market wary ü°™ Sell plain vanilla preferred**  
- **Market is unsure (sees potential for upside and potential for problems) ü°™ Sell convertible preferred**  
  * Liquidation preference if things go badly  
  * 9/10th of upside of common if things go well  
  * This is the security that VC‚Äôs use in a start-up

4\. Carl was your brother‚Äôs college roommate. He majored in computer science, and has been working for Apple. He and your brother meet you for coffee, and Carl mentions that he has an idea that could revolutionize digital streaming of video content, making it faster and more reliable. 

You mention that you have some money saved up, and you and some friends might be interested in investing. 

Carl says, ‚ÄúThat could be interesting, but I want to be open about something. This is a risky bet. The big tech companies are working on this too. I have a better idea, but I have to work fast. If they develop something that‚Äôs almost as good before I‚Äôm done, the industry is just going to go with that, and we will miss this opportunity.‚Äù You say you understand and you and Carl begin discussing terms. 

1.  Carl‚Äôs annual salary at Apple is $300,000. He says he wants the same salary from you \-- ‚Äúor it‚Äôs not worth it to me to do this‚Äù \-- and he offers you and your friends 100% of the equity in the newly-formed company. What incentive does this create for Carl? What signal do you think Carl‚Äôs proposal sends about his confidence in his idea?

- Low incentive for Carl and signals low confidence  
  * He is not interested in owning a part of the business  
2. Instead of the proposal in A, Carl says that he is giving up a $300k salary at Apple, and will work for a $75,000 cash salary \-- just enough to cover rent on his studio apartment, food, and health insurance. He wants to own 51% of the equity in the newly-formed company, even though you and your friends are supplying all of the capital. What incentive does this create for Carl? What signal do you think this proposal sends about his confidence in the idea?

- This incentives Carl to work hard  
  * He is taking a pay cut, but he doesn‚Äôt look at it like this because he thinks the cash he is giving up is worth less than the stock he is getting  
  * This is the way the industry works (less cash, more stock)

3. Carl‚Äôs initial proposal is that everyone gets voting common stock. Why might you be concerned about giving Carl 51% of the voting rights?

- Carl would make decisions on his own without consulting investors  
- This voting control is on top of the day to day control he has and the asymmetric information he had about the idea

4. You tell Carl that you want 51%, and he objects. ‚ÄúI‚Äôm going to develop a great idea, and I don‚Äôt want you to kick me out of the company as soon as it succeeds. That‚Äôs what happened to Steve Jobs the first time he was CEO of Apple, and I won‚Äôt make that mistake\!‚Äù  You explain that you are comfortable giving him control once the idea proves itself, but that you and your friends need control before. Assuming he agrees, is there a way to work this out using preferred stock? Is there also a way to do it without preferred stock?

- Allocation of control is a problem that needs to be figured out at the start of the company (each side worries that the other will take advantage of it)

- Preferred Stock Solution:  
  * Founder gets common stock ‚Äì one vote per share  
  * VC gets preferred stock with extra voting rights ‚Äì two votes per share  
    * Convertible preferred stock automatically converts, losing extra voting rights, when there is an IPO  
      * Capital providers‚Äô preferred stock converts automatically to common at IPO  
- **Control rights allocation is why convertible preferred stock is so popular/uniform in VC (virtually every deal done the same way)**  
  * Start out favoring the VC until the idea is proven at IPO and VC has recouped investment and more  
  * **Founder incentivized to get to IPO to get control back**

- How else could you do it with just common stock ü°™ Voting Rights Agreement:  
  * Founder enters into K with VC where investors get 20X voting rights and this K expires at IPO  
    * **Because allocating control rights can be accomplished through the voting rights agreement, there must be other reasons for the preferred stock practice**

5. Carl wants you and your friends to invest $2 million for your share of the equity. He is putting in only his idea and his effort, but no capital, as noted above. He will use the $2 million to cover salaries for a small team of programmers, office space, and computers. At the ‚Äúburn rate‚Äù he projects, the $2 million will last for just over 18 months. After 18 months, assume that another company wins the race to develop an idea like the one Carl is trying to implement, so Carl‚Äôs approach is unlikely to be adopted. If you have preferred stock, how valuable is your liquidation preference likely to be in this scenario?

- Liquidation Preference: Who gets paid first  
- Not useful to have a liquidation preference when there are no assets left

- **Liquidation Preference is Not valuable in a VC setting because the company is either a complete success or fails (there are usually no assets to claim)**  
  * **Another explanation that doesn‚Äôt work to justify the convertible preferred issuing**  
  * Two tiered capital structure (with VC getting preferred shares) is not meant to protect cash flows of VC because there are no assets remaining

6. Please compare this fact pattern with the one above about P\&S‚Äôs acquisition of M\&M. Is a liquidation preference in a high-tech start-up likely to be more or less meaningful than in a consumer products company? Why?

- Liquidation Preference is much less meaningful in startup than consumer products company

5\. The Gilson & Schizer (‚ÄúG\&S‚Äù) article analyzes the economic significance of various features of convertible preferred stock in high-tech startups financed by venture capitalists.

1. What are dividend preferences? How significant are they in VC-backed firms?

- Dividend preferences \= Who gets the dividend first  
  * Preferred shareholders with a dividend preference get paid before common stock  
- If common shareholders receive a dividend, then preferred shareholders have to receive that dividend first. But if common stock is not paid in dividends, preferred shareholders are not required to be paid  
- Is dividend preference valuable in consumer products context?  
  * Yes. These companies commonly pay dividends  
- Is dividend preference valuable in VC startup?  
  * No. No expectation of dividend  
- **Dividend preference is meaningless in VC**  
  * **Another explanation that doesn‚Äôt work to justify the convertible preferred issuing**

2. What are liquidation preferences? How significant are they when a VC-backed startup fails?

- Not valuable

3. How significant is a liquidation preference when a VC-backed start-up is sold to another firm? (Hint: the article focuses on what it calls a ‚Äúzombie‚Äù scenario.)

- In some situations, a liquidation preference can be valuable to a startup investor: when the startup gets bought by a bigger company, the money will first go to preferred stockholders with a liquidation preference (VC‚Äôs)

4. How significant is a liquidation preference when venture capitalists sell their shares in an initial public offering?

- **Not valuable at all if the company does well**  
  * **If startup fails ü°™ No liquidation**  
  * **If startup succeeds ü°™ With an IPO, the convertible preferred converts to common and liquidation preference goes away**  
    * **In both situations the liquidation preference is in the same position as the common (non-preferenced shares)**  
    * **The only situation where liquidation preference is helpful is when the start-up is bought by a bigger company. Then, the preferred shareholders (VC) will get their money first. But this is pretty insignificant because VC‚Äôs are not investing in start-ups wanting this result**

5. Some commentators argue that giving VCs convertible preferred pushes entrepreneurs to ‚Äúsignal‚Äù whether they really believe in their idea. Please explain this argument. Do you agree with it?

- Founders give VC‚Äôs liquidation preference to show confidence (if things go badly, they will be paid first)  
  * **But this doesn‚Äôt signal anything to VC‚Äôs who know that there will be no assets to collect and know the liquidation preference is meaningless**  
  * **Another explanation that doesn‚Äôt work to justify the convertible preferred issuing**

6. Some commentators argue that giving VCs convertible preferred motivates entrepreneurs to be successful enough that VCs sell shares in an IPO, instead of a sale to another company. Please explain this argument. Do entrepreneurs have other reasons to prefer an IPO to a sale to another company?

- VCs may want to sell a portfolio company to another larger company because the VC retains their preference in an acquisition but has to convert in an IPO. Founders want to encourage VCs to exit in acquisition rather than IPO because VC will control less of the company after acquisition  
- This also doesn‚Äôt explain the need for convertible preferred because either party can block the other from exiting  
  * **Another explanation that doesn‚Äôt work to justify the convertible preferred issuing**

7. Does convertible preferred give venture capitalists extra control rights? Does this explain the ubiquity of convertible preferred? Are there other ways to accomplish this goal?

- Convertible preferred gives VCs extra control rights  
  * This doesn‚Äôt necessarily explain why VCs want convertible preferred because although you allocate control rights through it, there are other ways to allocate this

- **VCs take convertible preferred because of tax reasons**

6\. Let‚Äôs turn to the tax planning issue, which is the key point in G\&S. As we discussed in an earlier class, in the United States there are two types of income. Salary income is classified as ‚Äúordinary income‚Äù (taxed at a maximum federal rate of 37%) while profit from a risky investment is classified as ‚Äúcapital gain‚Äù (taxed at a maximum federal rate of 23.8%). 

1. Assume that you hire Ed to run your privately-owned company, and you pay him $300,000 of cash. Is this payment ordinary income or capital gain?

- Ordinary income \= Salary

2. If you pay Ed $200,000 of cash and $100,000 in gold bullion, what tax rate applies to the gold?

- Ordinary income \= Salary  
  * The tax law looks at the reason you are getting the money on how to tax it. Not the form that the payment takes

3. Assume you pay Ed $200,000 of cash, and you also grant Ed $100,000 of stock in the company in 2021, which will vest in 2023\. Assume that in 2023, the stock is worth $500,000. Assuming Ed does not make any special tax elections, how much income does he have in 2021? Is it ordinary or capital? How much income does he have in 2023? Is it ordinary or capital?

- $200K cash is ordinary income  
- Stock hasn‚Äôt vested yet  
  * 2021: No tax  
    * **General Rule: You do not pay tax until stock vests**  
  * 2023: Ordinary income included at the full amount \= $500K

4. The same facts as in ‚ÄúC,‚Äù but Ed makes a so-called ‚Äú83(b) election,‚Äù in which he chooses to pay tax on the value of the stock when he receives it (even though the stock has not vested yet). This means he has $300,000 of income in 2021\. Is it ordinary or capital? In 2023, this stock is worth $500,000, so Ed sells it. What is Ed‚Äôs profit on this stock in 2023? Is this profit taxed as ordinary income or capital gain?

- 2021: $200K cash is ordinary \+ $100K is ordinary  
  * Pay tax at ordinary rates on stock for what it is worth when you receive it  
- 2023: $400K is capital gain  
  * Treated as if he bought it for its worth in 2023  
  * The profit is treated as capital gain  
- Very common for Founders to make 83(b) elections  
  * **Pay tax upfront at ordinary rate; Pay gains later**

5. If Ed expects the stock he receives as compensation to appreciate, does he increase or reduce his tax bill by making the election to pay tax when he receives the stock (instead of waiting and paying tax later when the stock vests)? Why?

- Reducing by conversion

6. So far, we have assumed that the value of Ed‚Äôs stock is $100,000 when he receives it. But let‚Äôs assume instead that the value isn‚Äôt clear. If Ed elects to pay tax when he receives the stock (the 83(b) election, described above), and his goal is to reduce his tax bill, is he motivated to offer a high estimate of the stock‚Äôs value when he receives it? Or a low estimate? In other words, as a matter of tax planning, does Ed want to claim that the stock is worth a lot, or very little, when he receives it?

- **Ed wants to claim that the stock is worth very little when he receives it because this is the amount he pays a higher, ordinary rate on** (then the appreciation of the stock he pays capital gains on so he would rather have more of the stock allocated to the capital gains rate)  
  * 83(b) election encourages claiming a lower valuation at first

7. Assume that at the same time that Ed receives stock, Frank \-- an outside investor \-- pays $100,000 for identical shares. Does Ed have any discretion in the value that he reports to the tax authorities?

- No. The value is given by fair market value (what a third party would pay for it)  
  * The outside investor leaves the Founder no room to change the valuation of his common shares

8. The same as in ‚Äúg,‚Äù but Frank pays $100,000 for convertible preferred shares, and Ed receives common shares. Does Ed still have to report $100,000 as the value of his stock?

- This time he can report less  
- **As soon as you can show that third party investor is paying for something different, Founder can say that the valuation of their convertible preferred is lower and that lower valuation means that they pay more in capital gains and less in ordinary on the stock if they elect**

9. G\&S offers reasons why convertible preferred shares actually should not be worth much more than the common stock (e.g., the dividend and liquidation preferences have only limited effect). If these observations about the economics of common and convertible preferred shares are correct, does the tax strategy still work?

- **All the criticisms about how convertible preferred is not that different from common undercut the ability of people to differentiate convertible preferred from common for tax reasons too**  
  * **However, litigating on valuation is something the government does not want to do because valuation challenges do not set precedents** (only single scenarios)  
- **A key reason why convertible preferred has to look different than common (even though the differences are not that great) is that Founder can report a much lower valuation for the common stock**

10. President Biden proposed to tax long term capital gains at the ordinary income rate for anyone earning more than $1 million per year. If this proposal is ever enacted, would this proposal affect this tax planning strategy?

- Yes. The tax planning strategy that benefits Founders which underpins why VCs take convertible preferred stock will no longer be viable so you will see more variety in VC capital structures  
- Schizer interviewed VCs about this as well

7\. In tax planning, it‚Äôs important to consider the tax consequences to *all* parties to the transaction \-- not just to your client, but to everyone. 

1. Assume a company has $10 million of revenue, and pays $2 million of compensation (and has no other expenses). How much is its taxable income? Is compensation tax-deductible?

- $8M  
- Compensation expense is deductible  
- Companies want full tax credit for compensation expenses

2. In problem \#6, we saw that there is an advantage to Ed in offering a low estimate of the value of the stock he receives \-- so he reports less ordinary income. Does this strategy affect the amount that the company can deduct as compensation?

- Ed has advantage in offering low estimate of value, but this lowers the deduction the company can claim  
  * The company‚Äôs deduction is limited to the amount that the employees report as ordinary income  
  * **This tax strategy that underpins the VC industry comes with a cost \= The startup (not VC) loses tax deduction**

3. If Ed‚Äôs planning strategy imposes a tax cost on the company, then won‚Äôt the company block this planning strategy? For example, can‚Äôt the company just force him to report a higher amount? In the venture capital context, why is the company often not concerned about its tax deduction?

- Yes. The company can assign its own valuation to the stock and the government would then audit Ed  
- **But in the VC context, the companies do not challenge the valuation the employee reports because the companies do not have income at the early stage, making deductions they could get nearly irrelevant**  
  * Company is willing to super-charge incentive compensation for its employees by making sure that they get capital gain, and the fact that the company loses its deduction doesn‚Äôt matter to it because it doesn‚Äôt have any income anyway  
  * Note: This is not free because the company could carry the losses forward (NOL). But they don‚Äôt need it still because they have so many losses and so little revenue  
- A consumer products company would not use this strategy because they have income, less losses, and do not want to play games with valuation

8\. G\&S argue that the tax strategy we are discussing is, in essence, a subsidy for venture-capital backed startups. 

1. What is the trigger for the subsidy?

- They need a VC investor to take convertible preferred shares and put money into the startup  
  * VC will take a different security so Founder can low ball the valuation of his different security

2. For the government to put money into a transaction (through a reduced effective tax rate), who else must have ‚Äúskin in the game‚Äù? If you were asked to design a subsidy for startups, would you want to include this feature?

- **Government is putting in money (by giving up tax revenue it could collect) only when expert VCs believe in the deal and put money into it**  
  * **The VCs trust in the company is the trigger for the government‚Äôs subsidy**  
  * If you can get a VC to put money in, government will put money in too. But they are not going to analyze it (unlike if the government made a grant and spent the resources to analyze it themselves)  
- 2 Approaches to Look at this:  
  * **1\) Grant-making model**: Government is releasing money without monitoring  
  * **2\) Expertise model**: Government doesn‚Äôt have the expertise to decide which technologies will succeed and which will fail (and there are political dynamics involved too), so we would rather not have a government agency make the decision. Instead, let government piggyback on the expertise of VC‚Äôs  
    * The tax subsidy is only triggered when VC is putting in money

- Couldn‚Äôt this subsidy occur with a non-expert VC putting in money?  
  * Yes, but that doesn‚Äôt happen often  
  * Additionally, subsidy is only happening (allowing Founder to pay at capital gains) when the investment is successful

9\. Let‚Äôs review the issues we have covered with another fact pattern. Gail, who was your closest friend in college, writes software for Apple. Her dream for years has been to launch her own startup. Using her savings and a contribution from Henry, her father, she starts a business called ‚ÄúGamer Gail,‚Äù which she forms as a Delaware corporation with 10,000 shares. Henry owns 5,000 shares and Gail owns 5,000 shares. 

Paying herself a minimal salary and working out of her basement for two years, Gail writes code for video games. Gail has creative ideas for games and she figures out a way for her graphics to be especially vivid. Her first game, in which the player rescues penguins from global warming, gets positive reviews and modest attention in gaming circles. 

After two years of struggling to stay afloat, Gail knows that she needs more capital to hire a marketing team and more programmers, so she can develop more games and bring them to market more quickly and more effectively. Gail cannot yet count on retained earnings to fund this expansion, since Gamer Gail is not yet profitable and probably won‚Äôt be profitable for at least three more years. 

Gail begins a discussion with Irene, who is a managing director at Gaming Ventures (‚ÄúGV‚Äù), a VC firm that specializes in funding video game startups. Irene proposes to invest $2 million. In return, she wants GV to own one-third of the company. She proposes that there should be five board members: Gail, Henry, Irene, and two of Irene‚Äôs partners from GV. 

Gail is willing to let GV control the company until Gamer Gail proves itself and becomes commercially successful, but once this happens, she wants control. Since she is doing the work, she also wants more shares as compensation for this effort. 

Gail‚Äôs counteroffer is that GV will get 5,000 shares on September 1, and the company will also issue 5,000 more shares to her on September 1, bringing her total share ownership up to 10,000 (with Henry and GV owning another 10,000). In principle, Irene agrees to Gail‚Äôs proposal (i.e., paying $2 million for ¬º of the company and having extra board seats and voting rights until Gamer Gail proves to be successful).

Gail knows that you are a lawyer and that you regularly represent entrepreneurs who run VC-backed startups. She asks you to represent her. Gail is a great programmer, but she doesn‚Äôt know anything about capital structure or tax planning.

1. What capital structure should Gamer Gail have? Specifically, what type of securities should GV receive in return for its investment? What type of securities should Gail receive in exchange for running the company?

- 2-Tiered Capital Structure:  
  * Investors get convertible preferred  
  * Founder and her team gets common stock

2. In light of the deal that Gail and Irene have struck, how should this capital structure allocate rights in liquidation, dividends, and control rights? Which of these issues are economically significant? Which are not?

- Dividend preference to convertible preferred stock  
  * Gail and common shareholders cannot receive a dividend until VC is paid  
  * This is economically insignificant because start-ups don‚Äôt pay dividends 

- Liquidation preference to convertible preferred stock  
  * This is more significant preference than the dividend preference but not very significant because high tech startups do not have hard assets to collect if insolvent  
    * But maybe a bigger company would acquire start-up and this would give the VC a right to paid first with allocation of sale proceeds

- Control rights to convertible preferred stock  
  * But there is a mandatory conversion to common stock at IPO where convertible preferred loses its control rights  
    * This incentivizes Founder to get to IPO

3. What tax advantages (if any) are offered by the capital structure you are recommending? Who benefits from these tax advantages? Is there an offsetting tax disadvantage to another taxpayer? If so, does it matter?  

- Gail will choose 83(b) election and pay ordinary rates on the common stock which she argues has a lower valuation than the convertible preferred  
  * Small amount of ordinary and more of capital gain

- Cost: Company loses a value on its compensation deduction  
  * But the company doesn‚Äôt care because the company is not profitable yet

- What would Founder rather have ‚Äì IPO or buy-out?  
  * IPO because it‚Äôs probably a bigger payday and also because the Founder maintains control

# 9: Nonprofits (Asymmetric Information and Organization as a substitute for Contract ‚Äì Like VC)

- Information asymmetry and incentive problems are in M\&A, VC, and non-profits too  
  * Solve some problems and the solutions beget new problems

- **Nonprofits exist to address 2 kinds of problems:**  
  * **1\) Information Asymmetry (Hansmann):**  
    * **A) Expertise: Buyer doesn‚Äôt understand the quality of what he is buying**  
    * **B) Contact: Buyer doesn‚Äôt know if the goods/services bought will actually be delivered with quality**  
    * **C) Impact: Buyer can monitor the goods/services provided but can‚Äôt tell if his specific purchase made a difference**  
  * **2\) Incentive Problems: The market doesn‚Äôt work when social benefits of a certain kind don‚Äôt get reflected in prices**  
    * **If a bad thing isn‚Äôt reflected in price ü°™ Too much of it (pollution)**  
      * **If a good thing isn‚Äôt reflected in price ü°™ Too little of it (neighbors won‚Äôt spray for mosquitos, free riding)**  
    * **Nonprofits can be a way for people to overcome the coordination problems**  
    * **Government subsidizes nonprofit sector to make it more likely that the organizations will solve these incentive problems**

- **How do nonprofits compare with the government?**  
  * **Where massive amounts of money are involved, government is more likely to be the provider because of its taxing power and economies of scale advantages**  
  * **Where there are services that require experimentation/risk, nonprofit is more likely to deliver the service because of its political isolation**  
    * **Nonprofits also create a Double Utility of Giving which the government does not: The person who gets the good or service is happy but so is the person paying for it**

1. Review: Information Problems in High-Tech Startups   
   1. Arlene is an engineer who has an idea about how to make artificial intelligence more effective. If she pitches her idea to average investors, how effectively can they evaluate it?  
- Information Asymmetry: They cannot evaluate it well

  2. Would it be easy to draft a detailed contract specifying exactly what Arlene should do at each stage of her work in developing her new approach to AI?  
- This is difficult to foresee  
- This is constraining on Arlene‚Äôs development  
- In a situation like this which is long and complex, contracts are not appropriate substitutes

  3. True or false: **Instead of this sort of detailed contract, the VC industry structures deals‚Äîand, indeed, companies--in particular ways to address information problems and produce the right incentives. In other words, the industry uses organizations as a substitute for contracts.**  
- True.

2. Structuring as a Substitute for Contract at Nonprofits: The Nondistribution Constraint  
   1. True or false: **Nonprofits also use organizational structure‚Äîthough, admittedly, a different structure‚Äîto solve information and incentive problems**.  
- True.

  2. The defining feature of a nonprofit is the ‚Äú**nondistribution constraint**.‚Äù According is Henry Hansmann, who pioneered the study of the law and economics of nonprofits, a ‚Äú**nonprofit organization is, in essence, an organization that is barred from distributing its net earnings, if any, to individuals who exercise control over it, such as members, officers, directors, or trustees. By ‚Äònet earnings‚Äô I mean here pure profits-that is, earnings in excess of the amount needed to pay for services rendered to the organization**.‚Äù

  3. Are nonprofits barred from earning a profit?  
- No. Nonprofits can earn a profit. They just can‚Äôt distribute that profit to owners/directors/management  
- **This is an organizational structure that doesn‚Äôt let you distribute profits**

3. Hansmann explained this nondistribution constraint‚Äìand, more generally, the justification for using nonprofits‚Äìas follows: ‚Äú\[O\]ccasionally, due either to the circumstances under which the product is purchased and consumed or to the nature of the product itself, consumers may be incapable of accurately evaluating the goods promised or delivered. . . . In situations of this type, consumers might be considerably better off if they deal with nonprofit producers rather than with for-profit producers.  
   1. What kind of problem is Hansmann invoking to explain why nonprofits are used? How does his point connect to the main themes of this course?  
- Information asymmetry problem  
- **Consumers cannot properly evaluate what they are paying for and so the non-profit, non-distribution constraint is there to assure them**

  2. Why might nonprofits be another tool in the toolkit for dealing with this sort of issue? Why is the nondistribution constraint a potential solution?  
- **Nonprofits solve information asymmetry: The constraint works because it shows that managers are not incentivized to cheat you like another company because they can‚Äôt keep the money anyway**  
    
4. First Type of Information Problem: Expertise  
   1. Ben has serious back pain. He has no medical training.   
      1. How effective is he personally in analyzing the diagnosis he receives from a surgeon that he needs surgery?  
- Doesn‚Äôt have a lot of expertise. Not in a position to evaluate this  
  2. Does this help explain why the oldest and most prestigious hospitals in the U.S. tend to be nonprofits?  
- Because hospitals are non-profits, the company has no incentive to misdiagnose patients for more expensive procedures  
  * **You aren‚Äôt going to be cheated by wrong information because the non-profits do not have incentives to take from you**  
  * Note: This doesn‚Äôt necessarily track with for-profit hospitals

  2. Chris‚Äôs car won‚Äôt start. He has no training as a mechanic.   
     1. How effective is he personally in analyzing the need for the repair recommended by a mechanic?  
- Not effective.

  2. In the U.S., are auto repair shops usually nonprofits or for-profit firms?  
- For profit firms  
- **There is information asymmetry in both settings but only one is a non-profit organization**  
- What difference is there between asymmetry in auto repair and hospital that can save this theory?  
  * If auto repair shop fixed the car then you can understand that it‚Äôs fixed (even if you don‚Äôt know why it‚Äôs fixed or they overcharged you)  
  * If hospital fixed your back there might be other things going on with your body so the fix is not as observable  
  * **Although there is information asymmetry in both settings, you can observe that a fix happened in auto repair (not as much in doctor)**

  3. Debbie needs a lawyer to sell her home. She has no legal training.  
     1. How effective is she personally in analyzing the need for the contractual terms recommended by her lawyer?  
- Not effective.  
  2. Do most lawyers in the U.S. work for nonprofits or for for-profit law firms?  
- For profit  
- Another example of information asymmetry not used to resolve through non-profits

- **Nonetheless, nonprofits can be reassuring in setting in which it is hard to evaluate quality**

5. Second Type of Information Problem: Personal Contact  
   1. Elizabeth, who lives in NY, wants to buy flowers for her mother, Francis, who lives in Chicago and is celebrating a special birthday.   
      1. Is Elizabeth likely to buy flowers from a nonprofit or a for-profit firm? Why?  
- For-profit firm because even though she can‚Äôt observe the quality of the goods/services herself, she is in touch with someone who can tell her about the quality  
  2. Gail, who lives in NY, wants to contribute to provide emergency relief for an earthquake in Asia.   
     1. Is she likely to contribute to a nonprofit or a for-profit firm?  Why?  
- Nonprofit firm because she is not in touch with anyone that can tell her about the quality of the goods/services so the nonprofit, which legally cannot take from her for their gain, assures her that the money is going to good use  
  * She knows there is a need for repairs but cannot evaluate the quality of the repairs

- **The concern that she might be misled is high because she cannot personally or indirectly monitor (without personal contact) but the nonprofit status assures her**  
  * **Asymmetric information is much greater in the case of disaster relief than in the case of giving the flowers (even if you are not the person monitoring either, in one you have a personal contact to monitor for you)**  
    2. How (if at all) is the information problem Gail faces different from the one Ben, Chris, and Debbie faced in question \# 4?  
- **All the situations involve information asymmetry but Gail doesn‚Äôt have a reliable source of information about quality of goods/services delivered but the others do because they are the ones getting the goods/services**  
  3. Could advances in technology change her assessment of this issue?  
- Yes. Advances in technology can help Gail monitor how her money is used better which reduces the information asymmetry  
  * But for now, gift to someone you know is different from the donation to someone you don‚Äôt know

6. Third Type of Information Problem: Impact  
   1. Ilene loves to run, stroll, and ride her bike in Riverside Park. She appreciates that everyone who wants to enjoy the park is allowed to do so free of charge. She knows that the park needs to be maintained, and that New York City only covers a portion of this cost, so she decides to make a donation.   
      1. Given how much time she spends in the park, does Ilene still face the same sort of challenge as Gail in question \# 5?  
- No. She can see the impact  
  * No personal contact issue because she can observe, monitor it  
    2. In deciding whether to donate, does she face a different information problem? Can the use of a nonprofit help to address the problem in ‚Äúii‚Äù?  
- Yes. **Even if you can monitor a non-profit‚Äôs work and see if the nonprofit is doing its mission, there is still a question of the marginal impact of your donation (what specifically is your money effecting)**  
  * **The non-distribution constraint mitigates but doesn‚Äôt eliminate the information asymmetry problem**  
    * CEO can‚Äôt take the money in a nonprofit but can do other things for personal benefit

7. Review: This course has emphasized two different types of problems that can undermine an otherwise beneficial transaction. One is about information. What is the other type of problem? (Hint: It also starts with an ‚Äúi.‚Äù)  
- Incentive problem

8. Externalities, Incentives, and Public Goods  
   1. Helen and Ilene are neighbors. Ilene likes to use an old wood-burning stove to heat her home. Jane sells her the wood. Unfortunately, the smoke tends to blow from her chimney into Helen‚Äôs open window, making Helen‚Äôs home unpleasantly smokey.  
      1. When Ilene buys wood from Jane, do the two of them consider the impact of their transaction on Helen?  
- No, they do not consider the impact

  2. What is an externality? Is the externality in this example positive or negative?  
- **Externality \= A type of incentive problem where two people‚Äôs transaction has an impact on a third party but the parties in the transaction do not consider the impact on the third party**  
- This is a negative externality (negative effect)

  3. True or false: **Externalities create flawed incentives because the parties to a transaction don‚Äôt have optimal incentives to avoid the relevant costs (in the case of negative externalities) or to create the relevant benefits (in the case of positive externalities).**  
- True.

  2. Helen and Ilene live on a private road, which is not maintained by their town. In the summer, there are a lot of mosquitos. A portion of the land along the road is owned by the town, but the town does not spray it with pesticides to reduce the mosquito population. Instead, the town has given permission to Helen and Ilene to spray it.  
     1. If Helen sprays the parcel owned by the town, will Ilene also benefit?  
- Yes.  
  2. If Ilene sprays the parcel, will Helen also benefit?  
- Yes.  
- **The decision that each might make to spray this has positive effect for the other (positive externality)**  
  3. What effect do your answers to part ‚Äúa‚Äù and ‚Äúb‚Äù have on Helen and Ilene‚Äôs incentive to spray?  
- Neither might spray  
  4. What does the term ‚Äúfree riding‚Äù mean?  
- When someone else benefits from another‚Äôs work/money  
- Each has the incentive to rely on the other to do it  
  * This means that there is less of the thing than there should be  
    5. Economists define a ‚Äúpublic good‚Äù as a commodity or service that costs the same‚Äìregardless of how many people use it‚Äìand is available even to those who don‚Äôt pay for it. Is a mosquito-free neighborhood a public good?  
- Yes.

9. A distinguishing feature of venture capital is that the sector‚Äôs products‚Äîhigh-tech startups‚Äîhave significant social value.  
   1. Is the same true of the nonprofit sector?  
- Yes.  
  2. Do nonprofits generate externalities?  
- Yes.  
  3. Do tech-startups also generate externalities? To the same extent as nonprofits?  
- Not to the extent of nonprofits  
- Tech startups operate for-profits but there could be some third-party benefits  
  4. True or false: **An important function of nonprofits, and of government subsidies to nonprofits, is to address negative externalities‚Äîand, thus, to deal with incentive problems**.  
- True.  
- **When you have situations where the market is failing (where someone needs to deal with something but everyone is waiting for another to address it), there are things that nonprofits step in to do (and get subsidized to do)**  
  * **Nonprofits aren‚Äôt only about information problems but also incentive problems**

10. Nonprofits as a Response to Public Goods and Other Incentive Problems  
    1. Mary is worried about climate change. She founds a research organization to study what causes climate change, how to slow or reverse it, and how to mitigate its effects.

    2. Is the knowledge that Mary‚Äôs organization develops a public good? Does it have positive externalities?  
- Yes, develops a public good  
- Has positive externalities

  3. Would you Mary‚Äôs organization to be a nonprofit or a for-profit firm?  
- Nonprofit because even though it is doing good for the world, it is doing good for everyone regardless of if they pay for the value created  
  * Also hard to charge people for the value creation

  4. Mary decides to work for the organization, and she obviously is passionate about its mission. How do expect her feeling about her work will compare with her friend Nick, who does data entry for a widget manufacturer?  
- She feels better about her work  
- **Incentive problem of nonprofits: The market doesn‚Äôt account for the social good that is being done and therefore it is undersupplied**  
- **Incentive advantage for nonprofits: The people who work at nonprofits are really passionate about what they do and will work hard**

  5. Based on your answer to ‚Äúd,‚Äù what comparative advantage to nonprofits sometimes have over for-profit firms?  
- Workforce advantage: Nonprofits employ people who want to help others and are committed to the cause

11. Tax Subsidy  
    1. Mary‚Äôs organization runs a surplus. Does it pay tax on this surplus? What if the widget manufacturer where Nick works runs a surplus?  
- Nonprofits do not pay tax on surplus/profit  
- For-profits do pay tax on surplus/profit  
- **The government is putting in money into the nonprofit because it is helping the public good and solving the collective action problems**

  2. Mary‚Äôs friend, Olivia, donates to $10,000 to Mary‚Äôs organization. What tax benefit can she claim?  
- Olivia can deduct that amount of the donation from her taxable income

  3. Assume that Olivia‚Äôs tax rate usually is 50% (a 40% federal rate and a 10% state and local tax rate). What is the real financial cost to Olivia in making this gift?  
- It doesn‚Äôt cost her $10K. Costs her $5K  
- She would have paid $5K in tax that she gets to avoid

12. Governments versus Nonprofits  
    1. In principle, either the government or nonprofits can provide public goods and respond to market failures. For example, social security is a program that ensures that elderly people have enough resources, so they don‚Äôt become dependent on the community for food, medicine, and other basic necessities.  
       1. Do you think the government or nonprofits have a comparative advantage in running a program like this?  
- Government has the advantage because they have robust systems for gathering revenue and sending checks to people which get cheaper to conduct per person as you add more people  
  * **Where massive amounts of money are involved, government is more likely to be the provider because of its taxing power and economies of scale advantage**  
    2. What does the term ‚Äúeconomies of scale‚Äù mean? Are there economies of scale in a program like this?
