- Economy of scale \= Cheaper program per person as you add more people  
  * Massive economies of scale with social security

  2. One way to prevent drug addicts from contracting HIV is to provide them with clean needles. Was this program first administered by the government or by nonprofits? Why?   
- Nonprofits  
- Government is politically unable to administer this  
  * **Nonprofits are able to experiment because they are independent from the political process**

  3. Where did the civil rights movement begin‚Äìin the government or in nonprofits? What about the movements for women‚Äôs rights and gay rights? What about the environmental movement? Why?  
- **Nonprofits because they have the independence to champion an idea which is not demanding a majority of support with the voting public (and hopefully will demand that support at one point)**

- **Nonprofits exist in ways that look like government functions particularly on the public good side, but they conduct themselves differently from the government on purpose**

  4. Rachel buys a birthday present for her brother Peter. Assume he loves the gift.  
     1. True or false: The gift makes Peter happier.  
- True.  
  2. True or false: The gift makes Rachel happier.  
- True.  
  3. In economics, there is a concept called ‚Äúthe double utility of giving.‚Äù Is illustrated by Rachel‚Äôs gift to Peter. What does this phrase mean?  
- **Double Utility of Giving: The person who gets the good or service is happy but so is the person paying for it**  
  * This is unique to voluntary giving  
    * This separate source of happiness exists for nonprofits but not for government  
    * **This is advantage for nonprofits because it creates more happiness**  
    4. When Rachel pays taxes, do you think she experiences the double utility of giving?  
- No.  
  5. What about when Rachel gives money to her favorite charity, which is a soup kitchen? Does her donation produce ‚Äúdouble utility‚Äù?  
- Yes.

# 10: Nonprofits (Measuring and Rewarding Progress)

- Nonprofits can be viewed as a solution to the information/incentive problems  
- There are also incentive problems like free riding/externalities that can justify the existence of nonprofits (especially government funding for nonprofits)  
- But nonprofits themselves create information and incentive problems  
- Nondistribution constraint prevents money from being distributed to nonprofit management but it does not prevent other forms of value from being taken (excessive compensation not tied to surplus, office space, overstaffing, travel)  
- Harder to police nonprofits because measuring progress is more challenging without profit metrics

- VC and Nonprofits: Same problems, different solutions  
  * Asymmetric information/Uncertainty:  
    * Goals are worthwhile but hard to accomplish  
    * People closer to the issues know whether these goals are achievable or not, but they have incentive to persuade you to invest  
  * Solution for VC‚Äôs: Incentive compensation. This is not available to nonprofits

1. Review of Challenges in Venture Capital  
   1. What information problems are there in venture capital?  
- Information asymmetry: Business owner knows more about business than investors  
- Uncertainty: No one knows if it will work because no one has ever tried it  
  * Note that uncertainty is a separate information problem than asymmetry

  2. What incentive problems are there in venture capital?  
- There is incentive for Founder to misrepresent her confidence in her idea  
  * Staged financing

  3. Do you think the information problems make the incentive problems harder?  
- Yes. **In situations where information problems are harder, incentive problems become increasingly harder because people have more discretion to do what is in their personal interest rather than what is in the best interest of company**

2. Special Problems at Nonproblems: To see them, let‚Äôs compare two scenarios‚Äîone involving a for-profit firm, and one involving a nonprofit  
   1. Alice is a board member at Forprofit Corp (‚ÄúFC‚Äù), and Ben is FC‚Äôs CEO. FC has two businesses: a restaurant and a catering business. At a board meeting, Ben tells Alice and the rest of the board that he wants to stop the restaurant, and keep only the catering business.    
      1. To vet this decision, what is the first question Alice should ask?  
- How profitable is the restaurant? How profitable is the catering part?

  2. Assume that running a restaurant requires more work from management than catering. Why might Ben try to hide this fact from Alice?  
- 2 Possible Explanations for what he wants to do. Motivations are hard to read because people will tell you what you want to hear  
  * 1\) He doesn‚Äôt want to work as hard ‚Äì Bad  
  * 2\) He wants to reallocate costs ‚Äì Good  
    * Is the self-interested course actually the best course for the company?

    3. If this is the reason why Ben wants to shut the restaurant, how feasible is it for Alice to figure this out?  
- Pretty easy. If the restaurant is more profitable, keep that open  
  * Alice can quickly check his claims by asking him about the bottom line


  2. Alice is also a board member at Nonprofit (‚ÄúNP‚Äù), and Candace is NP‚Äôs CEO. NP has two programs: they run a soup kitchen and they run an afterschool basketball program for disadvantaged young people. Candace tells Alice and the rest of the board that she wants to end the soup kitchen and focus only on basketball.

  3. Compared with Ben‚Äôs proposal in the previous question, is Candace‚Äôs plan easier or harder for Alice to vet?  
- Harder for Alice to vet because there are not financial metrics to evaluate the decision  
- **This is not as obvious because it is a lot harder to monitor without financial metrics**  
  * **Nonprofits provide opaque goods**  
  * **Hard to measure social value**

  4. Assume that the soup kitchen requires more work from management. Why might Candace try to hide this fact from Alice?  
- Candace will hide this fact because if Alice finds out she may assume that the reason why Candace wants to close the soup kitchen is because of the bad reason that she doesn‚Äôt want to do more work (which may or may not be true)

  5. If this is the reason why Candace wants to shut the soup kitchen, how feasible is it for Alice to figure this out?  
- Similarly, here is an information asymmetry situation that can be explained by two different kinds of motives (one good and one bad)  
  * But this time we do not have a profitability metric to make this determination

  6. The last two questions illustrate the fundamental challenge at nonprofits that is emphasized in *How to Save the World*.  
     1. What is the problem?  
- **Difficult to measure success without profits**  
  2. Is this a problem of information? Or of incentives? Or both?  
- This is a both problem  
  * It starts as an information problem: Is this more or less impactful than the other thing we are doing or what we could do?  
  * **But it becomes an incentive problem because when information is not available, incentive problems become harder to monitor**  
    * **When information is not available, incentive problems get worse**  
    3. What parallel do you see to venture capital?  
- **Unusually challenging information problems both in VC and nonprofits**  
- **Where there are information problems, incentive alignment becomes more challenging and monitoring is less likely to work and agency costs become more serious**  
  * These are parallel situations

3. Inertia  
   1. Doug and Ellen work at Institution, which provides goods and services to the public. To do this work, Institution has standard operating procedures, which it has used for many years. What it be practical for Doug and Ellen to rethink those standard operating procedures every morning when they arrive at work?  
- No. This would be impractical. You need standard operating procedures to function

  2. Does every organization needs standard operating procedures to do its work?  
- Yes.

  3. Institution‚Äôs standard operating procedures used to be really effective. But the market they serve has changed, so Institution should change some of these procedures to keep up with the times. Are there reasons why Doug and Ellen might resist changing these procedures?  
- **Why do institutions rarely want to change standard operating procedures:**  
  * **Good reason: It has worked. And people remember the success of the procedures**  
    * Response: The success is in the past  
  * **Bad reasons:**  
    * **It is hard to change and learn and transition to a new method (increased workload)**  
    * **Risk that they will be replaced if they do not have the proper skillset for the new procedures**  
  * Blend of reasons that people inside institution resist change

  4. Do you think inertia is a challenge at every institution? Why might it be a greater challenge at a nonprofit than at a for-profit firm?  
- Challenge for both but greater challenge in nonprofits because it is harder to measure there is no headline profit number  
  * **Inertia in for-profits is communicated to leadership, employees and investors through profit margins**  
    * Losing money ü°™ Bad sign. Can‚Äôt be explained away over time

  5. Is inertia caused by information problems? Or incentive problems? Or both?  
- Both:  
  * **Information Problem: Managers do not understand that the procedures are not working (because they do not have profit metrics)**  
  * **Incentive Problem: Managers not compelled to change even when they know it is not working**  
- **Inertia exists in every institution but it is more worrisome in nonprofits because success is hard to measure**  
    
4. Challenges in Resolving Conflicts  
   1. Frank and Georgia work at Investment Bank, while Hannah and Ike work at Nonprofit. Assume that all four of them are ambitious and capable, and that they enjoy their work. Assume also that Frank and Georgia disagree about the best strategy for Investment Bank to maximize profits, and that Hannah and Ike disagree about the best way to advance Nonprofit‚Äôs mission. Why might the disagreement at Nonprofit be more heated? What connection, if any, is there to the fundamental challenge at nonprofits discussed in question \#2.  
- Problem of information ‚Äì Difficult to measure performance  
- Members of nonprofit might have a different understanding of the goals they are trying to pursue

- **Disagreement at a nonprofit is more heated because:**  
  * **1\) Information:** The easiest way to resolve something is to produce objective information that both sides need to accept which is harder to have in a nonprofit because the goals are not as crisply measured  
  * **2\) Emotions:** People are at nonprofits are there because they care about the mission. They fight even more intensely in a nonprofit because they are more passionate about principle than they would be about money  
  * Compromises are harder at nonprofits

- Hansmen says that you are supposed to trust nonprofits because of non-distribution constraint, but he is only right to some degree

5. Effectiveness of the Nondistribution Constraint  
   1. Lucy, who lives in NY, wants to give money to help poor children in her hometown of Chicago. She finds out about a nonprofit called ‚ÄúChicago Kids‚Äù or ‚ÄúCK,‚Äù studies their website, and confirms that their mission is to help low-income children in Chicago. She also confirms that ‚ÄúCK‚Äù is a nonprofit, and that the CEO‚Äôs name is Marvin, and the Board Chair‚Äôs name is Natalie. If CK runs a surplus, can Marvin and Natalie take it as a dividend?  
- No, nonprofits are barred from distributing profits  
- Hansmen: Nondistribution constraint builds the trust for her to donate even if she cannot monitor the services/goods herself

  2. Which of the following is true?  
     1. Natalie cannot be compensated as a board member of a nonprofit.  
- Not true ‚Äì Possible. Some nonprofits do pay **board members**

  2. CK‚Äôs rent per square foot must be below the median rent in Chicago‚Äôs real estate market.  
- Not true ‚Äì you can **get nice office space**

  3. Natalie cannot be Marvin‚Äôs childhood friend or his relative, since this sort of personal tie between a board chair and CEO would represent an impermissible conflict.  
- Not true ‚Äì Marvin can **hire his relative**  
  4. Marvin cannot take 8 weeks of vacation every year.  
- Not true ‚Äì Marvin can take **extended** **vacation**  
  5. Marvin cannot receive an 8% raise three years in a row.  
- Not true ‚Äì Marvin can get a **raise every year**  
  6. Marvin cannot receive a bonus equal to 5% of the surplus that CK runs each year.  
- True ‚Äì Marvin cannot take any of the surplus in the form of compensation because it looks like a dividend  
  * However, Marvin could set certain KPI and if these are achieved give bonuses as long as it is not tied to the surplus  
    * This works especially well with fundraisers ‚Äì if they achieve X amount of fundraising then they will get X bonus  
    7. Marvin cannot hire his sister, Olivia, as his chief of staff.  
- Not true ‚Äì Marvin can hire his relative  
  8. Marvin cannot dedicate 5% of CK‚Äôs budget to pay for him to attend conferences in Europe.  
- Not true ‚Äì Marvin can use budget to **pay for conferences**  
  9. If Lucy calls the office at 9am or at 4pm on any given weekday, Marvin will usually be there.  
- Not true ‚Äì Marvin **doesn‚Äôt need to work hard**

- **The nondistribution constraint only goes so far. You can‚Äôt take money blatantly, but you can spend it in ways that give yourself a personal benefit instead of progressing the mission**  
  * **Nondistribution can be exploited with agency costs**

  3. How reliable is the nondistribution constraint in keeping nonprofit managers and boards from skimping on quality or overcharging beneficiaries?  
- Only somewhat reliable but not completely  
    
6. Incentive Compensation ‚Äì **Solution for VC but not for nonprofits**  
   1. What role does incentive compensation play in venture capital? Why?  
- Incentive compensation creates motivation for VC and Founder (low cash salary and big equity grant) to do well given the uncertainty and asymmetric information ‚Äì need to make them monitor themselves by giving equity  
- Allows Founder to self-monitor to overcome information/incentive problems

  2. As a legal matter, can similar compensation be used at nonprofits?  
- No. There is a comparable degree of information problems without the paramount solution

  3. Can a nonprofit designate specific goals and reward employees for achieving them? Can those goals relate to the size of the surplus? Can they relate to progress in achieving the mission?  
- You cannot give a percentage of the surplus  
- But if the targets are mission related, you can give bouses to that (bring in X clients, raise X money)

  4. Why is compensation described in ‚Äúc‚Äù potentially hard to implement?  
- Hard to measure the mission because you can‚Äôt use profitability  
  * Compensation tied to progress on the mission means that you need to measure progress on the mission, but this is hard to do  
  * And also can‚Äôt tie to surplus

  5. Peter is the new CEO of a for-profit hospital. Before the board hired him, the hospital‚Äôs profits were an unimpressive 4% of capital invested. The board wants the hospital to earn at least 8%.   
     1. How can the board motivate Peter to double the profits?  
- Compensation \= X  
  * 8% profit increase \= X \+ Y  
  * 10% profit increase \= X \+ Y \+ E

    2. What incentive does the board itself have to press Peter for better corporate performance?  
- Board is pressuring CEO and Board has incentive compensation of its own  
  * This is a key feature to overcome agency costs

    3. Do you think supervisors usually like to fire people? Or to eliminate perquisites that their colleagues like? Why do they do it?  
- They do not like to do but in a for-profit situation they have to do it to cut costs so that their compensation is higher  
  * **Agency Cost: Compassion of not firing is a personal benefit to you instead of doing what is best for the business**

  6. Quentin is the new CEO of a nonprofit hospital. Before the board hired him, the hospital was consistently losing money. The board wants the hospital to break even.   
     1. Can the board motivate Quentin in the same way that the for-profit hospital board is motivating Peter (in the last problem)?  
- No. Nondistribution constraint so no bonuses

  2. In a nonprofit, are there other reasons why professionals might be motivated to perform well, which are less likely in a for-profit firm?  
- Sometimes the way to eliminate temptation of managers to do something in their self-interest is by reminding the people why they are there ‚Äì they care about the mission  
  * Note that this is not the nondistribution constraint at work but maybe it lays the groundwork to let this happen

- **Ideally, if nonprofits hire the right people, they will monitor themselves (just like incentive compensation in VC)**

  3. How do the incentives of nonprofit boards compare with those of a for-profit board?  
- Nonprofit boards are rarely paid. They do it because they also believe in a mission. This can make them take the responsibility seriously

  4. Do you think Quentin is more or less likely than Peter to fire people?  
- Less likely because without high-powered financial incentives, it is harder to motivate people  
- Argument some people make: To do good, we need to start by taking care of the people that work for us  
  * Counter: Beneficiaries come first

7. Agency Costs   
   1. Richard is the new CEO of a chain of for-profit restaurants in major cities across the U.S., and you are a member of his company‚Äôs board. Richard‚Äôs predecessor, Sally, was on the road half the time, visiting the restaurants and meeting with their managers. Sally‚Äôs motto as a manager was, ‚Äúthere‚Äôs no substitute for being there.‚Äù She believed that her periodic visits motivated the manager of each restaurant to work harder, and that her visits allowed her to share good ideas from one restaurant with others. Sally also loved traveling, and was proud of having elite ‚Äúplatinum‚Äù status at a number of airlines and hotels. On each trip, when the work day ended, she made plans with one of her many friends in each of the relevant cities. Richard is going to try a different approach. He is scheduling an individual video conference with each restaurant manager every two weeks, and a group video chat for everyone every two weeks as well. He plans to travel once a month to visit a restaurant ‚Äúlive,‚Äù and will visit each of the 36 locations every three years (instead of every year, as Sally did). As a board member, you are trying to evaluate whether Richard is right to make this change. What information can help you to make that judgment over time?  
- 2 Explanations for Sally:  
  * Business Reason: More effective monitoring by being there, meeting things  
  * Agency Cost Reason: Sally is getting a personal benefit ‚Äì travel, platinum status  
- 2 Explanations for Richard:  
  * Business Reason: More frequently communicating with managers, saving the company money (even if video isn‚Äôt fully as good as in-person)  
  * Agency Cost Reason: Maybe he doesn‚Äôt want to travel that much

- Experiment of how Richard is doing it differently. Does this have an effect?  
  * **Look at profitability of restaurants and judge which strategy is right**


  2. Tina is the new CEO of a network of welfare centers and soup kitchens across the U.S., and you are a member of his nonprofit‚Äôs board. Tina‚Äôs predecessor, Ursula, was on the road half the time, visiting the welfare centers and meeting with their managers. Like Sally, Ursula‚Äôs motto as a manager was, ‚Äúthere‚Äôs no substitute for being there.‚Äù She believed that her periodic visits motivated the manager of each welfare center to work harder, and that her visits allowed her to share good ideas from one center with others. Like Sally, Urusla also loved traveling, accumulating frequent flier miles, and seeing friends in every city. Like Richard, Tina is going to try a different approach, relying more on video conferences and less on ‚Äúlive‚Äù visits. As a board member, you are trying to evaluate whether Tina is right to make this change. What information can help you to make that judgment over time? Is your job evaluating Tina‚Äôs judgment harder or easier or no different than the job of evaluating whether Richard is right in the last problem?  
- Same as above

- **Without revenue (something simple to measure), it will be harder to have the information and therefore harder to deal with incentive problems**  
  * People are smart enough to tell you the story that this is good for the job  
  * Information problems increase; Incentive problems increase


8. How to Save the World suggests ways to deal with incentive and information problems at nonprofits. Specifically, the book says that the best nonprofits are effective at two tasks.   
   1. What are these tasks?  
- **1\) Analysis: Successful businesses and nonprofits need to be analytical and rigorous in figuring out the best way to get the job done**  
  * **But having the knowledge is not enough**  
- **2\) Advocacy: Need to get the relevant people to believe in the plan and give it the effort it requires to do it the right way**

  2. The book also suggests synergies in these tasks‚Äìthat is, working on each one can help a nonprofit make progress on the other. What are those synergies?  
- If you completed the analysis and picked a good method, the smart people you hired will ask you why are we doing it this way  
  * This analysis, if it‚Äôs good will persuade them too  
  * **The analysis is critical with advocacy to convince others of the plan. Need to demonstrate you thought it through**

- **Not enough to just have a good idea to get people on board. You need to convince them by making the analysis a collective endeavor**  
  * In addition to get them on board with the analysis, you need to let them in to the planning process to get their buy in  
  * This is necessary extra time to spend. You need the people doing it with you to believe in it. **You need to make people think that this is their idea too. This makes them feel like they have ownership over the idea to make it work**  
  * ‚ÄúIf I‚Äôm doing my job correctly, then no one would ever think I had an idea of my own‚Äù

- **1\) Analysis prepares us to persuade others and hopefully persuades others too**  
  * **The collective endeavor can have others take ownership of the idea**  
- **2\) Advocacy because as people see problems in the analysis, they have buy in and are willing to help solve them**  
  * **The collective endeavor can make the idea itself better**

# 11: Nonprofits (Management as Response to Information and Incentive Problems)

- Management strategies as a solution to information and incentive problems  
- Planning process are helpful to:  
  * 1\) Focus on what the next things should be that you do; get feedback on this  
  * 2\) Opportunity to get people to agree with the strategy you are trying to develop because if they don‚Äôt agree they have incentive problem to not comply  
- Granular choices you can make during planning process and order of planning process can help get more buy in  
  * In theory, someone might want to just get everyone in a room to talk it through, but this doesn‚Äôt work  
  * Instead, must lay the groundwork for different people understand your arguments  
    * Start with the group that needs something from everyone else to master the details of the benefits you will get from taking budget/employees away from other groups  
    * But even before that, have a pre-conversation with the other groups who you will be taking from to give a general warning without giving details that there might be issues that might effect you  
      * Avoid the ‚Äúwhy didn‚Äôt you tell me earlier‚Äù  
- You cannot be a part of every decision so you need to impact others‚Äô decision making power by giving them criteria  
  * Help encourage them to think about an issue the way you would if you were consulted. Give them an approach that will help them get to the decision you would have come to  
- 3 Questions for Decision making criteria to allocate resources:  
  * 1\) How important is the problem?  
    * Can‚Äôt prove that it is the most important because that introduces comparisons that are conceptually hard to resolve but push people to show that it is important  
  * 2\) How effective is the response?  
    * Just because something is important doesn‚Äôt mean you should invest in it if you can‚Äôt move the needle with your resources  
      * **Want to know outcome (how effective is it) but can use output (what do we do) to gauge outcome because outcomes are harder to measure**  
        * Output \= How many people enrolled in job training program  
        * Outcome \= How many people went on to get better jobs  
  * 3\) Are we the right organization to do the job?

1. Review: To solve information and incentive problems‚Äîand, therefore, to be more effective in advancing their mission--what are the two goals nonprofits need to pursue?  
- 1\) Analysis: Figure out what you want to do  
- 2\) Advocacy: Coordinate people around you to accomplish goal

2. Planning  
   1. What is a planning process? Do you think it can help with either goal? With both goals?  
- Planning process can help with both goals:  
  * 1\) Figuring out what to do is the planning process  
    * Every organization has a planning process  
  * 2\) Planning can give you buy in through a collaborative process  
    * Planning processes regardless of if you know what is going to happen in the end (which path you will pick) are important to help others feel personally invested  
      * If people think that they helped come up with the idea, they will work harder  
    * Allocates responsibility around  
    * **Gets people‚Äôs input which makes them more invested, effectively targets motivating people. Collaborative process in key**  
    * Collaborative process negatives: Can lead to longer process, bad outcomes if others have bad ideas

  2. Why did JDC need a new planning process? What was the first step in launching it? Who was involved and why?  
- Schizer tried to get JDC to be more analytical to overcome its inertia  
- Just because you think you know doesn‚Äôt get you very far  
  * **To influence a big organization that you are leading (in a place where you won‚Äôt be in the room for every decision), influence the way people make decisions**  
- **Tried to get people to maximize social return (use scarce resources in best way) by implanting three decision making criteria:**  
  * **1\) How important is the problem we are addressing**  
  * **2\) How effective is the response**  
  * **3\) Are we the right organization to do it**  
    * Comparative advantage: Other organizations can address this better so we should use the resources to do something else

- **How did he get people to use these 3 criteria?**  
  * Not by announcing these criteria  
  * Instead, by having one on one conversations with every senior executive and encouraged them to talk about how they would make certain decisions. Once people were talking, he was able to listen but also pick out parts of what he heard that he liked (which is what he already thought) and make them think that his idea was their idea. Then sent document showing what he had heard but these were really his thoughts. Said that he needed their help, their buy in  
  * Came up with these three questions himself but each person felt personally invested in creating that framework. Then they were using the framework themselves and having colleagues use it when making decisions when he wasn‚Äôt in the room  
    * **Planning process gives you the opportunity to come up with the best thing but also to get buy in for it**

3. Adele is a new CEO of Community Center (CC), which hosts various cultural and athletic activities for members and also runs CC Academy, a private elementary school. Some of the largest donors (including board members) began their relationship with CC because they sent their children to CC Academy. Since CC Academy was launched 15 years ago, CC has subsidized its budget. But now CC Academy gets many applications, brings in significant tuition revenue, and also attracts a lot of philanthropic support. Bart, who is CCs chief planning officer, has told Adele that he thinks the subsidy should be discontinued, especially because CC is struggling a bit financially. In particular, the cultural activities department (‚ÄúCCD‚Äù), whose lecture series and concerts are core to CC‚Äôs brand, are struggling financially.  
   1. Assume that Adele is persuaded by Bart‚Äôs claim that the best thing for CC overall is to phase out the subsidy for CC Academy and redirect funds to CCD. Since Adele is the CEO, can‚Äôt she just make this decision on her own? In answering the question, please highlight the importance of incentives and information.  
- As a formal matter, the CEO can make this decision  
- But the smart way to do it is to get people on board for it to be successful

  2. Is this an issue that would appropriately be addressed in CC‚Äôs annual planning process?  
- Yes. The planning process is supposed to help with different priorities across initiatives  
  * This is a quintessential planning process issue

  3. Carlo is Nonprofit‚Äôs head of fundraising. He hears about Bart‚Äôs idea to shift resources away from CC Academy, so he comes to see Adele. ‚ÄúYou realize that most of our large donors now came to us through the school,‚Äù he says, ‚Äúand that includes three-quarters of the board.‚Äù What concern is Carlo expressing? How can Adele use the planning process to address this concern?  
- He is concerned that if they made this shift they would lose donors which would lead to less money to the overall budget  
- Additionally, warns about your job. Board hires and fires CEO so don‚Äôt anger them

  4. To reallocate money from CC Academy to CCD, Adele wants to get buy-in from a number of people, including the head of CC Academy, the head of CCD, the head of fundraising, key donors, and the board. Who do you think she should start with and why? Is there a benefit of having separate meetings, instead of having everyone together at one meeting?  
- Inefficient to gather everyone at the same time because there will be fighting between groups  
- Need to start by generating support gradually across different groups  
- Could be a good idea if the CEO was pursuing the idea to expose deep divisions to stop the proposal  
  * Way of stopping something without saying ‚Äúno‚Äù  
- Instead of bringing everyone together all at once, she could start with:  
  * Head of CC Academy: Need their buy in because they are most directly impacted  
  * Head of CCD: **Understand potential benefit of reallocating the money first. Meeting the person who will get the benefit will tell you what that benefit actually is.** This bolsters the case to Head of CC Academy because you can present to them what CC is going to lose if they do not cut CC Academy  
    * Morale issue with going to CCD: ‚ÄúWhy am I only hearing about this now?‚Äù  
      * **Often, people understand the merits about what you are doing so they default to qualms with the process if they are unhappy about it**  
    * Addressing the morale issue: Go to CC Academy and Board first for **pre-conversation** (saying we are having problems with CCD and this could have implications beyond CCD. I wanted to share immediately this with you, but I don‚Äôt have further information yet)  
      * This takes their argument of why didn‚Äôt you tell us first away  
- **Order of the conversations matters (even if it seems inefficient). Creates efficiency in the long-run**

  5. True or false: **An effective planning process helps a nonprofit allocate resources where they are needed the most**.  
- True.

  6. True or false: **An effective planning process helps build support internally among a nonprofit‚Äôs professionals, the board, and the nonprofit‚Äôs donors**.  
- True.

4. Erin, who is the head of CCD, comes to see Adele. ‚ÄúYou know how we have been trying for months to get Saylor Twift (a famous performer) to give a concert at CC,‚Äù Erin vents. ‚ÄúWell, it turns out that she came to sing to the kids at CC Academy.‚Äù When Adele is surprised, Erin explains that Saylor‚Äôs nephew is enrolled at CC Academy. Adele is frustrated that she, Erin, and the rest of the senior management did not know about this connection. ‚ÄúOnce Saylor was coming,‚Äù Erin vents, ‚Äúwe could have asked her to give a concert for CCD. I can‚Äôt believe we missed this opportunity\!‚Äù  
   1.  Pam is the principal of CC Academy. Can you think of two reasons‚Äîone innocent, and one less so--why Pam might not have mentioned Saylor‚Äôs link to CC Academy or the concert to Erin and Adele? (Hint: one is an information problem, and the other is an incentive problem.) When confronted, which reason is Pam likely to give? Why?  
- Information Problem: Didn‚Äôt occur to me that it would be of interest to CCD. It happened last minute  
- Incentive Problem: Pam wanted to keep the event exclusive to CC Academy to keep the money/reputation boost for her part of the organization to herself  
  * Pam is likely to give the innocent information problem explanation

  2. What is a ‚Äúsilo‚Äù and why is it a problem?  
- **Silo \= People from different parts of an organization do not coordinate with each other**  
- It is a problem based on information and incentive problems:  
  * Information Problem \= No information exchanged between departments  
  * Incentive Problem \= Why should we help another department when it doesn‚Äôt help us  
- Should we eliminate departments to eliminate silos? No. Departments are created through **specialization which creates efficiency**  
- **Silos follow directly from the willingness to be on the good side of your department (you will be rewarded for working for/sharing information with your department but not others)**  
  * **Incentive problem that compounds an information problem**  
  * You won‚Äôt be rewarded for sharing the information, working, answering questions for other departments ‚Äì only yours

  3. Adele consults a Mark, a management consultant, about silos. ‚ÄúHow do I get people in different departments to share information with each other and work together?‚Äù Mark mentions something they do at his firm: ‚ÄúWe require people to have a 15 minute conversation (assigned at random) every two weeks with someone who works in another part of the organization.‚Äù   
     1. What do you think is the rationale for this idea?  
- It gets the groups to work together

  2. What limitations (if any) do you see with this approach?  
- Fails to respond to people‚Äôs real incentives  
  * This will cost me time, cancel it, etc.

  4. The reading mentions ‚Äúpeer review.‚Äù What is it and why was it used in JDC‚Äôs planning process?  
- One department has to have proposals reviewed by person at the same level of another department  
- **If group A has plan vetted by group B head, group A‚Äôs plan will be improved**  
  * **Improves engagements between departments. People more invested in plan they were commenting on. And the person giving the comments can also learn about what the other group was doing and use that for their group**  
    * And then those people began forming task forces with colleagues from other groups  
      * This is equivalent to a 15 minute phone call but much more powerful because people chose to do it

- **Specialization creates information problems. Incentive problems compound them**  
  * **There are strategies you can use to overcome the incentive problems to get the information shared and get the proper expertise**


5. Recall that Bart is the head of CC‚Äôs fundraising. Erin and Pam each have innovative plans for the coming year.  
   1. Why is it important for Bart‚Äôs team to have current information about these plans, including the reason for launching these new initiatives, their goals, and the strategies for pursuing them?  
- **2 Reasons to give fundraisers the current information about new plans/goals:**  
  * 1\) Fundraisers can be more effective if they can speak in detail about what the organization wants to do and why the money is needed  
  * 2\) Fundraisers get measured by how much money they raise and if they don‚Äôt hear about new stuff organization is doing, they may be raising money for the old stuff that doesn‚Äôt help the organization

  2. Why might Bart‚Äôs team not have access to this information?  
- **Departments are in silos** so it is not common for department creating the plan to call the fundraisers right away to update them  
  * If the planner thinks that they will call the fundraising later, this is not going to work

  3. Can Adele use a planning process to help them get this information?  
- Yes. Good use of planning process  
  * Similar to a marketing team getting updated in a for-profit organization

6. Assume that Adele expects CC‚Äôs overall budget to increase by 3% next year.  
   1. Adele asks each of her direct reports to prepare a draft budget. She asks them to prepare three different scenarios. In one, they have a 1% increase over last year. In a second, their budget increases by 10%. In the third, their budget contracts by 5%. She asks them to attach a memo explaining the relevant changes in the second and third scenario. Why did they choose the increases and cuts that they are recommending? What information problem is this exercise seeking to address?  
- **Asymmetric Information Problem:** CEO has to make the decision but heads of departments have better information on the impact of the budget cuts  
- **Incentive Problem:** Heads of departments will not be incentivized to not share the proper information because they are worried that their budget will be cut

  2. A theme of this course is that sometimes an effort to solve an information problem creates a new incentive problem, and vice versa. What incentive problem can undercut the exercise in ‚Äúa‚Äù? Put another way, how might a self-interested member of Adele‚Äôs team try to manipulate this process? What (if anything) can Adele do to address this issue?  
- Head of Departments may be strategic and try to minimize their chance of the cut by each saying that the cuts will crush their department (in the 10% decrease scenario)  
  * They will choose to cut the things they know the CEO most values in the proposal so CEO would not rationally do that  
  * **Embellishing the cut scenario in a way and give a dishonest answer so that it seems so bad that no rational person would choose that route**  
    * **So the exercise is only worth doing if you can get an honest answer**  
  * **But CEO could change the outcome of the exercise (and decrease the manipulation) by:**  
    * **Saying that they will hold the dept. head to whatever they are going to cut**  
    * **Or CEO can call out the person‚Äôs plan using humor**  
      * Use false humor: **‚ÄúI know you might be tempted to tell me a false scenario‚Ä¶‚Äù**  
      * **Show them that you are on to them**: ‚ÄúIf this is the thing you were going to cut, wouldn‚Äôt all these other bad ramifications come from this?‚Äù  
      * Ask for a different plan

  3. Ned, who is the head of CC‚Äôs board, calls Adele and says: ‚ÄúI am hearing some grumbling about the multiple budget scenarios. Some of your colleagues feel that their time is being wasted. I am not saying that I agree with this criticism, but I wanted you to know that it‚Äôs out there.‚Äù How do you answer him?  
- The person calling you probably agrees with or is the source of this criticism  
- Give the sincere answer of this is what I am doing and why I am doing it

7. *How to Save the World* suggests allocation resources based on three questions. What are these three questions supposed to help a nonprofit measure?  
- 1\) How important is the problem we are solving?  
- 2\) How effective is the response?  
- 3\) Are we the right organization to do it?

8. The first question  
   1. What is the first of the ‚Äúthree questions‚Äù?  
- How important is the problem we are solving?

  2. When Lee Goldman started as dean of Columbia Medical School in 2005, how important was the challenge of responding to pandemics? When he concluded his service in June of 2020, how important was this challenge? What is the general lesson here?  
- Not very important in 2005 but very important in 2020  
- General lesson is that the question can change. **You want your organization to hardwire something into its framework to evaluate ‚Äúis the way we did things, what we should focus on now?‚Äù**  
  * **Planning process used the right way can do that**

  3. Is it more important to shelter the homeless or clean a polluted river? Why is this a hard question? If it‚Äôs not feasible to answer it, what suggestion does the reading make? What should nonprofits try to show instead?  
- **Since it is impossible for nonprofits to show what is more important, they should show what is important**  
- There is discipline involved in showing you are doing social good

  4. Helen is the CEO of Help\!, a humanitarian organization. She asks you to recommend whether to provide child care and medical care to children in Appalachia or in Westchester County (a suburb north of New York City)? What information do you need in order to answer the first question?  
- How many children are there?  
- Are there other organizations that can support each group?  
- What are the poverty rates?

- **You can‚Äôt evaluate whether feeding children is more or less important than cleaning a polluted river, but you can compare whether children in WV or children in Westchester need your services more**  
  * **The more focused the question is (comparing things that are more similar), the more applicable the data is to deciding where to allocate an organization‚Äôs resources (where you can do the most good)**

9. The second question   
   1. What is the second question?  
- How effective is the response

  2. What is the difference between an ‚Äúoutput‚Äù and an ‚Äúoutcome‚Äù?  
- **Output \= What you did/service provided (Number of children vaccinated)**  
  * **Easier to measure**  
- **Outcome \= Social return (How children‚Äôs health improved)**  
  * **Harder to measure**  
  * **Ideally, you want to know the outcome (the impact)**

  3. To help children, Helen‚Äôs team at Help\! is deciding whether to invest in providing Covid vaccines. If they decide to provide vaccines, they will track the number of children vaccinated. Is this an output or an outcome? Assuming the vaccine works, how reliable a measure of social return do you think the number of vaccinations is? Why?  
- Usually measuring outputs is not a sophisticated strategy, but in some particular situations it is pretty good  
  * Measuring amount of meals (output) delivered to 1000 starving people will tell you a lot about the outcome as well  
- **If you are very confident in what you are doing is working and you can show that it is working, then outputs are good enough**  
  * If you know that the vaccine improves childrens‚Äô health for a fact, and you distribute 1000 vaccines (output), that gives you a pretty good idea of what the social return will be (outcome)  
- **How effective the response is should be measured in outcomes. But sometimes measuring outputs alone if you know the product/service works is enough to get an idea of effectiveness**

  4. The reading uses the phrase ‚Äúimpact multiplier.‚Äù What does it mean?  
- Impact Multiplier \= Some steps that you take are especially effective because they lead to other good things

  5. If Help\! decides to vaccinate pediatricians instead, what ‚Äúimpact multiplier‚Äù would they seek to generate?  
- Doctors who can then treat other people  
  * Vaccine to a doctor is an impact multiplier because it does more good for the world than a vaccine to a lawyer

10. The third question   
    1. What is the third question?  
- Are we the right organization to do this? How do we compare with other organizations that might do it?  
  2. The mission of HerJustice is to provide legal services to underprivileged women who otherwise don‚Äôt have access to legal services. HerJustice does child support cases, but the organization does not represent clients in housing court who have been evicted. Why? Is the idea that deadbeat fathers are a more serious issue than eviction?  
- Trying to prevent mission creep (if you try to do too many things you won‚Äôt do any of them well  
- **There are already other organizations helping people in housing court so the incremental benefit that HerJustice can do in housing court is small (it just supplements the good work that others are doing). But because no one else is working on child support cases, or at least focusing on them, HerJustice can do particular good work in this area**  
  * **The question to ask when evaluating if you are the right organization to work on something is: ‚ÄúDo I have a comparative advantage?‚Äù**  
    * Is there an approach that I will take that is better than others? Do I have institutional characteristics that are unique?

  3. Legal Momentum also provides legal services to women. Two of their main initiatives are impact litigation and a helpline. Why?  
- They chose these initiatives because:  
  * **1\) Helpline can be valuable to people who have no idea where to go with their problems. Does general good in the world**  
  * **2\) Litigation sets important precedents that will change the law to benefit many people**  
  * **3\) To set important precedents, they need to find the right cases, which is another reason the helpline is important**  
    * **The Helpline and Impact Litigation complement each other well ‚Äì synergy between services**

  4. In focusing on the well-being of children, Help\!‚Äôs professionals are quite concerned about the rhetoric of City‚Äôs mayor, where Help\! runs a number of programs. He has been inflaming tensions and arguing (without evidence) that low-income families are the source of crime, when in fact they often are the victims. Ed, who is a board member of Help\!, has urged Helen (the CEO) to make a public statement condemning the mayor. Why might Helen hesitate to make this statement? How (if it all) is this relevant to the third question?   
- Help\! is the wrong organization to make a statement about the mayor says because another organization that does advocacy instead of social services should make statement condemning the mayor  
- If they start taking political stances, Help\! may risk losing the local government‚Äôs cooperation  
  * Need to speak through the right forum to criticize political leaders. **Some organizations are designed to do this. Other organizations provide social services. You don‚Äôt want to interfere with the help to the vulnerable populations by mixing the missions**

# 12: Nonprofits (Disclosure as Response to Information and Incentive Problems)

1. Easterbrook & Fishel (‚ÄúE\&F‚Äù) say that **fraud is inefficient**. What reasons do they give?  
- If investors do not know if something is true or not, they will invest time and resources to verify something. This is inefficient because it causes duplication of research (everyone is researching the same results)  
  * **The government can save everyone a lot of trouble by imposing a legal burden mandating that everyone speak accurately**  
  * A and B are considering an investment. A and B should work together to research the investment together to avoid duplication of effort  
  * **There is a lot of underinvestment because people just do not want to bother finding out more (they have finite time/resources to expend so they can‚Äôt spend it on everything and sometimes when they spend the time/resources they will get it wrong)**  
- Business owner has a comparative advantage (lowest cost provider) to get information over the investor  
- **A no-fraud rule helps to put the obligation to present information on the lowest cost provider of information, reducing duplicative effort and leading to better investments**

2. A central theme of this class is that information asymmetry can undermine mutually beneficial transactions. In principle, a solution to this problem is disclosure. But a key question is whether this disclosure be trusted. Let‚Äôs begin by assuming (incorrectly) that there is no ban on fraud, so we can explore whether disclosure would have any value.  
   1. Assume there are two firms: Competentco (CC) and Ineptco (IC). Does CC have the incentive to disclose information about itself? In doing so, does it have an incentive to do so in a way that is trustworthy?  
- The higher quality firms have incentive to share information and prove that they are higher quality, distinguishing themselves from the inept firms

  2. Does IC also have an incentive to disclose accurate information about itself?  
- Inept firms do not have an incentive to disclose accurate information about itself

  3. How do E\&F say that a high quality firm like CC can reassure potential investors that its information is accurate?  
- **If fraud is not illegal and disclosure is not required, investors have ways to tell that higher quality firms are better like checking dividends, third party certifications**

  4. Given your answers to ‚Äúa,‚Äù ‚Äúb‚Äù and ‚Äúc,‚Äù might investors who are thinking of investing in either CC or IC have ways to tell which is higher quality, even if no disclosure is required and even if fraud is not illegal?  
- **Even if the law isn‚Äôt helping companies by requiring only factually accurate statements, investors and quality firms can take steps to demonstrate that they are better**

  5. Easterbrook & Fishel concede that a firm‚Äôs have only a limited ability to convince investors that their information is accurate. Why? (Hint: can a firm disclose everything? Do investors want to verify everything?)  
- **Transactions costs impose a limit on disclosure**  
  * **Firms cannot disclose everything and investors do not want to verify everything. This requires too much time/resources (transactions costs)**  
  * There will always be distrust and discounting between firms and investors

  6. Review: What role can investment bankers and accountants plan in this process?  
- Third party expert verification can increase trust  
  * There are steps even in a world where fraud is legal that investors could do to vet the firms

  7. Review: What role does incentive compensation play? Can nonprofits use this strategy?  
- Incentive compensation can align incentives to get both parties ‚Äúskin in the game‚Äù and motivated towards the common goal of a better share price  
  * Nonprofits cannot use incentive compensation when it is tied to surplus 

3. E\&F say that a ban on fraud (without a requirement to disclose) can help reduce information asymmetry.  
- Intermediate Position: What if the government‚Äôs position was that firms were penalized for fraud but not required to disclose (not penalized for omissions)?

  1. In their view, without a ban on fraud, is information totally unreliable?  
- No, not totally unreliable  
- **Even when there is no ban on fraud and people have the legal ability to lie, there are mechanisms to look past some of that** 

  2. How does a ban on fraud help firms bridge information asymmetry?  
- **By penalizing deception, a fraud ban increases the credibility the quality firms have and decreases the advantage the inept firms have to mislead investors**  
  * **It is much better to not check everything yourself and ask questions where you can rely on the answers (check more things, spend less time)**

  3. E\&F say that a ban on fraud reduces the cost of signaling quality, but not to zero. Why doesn‚Äôt the rule eliminate these costs?  
- If you ask about the wrong thing then you might still get the wrong information  
  * No full disclosure requirement  
  * **Ban on fraud helps but doesn‚Äôt do everything**

  4. E\&F observe that a ban on fraud introduces costs of its own. What are these costs?  
- **Costs of ban on fraud:**  
  * **Enforcement and compliance costs to verify (legal fees)**  
  * **Chills market on putting out truthful but risky statements**

4. E\&F analyze what (if anything) a rule requiring disclosure adds to an anti-fraud rule.  
   1. Can a firm remain silent under both types of rules?  
- Yes, firm can still not volunteer some information 

  2. Is information a public good? If so, does this tell us anything about whether the right amount of information is produced?  
- **Information is a public good so sharing information can help a lot of people who didn‚Äôt pay for it**  
  * **This tells us that information will be underproduced because public goods cause free riding (others keep hoping that others will pay for it)**

  3. According to E\&F, why might too little information be produced? Why might too much be produced?  
- Too little information may be produced because the firms do not internalize all the benefits of producing  
- Too much information may be produced because the firms are legally required to disclose some irrelevant information

  4. Without a rule requiring disclosure, would a firm have the incentive to disclose good news?  
- Without a rule requiring disclosure, firm has incentive to disclose good news

  5. Without a rule requiring disclosure, why might a firm hesitate to disclose bad news?  
- Without a rule requiring disclosure, firm will hesitate to disclose bad news

  6. According to E\&F, if a firm always discloses good news, and decides to remain silent, how will investors interpret this silence?  
- If a firm always discloses good news and decides to remain silent, investors will interpret silence as negative (bad news)

  7. According to E\&F, given your answer to ‚Äúf,‚Äù does a firm have a reason to disclose bad news?  
- Firms have an incentive to disclose bad news so that investors do not read deeply into the silence and think that the news is actually worse than it is

5. Later in their article, E\&F say the following: ‚Äú**Imposition of a standard format and time of disclosure facilitates comparative use of what is disclosed and helps to create an efficient disclosure language. If every firm must disclose the same things, there will be reciprocal benefits to each firm's investors even though the firm will be compelled to disclose things of advantage to rivals**.‚Äù  
   1. What is a ‚Äúnetwork effect‚Äù? (This is not in the reading.)  
- **Network Effect \= Benefit of participation grows as the network expands**  
  * **The quality of a firm increases as more people are using (or more of a specific group of people are using it ‚Äì your friends/family)**  
  * **The use of the firm improves its quality**  
  * If your friends all use the same product, you might be inclined to use that product too even if it is the worst quality one

  2. Are network effects when firms use the same format and definitions when disclosing information? Do these network effects offer a potential justification for mandatory disclosure?  
- The more companies that use the same standard will make it easier for investors to compare across companies  
- Companies themselves have more standardized goals when planning strategies for business operations

- **In theory, every company could decide how to disclose information in its own manner (and probably has incentive to disclose even bad news). But non-mandatory disclosure creates 2 problems:**  
  * **1\) Learning/Compliance Costs: Investors must invest time to understand how this particular firm does its disclosure**  
  * **2\) Comparisons are lost: Part of what you want with the disclosure is to compare information across firms**  
    * Comparisons are easier when there is a standard, mandatory format  
    * There are network effects about mandatory disclosure that create a standard format (even if this is not the best method of disclosure). Best disclosure might not be preferable if everyone is not doing it this way (no benefit of comparison)  
- **Network effects offer a justification for mandatory disclosure because firms are forced to disclose the way other firms are disclosing and this will lead to a standard format which lowers learning curves and makes for easier comparisons**  
  * Even though over standardization can make people overlook industry specific characteristics and evolutions

- E\&F are free market advocates ‚Äì no mandatory disclosures. Their argument is that there are market dynamics that will encourage firms to behave as if disclosure is mandatory even when it is not  
  * Even if you have bad news, it is better to disclose so investors do not think it is worse than it is. And if it is the case that firms will share both good and bad information, maybe disclosure does not need to mandatory at all  
  * But they also recognize that mandatory disclosure has the benefit of people disclosing in a similar way which facilitates comparisons (network effect)  
  * But there is also a cost of mandatory disclosure if the mandatory way is not the best way (everyone stuck with inferior way of disclosing)

6. Adrian is the CEO of a nonprofit. Bob is her Chief Planning Officer, and Charlotte is the Chief Financial Officer. Bob and Charlotte would each like Adrian‚Äôs job. Adrian and Bob plan a new initiative, which does not go well. Diane is the donor who funded it. Adrian and Bob are deciding what to tell Diane about the unsuccessful experiment.  
   1. Could Adrian and Bob incur legal liability by saying, ‚ÄúThe experiment was bold and really illuminating, and we couldn‚Äôt have done it without you‚Äù?  
- No, this is spin. They haven‚Äôt said it succeeded/failed ‚Äì just that they couldn‚Äôt have done it without her

  2. What if instead Adrian and Bob say, ‚ÄúThe experiment showed us how to deliver service just as effectively for only ¬Ω the cost‚Äù when this is not accurate?  
- This is fraudulent. Inducing investor to provide money through misstatement

  3. Why might Adrian and Bob feel strongly that they need to tell the truth? Please offer reasons rooted in principle, as well as reasons rooted in self-interest.  
- Moral: Nonprofit might want to tell the truth because it is the right thing to do  
- Reputational: Nonprofit might want to tell the truth because if they are caught in a lie, they risk their future funding from this donor and others

  4. Why might Adrian and Bob be tempted to shade the truth, giving a more optimistic picture than is warranted?  
- They have a temptation to shade the truth to retain donors and keep the organization running 

  5. If Adrian and Bob do provide false or misleading information, please offer two reasons why Charlotte might tell Diane that she is being misled.  
- Moral: She wants to do the right thing  
- **Self-interested: Charlotte wants CEO job. She is focused on her own interest ‚Äì not that of the organization‚Äôs**  
  * **This is an agency cost (incentive problem)**

  6. Even when nonprofit professionals and boards are tempted to offer misleading information, do they still have reasons not to do so?  
- **Asymmetric information problem: The donor knows less than the management team**  
  * **Agency cost hopes to address information asymmetry**  
- **They can spin and leave donor with the impression that this went well legally (without fraud), but it is unlikely to work because there are others in management that will tell donor the full story who are sharing this information out of (integrity or) self-interest**  
- Incentives interact in organizations in interesting ways ‚Äì think about what others want to do to accurately predict results

  7. Assume that Adrian and Bob want the information they share to be more credible. They approach Ellen, a professor at the local university, asking her to provide an analysis, which they will disclose on their website. Why might this sort of expert opinion add value? Why might it not?  
- Expert opinion could add value because third party experts can increase the organization‚Äôs credibility  
- Expert opinion might not add value because the expert has incentive to slant their analysis to make the organization look good because they are hiring her  
  * **Third party analysis can be effective but is not always**

7. Your college roommate, Frank, is the CEO of an opera company. She has just launched two new productions, *A* and *B*. *A* has attracted a large audience (all productions sold out) visitors while *B* has attracted a disappointing audience (one-half of tickets unsold). Frank is thinking of posting the ticket sales on the nonprofit‚Äôs website, issuing press releases, and reporting these numbers to his board. Before deciding whether to do so, he calls you to ask for advice.  
   1. Do you think Frank should release the ticket sales for *A*? Will Frank be more or less enthusiastic about releasing the ticket sales for *B*?  
- He will want to release the sales for both because A is good news which could attract more donors/sponsors. But if he only releases A, people/donors will think that B sales are worse than it actually are  
  * People read bad news into the silence

  2. In light of your answer to ‚Äúb,‚Äù what is the case for *never* releasing these numbers for any production? What is the case for *always* releasing these numbers for any production?  
- Never releasing information for any production is a possibility, but always releasing information no matter the result is a better approach because people will assume the bad news is worse than it is if you only release good news

8. Jane is president of a university, which has a broad curriculum. Jane considers climate change a major challenge, so she has cut funding for other departments, including the classics department, in order to launch a climate school. Jane is thinking of posting a document on the university‚Äôs website explaining the new strategy.  
   1. Kim is a successful entrepreneur who has supported the university generously. She was a classics major, and believes that her engagement with the classics had a major influence on her life and career. What are the advantages and disadvantages of highlighting the new strategy in writing, so Kim will know the details?  
- Advantages of Disclosure:  
  * Kim is not the only audience. Other donors who want the climate school may be excited about this prospect  
  * Kim might better understand the decision and get on board with it (although this is unlikely)  
  * Kim might respect the school‚Äôs honesty  
  * Kim and other donors might think the news of the departments getting cut is worse than it is if you do not disclose  
- Disadvantages of Disclosure:  
  * Kim and other donors who like the classics may want to donate less because they do not approve of the school‚Äôs  
- **There are tradeoffs with how much to disclose because you have to exchange disappointing some people for credibility**

  2. Jane is eager to motivate her team to do their best work. What are the advantages and disadvantages of describing the results from the first year in writing (e.g., number of students enrolled in the climate school, cuts in enrollment in classics and other programs) and posting this report online?  
- Advantages: May motivate the workers in the climate school and the donors who support this (and they may think it is worse than it is without disclosure)  
- Disadvantages: May discourage the workers in the classics department and other programs when they see their funding is being cut

  3. Len is the university‚Äôs most generous donor. He supports the pivot to climate, and he wants to know the results. Do you think he can get them, even if Jane doesn‚Äôt share them publicly?  
- Yes.

  4. Mary donates $500 per year to the university. She also supports the pivot to climate and wants to know the details. Do you think she can get them, even if Jane doesn‚Äôt share them publicly?  
- Probably not. She isn‚Äôt a significant enough donor to have say

  5. Nan runs a rating agency that evaluates universities. She has heard about the pivot to climate and wants to know how this initiative is doing. Do you think she can get them, even if Jane doesn‚Äôt share them publicly?  
- Probably not. Jane doesn‚Äôt want a third party reporter getting her hands on the information because then Jane cannot control the narrative as easily

  6. Why might it be a good idea (or a bad idea) for Jane to post the report she sends to Len online, making it available to anyone who is interested to learn more about the university‚Äôs work?  
- More disclosure is better. If it‚Äôs good ü°™ Good; If it‚Äôs bad ü°™ People might think it would be worse

9. Form 990  
   1. Does Form 990 require nonprofits to share information about the compensation of senior professionals and the board? About key governance issues, including whether they have conflict of interest policies? Does Form 990 require nonprofits to provide a financial statement, which reports different types of income and expenses?  
- Form 990 requires nonprofits to share information about senior officers, key governance issues, conflicts of interest, and financial statements which reports income and expenses

  2. Does Form 990 require nonprofits to share information about the impact and cost-effectiveness of their programs?  
- Form 990 does not require nonprofits to report the cost-effectiveness of their programs  
  * **Schizer: Not enough in nonprofits gets disclosed. We need to tell people if your programs are working. What matters is if the mission is succeeding**

10. Overhead ratios  
    1. What are overhead ratios?  
- **Overhead Ratio \= Nonprofit‚Äôs percentage of their budget that is spent on expenses/administrative costs/fundraising**  
  * CEO salary  
  * Fundraising  
  * Office

  2. Why do rating agencies use them to evaluate the quality of nonprofits? What is the argument for relying on them?  
- Rating agencies use overhead ratios to evaluate the quality of nonprofits because they can tell you how efficient the nonprofit is (defining efficiency by how much of the money is going to the mission as opposed to the fundraising/administration). They are also used because they are easy to calculate (especially because this information is available on Form 990\)

  3. What is the argument against relying on them?  
- **The problem with using overhead ratios is that administrative costs are expensive when you get good ones**  
  * A good CEO/management is critical and that costs money  
  * Increasing the overhead ratio is good if you are doing it in a productive way because this will make serving the mission better  
- **Additionally, people are inconsistent with how they classify an administrative/fundraising expense (this goes back to network effects and comparisons) so it makes it hard to compare overhead ratios accurately**  
  * You can call a fundraiser a ‚Äúfundraiser‚Äù and count their salary in the overhead ratio or you can call a fundraiser an ‚Äúeducator‚Äù and count none of their salary towards overhead ratios  
    * Organizations try to manipulate overhead ratios constantly

- **Schizer: Problem with nonprofit reporting (on Form 990 and overhead ratios) is that this is the only data available so people have to use this to rate nonprofits, but it is misleading**

- **There are fundamental dynamics about disclosure ‚Äì competing impulses about what to disclose and what not to disclose**  
  * **There is a function for mandatory disclosure rules but it is narrow. There are already market incentives to get firms to disclose to deal with asymmetric information and incentive problems. Given that disclosure is a standard mechanism to deal with information and incentive problems, institutions are already on their own motivated to disclose. However, they might disclose on their own in different or deceiving ways so there is a rationale to disclose in a particular way so that firms can facilitate comparisons and present accurate results**  
  * **At the same time, we do not want to require a one size fits all approach because this can be wrong or because it can not fit certain situations**

11. Penelope is the head of a humanitarian organization, which provides job training to single mothers. To track her organization‚Äôs progress, Penelope measures the income of clients before they enroll in the program and compares it with their income six months after they finish the program. Quincy is the head of Foundation, who is considering a grant to Penelope‚Äôs organization.  
    1. Quincy sends Penelope a draft gift agreement. It would require Penelope to do a survey asking people enrolled in training programs to describe how satisfied they are with the program, answering various questions about the quality of the teaching, materials, space, and the like on a scale of 1 to 10\. Penelope has not been asking these questions. She calls you and asks whether she should agree to these terms. What do you tell her?  
- Penelope should not agree to these terms because this information will be not useful to her. She is running the organization and knows best. She is measuring the organization‚Äôs success based on more important, outcome-driven information like the salaries, not output-driven granular information like the materials, etc.

  2. You are on the board of Quincy‚Äôs foundation. Assume that you know about the questions he has asked Penelope to answer, and that Penelope has asked not to answer them, proposing the alternative described above instead (comparing incomes). Quincy tells you that he still wants Penelope to answer his questions. ‚ÄúWe ask all of our grantees to do this survey, and we then compare the scores.‚Äù Do you agree or disagree with Quincy on this?   
- I do not agree with Quincy on this. Penelope is using better evaluation information. However, I think Quincy‚Äôs strongest argument is that this information from Penelope will help him more easily compare her nonprofit to the others in deciding who to donate to. Although that makes it easier for Quincy to evaluate, it seems like a waste of Penelope‚Äôs time and resources

- **The people at the nonprofit know better about what they do than the donors, and they know if it is working**  
- **Nonprofits should disclose more information because they should be measuring these things themselves and if they are already measuring them, then just present it and donors will understand that you are already working on that**

# 13: Derivatives (Basics)

- 2 Kinds of Derivatives:  
  * 1\) Forward K \= Commitment to buy/sell underlying asset at a specified future moment and price  
    * Long \= Ownership (Bet on increase)  
    * Short \= Sale (Bet against increase)  
  * Forward (future) price always higher than spot (current) price because it is an interest rate, a loan from seller to buyer (buyer has ownership and exposure to asset because the price has already been set but seller doesn‚Äôt get money now)  
    * Current price is always the best indicator of what something is worth now and into the future  
    * Long person on forward K economically owns the property but does not need to pay for it yet  
  * 2\) Options:  
    * Call \= Option to buy  
      * Long a call option \= Option to buy if value of underlying is above exercise price (but if the value of the underlying falls below exercise price, I just lose premium and do not exercise the option)  
        * Do this because I think the value of the underlying asset will increase  
        * Buyer of call option pays a premium for choice to exercise or not  
      * Short a call option \= You sold someone the right to buy the underlying asset above the exercise price  
        * Seller of call option gets a premium for bearing the risk that the option will become valuable and they will honor commitment  
        * Do this because you think the value of the underlying asset will decrease  
    * Put \= Option to sell

- Long always means you think the underlying asset is going to increase in value and short means you think the underlying asset is going to decrease in value except in put options: If you buy a put option (option to sell) because you think the price is going down  
  * Technically if you buy a put option, you are long the put option, but you think the value is going down  
  * Similarly, if someone sells the put option, they short the put option, but they think the value is going up  
    * If A sells B a put option:  
      * B is long the put option (but thinks price is going down because he will get an option to sell if asset goes below exercise price)  
      * A is short the put option (but thinks price is going up because he will have to buy (be sold to from A) if asset goes below exercise price)

- Valuing Options:  
  * Black-Shoals 3 Factors:  
    * 1\) Time  
      * Longer is better   
    * 2\) Price of underlying  
      * Option to buy underlying at exercise price of $50 and the underlying is worth $5K, I am almost certain to use the option, so every dollar increase in the underlying‚Äôs price is a dollar increase in the value of my option  
      * Option to buy underlying at exercise price of $50 and the underlying is worth $2, I am almost certain to not use the option, so every dollar increase in the underlying‚Äôs price will only create pennies of value in my option  
      * The value of the underlying matters but how much it matters, depends on how likely the option is to be used  
    * 3\) Riskiness/Volatility  
      * Underlying asset has an expected value but how much the range of volatility might diverge from the expected price makes the option more valuable is the risk is higher because you are only getting the upside  
      * More risk means that the underlying asset‚Äôs value could go up or down a lot but with options you only get the upside

- Why buy forward K when you can buy options? Because you don‚Äôt have to pay premium  
  * Do you want to pay for downside protection?

  I.                ***Forward Contracts and Swaps***

1\. What is a derivative?

- A derivative is a contract with:  
  * Bilateral (2 Parties)  
  * Zero Sum (When one party makes money the other loses money). It is a bet  
  * Value derived from some other financial fact  
- **A derivative is a zero sum, bilateral K whose value derives from some other financial fact**  
  * Bet on value of something outside of the K: You can use a derivative to bet on the value of anything else (gold‚Äôs value, S\&P, interest rates)

2\. What is the difference between a ‚Äúlong‚Äù and ‚Äúshort‚Äù position?

- **Long \= You own something**  
  * Party betting on derivative to increase in value  
  * Betting the market will go up (most of the time)  
- **Short \= You owe something**  
  * Party betting on derivative to decrease in value  
  * Betting the market will go down (most of the time)

- To bet something is going to go up without owning it, you are long it  
- To bet something is going to go down without owning it, you short it  
  * Selling then buying a new share to replace it

3\. What is a forward?

- **Forward K \= Type of K to buy or to sell a specified underlying thing on a specified date in the future**  
  * The underlying could be anything

4\. Suppose the price of gold is $1,800 per ounce on June 1, 2022, and Debbie enters into the following contract.  She agrees that in two years (*i.e.*, June 1, 2024), she will buy one ounce of gold from Larry.  When she receives the gold in June of 2024, she will pay $1,900.  
A. Who is ‚Äúlong‚Äù under this contract?  Who is ‚Äúshort‚Äù?

- Debbie (buyer) is long. She owns something (the right to buy the ounce of gold)  
- Larry (seller) is short. He owes something (must sell the ounce of gold)

  B. If the price of gold in June of 2024 is $2,100 per ounce, what are Debbie‚Äôs economic consequences from entering into this contract?  What are Larry‚Äôs consequences?  (For simplicity‚Äôs sake, assume Larry did not own the gold upon entering into this contract; instead, he buys it in June of 2024, in order to deliver it to Debbie.)  
- Debbie makes $200. She paid $1900 for something worth $2100  
- Larry loses $200. He paid $2100 but only got $1,900  
  * Zero sum: The $200 Debbie makes is the $200 that Larry loses  
- To deliver the gold:  
  * Larry could do a **physical settlement:** Underlying property is actually delivered to settle the K (if Debbie was a jeweler)  
  * Larry could do a **cash settlement:** But if Debbie just wanted to make a bet on gold, Larry could settle the K by paying Debbie $200

  C. What if instead the price of gold in June 2024 is $1,500?

- Debbie pays Larry $400  
- Larry makes $400

  D. What would it mean for the parties to ‚Äúcash settle‚Äù this contract?  How is that different from ‚Äúphysical settlement‚Äù?

- See above

5\. On the forward contract in the prior question, please note that Debbie does not have to pay the purchase price for two years. 

1. Why do you think the price is $1,900, even though the current market price is $1,800? Is the ‚Äúforward‚Äù price higher than the ‚Äúspot‚Äù price (the current market price) because the parties predict that the price will rise?

- Note: Forward price (price on K) is $1900 but spot price (current price) was $1800  
  * **Forward price \= Price on the derivative**  
  * **Spot price \= Current market price**  
- Forward price will almost always be higher than spot price  
  * Not because the market thinks that the price is going to go up  
    * If people think that people the price is going to go up, the price will go up  
    * The current price is the aggregate of everyone‚Äôs expectations of the future price  
- **Forward price is always higher than current price not because of a market prediction but because the seller is being compensated for payment in the future**  
  * Privilege of paying in the future is like the seller lending buyer money  
    * Interest (credit risk and time value of money)  
- **Increase in the price is due to interest buyer pays for not having to pay now**  
- **Forward price \= Spot price increased by relevant interest rate**

- **In pre-paid forward K, buyer pays now and delivery is in the future**  
  * **In a pre-paid forward K, forward price \= current price**  
  * No implicit loan from seller to buyer

2. What is ‚Äúleverage‚Äù?  
- **Leverage \= Investing with borrowed money**  
  * Debbie‚Äôs forward K is completely leveraged  
- **Derivative is a 100 percent leveraged investment (immediate exposure, zero cash until performance)**  
- **The derivative transfers the economic exposure immediately (it is as if she owns the gold already)**  
  * **Debbie is exposed to the price of gold at the time of signing**

- Economically similar to a sale except with the dividends with stocks

3. Which is a more ‚Äúleveraged‚Äù investment ‚Äì buying the stock today, or entering into a forward contract to buy it at a fixed price in the future?

- **The forward K is a more leveraged investment than buying the stock today because you do not have to put the money up yet**  
  * **This is economically the same as borrowing the money of the entire purchase price of an underlying thing and buying the underlying thing**
