  * You can simulate the economics of a forward K by borrowing the money and buying the underlying thing

- **One reason to use derivatives:**  
  * **There are some situations where you cannot buy something with 100 percent borrowed money (margin rules of stock exchanges), but you can buy the derivative of that thing without borrowing the money**  
    * **Regulatory arbitrage: Economically similar things are treated differently**

- Note that in a pre-paid forward K on an asset that pays dividends:  
  * Buyer is essentially loaning the seller money and seller is keeping the dividends. This is factored into the purchase price

6\. Other derivatives share the same economic properties as a forward contract.

- Derivatives are the building blocks of transactions  
- **3 Types:**  
  * **Forward K**  
  * **Options**  
  * **Bonds**  
- **You can build any financial instrument with a collection of these 3 K‚Äôs**

1. What is a futures contract?

- **Futures K \= Type of forward K (purchase by one party, sale by another for specified underlying asset on a specified date) that is traded on an exchange**  
  * It is not negotiated between the parties  
  * **Certain parameters must be built into a forward K to qualify it for exchange trading**  
    * **Features of the K must be standardized (makes it fungible)**  
  * **A non-futures K (tailored just for you, more complex) is an over the counter derivative** 

2. What is a swap?  Are they ‚Äúcash settled‚Äù or ‚Äúphysically settled‚Äù?  Are they settled all at once or periodically?  
- **Swap \= Cash settled forward K that is settled in part over time**  
  * Still betting on up or down of underlying asset but the bet gets settled periodically

3. Suppose Debbie and Larry want to change the above arrangement in two respects.  First, Larry will not make physical delivery of the gold.  Second, Debbie and Larry will not wait the whole two years (*i.e.*, until June of 2024\) to settle up their bet on the price of gold.  Instead, they want to make two one-year bets.  Suggest a derivative they can use to achieve this goal. What kind of derivative is this?

- **Swap K \= Series of cash settled forward K that are settled in payments one after the next (a bunch of forward K‚Äôs strung together)**  
  * 3 months: Debbie was right. Larry pays her $25  
  * 6 months: Debbie was right. Larry pays her $75  
  * 9 months: Debbie was wrong. Debbie pays Larry $200

- **Swap Characteristics:**  
  * **1\) Derivative (forward K)**  
  * **2\) Cash settled**  
  * **3\) Settled in a series of transactions**  
  * **4\) Once you place the bet you are fully exposed to price movements (unlike an option but like a forward K)**

  II.              ***Options***

7\. When an ounce of Gold is trading at $1,800 per ounce, Debbie pays Larry $40 for an option to buy gold from him at $1,850 at any time in the next year.  

A. What is Debbie‚Äôs option called?  Is Debbie‚Äôs option American or European style?

- American style option  
  * **This is a call option \= A right to buy at a specified price but not obligated to buy**  
    * Different than forward K because she can buy at a specified price if she wants to but is not obligated to do so  
    * **She is paying for the right to buy ‚Äì not necessarily owning the underlying thing yet**  
    * She owns a long call option

  B. What is Larry‚Äôs position called?  What is the term for the payment he receives?

- **Larry has sold/written/granted a call option**  
  * **He gets a payment for giving the buyer the right to purchase at a specified price. This is the premium for giving her the right to buy**  
  * This is a short call

4. What is the economic result for Debbie and Larry if Gold is trading at $1,950 in one year?  
- Debbie \= $60 gain  
  * Will exercise the option  
  * **She does not make $100 profit (you must account for the premium she had to pay at the outset)**  
  * If you buy a call you think the price will go up by more than what the premium you paid i

- Larry \= $60 loss  
  * $40 for selling the call; $100 loss for option exercised  
  * **He would‚Äôve made money if the price went down or not up by more than $40**  
  * If you sell a call you think the price is going to go down by more than what the premium you got is

5. What is the economic result for Debbie and Larry if Gold is trading at $1,750 in one year?  
- Debbie \= Loses the $40 premium but nothing beyond that. She will not exercise the option  
  * She would just buy it in the market for the new current price

- Larry \= Gets the $40 premium but nothing beyond that  
  * Debbie won‚Äôt exercise the option

6. How would your answer change if, instead of buying an option, Debbie had entered into a forward contract to buy an ounce of gold from Larry at $1,850 in one year? (Review: Why would the forward price be $1,850, even though the current price is $1,800?)  
- With a forward K, she would lose $100 because she would be obligated to buy at the new $1750 price (but she paid $1850 for the forward K)  
- **The benefit of using a call option instead of a forward K is that she has the choice to buy or not. This limits her loss to the premium she pays for the option**

8\. Assume that Larry believes the price of gold is going to decline below its current $1,800 level.  What option should he buy from Debbie (assuming she is willing to sell it)?

- He can sell a call option and make money off of the premium  
- **He can buy a put option which gives him the right to sell gold at specified price**  
- If the price declines, then he can sell at a higher price than the new price and make money off of the difference  
  * If the price goes from $1800 to $1000, Larry exercises his put option and sells something worth $1000 but gets $1800 ($800 benefit)

- **If there is no doubt, use the forward K (and avoiding paying a premium)**  
- **If there is doubt and you want to limit losses to the amount of the premium, use the option (and pay the premium)**

9\. Assume that Debbie wants to enter into a forward contract to buy 1 ounce of Gold from Larry for $1,850 in one year.  Instead of using a forward contract, can she achieve the same economic result with one or more option transactions?  In other words, how can you synthesize a forward contract with options?

- **Simulate a forward K with options:**  
  * **Buy a call option at forward price ü°™ Exposure to price increases**  
    * **Debbie will exercise call option if price increases and pays premium**  
  * **Sell a put option at forward price ü°™ Exposure to price decreases**  
    * **Buyer will exercise put option if price decreases (Debbie must sell) and gets premium**

- **If I buy gold for $1850 market price:**  
  * **Price goes up to $2000, I benefit $150**  
  * **Price goes down to $1700, I lose $150**  
- **In long forward K for $1850:**  
  * **Price goes up to $2000, I benefit $150 (I spent $1850 on something worth $2000)**  
  * **Price goes down to $1700, I lose $150 (I spent $1850 on something worth $1700)**  
- **Options:**  
  * **I buy a (long) call option for $1850 and pay $10 premium (gives me right to buy at $1850)**  
    * **Price goes up to $2000, I exercise option and benefit $150 (I buy something worth $2000 for $1850) and lose my $10 premium**  
    * **Price goes down to $1700, I do not exercise and lose my $10 premium**  
  * **I sell a (short) put option for $1850 and get $10 premium (gives buyer right to sell to me at $1850)**  
    * **Price goes up to $2000, buyer does not exercise, and I keep my $10 premium (I would pay buyer $1850 for something worth $2000 ‚Äì buyer is not going to sell me something for $1850 when he can sell for $2000 in market)**  
    * **Price goes down to $1700, buyer exercises and I lose $150 and keep my $10 premium (I pay buyer $1850 for something worth $1700 ‚Äì buyer is going to sell me something for $1850 worth $1700)**  
  * Economically, buying a call option and selling a put option are both bets that the value of the underlying thing will go in the same direction  
    * Buy long call option \= Participate in appreciation as price goes up  
    * Sell short put option \= Participate in decrease as price goes down  
      * Put these two together \= Forward K

- The premiums will wash out when the price on the option (the price you exercise at) is the forward price. If not, one of the options will be more expensive than the other and the forward K will not perfectly be replicated  
- Derivatives that do not set a price are waiting on the market price at the time of exercising

10\. Rank from most leveraged to least leveraged: a) a direct investment in gold; b) a forward contract to buy gold; and c) a call option to buy gold.

- Least Leveraged: Direct investment (must provide purchase price in cash)  
- Middle Leveraged: Call option to buy gold (must pay premium)  
- Most Leveraged: Forward K (not payment required)  
  * No premiums/out of pocket costs with a forward K. Just a K

11\. Assume that Debbie believes the price of gold will rise from $1,800 to $2,000 ‚Äì but will go no higher.  She wants to make a profit on this prediction, but she wants to minimize her risk of being wrong, and she wants to pay as little money as possible to place this bet.  How can she do it? (Hint \-- she buys one option and sells another.)

- Debbie could just buy the gold at $1,800 and if it appreciates, she benefits from $200 increase  
  * But then she also gets exposure above $2,000 but she doesn‚Äôt value this additional exposure

- Instead, she should:  
  * **1\) Buy call option at $1800: Gets exposure from $1800 up and pays premium for this exposure**  
    * If gold goes up from $1800, she can exercise and buy it for $1800  
      * Focusing only on upside and downside doesn‚Äôt impact her  
  * **2\) Sell call option at $2000: Not exposed to appreciation over $2000 and gets premium for selling this exposure**  
    * If gold goes up over $2000, buyer can exercise and buy it for $2000 from her  
      * Focusing only on upside but limits amount of upside  
    * Through the second option, she has transferred the exposure above $2,000 for a premium (she gets money for selling the exposure)  
    * The premium allows her to reduce the amount of money she invest to place the bet  
      * **Net premium becomes smaller because she sold the $2000 call option for a premium which makes the premium she has to pay for the $1800 call option less**  
    * **Call spread \= Way of betting on asset will go up but not above a certain level**

  * Why not just buy at $1,800 and sell at $2,000?  
    * Buying the actual underlying asset requires more capital and requires you to monitor the market in a way so that you know the exact moment to sell. Just accumulate various financial positions together to get the precise exposure that you want

- **Derivatives are better because they allow you to make a much more precise bet**

12\. When the price of gold is $1,800, Larry will charge Debbie $40 for an option to buy gold at $1,850 at any time in the next year.  Will he increase or decrease the price, if the option changes as follows?  Why?

- Black-Shoals Formula: Values options  
  * Underpins the derivatives market to actually value derivates/options  
- Changes in economic terms make options more or less valuable

1. Debbie is allowed to use the option at any time in the next three years.  
- **More time on call option is more valuable (because it gives option more time for the option to go up)**

2. The price of gold falls to $1,799.   Will the price of the option change by more or less than a dollar?  Alternatively, what if the price rises to $1,801?  
- Yes. Because even if you don‚Äôt reach $1,850 where exercising the option makes sense, the change in price makes the probability the option will be exercised at $1850 more or less likely

- **If the price of the underlying asset changes, this impacts the value of the option regardless of if the option is at the exercise price because it makes it more or less likely that the option will be exercised**

- **If the price falls by $1, the option does not change by $1 always**  
  * The correlation of the underlying to the option is depends on how likely the option is to be exercised. If it is certain to be exercised, it is a dollar for dollar move. If it is certain not to be exercised, it will not move that much

- **2 Factors Impact Value of the Option:**  
  * **1\) Time (more time means more value)**  
  * **2\) Value of the underlying and its relation to the price on the option (did the value of the asset move in the direction of you exercising your option? ü°™ More value)**  
  * **3\) Riskiness of the underlying (riskier, more volatile underlying asset means more value)**

13\. Debbie and Mimi have different predictions about the price of gold in one year.  Debbie believes there is a 50% chance that Gold will trade at $1,900, and a 50% chance that it will trade at $1,700.  In contrast, Mimi believes there is a 50% chance that Gold will trade at $2,300 and a 50% chance that gold will trade at $1,300. 

1. Who will be willing to pay more for gold today ‚Äì or would they both pay the same price?

- They would both pay the same price for gold today because the expected value is the same \= $1,800  
  * **Expected value \= Midpoint, the average of the expected outcomes**

- Debbie:  
  * 50%($1,900) \= $950  
  * 50%($1,700) \= $850  
  * Expected Value \= $1800  
- Mimi:  
  * 50%($2,300) \= $1150  
  * 50%($1,300) \= $650  
  * Expected Value \= $1800

2. Who would be willing to pay more for a call option to buy gold at $1,800 ‚Äì or would they both pay the same price?

- Mimi would pay more for a call option because she has a higher upside

- When you are buying an option, you don‚Äôt care about the downside anymore  
  * **Call option allows you to only focus on the upside and so for Mimi the upside is more attractive (worth more) so the option is worth more**

- **Riskiness of the option: Two investors who have the same expected value, can have a different risk assessment (Mimi has more variability)**  
  * Debbie‚Äôs expected value of gold is $1800 but the range of what it might be worth is narrow  
  * Mimi‚Äôs expected value of gold is $1800 but the range of what it might be worth is wide

- 3 Variables that Influence the Value of an Option:  
  * 1\) Time (longer is better)  
  * 2\) Value of the underlying (higher is better for call option)  
  * 3\) Riskiness (riskier means more value because option only gives you half of the price distribution)

- **With any option, your return comes from a change in price**

# 14: Derivatives (Hedging and Tax)

I.      ***Hedging***

1\. What is the difference between hedging and speculation?

- **Hedging \= You are already exposed to a risk that you can want to get rid of**  
  * You can get rid of the risk by using a hedging transaction, a targeted strategy to transfer specific risk you do not want  
- **Speculation \= You do not yet have the risk but you want to get that risk**  
  * There is a bet out there that you want

- **Derivatives can be used to take on risk or transfer risk to someone else**

2\. Issuer, a corporation, needs to borrow money for two years.  If it issued a typical fixed rate debt instrument, Issuer would pay a coupon of 6% every year.  Instead, Issuer offers something a bit fancier. Investors pay $100, and receive their $100 back after two years. In addition, instead of receiving $6 of interest each year, the interest they receive varies, depending on the level of hurricane damage in Fort Lauderdale, Florida during that year.  Specifically, if the dollar value of the hurricane damage (as measured by a federal agency) is less than $1 billion, the Issuer pays $12.  But if the dollar value of the hurricane exceeds $1 billion, the Issuer pays no coupon for that year.  

A. Why might an investor want to buy this kind of bond?

- This is a CAT (catastrophe) bond is a type of contingent bond. It is a type of derivative (value derives from underlying asset)

- Speculation: Investor may want to buy this bond because this bond provides diversification because the payment is from weather changes, not financial market changes  
  * **Company specific risk** is bad. Diversification is important because you can reduce exposure to one company. So invest in index fund  
  * **Market specific risk** is also bad. When you invest in an index fund, there is still market-specific risk because you are still subject to general economic trends impacting the entire financial market  
    * You still have market risk when you diversify by buying international index funds because there are economic shocks that impact the global economy (trade war)  
    * There are limits to diversification. You cannot diversify away market specific risk with index funds  
  * **But you can buy a CAT bond that does not track the financial markets. This is an investment that does not correlate with overall market conditions and diversify away your market specific risk**  
    * (May correlate a little with U.S. financial markets if FL is hit hard by hurricane, U.S. economy may take a hit too)  
- **Derivatives can enable you to take specific kinds of risks that you might want that are not otherwise available**  
  * **This is a new kind of risk (speculation) that does not correlate with the market**

- CAT bond is subject to interest rate risk because if you fix the rate then interest rates may change with changes to the market economy  
  * To solve this, you would need to get a CAT bond with a variable interest rate that tracks the changes in the market

  B. Why might an issuer want to raise money with this bond, instead of a more traditional one?  Do you expect that this bond would be an especially attractive source of funding for issuers in a particular business?

- Hedge: Issuer might want to raise the money this way if they are an insurance company  
  * If insurance company sold insurance to lots of FL businesses/homes:  
    * No hurricane damage: Insurance company keeps the premiums from those businesses/homes and doesn‚Äôt have to pay  
    * A lot of hurricane damage: Insurance company has to pay out many claims  
  * Insurance company can structure borrowing so that they:  
    * No hurricane damage: Pay out high interest on notes when they do not have to pay out claims  
    * A lot of hurricane damage: Pay no interest on notes when they have to pay out a lot on claims  
  * **CAT bond transfers the weather-related risk from insurance company to investors**

- In this example:  
  * Investor \= Speculator  
    * **Derivative helps investor diversify further to shed market specific risk**   
  * Issuer \= Hedging  
    * **Derivative helps business owner who does not like a risk he is exposed to pass on some of it**

- Hotels may also buy these kind of CAT bonds to transfer some of their weather-related risk  
  * Not as common

- Credit risk on CAT bonds:  
  * The issuer (insurance company) may have to pay additional premium to borrower (investor) because the borrower is also taking on credit risk of the insurance company going under and not being able to pay. Or use credit default swaps:  
    * Credit Default Swaps \= Another financial institution will say even if they can‚Äôt pay, we will  
      * This is another derivative that is underpinning the CAT bonds to get shed the credit risk of the issuer from the investor to the new financial institution

- Another strategy to protect from market specific risk: Long-Short Funds  
  * These firms try to outperform the market in both directions. Making portfolio that protects you both if market goes up and if market goes down  
  * One of the challenges: Your total return will be smaller because one part of your portfolio will be going in the wrong direction

- Gaming Laws: You cannot tie a derivative to any underlying bet/asset  
  * Outcomes of a pro sports game ü°™ Blocked by gaming laws  
  * Weather conditions are financial (that‚Äôs why CAT bonds work)

3\. Jane makes fine jewelry for sale over the Internet.  Her father taught her the trade, and her ‚Äúvalue added‚Äù is that of an artist.  She has an eye for beauty, and can turn molten metal into works of art.  But Jane has never liked the business world and, in particular, she has no idea which way the price of gold will go.  Her problem is that gold and other precious metals are essential ingredients, and they make her profit margin hard to predict.  What should Jane do?

- Business owner is exposed to a risk she does not want to be exposed to  
  * She could change pricing to remain steady despite fluctuations in price of gold  
- **But instead, she should hedge through a derivative**  
  * **What is the problem for Jane? Increases in price**  
  * **1\) She could buy a call option in gold**  
    * **If the price of gold increases ü°™ She can exercise her option**  
      * **She makes money on the option**  
      * **She loses money on the profit margins of her jewelry**  
    * **If the price of gold decreases ü°™ She does not exercise her option**  
      * **She loses on the option**  
      * **She makes money on the profit margins of her jewelry**  
  * **2\) She could enter into a forward K to buy gold (she could make exercise price her average gold price for jewelry or spot price if that is a good price for her business)**  
    * **If the price of gold increases**  
      * **She makes money on the forward K (She will pay less for something worth more)**  
      * **She loses on her jewelry profit margins (costing her more to make jewelry)**  
    * **If the price of gold deceases**  
      * **She loses on the forward K (She will pay more for something worth less)**  
      * **She makes money on her jewelry profit margins (costing her less to make jewelry)**

- She can use the derivative to transfer the precious metal risk to the counterparty on the K that wants it

4\.  Oilco has a new well that its geologists believe is very promising.  If Oilco invests $34 million now, it will be able to bring 1 million barrels of oil to market in one year ‚Äì something that would be profitable at today‚Äôs $65 per barrel price of oil.  Yet Oilco is worried that the price of oil might decline below $65.  Indeed, Oilco is considering abandoning the project as too risky, given the volatility in the spot oil markets.  

1. Suggest various solutions to this problem.

- **Oilco is a business that is exposed to a risk they do not want**  
- **What is the problem for Oilco? Decreases in price**  
  * **1\) Oilco sells a forward K on oil at exercise price of $65**  
    * **If price of oil increases**  
      * **Oilco loses money on the derivative forward K (Oilco has to let someone buy ‚Äì Oilco sells ‚Äì something worth more at a lower price)**  
      * **Oilco makes money on their oil reserve investment (Oilco charges more in the market for their newfound oil than the market price when they found the well)**  
    * **If price of oil decreases**  
      * **Oilco makes money on the derivative forward K (Someone buys something from Oilco ‚Äì Oilco sells ‚Äì something worth less at a higher price)**  
      * **Oilco loses money on their oil reserve investment (Oilco charges less in the market for their newfound oil than the market price when they found the well)**  
  * **2\) Oilco can buy a put option at $65 current oil price**  
    * **If price of oil increases ($80)**  
      * **Oilco loses money on the put option premium (Oilco has right to sell oil worth $80 for $65. They won‚Äôt do this)**  
      * **Oilco makes money on their oil reserve investment (Oilco charges more in the market for their newfound oil than the market price when they found the well)**  
    * **If price of oil decreases ($50)**  
      * **Oilco makes money the put option (Oilco has the right to sell oil worth $50 for $65)**  
      * **Oilco loses money on their oil reserve investment (Oilco charges less in the market for their newfound oil than the market price when they found the well)**

2. You are a diversified shareholder of Oilco, which is a public company.  Why might you oppose the use of derivatives by management to hedge the above risk?  Why might you favor such hedging?

- Shareholder might (like this exposure) and not like the derivative hedging because a diversified shareholder will not be tied to the risk in fluctuation in price of oil like Oilco is. Therefore, the extra transactions costs associated with hedging are just a drag on the diversified shareholder‚Äôs portfolio  
  * Oil prices decrease means that this shareholder‚Äôs stock in Oilco decreases but his stock in another company he owns like an airline or hotel increases  
  * **If this is the case, why do companies hedge?**  
    * **Agency Cost ‚Äì Shareholder is diversified and doesn‚Äôt need the derivative but management has more at stake in the success of this company (jobs, more options, stock) so they will want to decrease the risk**  
- **Defending Hedging (Response to Asymmetric Information): Shareholder might (not like this exposure) and like derivative hedging because the most efficient way to do get the money to finance high-value projects for the company is to stabilize the company‚Äôs cashflows internally (not borrow from an outside lender who does not have the information that the company has about the project so will be more risk averse)**  
  * **And then if you need additional financing, lenders will be more willing to lend to you if you have stable cashflows**  
  * Getting loans to finance the new well from financial institutions is harder for the oil company because oil company has the best information about whether to proceed

- Hedging is about businessowners shedding risk they do not want  
- They want to transfer this risk to another party  
- Is that worthwhile if companies are owned by diversified SH?  
  * No. Hedging is just a transactions cost to make management secure their jobs (Agency Cost)  
  * Yes. Management has access to information about promising projects that will be hard to communicate credibly how good these projects are to lenders because of asymmetric information. So better to not depend on outside funding or at least show lenders that your cashflows are stable to get capital more easily. This makes the company more money (even maybe lowering transactions costs to lenders) and makes shareholders more money  
    * Managers are trying to solve a (information asymmetry) problem for the shareholders

- Common Ownership diversification: If an oil company buys an airline, then increase in oil price helps oil company and hurts airline and visa versa  
  * Problem: Is management that is skilled at running an oil company going to be as skilled at running an airline

  II. ***Tax Deferral*** 

5\. Assume that interest rates are 10%. (This obviously is not the case, but it‚Äôs helpful to use round numbers.) What is it worth if someone lends you $100 for a year and charges you no interest?

- $10  
  * Interest-free loan is worth what you would have paid in interest

6\. If you have to pay someone $100 in one year, and interest rates are 10%, how much do you have to set aside now in order to pay $100 in one year?  This amount is called the present value of your obligation.

- $91  
  * By pushing a payment into the future you reduce its cost

7\. Assume interest rates are 10%, and that you owe $100 of tax this year.  If you are able to pay the tax next year instead (and don‚Äôt have to pay any interest in putting off your tax), do you save any money by delaying the tax?  If so, how much?

- Yes. Deferral is a way to reduce the present value of the payment

8\. True or false: **By delaying (or ‚Äúdeferring‚Äù) your tax, you reduce its present value**.

- True.

9\. To see an example of basic tax planning, you will need to know the following four rules:

**A.  If you make money on a derivative, you have to pay tax on the amount of your profit.  Let‚Äôs assume you make $1000 and the tax rate is 50%.  You keep $500 and the government receives $500.** 

**B. 	If you lose money on a derivative, you can use the loss to reduce your tax bill.  This means the government will also lose money when you lose money.  If you lose $1000 and the tax rate is 50%, you lose $500 and the government loses $500 (because you pay $500 less in taxes).**

- Government shares in your gains and your losses  
- The government‚Äôs share is determined by the tax rate

  **C.  Assume that you don‚Äôt have to pay tax (and cannot claim a loss) until you settle the derivative.  That can happen when the derivative reaches its ‚Äúmaturity date (so that it is scheduled to expire), or it can happen you can choose to settle it early.**

- No tax until I am done with the derivative means that I can control the timing of when I pay tax

4. **Finally, assume that you compute how much tax you owe every year.  Money you earn before midnight on December 31 is taxed this year, while money earned after midnight is taxed next year.**

10\. Ellen earns $100,000 each year in her job and pays a 50% tax on what she earns, so that she ends up keeping only $50,000. She would like to keep more of her money.  So she tries the following tax planning strategy, which is called a ‚Äústraddle.‚Äù  She enters into a forward contract to buy an amount of a commodity (‚ÄúCommodity‚Äù) on January 1, 2026 for $100,000 (‚Äúthe long forward‚Äù).  At the same time, she enters into a second forward contract, obligating her to sell exactly the same amount of Commodity on January 1, 2026 for $100,000 (‚Äúthe short forward‚Äù). 

A. Can she make any money on these contracts?  For example, let‚Äôs say Commodity is worth $120,000 on January 1, 2026\.  How much money does she earn (pretax) on the long?  How much does she lose (pretax) on the short?  What is the effect of the two together?

- She cannot make any money on these derivatives because they are perfectly offsetting. Any money that one of these K‚Äôs makes, the other loses the same amount at the same time  
  * She earns $20K on the long K  
  * She loses $20K on the short K

  B. Let‚Äôs say Commodity is worth $70,000 on January 1, 2026\.  How much money does she lose (pretax) on the long forward?  How much does she earn (pretax) on the short forward?  What is the effect of the two together?

- No effect economically regardless of which way the price of Commodity goes  
  * She loses $30K on the long K  
  * She earned $30K on the short K

  C.  Assume that Commodity prices are $120,000 at 11:59pm on December 31, 2025, so Ellen cancels her short contract, and pays $20,000 to settle it a day early (on December 31).  Then at 12:01am on January 1, 2026, she cancels the long contract, receiving $20,000.  When you look at both the long and the short forwards combined, how much money (pretax) has she earned or lost?

- Ellen lost $20K on the short K  
- Ellen made $20K on the long K  
  * **Combined, Ellen made $0 pretax**

  D. Remember that Ellen can subtract her losses from her taxable income.  In 2025, she earned $100,000 in her job, but lost $20,000 in cancelling her short forward (a day early on December 31).  What is her taxable income?  If she pays a 50% tax, how much tax does she owe?  If instead she earned $100,000 and had no losses on derivatives, how much would her taxable income be and how much tax would she pay?  How much (if any) tax does she save in 2025 by entering into this straddle?

- 2025  
  * Gross Income \= $100K  
  * She deducts a $20K loss  
  * Taxable Income \= $80K  
  * After-Tax Income \= $40K  
    * **The $20K loss inclusion saved her $10K in taxes**  
- 2026  
  * Gross Income \= $100K  
  * She includes a $20K gain  
  * Taxable Income \= $120K  
  * After-Tax Income \= $60K  
    * **The $20K gain inclusion added $10K to her taxes but it deferred that $10K one year**

- **By using derivatives to make two perfectly offsetting K‚Äôs and settling the loser this year and the winner immediately after the start of the next year, she is deferring her tax which is lowering its present value**

- **You can also just keep deferring every year by using the same strategy and constantly pushing off the tax**

11\. Below are excerpts from Section 1092 of the Internal Revenue Code. Please read the excerpts and answer the questions below:

	1092**(a)Recognition of loss in case of straddles, etc.**

		**1)Limitation on recognition of loss**

**(A)In general** Any loss with respect to 1 or more¬†[positions](https://www.law.cornell.edu/definitions/uscode.php?width=840&height=800&iframe=true&def_id=26-USC-747804969-314747214&term_occur=999&term_src=)¬†shall be taken into account for any taxable year only to the extent that the amount of such loss exceeds the¬†[unrecognized gain](https://www.law.cornell.edu/definitions/uscode.php?width=840&height=800&iframe=true&def_id=26-USC-966298640-314744332&term_occur=999&term_src=title:26:subtitle:A:chapter:1:subchapter:O:part:VII:section:1092)¬†(if any) with respect to 1 or more[¬†positions¬†](https://www.law.cornell.edu/definitions/uscode.php?width=840&height=800&iframe=true&def_id=26-USC-747804969-314747214&term_occur=999&term_src=)which were offsetting[¬†positions¬†](https://www.law.cornell.edu/definitions/uscode.php?width=840&height=800&iframe=true&def_id=26-USC-747804969-314747214&term_occur=999&term_src=)with respect to 1 or more[¬†positions¬†](https://www.law.cornell.edu/definitions/uscode.php?width=840&height=800&iframe=true&def_id=26-USC-747804969-314747214&term_occur=999&term_src=)from which the loss arose.

**(B)Carryover of loss** Any loss which may not be taken into account under subparagraph (A) for any taxable year shall, subject to the limitations under subparagraph (A), be treated as sustained in the succeeding taxable year. . . . 

**(c)Straddle defined**For purposes of this section‚Äî

**(1)In general**: The term ‚Äú[straddle](https://www.law.cornell.edu/definitions/uscode.php?width=840&height=800&iframe=true&def_id=26-USC-1787320905-314746252&term_occur=999&term_src=)‚Äù means offsetting¬†[positions](https://www.law.cornell.edu/definitions/uscode.php?width=840&height=800&iframe=true&def_id=26-USC-747804969-314747214&term_occur=999&term_src=)¬†with respect to¬†[personal property](https://www.law.cornell.edu/definitions/uscode.php?width=840&height=800&iframe=true&def_id=26-USC-1129881525-314747215&term_occur=999&term_src=).

**(2)Offsetting positions**

**(A)In general:** A taxpayer holds offsetting¬†[positions](https://www.law.cornell.edu/definitions/uscode.php?width=840&height=800&iframe=true&def_id=26-USC-747804969-314747214&term_occur=999&term_src=)¬†with respect to¬†[personal property](https://www.law.cornell.edu/definitions/uscode.php?width=840&height=800&iframe=true&def_id=26-USC-1129881525-314747215&term_occur=999&term_src=)¬†if there is a substantial diminution of the taxpayer‚Äôs risk of loss from holding any[¬†position¬†](https://www.law.cornell.edu/definitions/uscode.php?width=840&height=800&iframe=true&def_id=26-USC-747804969-314747214&term_occur=999&term_src=)with respect to¬†[personal property](https://www.law.cornell.edu/definitions/uscode.php?width=840&height=800&iframe=true&def_id=26-USC-1129881525-314747215&term_occur=999&term_src=)¬†by reason of his holding 1 or more other[¬†positions¬†](https://www.law.cornell.edu/definitions/uscode.php?width=840&height=800&iframe=true&def_id=26-USC-747804969-314747214&term_occur=999&term_src=)with respect to¬†[personal property](https://www.law.cornell.edu/definitions/uscode.php?width=840&height=800&iframe=true&def_id=26-USC-1129881525-314747215&term_occur=999&term_src=)¬†(whether or not of the same kind).

A. Is Ellen‚Äôs transaction a straddle?

- Eligibility:  
  * Yes. A straddle is having offsetting positions of personal property

  B. If it is, what is the effect of Section 1092?

- **Consequences:**  
  * **If you have loss on a straddle, you must wait for the gain on a straddle to take the loss**  
  * **You can only take the loss with the gain ü°™ Matching the timing**

- **Commercial Uses of Derivatives:**  
  * **1\) Hedging (pass on risk in a precise way)**  
  * **2\) Speculation (packaging a risk you want in a precise way)**  
  * **3\) Tax planning**  
    * **Because derivatives are so precise and calibrated, they are useful in tax planning because you can see what the rule says and do something economically similar through derivatives that avoids the rule**

# 15: Negotiations

Avery Katz Lecture:

- Deals and Negotiation:  
  * Deals \= Substance  
    * Why do contracts/organizations look the way they do?  
    * What terms and organizational structures create the most joint surplus?  
    * What do optimal terms look like?  
  * Negotiation \= Process  
    * How do the deals get in place?  
    * You have some but not all of the information  
    * You have a choice to share information or not  
    * Why do parties agree to suboptimal terms?

- Brookside Negotiation:  
  * What was the source of this conflict?  
    * Black: Should have charged a premium or did more DD to know they were bankrupt  
    * Lawyers: We would have spotted this. How do you persuade clients to keep you in the loop  
      * Part of your job to negotiate your client‚Äôs attention and interest  
      * You can always spend more at the front end to save on the back end  
  * How did it go?  
    * Anchoring too high is a problem when you don‚Äôt have all the information you want because it can ruin your credibility  
  * Bulwarism: Negotiating approach  
    * Pick a fair offer and present it and refuse to move  
    * Reasons this fails:  
      * 1\) You learn in the negotiation  
      * 2\) Ignores the human element and process. Humans like it when there is give and take

- Structure of Negotiation  
  * ZOPA  
  * BATNA

- Building Blocks:  
  * Power: Ability to bring about particular outcomes (usually that the other party doesn‚Äôt like)  
  * Rights: Independent standards of perceived legitimacy or fairness (‚ÄúYou promised‚Äù)  
  * Single most critical distinction:  
    * Positions: Demands, claims, assertions  
    * Interests: Underlying needs, concerns, motivations  
      * Don‚Äôt need to be material (respect)  
    * **Good negotiations focus on interests**  
      * Think about what the interests that underly the positions are

- Who had the power?  
  * Brookside: Power to go into bankruptcy and make litigation difficult; deny good client relationship  
    * Bankruptcy can give more power if played the right way  
    * Power to harm them to get good customers  
  * Black: Brookside was in a bad financial situation; confidence of winning litigation; can damage their reputation (this system was supposed to save them money); Power to hurt and help them

- Rights:  
  * Black: Their claim is based on a pre-text. We can‚Äôt trust them; Winning claim at trial given the ambiguous language is beneficial to us  
  * Brookside: The instructions were clear enough. It is part of their DD to have figured this out. They are a hospital. Industry standard is the custom software. They are the experts and they should have known  
    * You are in the right morally

- Interests:  
  * Brookside: Not go bankrupt, New system, Don‚Äôt want to be cheated, Growth, Avoid wasting money litigating, Annual updating system, Serve their patients properly, Avoid training costs  
  * Black: Recover K price, New market, Reputation for having a good product, develop the software, maintain reputation for not being pushed around but not being too harsh

- BATNA:  
  * Litigation for both  
    * But BATNA‚Äôs within litigation changed

- Possible Integrative Solutions:  
  * Installment payments  
  * Marketing campaign for hospital  
    * Good for hospital: Gives them security that the software will be delivered  
    * Good for computer: Helps them enter the new market  
      * Both suiting both parties interests and is self-enforcing  
  * NDA  
  * Contingency pricing  
    * Contingent on timing, satisfaction, profits  
    * Joint venture  
    * Market development/Product development  
    * Help with the training

- Sometimes the most valuable thing you can get in negotiations is the promise of a second meeting

- Negotiator‚Äôs Dilemma: Must share information about your interests to achieve a win-win but sharing information can make you vulnerable  
  * Never give away what your reservation price is

- 15% of value is at stake with this negotiation. This was the difference between good vs bad negotiations

- Custom vs. Universal:  
  * More than half choose custom  
  * Advantage to Brookside is $1M; Cost to Black is $1M  
  * So K for custom is a wash on cost but the 9 months are worth $5.6M to Brookside  
  * Therefore, if you chose custom, you did not maximize value  
    * How does this happen?  
      * No one is sharing complete information  
      * Brookside missed the point that it would cost $5.6M to wait  
      * Think about money in different ways  
      * Stupid client or attaches non-monetary value to something for emotional/marketing reasons

- Sometimes the story is more powerful than numbers

- Lawyer-client dynamic is interesting  
  * Do lawyers help or hurt?

\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_

- ZOPA means there can be a deal (reservation prices line up)  
- Bargaining power of each party relies on its BATNA  
- How can parties improve negotiating position?  
  * 1\) Tie your hands  
  * 2\) Learn more about other side‚Äôs BATNA  
  * 3\) Improve your BATNA

1. Alice Advocate is a partner at Big Law, whose expertise is mergers and acquisitions. Big Law pays her 100X. Her long-time client, Betty Banker, has been Head of M\&A at Investment Bank. Betty has just been promoted to Head of Investment Banking. She needs to hire someone to replace her as Head of M\&A, and would like to hire Alice.   
     
   Betty also would consider hiring her deputy, Chris, who has been her second-in-command in nearly all of the transactions she has done in the past four years. She thinks Chris would be a good fit as well, but he may be a bit young for this role.  
     
   Alice has sometimes considered leaving her law firm for an investment bank. She sees benefits and costs in making this switch. On the positive side, she expects to earn more money. On the negative side, she likes the craft of lawyering, the ability to be involved in more transactions, and the fact that her law firm is probably more collegial than an investment bank would be. Given these tradeoffs, Alice would leave Big Law for law firm if she is paid $140X, but she won‚Äôt make this change for less.   
     
   Meanwhile, Betty meets with Alice and asks her to consider coming to be Head of M\&A at investment bank, and this kicks off a negotiation between them.   
      
   1. What does ‚ÄúBATNA‚Äù stand for? In this negotiation, what is Betty‚Äôs BATNA? What is Alice‚Äôs BATNA?  
- **BATNA \= Best Alternative to Negotiated Agreement**  
  * Betty‚Äôs BATNA \= Hire Chris (good but young)  
  * Alice‚Äôs BATNA \= Stay at firm (likes some components but less money)  
- **BATNA creates a framework for what the deal means to you**  
- **Better BATNA \= Better bargaining power**  
  * **Bargaining power means how appealing are your alternatives to doing the deal**  
  * BATNA translates to price of deal and other terms   
- Bargaining power is overused to explain how a deal played out  
  * **Just because Alice has bargaining power doesn‚Äôt mean she would choose to use it on this issue rather than another issue**  
  * But there is no question that it plays a role  
- BATNA helps you understand what bargaining power is and where it comes from  
- How to use bargaining power?  
  * Use it properly ‚Äì Can lose a deal you should have gotten if you abuse bargaining power

  2. What does the phrase ‚Äúreservation price‚Äù mean? What is Alice‚Äôs reservation price as she considers whether to work at Investment Bank?  
- **Reservation Price \= Lowest price a party would accept on deal (and not take BATNA)**  
  * Alice‚Äôs Reservation Price \= $140X

  3. What does the term ‚ÄúZOPA‚Äù?  
- **ZOPA \= Zone of Possible Agreement**  
  * Available spread the parties allocate amongst themselves if the deal gets made

  4. Assume that Betty is willing to pay up to 160X to hire Alice. Is there a ZOPA here?  
- Yes, the ZOPA is 20X ‚Äì from 160X to 140X an agreement can be struck

  5. When there is a ZOPA, does this mean there is definitely going to be an agreement? Why or why not?  
- **Existence of a ZOPA does not guarantee an agreement because (1) offers out of the ZOPA can mischaracterize what each party‚Äôs reservation price is and encourage the other party to walk. Or (2) one party may think they can still can get a better deal even if there is an offer in the ZOPA**

- **2 Aspects to This:**  
  * **1\) Are the parties able to figure out the ZOPA (figuring out what the other party‚Äôs reservation price is will tell them what the ZOPA is because they know their own reservation price)?**  
    * The parties do not know what the others‚Äô reservation price is so they do not know what the ZOPA is  
      * Alice might think Betty‚Äôs reservation price is actually higher (she thinks she can pay more) even if Alice is only asking for half of what she thinks the surplus is  
  * **2\) What is their view about the amount of the surplus they are willing to claim?**  
    * If both parties are accurate in figuring out the ZOPA and both insist on 60 percent of surplus they will not make a deal

- You can never get a deal without a ZOPA, but even with a ZOPA you won‚Äôt necessarily get a deal if parties cannot figure it out or think they should get more of the surplus  
    
2. Continuing with the facts above, Betty knows that Alice is very tough in negotiations about money. Indeed, it‚Äôs one of the qualities that makes Alice such a strong candidate. So Betty decides to drive a hard bargain, expecting to come up (slowly) in the negotiation, so Alice will feel good about the ‚Äúconcessions‚Äù she extracts.   
     
   Meanwhile, Alice is conflicted about whether to leave Big Law. The managing partner, Diane, has been a good friend and mentor to her.   
     
   When Alice asks what compensation Betty has in mind, Betty says, ‚ÄúThe main reason to come to Investment Bank is not money. It‚Äôs the excitement and professional growth from being a principal in a transaction. Alice, you are so good at representing the principal, but don‚Äôt you want to BE the principal? Don‚Äôt get me wrong. We will pay you generously. We are thinking of paying you 120X.   
     
1. How is Betty hoping Alice will respond? How does she see the negotiation evolving?  
- Betty is hoping to shift Alice‚Äôs focus away from money by adding a new incentive to join Investment Bank  
- **She is starting with a number below her reservation price so that:**  
  * **1\) She can get as much of surplus she can (if she starts at $160X then she gets no surplus)**  
  * **2\) If you make the other side work for it, they will feel as though they got a good deal in the end**  
    * Think about the other party and how they will view things  
      * She knows Alice is scrappy and likes a good negotiation

2. Assume that Alice is very tough when she negotiates about money for her clients, but is not comfortable doing so about herself. If this is the case, how might Betty‚Äôs strategy backfire?  
- Betty may misestimate:  
  * 1\) Alice may not want to engage with Betty about other offers and just walk away because she is offended by the low offer  
  * 2\) How Alice will negotiate. Betty‚Äôs strategy depends on a strategy that evolves over the conversation  
- **There is an advantage to leaving room but leaving too much room can end the conversation**

3. Instead of ‚Äúb,‚Äù assume that Alice does not mind a tough negotiation on her own behalf, but money isn‚Äôt the only thing she cares about. When Betty offers 120X, she replies, ‚Äúthat‚Äôs not the number expected, but I‚Äôll think about it.‚Äù She ends the conversation because she is supposed to have dinner with Diane that evening and does not want to be late. Again, how might Betty‚Äôs strategy not lead to a deal?  
- This might not lead to a deal because it is not enough money for Alice to forgo the parts of her job she likes at Big Law. Further, she might get a better offer to stay from Diane  
- **Starting low means that the negotiation will take a longer time which allows other events to intervene that increase the other party‚Äôs BATNA or convince them to walk away from the deal**

4. Instead of ‚Äúb‚Äù and ‚Äúc,‚Äù assume that Alice decides to play tough, knowing that Betty values this quality in her. She decides to exaggerate her demands, expecting to meet in the middle. ‚ÄúI‚Äôd love to work more closely with you,‚Äù Alice says, ‚Äùbut not for 120X. To be honest, I don‚Äôt think I would get out of bed in the morning for that amount. I would need at least 200X to take this job.‚Äù Betty is a bit shocked by this number, which is significantly more than will be paid as Head of Investment Banking. How might Alice‚Äôs plan backfire?  
- Alice‚Äôs misrepresentation of her reservation price might let Betty think that there is no ZOPA/room for agreement  
- **Coming back too high (just like starting too low) is a strategy that is risky because it will only work if you are confident that the other side will continue negotiating**  
  * But starting low or coming in high are good strategies to capture more of the surplus and convince the other side that they got something

5. Klausner & Subramanian (K\&S) say that even when there is a ZOPA, ‚Äú**attempts at strategic bargaining can result in no deal**.‚Äù What do they mean?  
- Even when the parties are negotiating within the ZOPA, they both want the best deal they can get and thus may not arrive at a deal. Or they may misrepresent their positions to get the best deal, miss the ZOPA and miss the value between them  
  * **If strategic bargaining (I will do this because they will do that work) is wrong, you might lose the deal**

6. Continuing with ‚Äúd,‚Äù assume now that Betty smiles at Alice‚Äôs negotiating style. ‚ÄúI wish I could pay you 200X. To be honest, I think you would be worth it,‚Äù Betty responds. ‚ÄúBut that‚Äôs a great deal more than I earned in this role, and I‚Äôm sure you would agree that I was pretty good at it. My starting salary was 120X‚Äìthat‚Äôs why I offered that amount to you‚Äìbut it‚Äôs been a few years since then, so let‚Äôs make it \_\_\_.‚Äù What do you think Betty should offer here? As noted above, Alice would take 140X. Do you think Betty needs to offer that much at this point? What if she offers 125X?  
- If Betty offers 125X, Alice is unlikely to accept but this could be a good strategy regardless to bring Alice closer to her reserve price. When Betty gets near Alice‚Äôs reserve price, she will accept, feeling as though she got a fair deal

7. Assuming the negotiation continues, what counter-offer do you think Alice should make? How much should she come down from 200X at this point? What are the competing considerations?  
- Alice should counter with 170X. This number is unlikely to be accepted because it is above Betty‚Äôs reserve price. But this is a good counter because it is between the initial counter Alice gave of 200X and the initial offer Betty gave of 120X  
  * Alice needs to consider lowering her amount so that she can show Betty she is willing to negotiate and make an agreement while not lowering it too much because this would not only harm Alice‚Äôs chance and taking more of the ZOPA but also risk her seeming untruthful or too far away from Betty‚Äôs reserve price that Betty will walk

3. Bidder, a widget manufacturer, is considering buying Target, another widget manufacturer. Target‚Äôs board has determined that they would sell for as low as $900X, while Bidder‚Äôs board has decided that it is willing to pay up to $1000X.  
   1. What is the ZOPA?  
- $100X from $900X to $1000X

  2. There is $100X of value which can be gained in this transaction. How will the parties determine how it is divided? According to K\&S, what role does bargaining power play?  
- Bargaining determines how much of the ZOPA you can take. The parties divide the ZOPA through negotiations. The party that cares the least about the deal will take more of the ZOPA  
  * **External factors and the skill of negotiating will play into bargaining power but mainly it will be improving your BATNA**

  3. K\&S mention different ways to improve bargaining power. The first is ‚Äúimprove your BATNA.‚Äù How could Bidder use this strategy? How could Target use it?  
- If Bidder had a BATNA (another company) of $950X, then they have bargaining power to tell Target that they will not accept a deal under that price  
  * Same goes for Target if they had another offer of $950X  
- **Benefits of bettering BATNA (another acquisition target):**  
  * **1\) Gives you another option, safety valve**  
  * **2\) Weakens the other parties‚Äô position (they are scared you walk)**

  4. The second is ‚Äútie your hands.‚Äù Give an example of how bidder could use this strategy?  
- Bidder could use the tie your hands strategy by saying and accurately representing to Target that they do not have the money to buy them for any more than $920X

  5. How could Target tie its hands?  
- Target could use the tie your hands strategy by saying and accurately representing to Bidder that they will not be able to get shareholder approval of any deal less than $980X  
  * Help me help you  
- **Ironic: Negotiation strategy could be to represent that you have less power as opposed to more**  
  * **But then people will worry that you are misrepresenting your lack of power so you can make it real by guaranteeing shareholders you will not accept a deal below X price**  
  * **This also risks the other party walking away at the lower number when you actually would have accepted that price**

  6. K\&S mentions a third strategy: ‚Äúlearn about the other side‚Äôs BATNA.‚Äù How could each side do this in the above transaction?  
- Each party could assemble a ‚Äúclean team‚Äù like K\&S suggest that is not involved in the transaction to research the other side‚Äôs wants, needs, and overall leverage

  7. Sellers sometimes try to trigger an auction. Which of the three bargaining power strategies is this?  
- Improve your BATNA

  8. As we discussed in an earlier class, sellers that are public companies must get the approval of their shareholders to sell the company. Which of the three bargaining power strategies is this?  
- Tie your hands

  9. How would the parties negotiate this deal using ‚ÄúFull Open and Truthful Exchange‚Äù or ‚ÄúFOTE‚Äù model?  
- Bidder would tell Target that they are willing to pay up to $1,000X, and Target will tell Bidder that they are willing to accept $900X. The parties will settle somewhere in the middle of the ZOPA, probably around $950X

  10. How would they negotiate it with the ‚ÄúPoker School‚Äù approach?  
- The Poker School Approach employs deception to negotiate

  11. If Target uses the FOTE approach and Bidder uses the Poker School approach, how will this negotiation play out?  
- Bidder will dominate Target and capture most of the ZOPA because they will have accurate information about Target which they can use to deceive Target and create a better deal for themselves

- **In a world where everyone has access to Poker School and FOTE methods, everyone will use Poker School because they will assume that the other party is doing the same. If they use FOTE they will risk being at a great disadvantage**  
  * **The theoretical benefits of FOTE cannot exist**  
- This is just a version of the asymmetric information and incentive problems in new light

  12. Same as ‚Äúk,‚Äù but Target catches Bidder in an exaggeration. What will happen?  
- Target may walk from the deal and Bidder may lose out entirely or at the least will change their approach to Poker School not FOTE  
  * Any claims Bidder makes thereafter will be discounted

4. Dean Dana is speaking with Professor Paul about whether he will accept an offer to join Dean Dana‚Äôs faculty. They need to agree on six issues: Paul‚Äôs salary, which courses he will teach; whether he will have a named professorship; which office he will have; what administrative responsibilities he will take on; and how much secretarial support he will receive.  
   1. Paul wants to work his way down the list, deciding one at a time. Dana wants to hear his views on each issue before deciding any of them. Which approach is better and why?  
- Dana‚Äôs approach of hearing his views on all issues and then deciding is better because it is easier to make trade-offs and therefore a deal this way. Paul‚Äôs approach of deciding each term one by one in isolation makes each a zero sum game  
  * **It is always better to have the full picture in a negotiation before agreeing to terms one by one because you will feel stronger about some issues over others which creates room to make a deal (ZOPA)**

  2. Assume that Dana has a large corner office available (because it‚Äôs occupant just retired). She really needs someone to teach the courses Paul usually teaches. She does not have a named professorship at the moment, but expects to have one in three years. Assume also that Paul has no need for secretarial assistance, hates administrative work, and wants to maximize his salary.  
     1. Assuming that all that matters is the preferences and needs of Dana and Paul, what would their deal look like?  
- Big salary, Corner office, No administrative work, Courses Paul usually teaches, No secretarial assistant, No named professorship but guaranteed one in three years  
- **Each has things they need, they can put all the issues together**  
  2. Now let‚Äôs add another variable: Dana worries about setting precedents, so that other professors would ask for what Paul got. How does this complicate the negotiation?  
- This may complicate things because even though Dana has the ability to offer Paul some things like no administrative work, she doesn‚Äôt want to because she then she will have to offer those things to others. (And if no one is doing administrative work that would be a problem)  
- If you use a wholistic approach and view all the issues together you will probably produce a better deal for everyone because you can give/make concessions. This is weakened by precedent setting  
  * **Precedent undercuts the flexibility of a party to make concessions across issues because you may not be able to give them to others**  
  * **To know what precedents will be set, you also need to know how much of them will be observable**  
    * **Selective Use of Parts of the Deal: Unless all aspects of the deal are public, it is unlikely that all dimensions of the deal will be compared to establish precedents. Instead, specific parts of the deal will get compared**  
    3. In iii, is the negotiation easier or harder if other faculty might be able to observe some, but not all, of what Paul received?  
- This would make it easier because if others cannot observe what Paul received, then they will not know to ask for it. And therefore, Dana will not need to worry about setting a bad precedent  
- This will weaken the all-terms-at the same time approach and strengthen the line by line approach because once you think about precedent you need to think about how much of it will be observable. Unless all aspects of the deal are public, it is unlikely that future deals will be negotiated with this deal as precedent in its entirety (instead parts of it will be used as precedent)  
  * Instead, specific parts of the deal will be compared and then specific parts will be used as precedent  
    4. How (if at all) can Dana turn her precedent problem into a negotiation advantage? (Hint: consider the three bargaining power principles discussed above.)  
- **Tie your hands strategy: Dana can say to Paul that she wants to offer him these things, but she cannot or else she would have to offer them to everyone**  
  * ‚ÄúI wish I could because you deserve it but \[if I do that for you, I have to do that for others\] or \[I cannot get shareholder approval\]  
    5. Assume that Paul has the non-salary terms he wants at his current employer. How can he use this as a negotiating advantage? (Hint: consider the three bargaining power principles discussed above.)  
- **Improving BANTA:** If Paul has all the non-salary terms at his other employer, his next best alternative, staying is pretty good. He will demand these or at least components of them or some of them from Dana in order to engage in a deal  
  * **If you are able to use this outside opportunity to improve your current situation, the existence of each alternative strengthens your hand with the other**  
    * **Less at stake with either option because you like both of them**  
    6. If Paul tells Dana that he has the non-salary terms he wants at his current school, is there a reason why Dana might not find this persuasive? (Hint: remember the Poker School.) Is it easy or hard for her to tell him why she is not persuaded? How necessary (if it all) is it for her to tell him?  
- Dana may not be persuaded because she may think Paul is intentionally trying to deceive her to get a better deal for himself by inflating his BATNA  
  * Lack of credibility in the claims because you do not know what is true and what is not  
- It is pretty easy for Dana to tell Paul that she is not persuaded. She can just talk to him  
- It may not be necessary for Dana to tell Paul that she is not persuaded. Instead, she can just call his bluff not through words but by just offering a worse deal than Paul is asking for and see if accepts anyways

- **Asymmetric Information and Incentive Problems: You don‚Äôt know if the demands of the other party are accurately what he already has in his current situation (his BATNA) and he has an incentive to lie to you to get more concessions from you**  
  * **It is hard for you to know if the claims are credible because you cannot call up his current employer and say you are trying to hire him. So his claims are not credible**  
    * **These information and incentive problems cause fewer transactions to happen**

# 16: Literary Industry

- See literary industry as incentive and information problems and agency costs  
  * The way industries develop is to address these problems  
- Information Problems:  
  * Uncertainty: Will new author sell the book  
  * Asymmetry: Even with authors who aren‚Äôt first timers, they better understand how the book will be and how appealing it will be than publisher  
- Incentive Problems:  
  * Agency costs: Author isn‚Äôt going to be forthcoming about how good the book will be  
- Search costs: Publishing companies need to narrow the way they look for books  
  * Creates alignment between publishers and agents (publishing companies give agents a privileged position but agent needs to narrow the field to the books publisher will like)

- Agent and author: Align complexities  
  * Agent paid only when author paid  
  * Agent paid in percentage: More agent gets, more author gets  
  * Agent wants fixed amount \+ contingency:  
    * Agent wants this because she will have to work to sell the book. Doesn‚Äôt want to walk away with nothing if it doesn‚Äôt get sold because the author is new  
    * Providing fixed payment is hard to know whether agent is going to actually work that hard  
      * Deal with this through:  
        * **Gathering information:** Track record, how did other books do?  
        * **Compensation formula:** Flawed. Agent gets a better deal with a lower royalty when the signing deal is above a certain threshold  
          * Perverse incentive: Agent is better keeping royalty below the threshold. Incentivized to get a less good deal  
          * Some lawyers may not address the business terms of the parties. But lawyers are there to help clients identify risks and structure alternatives and address the risks  
            * Lawyers need to:  
              * 1\) See the odd result  
              * 2\) Figure out what is causing the problem  
              * 3\) Offer alternative  
          * Problem:  
            * Fixed payment of $6K \+ 15% royalty  
            * Phasing out of fixed payment at a certain level of advance  
              * Agent is better off below that level  
          * Alternatives:  
            * Same fixed payment without phasing out \+ Same percentage  
            * Smaller fixed payment without phasing out \+ Same percentage  
            * Same fixed payment without phasing out \+ Smaller percentage

- Need to have a greater ability to reading K drafts  
  * Author-profit participation agreement

1. Your college roommate, Barbara, is a management consultant. Her job is to do an assessment of troubled companies and suggest ways to turn them around. She is the founder and principal of ‚ÄúTurnaround Consulting‚Äù or ‚ÄúTC.‚Äù   
     
   Barbara decides to write a business book, *Turning It Around: How to Turn a Failed Business into a Market Leader*. She has the urge to share what she knows, and she also wants to use the book to raise her profile, hoping it will help her broaden her base of clients.  
     
   Barbara doesn‚Äôt get much repeat business, since the executives she advises usually are a bit embarrassed about having to hire her and want to forget the anxious experience of nearly going bankrupt, so they don‚Äôt usually stay in touch or hire her again. This means she needs a constant stream of new work to stay busy.  
     
   To help her attract more consulting work, Barbara is hoping to get a publishing contract with an elite publishing house, which has the resources to help her make it a best seller. But these publishing houses generally won‚Äôt deal with an author directly. They usually accept proposals only from a literary agent, who is supposed to submit the book proposal on the author‚Äôs behalf.   
     
   As a backup, Barbara is also open to publishing the book in an academic press, although she knows that her royalty on the book will be significantly lower and the academic press will not do as much to market the book. In this situation, she will do her best to market it on her own.  
     
   This is all new territory for Barbara, and she appreciates that you are willing to give her legal and business advice when she needs it. She starts by calling you with two questions:

   1. Why do you think elite publishing houses work only with agents? What incentive or information problems are the agents meant to solve?  
- **Efficiency:** Delegates educational function to third party (agent) so that an author doesn‚Äôt have to learn a new business and take up the time of the publishing company  
  * Agents have the relationships so there is ease of access  
  * Publishing company doesn‚Äôt have to explain everything to every author. Agent does that for them

- **Incentive Problem:** Authors do not have a realistic assessment of the quality of the book they are going to write  
  * Every author thinks that their book is the best. Agent and publishing house take emotional element out of it and can evaluate it realistically  
    * Every author thinks their book will sell so publishing house has no way to gauge its prospects  
  * Agent also has this problem because they want to represent client to best of abilities, oversell book. However, this is better than the author because agents have a repeat relationships with the publishing houses (while authors who don‚Äôt have these repeat relationships have unlimited incentives to advocate for themselves  
    * Agent dampens the incentive problem

- **Asymmetric Information Problem:** There is uncertainty because usually book hasn‚Äôt been written yet. And the author knows best what the book will look like  
  * Agents do preliminary screen of authors. They are selective about which to take on as clients which gives them credibility to publishing houses. The publishing house delegates the diligence work to the agent

  2. Why do you think this function is thought to be less important in academic presses?  
- Academic presses care less about commercial aspect  
- But problems still remain:  
  * Incentive Problem: Authors tend to be generous of assessment of own abilities  
  * Asymmetric Info. Problem: Book hasn‚Äôt been written so hard to know if it will be good  
    * **But there is less asymmetric info. problem with academics because they have probably written other works and also are hired by institutions that credential them (this seal of approval would normally be provided by the agent). So the need for agent is less**

Schizer OH:

- Hiring process outsourced to colleges, law schools. Like how the publishing houses outsource author credentialing to agents  
- Agent structure ‚Äì like broker agreements. The list provided to author at the end of the agreement of potential clients  
- Where did we leave off with leverage? Is that a synonym for bargaining power?  
- In the situation where you have asymmetry of information and someone has multiple motives for saying it or representing that information and you are not sure which (like why agent won't share list of publishers she knows), is what we have said the main solution should be to try to get more information? Or push further? Or then align incentives so that you no longer need that information?

2. Barbara has a friend, Chris, who published a book recently after hiring Alice, a literary agent. After Chris introduces Barbara to Alice, Barbara shares her a draft of the book proposal she plans to use, which gives an overview of the book, summarizes her own experience, and explains the potential market for the book. They meet for breakfast, and Alice says she is open to representing Barbara.  
   1. Barbara asks what Alice‚Äôs strategy will be for selling the book to a publisher. Alice says she will put together a list of ten publishing houses, run the list by Barbara, and then send the proposal to editors she knows at these houses. When Barbara asks for examples of the houses Alice has in mind, Alice smiles and says, ‚ÄúWell, you have to hire me first, and then we can talk in detail about that.‚Äù After the breakfast, Barbara calls and repeats this to you and asks if you think Alice‚Äôs position is reasonable. Or should Barbara be worried?  
- Alice Perspective (Innocent): Alice‚Äôs position is reasonable because her client list and strategy for publication is her asset. She shouldn‚Äôt give this away for free. This is her value  
- Barbara Perspective (Concerning): Asymmetry of information. Alice may be trying to just get hired but actually has no idea about this. She may not have any expertise about this  
  * The unwillingness to say anything may signal that she knows nothing about this or that she knows a lot about this

- **2 Different Sources of Value that Alice could offer:**  
  * **1\) Personal Relationships ‚Äì Friends with execs.**  
    * **Telling author about these would not be given away for free. She knows people at the publishing houses and can get author in there, but by telling the author she is not sacrificing these relationships**  
  * **2\) Expertise ‚Äì Knowing the best fit**  
    * **Telling author about this would be value given away for free. She can tell you which publishing house is best for this book, but if she tells you her information on which publishing houses are best, she is giving that away**

  2. If the above comment can be read as either harmless or concerning, how (if at all) can Barbara learn more to try to make a judgment about this?  
- Since her unwillingness to explain information raises the possibility that she is fraud, you need to try to see past it. What can Barbara do?  
  * **Ask others in the industry**  
  * **Ask Alice her track record with other authors**  
    * Who are you represented? Where have you been placing books?


3. Alice sends Barbara a draft contract to formalize her status as Barbara‚Äôs agent. ‚ÄúTake a look at it and let me know if you have any question,‚Äù Alice says in her email.   
     
   Alice charges a commission that (among other things) provides her with 15% of ‚ÄúGross Proceeds‚Äù from ‚Äúthe Property.‚Äù She sends you the relevant language:   
     
   *You, Barbara (the ‚ÄúAuthor‚Äù), hereby engage Alice (the ‚ÄúAgent‚Äù) as the Author‚Äôs **sole and exclusive Agent** to negotiate for the publication, and other disposition throughout the world of the hardcover, paperback rights, books on tape, television, motion picture, electronic and ancillary and allied rights whether now or to become known in the future, as customarily understood within the entertainment industry (‚ÄúRights‚Äù) in and to the Author‚Äôs properties (the ‚ÄúProperties‚Äù) a book tentatively entitled ‚ÄúTurning It Around: How to Turn a Failed Business into a Market Leader.‚Äù*  
     
   *In full consideration of Agent‚Äôs services hereunder, the Author agrees to pay Agent a commission equal to Fifteen Percent (15%) of all ‚ÄúGross Proceeds‚Äù derived from the exploitation of the Rights except Ten Percent (10%) from the exploitation of television and film (‚ÄúCompensation‚Äù).  ‚ÄúGross Proceeds‚Äù are hereby defined as meaning all monies, receipts and consideration of every kind (including salaries, royalties, bonuses, shares of profits, loans, speaking fees, consulting fees, and any and all other forms of cash compensation), paid to the Author or to any designee of the Author, arising out of or paid pursuant to the disposition and/or exploitation of the Rights and the subject matter discussed in the Properties, pursuant to written or oral agreements negotiated by Agent or the Author (including any renewals, modifications, options or extensions thereof, regardless of when made and regardless of by whom negotiated)*   
     
- Alice gets paid for these services by selling book rights and generating an income stream (10% TV and film, 15% Gross proceeds)  
  * Gross proceeds \= All money that comes in  
  * Gross proceeds not only includes 15% of core royalty from copies sold but also of outside work. Very broad

1. Given what you know about Barbara‚Äôs professional life, what concern do you have about the definition of ‚Äúgross proceeds‚Äù?  
- Author Concern: Barbara is also a consultant separately from being an author so Alice could take her future consulting fees that are not tied to the book  
  * The agent should not share in consulting business

- **Concerning passages:**  
  * **Delete ‚Äúconsulting fees‚Äù ‚Äì These do not arise out of the book**  
  * **Rewrite ‚Äúthe Rights and the subject matter discussed in the Properties‚Äù**  
    * **Book is about her business so need to delete ‚Äúsubject matter discussed in the Properties‚Äù because agent gets a piece of the book ‚Äì not the subject matter of the book which is her business**

2. How might Alice try to justify a broad definition of ‚Äúgross proceeds‚Äù?  
- **Agent‚Äôs 2 Arguments:**  
  * **1\) She has had a problem with some authors where they cut a side deal to give away book for free but get paid a lot for a speaking fee. You shouldn‚Äôt be able to reclassify payments to avoid my royalty**  
    * **Could be dealt with more directly**  
  * **2\) Alice wants to become partner in consulting business. Her additional consulting revenue is because of the book and Alice‚Äôs work**  
    * **Barabara should say no, the book exists because of my business. She was doing consulting for a long time before the book**

3. What modifications in the language would you suggest‚Äìeither to modify the definition or to add other language?  
- The specific words that create the broad gross proceeds issue:  
  * ‚ÄúConsulting fees‚Äù ‚Äì Take this out  
    * These are not tied to a book. It goes beyond the book  
  * ‚Äúand the subject matter discussed in the properties‚Äù ‚Äì Take this out  
    * Book is about her business  
    * Agent‚Äôs money should come from the book. Not the other parts of the book  
    * Best kind of re-writing: The less intrusive it seems upon mark-up, the more likely you are to get away with it  
      * Deleting words is key for this

4. Alice‚Äôs draft agreement includes the following language about terminating the agreement:  
     
   *Agent agrees to use such reasonable efforts on the Author‚Äôs behalf as Agent deems appropriate to cause the disposition of the Rights.  However, Agent makes no representation that Agent will in fact effect such disposition.  If within 12 months of execution of this Agreement Agent has failed to obtain a bona fide offer for the disposition of one of the Rights to the Property, acceptable to the Author, the Author may terminate this Agreement by written notice to Agent upon written receipt by Agent subject to the Continuation period pursuant to 2(b).*  

   
*If the Author receives a proposal with respect to the Rights during the Term of this Agreement, the Author will promptly refer such proposal to Agent.  All contracts and other arrangements negotiated by Agent on the Author‚Äôs behalf shall be subject to the Author‚Äôs approval and shall be in the Author‚Äôs name or in such other name as designated by the Author in writing.  Notwithstanding anything herein contained to the contrary, the Author reserves all rights to himself to accept or reject any offer for the disposition of any Rights to the Property and the Agent has no right or authority to bind, or otherwise create an obligation for, the Author or his Rights or the Property.*  

*The Author shall have no obligation to Agent, at any time with regard to any other existing or future literary projects or any other project the Author may wish to undertake and exploit, by themselves or through any other person, firm or entity acting as the Author‚Äôs agent except for the obligations set forth in this Agreement.  Agent has the authority to represent to others that she is the Author‚Äôs Agent.   Irrespective of whether or not Author hires a subsequent agent, Agent shall be the agent on the next book published if the publisher (‚ÄúPublisher‚Äù) of the Property publishes the Author‚Äôs next book.*

1. Why is it important for the agreement not to be indefinite? What information or incentive problems does this solve?  
- Important for services to be fixed so that obligations are clear/creates certainty

- **Time limit protects the author (Incentive problem)**  
  * **The royalty creates an incentive to get the books sold. Part of the effort to align incentives is contingent compensation but it doesn‚Äôt work if indefinite because then agent could just say, I‚Äôll turn to this when I can**  
- **Time limit also protects the agent (Asymmetric information)**  
  * **If the author is bad, the agent wants to walk away**  
- **Time limit can serve both of their interests**

  2. Why is it important for the agreement to have a stated term, so that Barbara cannot terminate it at will? What information or incentive problems does this solve? What information or incentive problems does this term create?  
- Contractual provisions have 2 rationales:  
  * 1\) One that is true of ordinary business events  
  * 2\) But there is also an anti-abuse, anti-exploitation element to this

- **Not terminable at will protects both parties**
