  * **Ordinary Business Events (Protect Agent): You need to give agent a runway to see the fruits of her labor and get the book to be successful. Not canceled at will prevents author from abandoning her after she has worked hard on getting the book sold but it is too early for it to sell a lot**  
  * **Ordinary Business Events (Protect Author): Need to give the author an out if after a certain time agent is not doing a good job**  
  * **Anti-Abuse/Anti-Exploitation (Protect Agent): If author could terminate at-will, she could glean everything she wanted out of Alice and get the publishing house contacts and then cancel agreement and undercut her**  
    * If she could just terminate at-will, author could just get the contacts from agent and not include agent

  3. The contract provides that even after the agreement is terminated, Alice still earns a commission on gross proceeds from a deal ‚Äúwithin six (6) months after the end of the Term (‚ÄúContinuation Period‚Äù) with any purchaser who evidenced during the Term an indication of interest in negotiating or executing such an Agreement.  Upon such termination and upon Author‚Äôs request, Agent will furnish to you a list of publishers and other parties to whom she submitted the materials related to the Property (or the Property itself). If during the Continuation Period, the Author sells or transfers any of the Rights in the Book to a person or company on this list, Agent shall be entitled to her full commission under this Agreement with respect to the Property.‚Äù  
     1. Is it reasonable for Barbara to agree to this?  
- **Protects Agent:** Yes. This prevents the scenario where the publishing house conversation is happening at the end of the K term and author lets it drag out so that agent‚Äôs K expires and author can go directly to the publishing house, undercutting the agent

  2. Why should Alice have to provide the list to Barbara? In what context is she likely to use this list?  
- **Protection against abuse:** The extension period can protect agent from author (above). But **author needs to be protected from agent too** in this extension provision  
  * **The list shows author that agent was actually involved and can‚Äôt just claim it was her work after the fact**  
- **Incentive problems going both ways here**

  4. Barbara also asks you the following question: ‚ÄúI see that Alice is automatically my agent on my second book, if I publish it with the publisher who published my first book. Is it reasonable for Alice to ask for that?‚Äù What is your answer?  
- Reasonable because she would only have this connection because of Alice

  5. Assuming Barbara is generally willing to agree to the term discussed in ‚Äúd,‚Äù should Barbara ask for any limits on Alice‚Äôs claim automatically to be the agent on the second book? (First hint: assume Alice is seventy-five years old.) (Second hint: assume that Barbara is very demanding and at times can come across as abrasive, a reality that reinforces the pattern, noted above, that she does not usually have repeat clients.)  
- 1\) Alice‚Äôs estate should be compensated even if Alice is no longer around because there is no work to place the next book with the same publisher  
  * Author may not accept this  
- 2\) If agent is unwilling to work with you, they shouldn‚Äôt be paid even if you place the book at the same publisher


5. The contract uses the following (complicated) clause to define Alice‚Äôs commission. Barbara has a number of questions about it:  
     
   *Compensation.  In full consideration of Agent‚Äôs services hereunder, the Author agree to pay Agent a commission equal to Fifteen Percent (15%) of all ‚ÄúGross Proceeds‚Äù derived from the exploitation of the Rights.*   
   *Moreover, a minimum fee of Six Thousand  Dollars ($6000) (‚ÄúMinimum Payment‚Äù) is owed to Agent. Three Thousand ($3000) is due upon signing this Agreement and Three Thousand is due when  the advance accepted by the Author is equal to or less than Twenty-Five Thousand Dollars ($25,000).  By way of example: if the advance is Twenty-Five Thousand Dollars ($25,000) or less, the second payment of $3000 shall be due to the Agent within ten days of accepting publisher‚Äôs offer.*   
     
   *If the advance is greater than Twenty-Five Thousand Dollars ($25,000), the second payment is not owed and the $3000 already paid shall be deducted from the Agent‚Äôs share paid by the Publisher.*   
     
   *If the Author does not accept an offer or after the end of one year no contract is available, then Agent retains the Three Thousand Dollars ($3000). The Minimum Payment shall be in addition to the commission due to Agent in accordance with this section as stated above for Fifteen (15%) of Gross Proceeds.‚Äù* 

   1. A minimum fee is not customary for literary agents. Why do you think that is the case?  
- **2 Ways the agent gets paid:**  
  * **Usual agent compensation \= 15%**  
    * **If advance is more than $25K ü°™ Compensation \= 15% (Agent does not take additional $3K and deducts initial $3K from their 15% compensation)**  
    * **If advance is equal or less than $25K ü°™ Compensation \= $6K \+ 15% (Agent will already have taken $3K, gets an additional $3K in 10 days if there is a deal, and gets 15%) (If there is no deal, agent gets $3K only ‚Äì no additional $3K if no deal and no 15%)**

- **No minimum fee because you want to align incentives. This structure changes the incentives in the face of an information problem (Agent could say she is going to do a great job and mean it or she might do nothing for you)**  
  * **Like VC: You could write a K that specifies everything agent must do. But it is more efficient to share the profits instead of monitoring each other**

  2. When Barbara asks Alice to remove the minimum fee, Alice declines to do so. What explanation do you think Alice is likely to give? (Hint: One of her specialties is to represent first-time authors.)  
- First-time authors have uncertainty  
  * If agent‚Äôs compensation is tied to proceeds and there aren‚Äôt any, then she is out of luck  
  * If there was no minimum fee, agents would only take on accomplished authors to get the royalty

  3. In analyzing whether she is comfortable with the minimum fee, what question should Barbara ask Alice? What information would be relevant?  
- Concern: Agent is signaling to author that the book might not sell ‚Äì if she knew it would sell, she wouldn‚Äôt need fixed payment  
- So then author might say why are you representing me if you don‚Äôt believe in me?  
  * Agent can respond that she just needed that added insurance  
  * But is also could be that agent is going to take the minimum fee and not work hard  
  * It is hard for author to figure out which of these is the truth

  4. If Alice insists on keeping the minimum fee in the contract, what signal, if any, does this send to Barbara? Is this new information to Barbara?  
- Agent is signaling that she is not that confident in author‚Äôs work by insisting on minimum fee which is new information to author

  5. If Barbara is hesitant about agreeing to pay a minimum fee, what signal, if any, does this send to Alice? Is this new information to Alice?  
- By being hesitant to pay minimum fee, author is signaling that she is not that confident that her own work will sell. This is new information to agent  
  * The inclusion of the minimum fee is inconsistent with the way the market is supposed to work. Ideally, each party is so confident that they are willing to work purely off of sharing the profits (contingency)  
    * **This minimum fee might chill the interest of the parties to deal with each other**  
  * Agent‚Äôs lack of confidence is even more concerning because she understands the industry better

  6. Recall that Alice has the right automatically to be the agent on Barbara‚Äôs second book in certain circumstances. In light of this right, what change should Barbara request in the above paragraph on Alice‚Äôs commission?  
- Author should request no minimum fee if Alice continues to be her agent on the second book because by then she has proven herself and earned agent‚Äôs trust that the book will be successful

  7. Assume that Barbara‚Äôs advance for the book (the cash payment she receives from the publisher upon signing the contract) is $25,000. What is Alice‚Äôs commission?  
- $6K in minimum fee (two advances) \+ 15%($25K) \= $9,750  
  * (Has to be more than $25K to get 15% alone)

  8. Assume that the advance is much higher‚Äì$50,000. What is Alice‚Äôs commission?  
- The commission does not double because there is no minimum fee anymore  
  * All Alice gets is 15%($50K) \= $7,500

  9. Compare the answers in ‚Äúg‚Äù and ‚Äúh.‚Äù What problem do you see? What is the source of this problem?  
- $25K advance \= $9,750 fee to agent  
- $50K advance \= $7,500 fee to agent  
  * **Incentive Problem: This is a problem because the agent is better off getting you a lower advance**

  10. What changes in the formula could fix the problem identified in ‚Äúi‚Äù? Please suggest a couple of options. What other problems (if any) do these options create?  
- **2 Problems:**  
  * **1\) Inclusion of the fixed fee**  
  * **2\) The phasing out of the fixed fee**  
    * **She stops getting the $6K is a reward for keeping the advance low**

- **Fixing the incentive problem:**  
  * **15 percent contingent payment with no minimum fee**  
    * But Alice might require the fix fee  
  * **$6K \+ 15% royalty all the way up**  
    * Too big an advance but no incentive problem (as the agent got a bigger book deal, they keep more so they are incentivized to get you more)  
  * **$3K \+ 20% royalty all the way up**  
    * Similar to the problems that the tax system faces but less drastic because minimum fee is smaller

    

6. Barbara hires Alice, and Alice sends the book proposal to a number of publishers. Erin Editor from Packaging Press calls the next day to say that she is interested, but with a caveat. ‚ÄúPublishing a book likes this costs money,‚Äù Erin says. ‚ÄúThe up front cost is about $100,000.‚Äù Barbara is a first time author, so there is a lot of uncertainty about whether her book will sell, and it‚Äôs a book for a targeted professional audience. ‚ÄúBarbara will have to commit to buy 5,000 copies of the book,‚Äù Erin says, ‚Äúand we will charge her $12 per copy.‚Äù   
   1. As Erin said, there is always a risk that the book won‚Äôt sell. Under Erin‚Äôs proposal, how is that risk allocated? Who loses money?  
- $100K risk is allocated between author and publisher  
  * Author bears: 60% (Paid $60K \- $12(5K copies))  
    * Shifts risk to author that otherwise publisher might bear  
  * Publisher bears: 40% (Got $60K and bears $40K)

  2. Alice asks Erin how Barbara can make money on the book. ‚ÄúShe can then resell them to her clients at whatever price she wants,‚Äù Erin says. Barbara calls to ask you for business advice about this proposal. In deciding whether to do this, what issues should she consider?  
- Market capacity potential and her capacity to take advantage of it  
  * Market Capacity: They made her cover a portion of the cost but if she can resell for $24, she is getting a 50% discount (she needs to know what her discount is going to be)  
    * They made her cover a portion of the cost but she didn‚Äôt pay market price  
  * Capacity to take advantage of it: Publisher has better capacity to sell book because of expertise, sales relationships, etc. but they are outsourcing some of this to author  
    * She has the job of selling 5K copies. Can she do that by herself?

  3. Alice asks Erin whether Packaging Press will also try to sell the book. ‚ÄúYes,‚Äù Erin says. ‚ÄúWe will sell it on Amazon.‚Äù This is both good and bad for Barbara. Why?  
- This is good because Amazon will help her reach more buyers  
- This is bad because publisher on Amazon competes with author if she needs to resell the books

- The publisher and the author are competing to sell 5K copies  
  * If the audience is 100K this isn‚Äôt a problem  
  * If the audience is 5K this is a problem

  4. Barbara asks how Barbara would be compensated for books that Packaging Press sells on Amazon. ‚ÄúIt‚Äôs the usual deal in the industry,‚Äù Erin says. ‚ÄúBarbara will get a 10% royalty for the first 5,000 copies sold, a 12% royalty for the next 5,000 copies sold, and a 15% royalty for the rest of the books.‚Äù Is Barbara likely to make more on the books she sells herself or on the books Packaging sells on Amazon?  
- Author can earn more by selling the book herself, but it is riskier and more labor intensive  
  * 10% royalty \= $2.40 on a $24 book  
  * As long as she sells her own copies for more than $14.40 ($12 book \+ $2.40 royalty) she will make more on the copies she sells herself  
    * But this is also riskier because it is unclear that she is able to do that

  5. To evaluate the royalties in part ‚Äúd,‚Äù what other information do you need? (Hint: there are ‚Äúlist‚Äù royalties and ‚Äúnet sale‚Äù royalties and one is better than the other.)  
- Author needs to know how the royalty is calculated  
  * **List Royalty \= Percentage of the book‚Äôs retail price**  
    * $24 list price \= $2.40 royalty  
    * This is better for author  
  * **Net Sale Royalty \= Based on the amount the publisher receives after the retailers discounts**  
    * The retailer will discount the book to get more sold

7. The next day, Alice gets a call from Ed Editor, who works at Publisher Press (‚ÄúPP‚Äù). Ed also is interested in publishing the book, so he sends Alice a draft contract (which was assigned as reading) and Alice shares it with Barbara.   
   1. In this deal, who bears the risk that the book won‚Äôt sell well?  
- The publisher bears the risk because the publisher shares 50% of **profit**  
  * **Publisher bears the risk that the expenses do not get recovered (the expenses get deducted from revenue and author gets 50% of profit)**  
- This is different from the last deal where the publisher forced the author to cover 60% of costs by purchasing books

  2. In Ed‚Äôs draft contract, when (if ever) does Barbara have to buy books? (Hint: Look at section 8.) If it is a priority to Barbara not to have to buy books, how concerned should she be about the offer from PP? To what extent (if at all) does Barbara control whether she has to buy books?  
- Author only has to buy books in the event that there is excess production in which case she pays a heavily discounted price  
  * Mostly she doesn‚Äôt have to buy books unlike the other deal where author buys books to bear some of the cost  
  * She might be able to influence this by having them print fewer books

  3. In PP‚Äôs offer, how is Barbara compensated? How is this different from Erin‚Äôs offer? Which is better? What other information do you want in order to compare them?  
- In Erin‚Äôs offer, author has to sell books herself but keeps 100% of her profit on these copies  
- In Ed‚Äôs offer, publisher sells all the books but author keeps 50% of profits

- Want to know the price at which author is going to sell her books  
  * Is the $12 in Erin‚Äôs offer really a discounted price? Is she willing to bear this downside risk? How likely is it that she will profit from selling these?  
- In the second deal, she bears no risk with potential for 50% of profits  
  * She can‚Äôt lose money like she can in the first deal

  4. ‚ÄúRemaindering‚Äù a book means that the publisher has too much stock of a book, so they sell it off at a very low price. There are companies who buy and distribute remainders. Alice Agent objects to the way remainder sales are structured in PP‚Äôs draft contract. What do you think is her concern? What argument should she make to change it? (Hint‚Äìhow are these sales treated differently than other sales?)  What argument do you think PP will make? (Hint‚Äìit has to do with incentives.)  
- Remainder sales are not subject to profit participation which is bad for author  
  * PP will argue that it needs to get rid of other copies and recoup its expenses  
  * PP will also argue that they are remaindering the books because it is more expensive to store them than to just get rid of them. **Author won‚Äôt get profits from these so she is incentivized to not get remaindered and will help sell the books**  
    * Author Counter: I don‚Äôt control when you remainder ‚Äì this is about your storage costs. Same 50% should most likely apply regardless of how the books are used

8. Since you are a lawyer, Barbara calls you with some questions about PP‚Äôs proposed contract.   
   1. Barbara is very meticulous about language. She thinks carefully about every word, and she writes and rewrites and rewrites again in order to get it just right. Who has the final say about the language under this contract? What change, if any, might Barbara want to make in the contract? (2e ‚Äì Editing)  
- Publisher submits edits to the author for approval  
- **Exceptions: 2 Potential Scenarios where Barbara doesn‚Äôt have the final say:**  
  * **1\) She says no and they claim she is being unreasonable**  
  * **2\) Non-habitual: They messed up and forgot to share the edits (if they forget rarely it‚Äôs okay)**  
    * **Author will push back on this**

  2. Barbara wants an index. Under the proposed contract, will PP provide one?  
- 1c ‚Äì Parties agree that no index will be included in the book

  3. Barbara wants an e-book version made. Is PP committing to do one? If not, can Barbara arrange for this separately with another publisher? What changes, if any, do you think should be made to this provision? (Hint: see Secton 3.)  
- Publisher has the option to make an e-book. They are given the right to make the e-book version but do not have to  
- **3(a)(iii): If the publisher does not exploit this right within one year, author may find another publisher to do it**  
  * **But this is a bad situation for author because you want the e-book to come out at the same time as the regular book**

  4. Barbara has a temper. She is blunt and sometimes says things that offend people. She‚Äôs proud of the fact that she‚Äôs not ‚Äúpolitically correct.‚Äù As her lawyer, what warning do you need to give her about the draft contract?  
- **Morality Provision (iv):** Marketability of the author‚Äôs image is negatively affected  
  * She loses the book deal if she blows up in the wrong way at the wrong time

  5. Barbara plans to urge consulting clients to buy the book in bulk. She‚Äôd like for them to get a discount when they do. What (if anything) does the contract say about that? What changes, if any, do you think she should request?  
- **7(c): Bulk purchases by third parties**  
  * Discounts are on a case-by-case basis  
  * **You could negotiate:**  
    * **1\) That bulk sales should also be subject to discount scheme**  
    * **2\) To clarify what the discount is**  
      * **Treating third party bulk purchases differently than the author who has a specified discount**  
      * **Even if it is going to be a case-by-case basis, she would want some parameters (if purchaser orders X number of copies, they get Y percent discount)**

  6. Barbara is willing to travel to try to drum up book sales, giving book talks, signing books, etc. If PP arranges this sort of event, who will bear Barbara‚Äôs travel costs?  
- 9: If publisher arranges the event, publisher pays for costs, but otherwise they won‚Äôt  
  * Publisher may think that this is good for them because they can decide if an event if worth the money

- Also, ‚Äúcovering the costs‚Äù might include the expenses that impact profits which she gets paid off of  
  * The travel might count as expenses, part of which she bears in taking the profits (half the cost actually comes from her)

  7. Assume that Barbara hopes to write a sequel, ‚ÄúTurning it Around Again.‚Äù Is she obligated to publish it with PP?  
- **3(c): Publisher has option to make an offer on a sequel but no obligation to publish sequel (ROFR)**  
  * ‚ÄúTurning It Around Again‚Äù will probably be covered by this because it seems like the same subject matter  
- **But if she doesn‚Äôt want to work with this publisher again, she doesn‚Äôt have to. The publisher‚Äôs only right is to make an offer**  
  * **BUT the offer needs to go to PP first and then wait 30 days to field other offers**  
  * **She cannot skip this step and offer to other publishers first**

  8. If Barbara thinks the payments she is getting seem low, what rights would she have to verify that they are correct? Keep in mind that Barbara is a ‚Äúnumbers person,‚Äù who is very comfortable checking this sort of information on her own. Since you know this about her, what change would you suggest in the contract?  
- **K requires her to send independent CPA to look at books at her cost**  
- **She wants to change the language to say: Author, agent, or independent CPA can inspect**

  9. Barbara lives in NY. If she has a dispute with Publishing Press, and needs a lawyer‚Äôs help, what disadvantage does she face?  
- Any disputes on the K must be resolved in OH (probably inconvenient)  
- They are unlikely to agree to changing the governing laws of the state they do business in

9. Barbara needs to decide between Erin and Ed‚Äôs offer. She knows that Erin wants an answer and, if it‚Äôs ‚Äúno,‚Äù she misses that opportunity.   
   1. If she otherwise prefers PP, what concern might she have about whether a deal with PP actually is final? Once PP signs their draft contract, are they committing to publish her book?  
- **Part 4: They are not obligated to publish the book**  
  * **If the book creates legal problems ü°™ That is reasonable**  
  * **‚ÄúPublisher shall not be obligated to publish the work‚Äù ü°™ That is unreasonable**

  2. Continuing with the issue in part ‚Äúa‚Äù--that is, PP‚Äôs obligation to publish the book‚Äìwhy do you think PP has structured the contract in this way? What change should Barbara try to get? What argument should she make to justify it?  
- **What is publisher‚Äôs justification for this?:**  
  * **If they become insolvent**  
    * **Author won‚Äôt agree to this. They are making a commitment**  
  * **If the book comes back very different, then we want to walk away**

- **Author: Okay, so add ‚Äúif the manuscript materially diverges from the proposal, then publisher has no obligation to publish.‚Äù**  
  * There are a lot of situations where you need to read deeply into the K to understand what you are getting and if the things you thought you were getting are guaranteed

- **Aspects to Being a Lawyer:**  
  * 1\) Big picture concept of what deal should be  
  * 2\) Negotiate your way to where the deal should be and getting people on board  
  * 3\) Operationalize concept. Does the paperwork match what you are supposed to get?  
    * This goes for junior and senior lawyers  
    * All that matters are the words that you sign

# 17: Film (Vertical Integration Anti-competitive?)

- Same problems and solutions arise in different transactional settings

- Different possible explanations for vertical integration:  
  * Information/Incentive problems  
  * Anti-competitive (government position)

- SC says that this is anti-competitive behavior

- Many commentators since have argued that this was not really anticompetitive  
  * Really, these were solutions for information and incentive problems

- Can vertical integration be anti-competitive?  
  * Yes, but not necessarily. It could also be formed to solve other problems

- 3 stages all owned by one studio rejected by SC. Forced sale of exhibition  
- Didn‚Äôt ask the proper questions but it is possible that leverage in theater segment of the industry through vertical integration allowed for market power in other areas of the industry (market forfeiture)  
  * If you own all movie theaters that could give you preferred treatment in the production phase because you can get only your movies into theaters while disfavoring competitors‚Äô movies in production phase  
- Vertical integration being anti-competitive is possible but not as readily evident as horizontal integration  
  * This theory (Vertical integration being anti-competitive) assumes market concentration in at least one phase of the industry (which could be accomplished through horizontal integration)

1. A trust dominated the motion picture industry in its early years until about 1915\. (The trust was dissolved in 1917.)   
   1. Why was it sometimes called the ‚ÄúEdison trust‚Äù?  
- They held Edison‚Äôs invention patents for the intellectual property for making/showing movies  
  * Invented the movie projector

  2. What was the source of the trust‚Äôs leverage? What was the key source of value in the industry in those early years?  
- It controlled all aspects of the movie-making supply chain

  3. The trust prohibited the identification of actors and actresses in those early years. Why?  
- So that they could not be identified, become famous, and demand higher salaries

- The value-add of a movie was the big screen technology (not the plot or cast like today)

  4. What other constraint did it impose on movies being made at the time? Why? Was this assessment about the potential audience for motion pictures more plausible then than it is now?  
- It limited the length of films to one and two reels (10 to 20 minutes) because movie audiences were believed incapable of enjoying longer entertainment  
- This may have been more plausible at the time because movies were more boring. They were black and white, no sound

  5. The trust was based in New York and on the east coast of the U.S. Where is the film industry in the U.S. located? Is there any causal connection here?  
- Hollywood is the current film industry location. Actors and filmmakers left NY to go to Hollywood to escape some of the Trust‚Äôs dominant east coast influence

2. Three Phases in Producing a Motion Picture  
   1. What is ‚Äúproduction‚Äù?  
- Production \= The act of making the film

  2. What is ‚Äúdistribution‚Äù?  
- Distribution \= Studio selling film to theaters

  3. What is ‚Äúexhibition‚Äù?  
- Exhibition \= Theaters showing the movie

3. The ‚ÄúStudio Era‚Äù  
   1. Between 1915 and 1948, movies were made in what is called the ‚Äústudio era.‚Äù What was its defining feature?  
- This era was dominated by the same company who controlled the entire industry from production to exhibition

  2. What event marked the end of the studio era?  
- *Paramount* case which broke up the fully vertically integrated company. Court said that studios could no longer own the exhibition phase too

  3. What is ‚Äúvertical integration‚Äù?  
- Vertical Integration \= Different phases of production are owned by the same company  
  * The studios controlled all aspects of the supply chain from production to exhibition  
  * But studios still competed with each other

- Horizontal Integration \= Competitors merging (Companies at the same stage of production joining forces)

4. *Paramount*

   Note: In arguing that the moving picture industry violated the antitrust laws, the government invoked both its ownership structure and some of its practices. This class focuses on the ownership structure; the next unit focuses on the other unique practices.

   1. There are three groups of defendants. How does the first group (the only one charged on this issue of the case) differ from the other two?  
- The first group of defendants are companies that produce motion pictures, distribute films, **and own/control theaters (full integration)**  
  * The owning/controlling of theaters separates this group of defendants from the others

  2. What did the government allege about this group of defendants?  
- The government said that the first group of defendants conspired to and did restrain and monopolize interstate trade in the exhibition of motion pictures in most of the larger cities of the country because of the combination of owning each stage of movie industry. This violated the Sherman Act regulating antitrust laws

  3. According to the district court, did any of the defendants individually wield enough market power to have a national monopoly? Was this finding important to the Supreme Court (‚Äúthe Court‚Äù)?  
- District Court: No national monopoly  
- SC: Doesn‚Äôt matter if there is no national monopoly  
  * ‚ÄúIt is, therefore, not enough in determining the need for divestiture to conclude with the District Court that none of the defendants was organized or has been maintained for the purpose of achieving a "national monopoly," nor that the five majors through their present theatre holdings "alone" do not and cannot collectively or individually have a monopoly of exhibition.‚Äù

  4. What percentage of all theaters in the U.S. did the defendants control? Does that seem like enough to dictate prices and other terms?  
- They owned 17 percent of the theaters but held a significant amount of the theater revenues  
  * 17 percent is probably not enough to dictate prices and other terms

  5. The Court observes that the defendants had more market power in a subset of the exhibition market. Which subset was that? Did the Court consider this important?  
- However, even though they only controlled 17 percent of the total market, they had more influence over first-run theaters. They had a bigger presence over these and over major cities  
  * In first-run theaters and major cities, the studios controlled much more

- There was still competition in first-run theaters. You can wait until the movie gets to a non-first-run theater and buy a cheaper ticket

  6. Is there competition within this subset of theaters?  
- Yes. There were multiple studios competing with each other and they were competing even within the first-run theater subset  
  * There wasn‚Äôt a lot of fact-finding in this opinion

  7. Is vertical integration illegal per se? What is the Court‚Äôs test for determining when it violates the antitrust laws?  
- **Vertical integration is not per se illegal**  
- The Court‚Äôs test is: In the opinion of the majority the legality of vertical integration under the Sherman Act turns on (1) the purpose or intent with which it was conceived, or (2) the power it creates and the attendant purpose or intent.  
  * **1\) Were the companies trying to gain control**  
  * **2\) Were the companies able to do that**

  8. What kind of evidence does the Court invoke? Is the Court reviewing evidence of *the effects* of the defendants‚Äô market power?  
- **The Court only talks about the percentage of ownership (no concrete evidence) ‚Äì the collective percentage of all 5\. They don‚Äôt concentrate at all on an individual company‚Äôs stronghold**  
- **They make no effort to show that the 17 percent stronghold translated to impact on prices or quantities**

  9. Had the district court held that the defendants had to sell their theaters? Did the Supreme Court affirm the district court on this issue?  
- No, the district court did not talk at all about D selling theaters  
- The Supreme Court made this theory up instead of remanding

  10. The Court says that courts should consider the defendants‚Äô intent (what they meant to do), and it also says that monopoly power can be illegal ‚Äúthough it remains unexercised.‚Äù Is there any tension between these two assertions?  
- **The tension between these two statements is that it is hard to figure out what D‚Äôs intent is without any evidence of how they acted**  
  * **This decision of concluding that vertical integration is anti-competitive transformed the industry**

  11. Would you say that the Court‚Äôs analysis is economically sophisticated? For example, how does it compare with Andrew Hanssen‚Äôs analysis in the article assigned for today?  
- Unsophisticated

  12. The U.S. government has a track record of bringing antitrust actions against companies that are on the verge of confronting a formidable new competitor (e.g., IBM and Microsoft). What new entertainment platform was emerging in the late 1940s in the U.S.?  
- Televisions. Rare in 1940‚Äôs to have TV but popular in 1950‚Äôs  
  * Government was oblivious to the fact that there was a new competitor on the rise

5. Can Vertical Integration Be Anti-competitive?  
   1. Why might it be in the interest of movie producers to limit the number of movies that are made?  
- Lowering the supply, increases the demand  
  * The most straightforward way to limit the amount of movies is to do horizontal integration ‚Äì this definitely can be anti-competitive (one company buys its competitors and can control price and quantity through dominating one phase of production)  
  * But it is not as obvious that vertical integration is anti-competitive

  2. Assume that the profit-maximizing number of new movies released each year is 250, that there are five movie production companies, and that you are the head of one of them. Assume also (counterfactually) that the law does not prevent you from striking a deal with the other four CEOs, so that each of you commit to make only 50 movies each year, keeping the total at 250\. If the other four CEOs honor the agreement, is it better for you also to honor it? If you have the ability to ‚Äúcheat‚Äù and secretly make and sell another five movies, would it be in your interest to do so?  
- Just like how one company that controls the market could set quantity and price, a small enough number of companies can set quantity and price if they collude with each other ‚Äì both are illegal

- It is not better to honor the agreement because it would be in one company‚Äôs interest to cheat and release the additional movies if no one else cheats  
  * The revenue that the cheater gets will offset the decrease in price from more movies in the market  
  * **If each party has an incentive to cheat, the agreement is unstable**  
    * **Every party will see that if they cheat, others can, and that will ruin the agreement**  
  * It is better to honor the agreement because: If the other CEOs find out that you did not honor the agreement, they may cheat as well

  3. Given your answer to ‚Äúb,‚Äù how easy or hard is it to strike an agreement limiting supply? How important is the ability to monitor whether everyone is keeping the agreement? How important is the ability to enforce the agreement?  
- Hard to agree to limit supply  
- Hard to monitor the agreement but very important  
- Hard to enforce the agreement but very important

- OPEC runs a cartel: They set oil prices for each company  
  * Everyone cheats so OPEC set a level a little lower than they would otherwise ‚Äì it bakes cheating in  
    * **Very hard to police a cartel because it is hard to monitor and enforce against the cheater**

  4. If each studio owns some movie theaters and no one else does‚Äìso the five studios control all the movie theaters in your market‚Äìdoes that make it easier or harder for the five of you to monitor and enforce an agreement limiting the number of movies that are made?  
- Easier. **Enforcement and monitoring challenge of cartel**  
  * **Vertical integration could make it easier to exercise monopoly power in one part of the chain**  
  * **Studios could have limited number of movies that they made, but doing that is very hard because they all would have cheated (cartels are hard to monitor)**  
    * **But if each studio could only show movies through the theaters that it owned, then owning theaters became an enforcement mechanism for limiting the number of movies**  
      * **This is how vertical integration might have been anti-competitive**

  5. True or false: **By owning movie theaters, studios have the ability to prevent new studios (who don‚Äôt own theaters) from displaying their movies. This control over exhibition can give them added market power in production and distribution**.  
- True.  
  * **Control of one part of the production chain (of the display of the movies), could have given the studios extra market power in other parts of the production cycle**

  6. Same facts as in ‚Äúb,‚Äù but there is a sixth ‚Äúindependent‚Äù studio, which does not own any theaters. It produces a movie that you think could be a big draw, which would fill your theaters if you agree to exhibit it. On the one hand, what incentive do you have to refuse to exhibit it? On the other hand, what incentive do you have to agree to exhibit it?  
- Incentive to not exhibit: Breaks cartel agreement  
- Incentive to exhibit: Big hit, revenue

- This is a coordination problem: Each of the partners in the cartel has incentive to limit everyone else‚Äôs production while they are incentivized to cheat and increase their own production

  7. Two prominent economists, Oliver Hart and Jean Tirole, wrote an article called ‚ÄúVertical Integration and Market Forfeiture‚Äù (which is not assigned for class). They say the following: ‚ÄúFew people would disagree that horizontal mergers have the potential to restrict output and raise consumer prices.‚Äù Do you know what a ‚Äúhorizontal merger‚Äù is? Why do they say that it has this potential?  
- Horizontal Merger \= Two or more companies at the same level of supply chain merge together  
  * Fewer competitors mean more market power, making it easier for the remaining companies to control price and quantity

  8. Hart and Tirole go on to say that there is less agreement about whether vertical mergers also have the potential to restrict output and raise consumer prices ‚Äúbecause the merger of an upstream and downstream firm, each of which controls, say, 10% of its market, does not change market shares: other firms continue to control 90% of each market.‚Äù Please explain this statement.  
- Vertical mergers do not change the number of competitors in the market. Vertical integration does not have the same effect to control price and quantity. There are still competitors competing at each stage of the market

  9. Hart & Tirole argue, though, that **vertical integration actually can help a firm with market power in one market (such as theaters) to leverage that power in another market (such as movie production).** **They say that the key to this leverage across markets, which is known as ‚Äúmarket forfeiture, is that integration allows the upstream and downstream firms, once they merge, to share profits more effectively. In contrast, if they remain independent, they could ‚Äúdivert money and misrepresent profits.‚Äù** Please explain this statement.  
- **If what you are doing is vertically integrating after you already control one market, you can use control in one market segment to exert control over and defeat market power in another**

- **The companies that own theaters are more powerful than the non-theater owning companies**  
  * **They can prevent the other companies from showing other company movies in their theaters**  
- **Counter: The non-theater owning companies can just buy a theater**

- **We had vertical integration in studios until SC struck it down as anti-competitive and prevented studios from owning theaters**  
  * **SC did not ask the right economically rigorous questions**  
    * **No effects, just assumptions based on presence in markets (that wasn‚Äôt that large)**

- **Evaluating SC decision:**  
  * **Wrong Decision: Not obvious that vertical integration actually creates anti-competitive behavior because you still have competition in each market**  
  * **Right Decision: This was an attempt to dominate the industry indirectly through the market forfeiture thesis (exerting pressure from the theater segment to constrain supply and dictate price)**

  10. During the studio era, actors were salaried employees at studios‚Äìmuch as auto workers are salaried employes at car companies and musicians are salaried employees at orchestras. Someone on salary at Warner would not be in movies made by Fox, and vice versa. After the old system ended, this practice ended with it. Actors were hired to do a particular movie, and they stopped working exclusively for a single studio. The pay of star talent increased significantly. Could the lower salaries under the studio system have been caused by the anticompetitive effects of vertical integration?  
- Yes. **Vertical integration could have suppressed wages because through control of exhibition, companies had a lot of market power. The studios used their market power in the exhibition stage to quietly collude with each other and suppress actors‚Äô wages, making them take smaller salaries than what they would eventually get after the studio system broke up**  
  * **You work with the talent you have and we will work with the talent we have instead of bidding/poach against each other and both pay more**  
  * **This is indirect evidence that the Court was right**

# 18: Film (Vertical Integration as Response to Information and Incentive Problems)

- Was Hollywood vertical integration anti-competitive? Or was it just an attempt to solve the information and incentive problems?  
  * Was not meant to suppress supply and drive up prices but just focus on issues that are inevitable

- If demand for movies is hard to predict, you need flexible negotiation between theater and distributor  
  * But the theater and production company have different incentives  
    * Theater: Cut the movie and move to next; Keep more revenue away from ticket price and quantity  
    * Production: Keep the movie and see if it attracts more people; Keep more revenue in ticket price and quantity  
  * When under common ownership, the differences in incentives become unimportant and the goal is to maximize overall profit without regard to which part is making the money

- Entertainment industry now relies on revenue sharing, incentive compensation

6. Demand for Specific Movies  
   1. **Sometimes a market practice exists not because (or, at least, not solely because) it gives the relevant firms market power; sometimes the practice also is efficient**. To explore whether this is the case with vertical integration, we need to dig into the economics of the industry. According to Arthur De Vany, what factors drive the demand for movies? What role does the dissemination of information play?  
- Part Imitation, Part Information  
  * Imitation: Number of people who saw it before; Level of revenue already earned  
  * Information: Word of mouth communication if the movie is good or bad

- **Early people who see the movie have a lot of influence over who else sees the movie (this is impacted by reviewers, weather, etc.)**  
  * **Makes it very difficult to know how a movie is going to do**  
    * **Pure uncertainty \= No one knows**  
    * Not asymmetric information where some people know more than others

  2. According to De Vany, what is the key moment that determines the success of a movie? Is it the opening? Or another moment?  
- Turning Point: Information from positive word of mouth and growing box office revenues confirm one another. All of the dynamics explode or wither  
  * About 4 weeks after its release

  3. According to Arthur De Vany, do the revenues from movies plot onto a standard ‚Äúbell curve‚Äù? Why or why not?  
- They do not plot on a bell curve because a few blockbuster movies dominate the market so box office revenues do not converge to an average  
- **This industry lives and dies based on a small number of movies that do extremely well**  
  * **Very skewed distribution**  
- **Lots of uncertainty about how movies are going to do and uncertainty as to which are the blockbusters**

  4. Does Devany think that when a movie is being made, its chances of success are a matter of uncertainty? Or asymmetric information? What is the difference?  
- Devany thinks that success is a matter of uncertainty for movies because they need the audience to tell them if the movie is going to be a hit  
- Asymmetric information is when in deal with two parties, one has more insight so they are in a better position to make a deal. With uncertainty, neither party knows

  5. How effective does De Vany think movie producers are in predicting the success of movies? Do you think De Vany‚Äôs view is universally shared?  
- He thinks they are not effective at all because of the uncertainty  
- This view is probably not universally shared. Some producers think they are very good

- What can you do through K‚Äôs to deal with controversy in advance or afterwards

  6. Is it controversial to say that predictions about a movie‚Äôs popularity *before* it is released can never be as accurate as evidence gathered *after it already has been released*?  
- This is not that controversial a statement. Once you have the data, you can measure how a movie is doing


7. Vertical Integration as a Response to Uncertainty and Asymmetric Information  
   1. According to Hanssen, why is it important to make commitments about exhibiting a movie before the movie has been released?  
- It is important to make commitments about exhibiting a movie before the movie has been released because the schedule for the movie must be established and you need the money upfront for the budget

- **Need to make plans**  
  * **It costs you money to make the movie so need preliminary commitments**  
  * **Want a schedule of how it is going to be distributed**

  2. According to Hanssen, what is the downside of making these commitments before the movie has been released?  
- The demand for the movie is uncertain

- **On one hand you need to make some prediction to make the movie and know that it will be shown in theaters, but all the parties are aware of the fact that the demand for the movie is uncertain**  
  * **Uncertainty could limit the industry: No one is confident in the movie, pre-commitments could mean no capital for production**

  3. To balance the tradeoff Hanssen identifies, one option is to include a mechanism in the contract to shorten or lengthen the running time of a move. Why is this hard to do?  
- Adjusting the K after it has been made and enforcing it is hard because the penalties for run length would have had to compensate the injured producer without effecting the producer‚Äôs incentives regarding the ex ante quality of its films 

- **Producer and exhibitor do not have the same interest. Producer will want another chance to keep the movie in theaters. Exhibitors will want to shorten the term right away**

  4. Who has better information about the quality of the movie‚Äìthe distributor or the theater owner? How reliable is this inside information?  
- Distributor   
- This is not that reliable because it is an uncertain industry

  5. Who has better information about the audience‚Äìthe distributor or the theater owner? How reliable is this inside information?  
- Theater owner  
- This is not that reliable because it is an uncertain industry

  6. Hanssen argues that vertical integration avoided the need for nuanced contracts that provide for resetting the length of a movie‚Äôs run by meeting three needs. What was the first?  
- Reduced the need for film-by-film haggling by functioning as a de facto side payment, allowing vertically integrated producers to share in the surplus generated by the early replacement of their unpopular films

- **Vertical integration wasn‚Äôt about market forfeiture or concentrating market power. Instead, it solved information (uncertainty) problems:**  
  * **1\) Changes the incentive for the theater owner if owned by the producer: Both have aligned interest in showing the movie for the right amount of time (because they are the same company, if they think movie was actually bad they cut it but if they think the movie just got bad press early and will recover they give it another shot). Incentives align to maximize revenue overall**  
  * **2\) Theater owner and production company share information about knowing the movie and audience better with each other if they are vertically integrated**  
  * **3\) Economize Drafting/Transaction Costs:** **Makes the K agreement drafting between the producer and exhibitor much easier or unnecessary because now they are part of the same company**

  7. What was the second?  
- Reduced information asymmetry between producers and exhibitors that could have led to inefficiently too many or too few replacements if there was only a contractual remedy

  8. What was the third?  
- Made the informal arrangement self-enforcing, allowing theaters to swap new movies with each other

  9. To determine whether vertical integration actually was facilitating adjustments in run times, Hanssen compared the frequency of these adjustments at vertically integrated theaters with adjustments at independently owned theaters. What did he find?  
- The frequency of adjustments in run time is lower in the independent theaters than in the studio-owned theaters (vertically integrated theaters)  
  * Independent theaters were adjusting run times less often because they couldn‚Äôt reap the three benefits above  
  * **More adjustments to how long a film runs in vertically integrated theaters than independent theaters because vertically integrated theaters felt more free to maximize combined revenue and cut movies because they were on both sides of the transaction**  
  * A lot more changes in run time in vertically integrated studios because they felt more free to maximize the combined revenue because they were on both side of the transaction

  10. Once vertical integration became illegal, what else could the industry do to base run rates for a film on ex post evidence (of whether the audience actually liked it), instead of ex ante predictions (of whether they will like it once they see it)? (Hint: Does a movie have to be released everywhere at the same time?)  
- **Pre-release model: Limited release in a few big city theaters allow for measurement of success in a handful of theaters so you can be more flexible in a few cities**  
  * **Instead of making predictions of how the movie will do, you can base it on audience reactions. Then you can make agreements with the other theaters based on information you have already.** You can do a better job of predicting how the movie will do when you have audience reactions  
    * **Vertical integration eliminated the need for this. Now, without vertical integration, movies need to do pre-releases**

  11.  True or False: **Vertical integration can be a response to uncertainty and asymmetric information.**  
- True.

  12. In what other industry did we see organizations serving as a substitute for contracts?  
- **VC and movie industry: Both have too much uncertainty to rely on K‚Äôs. Instead, organize ownership to create flexibility as you learn more information**  
    
8. Providing the public with the opportunity to see movies requires cooperation from multiple parties. **A central theme of this course is that cooperation requires trust**. Since the parties have to perform their obligations at different times, the other parties have **‚Äúhold up‚Äù opportunities, which take advantage of the fact that their counterparties have already performed**. In the long-run, this strategic behavior‚Äìand, indeed, the mere possibility of it‚Äìcan **undercut cooperation and reduce the supply of movies to the public**. De Vany highlights a few of these strategic opportunities.  
   1. Once the producers‚Äô costs in making a specific movie are ‚Äúsunk,‚Äù is it rational for them to accept unfavorable terms to exhibit it, so they can recover *at least some* of their costs?  
- Yes. Sunk costs cannot be recovered so if producer depends on theater to show movie, producers may need to accept cut-offs because it is better for them to get some revenue than none

  2. Could this unfavorable deal on a specific movie affect the producers‚Äô willingness to make other movies in the future (whose costs have not yet been incurred)?  
- Yes. If the producer makes a bad deal, they will be more cautious about the making future movies  
- There is nothing sunk about the costs of your next movie

  3. True or false: **Exhibitors have a short term interest in striking very favorable terms with producers but a long term interest in keeping producers in business**.  
- True. In the short-term exhibitors have an interest to hold-up, but in the long-term, they have a mutual interest to keep the industry going

  4. Once an exhibitor has agreed to show a film, does a distributor have a short-term incentive to rent the film to other exhibitors in the neighborhood, who might compete with the exhibitor? If a distributor takes this step, could this influence the exhibitor‚Äôs willingness to exhibit the next film?  
- Yes, distributor has a short-term incentive to rent the film to more exhibitors to make more money. This will hurt the distributor‚Äôs relationship with the initial exhibitor and potentially damage the long-term interest of the indusrty  
  * **Hold up opportunities on both sides**

  5. True or false: **Distributors have a short term interest in renting the film widely, but a long-term interest in being more selective to protect their relationships with exhibitors and earn licensing income on future films**.  
- True.

- **There are short-term adverse interests (hold-up opportunities) and long-term shared interests (keep industry going). The parties need a mechanism to develop trust to not worry about the short-term adverse interests and focus on the long-term shared interest**

  6. If exhibitors share revenue with producers, do the exhibitors recoup the full benefit of keeping their theater cleaner and more attractive than other theaters? If not, could this affect their incentives to invest in the quality of their theaters?  
- Exhibitors have to share the benefit of keeping the cleaner and more attractive theater with the producers in this situation because they share royalties for the films. Therefore, they have less incentive to invest in the quality of their theaters  
  * **Another situation where there is imperfect alignment of incentives because the producers want exhibitors to have nice theaters**

  7. Distributors want exhibitors to pay on time. What leverage do they have if they only produce movies occasionally? What if they have a regular stream of product? Does it help for the parties to deal with each other regularly?  
- Distributors that only produce movies occasionally have less leverage with exhibitors than they would if they produced a regular product stream because the exhibitors rely on them less for movies ‚Äì their product  
- Repeat dealing does help the distributors create leverage because the exhibitors rely on them more and builds a historical basis to compare box office reports

- **Repeat Relationships held address these problems because they create long term interests that can take priority over the short-term interest to not deliver**

  8. True or false: **While producers, distributors, and exhibitors all have an interest in cooperating‚Äìif only because they all depend on each other‚Äìthey have short-term incentives to take advantage of each other, and opportunities to do so arise because they perform their obligations at different times. As a result, creative contracting and organizational approaches are needed to maintain trust and cooperation.**  
- True.


9. Minimum Price   
   1. In the studio era, when a distributor licensed a film to an exhibitor, the license typically required the exhibitor to charge a minimum ticket price in showing the movie to the public. In *Paramount*, **the Court deemed this minimum ticket price an illegal price fixing conspiracy**: ‚Äú‚Äòa price structure is erected which regulates the licensees' ability to compete against one another in admission price,‚Äô" the Court said, quoting the district court. ‚ÄúThat consequence seems to us to be incontestable.‚Äù Please offer a defense of the Court‚Äôs conclusion. Why might this be the case, at least in some cases?  
- Horizontal price fixing (cartel) is monopolistic practice

- Without the fixed admission price, individual exhibitor‚Äôs gain revenue from rivals by cutting the admission price. This fails to maximize the distributor‚Äôs royalty revenue  
- Also it is hard for distributors to know if exhibitors are correctly representing their box office revenue to them so they could be getting cut out of their royalty if it wasn‚Äôt fixed at a minimum price

  2. Does it strengthen or weaken the Court‚Äôs argument if it is easy for new theaters to be opened? Does De Vany say that this sort of entry was easy or hard?  
- If it is easy for new theaters to be opened, this weakens the Court‚Äôs argument because it would create more competition and make it less likely for distributors to have a monopoly  
- Opening of new theaters was relatively easy

  3. As De Vany explains, there can be other reasons for setting a minimum ticket price. Assume that Danny Distributor is looking to distribute a film in City. He is considering either licensing it exclusively to Ellen Exhibitor, or licensing it both to her and to Eddie Exhibitor. Through the license, Danny will earn 50% of ticket revenue after the exhibitor has recovered its costs.

  4. Review: why is Danny likely to license the movie for a percentage of ticket sales, rather than a fixed price?  
- Because it is hard for Danny to predict if the movie will be a hit or a bomb  
  * **Revenue sharing:** **Approximate some of the benefit you would get from vertical integration (which you can‚Äôt do) between the parties**  
    * This aligns the incentives of the producer and exhibitor again to keep the movie in the theater for the right amount of time  
    * This can help deal with uncertainty if it works

  5. What is downside of licensing only to Ellen?  
- Less people can see the movie. Two theaters have more space than Ellen‚Äôs alone. You might not capture the full market

  6. What is the risk of licensing to both Ellen and Eddie? How would specifying a minimum ticket price help address this downside? Why should the minimum price be Eddie and Ellen‚Äôs customary price?  
- By licensing to both Ellen and Eddie, Danny risks that his revenues will be cut because both licensees will cut admission prices to compete with each other  
- Minimum ticket price guarantees that Danny will collect the revenue he wants from the licensees while allowing him to distribute the film to multiple theaters  
- The minimum price being the customary price gives theaters the option to raise the price for films they think are doing well

- **Production company wants to maximize the total tickets sold, but the exhibitors don‚Äôt care about the number of tickets that other theaters sell ‚Äì they are competing**  
  * **Not full alignment of incentives with exhibitors and distributor because the distributor doesn‚Äôt the exhibitors to compete. Distributor is worried that by cutting prices, exhibitors will compete with each other and drive down overall ticket revenue**  
    * **Schizer: This has an antitrust component to it (but it is the customary price for minimum price)**  
      * Also, this is assuming that there is a finite number of people who would come see the movie and a price cut would not bring in more people

- **Need revenue sharing to address the information asymmetry (uncertainty of movie)**  
  * **But the revenue sharing creates an incentive problem (price cuts)**  
  * **Setting minimum price at the customary price can help avoid the incentive problems (which could defeat the revenue sharing solution to the information problem)**


10. Assume that Danny grants an exclusive license to Ellen in exchange for 50% of ticket revenue after Ellen has recovered her costs. Ellen decides to offer tickets for $8 (instead of the usual $10) to any customer who spends at least $5 on snacks. How might this promotion disserve Danny‚Äôs interests? What condition could he specify to protect those interests?  
- Danny‚Äôs interests are disserved because consumers pay less in ticket revenue, which is shared with him, in exchange for paying more in concession revenue which is not shared with him  
- Danny should make sure that his royalty is based off of box office sales exclusive of concession revenue

- **Another divergence of incentives for exhibitor and distributor**  
  * Exhibitor only keeps half of ticket profit but keeps full profit on concessions. So she wants to sell concessions and shift revenue from tickets to concessions  
  * **Incentive Problem:** Distributor could look to get in on the concession revenue too, but exhibitor won‚Äôt agree because distributor didn‚Äôt produce the popcorn. Instead, **distributor should require a minimum ticket price to prevent discounts that divert money from tickets (distributor sharing in revenue) to concessions (distributor not sharing in revenue)**  
- **The minimum ticket price is not about anti-competitive behavior and overcharging the public. Instead, it is a way to prevent cheating (incentive problems) that come from revenue sharing which is there because of uncertainty/information problem**

11. Assume again that Danny grants an exclusive license to Ellen in exchange for 50% of ticket revenue after Ellen has recovered her costs. If Ellen is unscrupulous enough to report dishonest numbers as a way to keep more of the ticket revenue, what are the two numbers she might understate. How, if at all, does a minimum ticket price help to protect Danny?  
- Exhibitor will report less in quantity of tickets sold and less in price of tickets sold  
- **A minimum ticket price protects distributor by guaranteeing that at least one of these numbers, the price of tickets will not drop below a certain rate**

12. Clearance  
    1. What is ‚Äúclearance‚Äù?  
- **Clearance was a commitment from distributor to exhibitor to protect the play date from rival showings of the picture**  
  * If you have three theaters in a neighborhood, you don‚Äôt want them all showing the same movie

  2. Danny is negotiating a license with Ellen. Erik has just opened another theater down the street from Ellen, and he also wants a license. Danny offers Ellen the license to exhibit the movie first, and is willing to commit not to let Erik have a license during the first two weeks.  
     1. The Court said that clearances are not per se illegal. ‚ÄúA clearance when used to protect that interest of the exhibitor was reasonable,‚Äù the Court explained, referencing the district court‚Äôs conclusion, ‚Äúwhen not unduly extended as to area or duration.‚Äù Under this standard, is the clearance Danny proposed above acceptable?  
- This clearance is acceptable  
  * Protects exhibitor  
  * Only 2 weeks

- **Court did not think that clearance was pre se anti-competitive, but they were skeptical of clearances that were consistently given or given for a large number of movies at one time**

  2. What if Danny offers the same clearance for ten consecutive movies? The Court expressed suspicion about ‚Äúthe clearances . . . in vogue,‚Äù which ‚Äúhad . . . acquired a fixed  and uniform character and were made applicable to situations without regard to the special circumstances which are necessary to sustain them as reasonable restraints of trade.‚Äù Do you think the Court would view the multi-movie license to Ellen as violating the antitrust laws?  
- Yes, the Court would think that a clearance for ten movies violated antitrust laws because they have a fixed and uniform character and would go beyond what is necessary

  3. Please defend the multi-movie clearance provision. Are there reasons why the clearance in a particular place should remain the same for essentially every movie? Does De Vany think this is evidence of anti-competitive motives?  
- The clearance helped to maximize the distributor‚Äôs royalty because it ordered exhibitors as to when they could show the film. The largest and highest priced theaters would pay more for the first runs of the film  
- De Vany thinks that clearances allowed distributors and exhibitors to build trust and reputation with each other and were not anti-competitive  
  * **If there is a reason that the distributor decided on the exhibitor for one movie and liked them, there are reasons to believe that they would want to work with them again which are not anti-competitive**

    4. Although the same clearance might be specified ex ante for different movies, is the ‚Äúrun‚Äù for each certain to be the same? Why or why not? Should this be relevant to the antitrust analysis?  
- Even if the same clearance was specified, the run times changes because the run time depended on the success of the film with the audience  
- This is relevant to an antitrust analysis because it is evidence that the distributors were not actually engaging in anticompetitive behavior ‚Äì only on its face did it look like that. The variable run times allowed for new entrants  
  * **Getting the clearance wasn‚Äôt that great because it was regularly renegotiated**

    5. Ellen licenses one of Danny‚Äôs movies, *All the President‚Äôs Dogs*, for a three week ‚Äúfirst run.‚Äù The license also specifies that she will continue to show the movie for another two weeks if the average daily box office total in the third week exceeds $30,000. Ellen has another license to show another movie, *The Phantom Matrix*, starting in three weeks. If the first movie meets its ‚Äúholdover‚Äù target, how does Ellen work out her conflicting commitment to two different movies? What was the market practice in the Studio era?  
- During the Studio era, exhibitors showed the movies of one distributor because of the clearances, so exhibitors would keep showing the successful movie despite being obligated to show the new one, but both parties would want that  
  * **Vertical integration aligns incentives: The studios just want to maximize revenue for each movie, and this is what the theater cared about too (because they were owned by the studio). They don‚Äôt care which movie is being played as long as it is successful**  
    * **Make adjustments later**

- **Clearances: Distributor says to exhibitor we want to work with you on the next 10 movies. Justifications:**  
  * **Anti-competitive rationale: You are shutting out other theaters from access to these next movies**  
  * **Reputation: If distributor likes exhibitor for one, they will like them for the next 9**  
  * **Incentive Problem Solving: If distributor owns the theater, the re-negotiation of run times after the K is made is a lot easier**  
    * **They will be on the same page with how long to run each movie because they want to maximize revenue. There won‚Äôt be another commitment to bring in a different movie from a different studio that they have to abide by. If there is a big hit, let that ride and push the other movies out**

    6. We have discussed why clearance policies are good for Danny and Ellen. Why are they also good for the viewing public?  
- **Clearances provided more variety of films for the public to see.** This is because when a distributor limits the number of theaters exhibiting one of its films, there are more available theaters to show films. If they didn‚Äôt limit the distribution to a few exhibitors, the exhibitors would all want to show the same top hits  
    
13. Formula Deals and Franchises  
    1. Ellen owns fifteen theaters across the country, while Eddie and Erik each own only one theater. Danny offers Ellen a more favorable deal, giving her discretion to choose which of her theaters should display his films (a ‚Äúblanket deal‚Äù). Danny gives Ellen a license for all his movies for more than one season (a ‚Äúfranchise‚Äù). Ellen‚Äôs royalty is based on ticket sales nationally, not just in the specific theater showing the movie (a ‚Äúformula deal‚Äù). The Court invalidated these contractual terms as ‚Äúdevices for stifling competition and diverting the cream of the business to the large operators.‚Äù What do you think troubled the Court?  
- Danny wants to make a deal with Ellen because she has a better chance to make the movie successful with her multiple theaters than can reach more audiences. She can gather the information from the audience that tells if the movie is a success or not and adapt her exhibition accordingly. Danny should want to cut her these favorable terms because he will make more money this way too  
- Court is concerned that large theater chains reduce competition (bigness dictates prices)

  2. Are there other explanations for these contractual terms? Specifically, why is it advantageous both to Ellen and to Danny for Ellen to have discretion about which movie to show?  
- Yes. Both Ellen and Danny want Ellen to have discretion over which movie to show so that she will show the successes and cut the bombs

- **This is similar to vertical integration: Parties just care about all of the theaters making the maximum amount of money so the distributor gives the exhibitor a few movies (blanket deal) in order for exhibitor to best use her knowledge of the audiences and put them in the best-fit theaters so they make the most money on the whole**  
  * If we can‚Äôt have vertical integration, we can simulate it

  3. What is the advantage to Ellen and Danny in basing the royalty on national ticket sales, instead of sales in her theaters?  
- Basing the royalty on national sales allows the exhibitor to move the movie around in its different theaters to maximize its revenue

- **Minimum ticket price regulates the price aspect of the cheating the theater owner could do under revenue sharing**  
- **National number regulates the quantity aspect of the cheating the theater owner could do under revenue sharing**  
  * **Instead of having a theater specific count, they used a national number because it is harder to falsify this**


14. Blind Selling and Block Booking  
    9. Danny and Ellen sign a licensing agreement for a movie that has not been produced yet. As a result, Ellen obviously cannot see the movie before committing to exhibit it. The Court expressed concern that this was a sign that the market was not sufficiently competitive: ‚ÄúBy this practice a distributor could promise a picture of good quality or of a certain type which when produced might prove to be of poor quality or of another type \-- a competing distributor meanwhile being unable to market its product and in the end losing its outlets for future pictures.‚Äù Is there another reason why Danny wants to line up licenses for a movie before it is made?  
- **Blind selling allowed the exhibitor to get a reliable stream of films lined up and it ensured that the distributors had the money in advance to make the movies**

  10. Danny offers Ellen a license not just for a single movie, but for all forty movies that Danny plans to distribute in the coming year. The Court disallowed ‚Äúblock booking‚Äù contracts, viewing them as ‚Äúdevices for stifling competition and diverting the cream of the business to the large operators.‚Äù Instead, the Court required the contract to allow Ellen to reject any film she did not want. What do you think was the Court‚Äôs concern?  
- Court was concerned that producers were forcing exhibitors to lock into contracts where they had to buy bad movies to get the good ones  
  * Viewed as anti-competitive

  11. Assume that Film A is expected to be really popular and Film B is of much lower quality. In principle, the distributor might try to use her leverage through Film A‚Äìsomething everyone wants‚Äìto force exhibitors to take Film B as well. But isn‚Äôt there a more straightforward way for the distributor to take advantage of the popularity of Film A? Why not just do that?  
- Producers could just raise the price or royalty rate on Film A  
  * You don‚Äôt need to sell them as a package to take advantage of bargaining power you might have  
  * Schizer: Economic nativit√©

  12. There are other explanations for block booking, aside from the anticompetitive one that concerned the Court. Why might it be helpful both to Danny and to Ellen to license a block of movies, instead of licensing one at a time?  
- **Block booking: Allowed parties to economize and lower transactions costs. Making movies available together means you don‚Äôt have to do as much individual negotiating**  
  * **Further, if it is hard to predict the hits, then no one really knows, and you should just sell the whole package as a group because no one can really tell what is a good or bad movie at the outset**

Recap:

- Looked at old structure of entertainment industry. Pattern is vertical integration and related practices  
  * Looked at anti-competitive explanations that caused them to be illegal  
  * Looked at other explanations that should not be illegal  
- Next, look at modern practices that have addressed information and incentive problems which has kept the industry going

# 19: Film (Bargaining Power, Gross-Up, Profits Interest)

- Fundamental problems of information and incentives arise in every business setting  
- Key challenge for transactional lawyers:  
  * 1\) Identify incentive/information problems  
  * 2\) What tools can you use to address the information/incentive problems  
    * Depending on the kind of incentive/information problem, there are some tools that are better or worse  
    * In so many situations a tool used to address the initial problem begets a new problem

- Exam:  
  * Focus of Discussion Questions  
  * Not facts/details/obscure legal doctrines  
  * It is about information/incentive problems  
    * Spot them before they arise  
    * Know how to deal with them  
      * The real value of a lawyer is the recommendation to refine transactions  
  * Hypothetical business situations where you see the problems and make recommendations  
    * Offer thoughts and how to address them  
  * 3 Hours  
  * Open laptop but no internet/downloading

- **Profit sharing agreements are not written to be accurate reflection of whether a movie is profitable**  
  * **Net profits interest** (name doesn‚Äôt tell you the features ‚Äì Batfilm)  
    * **Undercounts revenue and overcounts costs**  
    * Parties don‚Äôt do as well as if they were genuine partners  
  * Sophisticated party transactions/contracts are not overruled by courts  
    * Judges do not read intent into the K  
    * Courts will not overrule a K because it is unfair  
    * Court‚Äôs job is to just enforce the bargain  
      * **If courts get involved in re-writing K‚Äôs predictability in business is lowered and transactions are chilled for fear that they will be overruled**  
      * No implied duty of good faith in sophisticated transactions  
    * Like the bus K case

  * **Gross Profits Interest** (generous)  
    * **Limited subtractions, generous revenue calculation**

- **Why do these two interests exist? Why does one person get one and not the other?**  
  * Because some do not have the bargaining power so studios stick them with net profits interest?  
    * **But bargaining power infuses the entire transaction. You can use it in many kinds of ways**  
      * **You could still get a gross profits interest but just a much lower percentage or something like that**  
- Need to understand why specific terms might make sense/not make sense

- **Two versions of why some people get net and not gross:**  
  * **Goldberg: Incentives**  
  * **Weinstein: This is about risk spreading**

1. *George Lucas and Bargaining Power.* After the *Paramount* decision put an end to ‚Äúthe studio era,‚Äù contracts allowing talent to participate in the studio‚Äôs profits became more common. Before we turn to the standard contracts, let‚Äôs start with an arrangement that was unusual: George Lucas‚Äôs contract with Twentieth Century Fox to make *Star Wars.* (Some answers are not in the reading.)  
1. Initially, Lucas had a contract to make two movies. What were they? How much was he going to get paid for the second movie? What happened to give him more bargaining power?  
- Lucas had K to make *American Graffiti* and *Star Wars*  
  * Studio wanted *American Graffiti* and Lucas wanted *Star Wars*  
    * *American Graffiti* is successful and gives him more bargaining power

2. What did his agent suggest as a way to use this bargaining power? What did he do instead?  
- His agent wants to re-negotiate for more salary  
  * Lucas instead uses his bargaining power to ask for other things ‚Äì opportunity  
    * 1\) Merchandising right  
    * 2\) Maintain rights to sequels  
  * **If there are things that matter to you that do not matter to the other side, snap them up in negotiation**  
    * **You also need to identify what things these might be**  
    * **People do not always use bargaining power for a better price term**

3. Why was the studio willing to accept his suggestion?  
- Merchandising: Yes. A recent movie flopped on merchandising so studio was willing to give that to him  
- Sequel Rights: Studio didn‚Äôt like the movie concept to begin with so was fine to give that away too

4. How did Lucas‚Äôs deal compare with the deals struck by other successful writers and directors? Was Lucas‚Äôs main motivation money or something else in making this deal?  
- One of the two wealthiest people in Hollywood (Oprah because she has been compensated outside of her services)

5. Was Lucas able to negotiate a similar deal when he and Steven Spielberg teamed up to make *Raiders of the Lost Arc*? Why or why not?  
- Studio did not give the same concessions because they got burned by the first deal

6. Coda: Media reports suggest that Lucas turned out to be disillusioned with the deal‚Äìnot the money he received, but something else (which arguably was even more important to him). Do you know what his concern was? (This is not in the reading).  
- Disney did not commit to use his three remaining storylines (he made 9 movies but only the first six he made)  
  * Last three were supposed to be Leila‚Äôs story

2. *Batfilm Prod. v. Warner Brothers*  
   1. The more common deal is not the one between Lucas and Twentieth Century Fox, but the one litigated in *Batfilm*. What role did the plaintiffs play in the movie that is the subject of this ligitation?  
- P‚Äôs thought that someone would want to make the rights to make the Batman movie so they negotiated for the rights from comic book and then sold them to the studio

  1. What fixed compensation did plaintiffs get? What contingent compensation was offered to them? What was the dispute about?  
- Yes. They were entitled to $300K which they were paid plus a net profit‚Äôs interest in the movie (which wound up being worthless)  
  * They were supposed to get 13% of net profits but the way net profits was defined in the K they got nothing even though the movie was successful  
  * They brought suit to say that the K is unfair

  2. Is the court willing to consider whether the contract is fair? Does the court think that this approach is well suited to the plaintiffs in this case?  
- A K is not unconscionable just because it is not fair. It is what you agreed to  
- **Hollywood Accounting: Net profits was defined in a precise way that diverged from the way profits of the movie were in reality**

  3. What doctrine is the court applying? What defense does the court offer for the approach it is applying? What does the court say is at stake for the motion pictures industry? Do you agree?  
- P‚Äôs were evoking the doctrine of unconscionability. We got nothing from this profitable movie  
- Court: Too bad. **You are senior movie studio executives. You are a sophisticated party that got what you signed**  
  * **Honor the K to ensure predictability in industry**

- **Unconscionability is normally used when there is a consumer K (unsophisticated party)**
