  * Not a true bargain between people where there is total unequal bargaining power

  4. This excerpt of the opinion considers only some of the plaintiffs‚Äô claims. What is ‚Äúoverhead‚Äù? What was the plaintiffs‚Äô complaint about it? What did the court conclude?  
- **Overhead \= Percentage based fee that studio charges to cover costs regarding rent, utilities, CEO pay, management pay**  
  * **Not allocated budget for a particular movie (camera or actor)**

- **P‚Äôs claimed that Warner Brothers charged excessive overhead costs which reduced their net profits**  
  * **Overhead was a defined percentage term in the K (15%)**  
    * P is saying that is not really what it cost  
    * But court does not care. It was a defined term. It was unfavorable to you but that is too bad. You signed it. No ambiguity

  5. Assume for the moment that 15% is too high an estimate of ‚Äútrue‚Äù overhead, which really is more like 5%. Does this matter? If the parties understand that 15% overstates the studios costs‚Äìand thus makes it harder to reach the net profit break-even point‚Äìthen isn‚Äôt this just the deal the parties agreed to strike? Is judicial intervention required here?  
- If a term is unreasonable, judicial intervention would not be permissible still  
  * They could have specified a term as anything and this is what court would have gone with  
  * It is a term in the K so the parties must follow it

  6. What was the plaintiffs‚Äô complaint about videocassette royalties? What did the court conclude?  
- P‚Äôs say the 15% royalty used to calculate net profit understated actual home video revenue  
  * Court upheld it as consistent with the K  
- **Overstatement of costs, understatement of home-showing revenue decreased the net profit margin**  
  * **It might be inaccurate, but it was defined in the K to be computed in this way**

  7. Given the contract‚Äôs treatment of videocassette royalties, is ‚Äúnet profit‚Äù under the contract an accurate measure of a movie‚Äôs profitability? Would accountants produce the same number in the profits they report to shareholders?  
- Net profit is an inaccurate measure of a movie‚Äôs profitability

  8. In light of your answer to ‚Äúi,‚Äù is it possible for a movie to be very profitable, but for a writer or director with a ‚Äúnet profits‚Äù clause to receive no royalty under the clause?  
- **It is possible for a movie to be very profitable, but for a writer or director with a ‚Äúnet profits‚Äù clause to receive no royalty under the clause**

2. Terminator 3  
   1. Let‚Äôs compare Arnold Schwarzenneger‚Äôs contract in Terminator 3 with the contracts in *Batfilm*? Obviously, Schwarzenneger‚Äôs minimum compensation was a lot higher. How much was it?  
- Schwarzenneger had a pay to play K (even if they didn‚Äôt make the movie he was going to get this money)  
  * Minimum of $29.25M

  2. In addition, his contingent contract also was structured differently. What was the key difference?  
- He received a percentage of adjusted gross receipts after the cash breakeven point for the movie 

- **There are 2 Kinds of profit interests:**  
  * **1\) Net profit: Not very likely to generate a payment**  
  * **2\) Gross profit: Very likely to generate a payment**

  3. Why did Schwarzenneger have so much bargaining power in this deal?  
- There is a video game with Schwarzenneger in it and so the people financing the deal, banking on the video game to make the Terminator franchise money, required Schwarzenneger be in it  
  * Even though the first two movies didn‚Äôt sell very well, Schwarzenneger had massive bargaining power

  4. What nonfinancial benefits did the contract give Schwarzenneger? Did he have any creative control?  
- He also had some control over the production

  5. What percentage of receipts did he get after the movie reached the cash ‚Äúbreak even‚Äù point?  
- 20% of gross receipts from all global markets after the cash breakeven point

  6. Please answer the following questions, applying the contract‚Äôs definition of ‚Äúcash breakeven‚Äù: 

     *Cash Breakeven shall be defined as the point at which there shall have been recouped from Adjusted Gross Receipts an amount equaling all actual distribution expenses attributable to the Picture (provided there shall be no double deductions for any item, including without limitation residuals), all costs of production of the Picture (including without limitation any pre-break participations, mutually-approved deferments and completion bond fee), actual interest and actual financing costs related to the Picture, a producer fee in the aggregate amount of $5,000,000 for Andy Vajna and Mario Kassar and an overhead charge to Intermedia Film Equities Limited equal to ten percent (10%) of the bonded budget (with no interest on overhead or overhead on interest). For purposes of calculating Cash Breakeven only, Adjusted Gross Receipts shall include a 100% home video royalty (i.e. home video revenues less costs, provided no such costs shall be deducted if such costs were previously deducted hereunder) to the extent that Producer is accounted by distributors at a 100% home video royalty or if Producer is not accounted for at a 100% home video royalty, with respect to any Adjusted Gross Receipts, such Adjusted Gross Receipts shall include and be calculated with a home video royalty equal to the home video royalty Producer receives with respect to such Adjusted Gross Receipts, but in no event less than a 35% home video royalty. For all other purposes (other than calculating Cash Breakeven), including the calculation of \[Schwarzenegger\] Participation and the Deferred Participation, Adjusted Gross Receipts shall include a 35% home video royalty, or if the agreement for the services of the director of the Picture so provides, then such greater home video royalty shall be included in the Adjusted Gross Receipts of the Picture for purposes of calculating \[Schwarzenegger\] Participation and the Deferred Participation.*

     1. What expenses are subtracted from adjusted gross receipts to calculate cash breakeven for Schwarzenneger?  
- To calculate gross receipts, subtracting not that many expenses:  
  * Subtract actual distribution expenses  
  * Subtract costs of production  
  * Subtract actual interest and financing costs  
  * Subtract producer fees  
  * No double counting (standard Hollywood accounting procedure)  
    * **Subtractions are minimal and accurate in gross receipts**

- Net profits interest undercounts revenue and overcounts expenses (bigger overhead than there actually was etc.)

  2. In calculating the breakeven point for Schwarzenneger, what percentage of receipts from home video royalties are included in ‚Äúadjusted gross receipts‚Äù? How does this compare with the analogous provision in *Batfilm*?  
- Batfilm: Include a small percentage of the home viewing revenue  
- Schwarzenneger: Include 100 percent of home video revenues less costs  
  * Minimum inclusion of 35%  
    * **Gross profits interest is a much more generous formula (accurate revenue and cost analysis)**

    

  7. What is the purpose of the following provision in Schwarzenneger‚Äôs contract?:  
       
     *A. Contractor and Artist have entered into an Actor Loanout Agreement dated as of Jun 6, 2001 (the "Agreement") pursuant to which, inter alia, Artist is rendering acting services in connection with the production of that certain feature length motion picture tentatively entitled "Terminator 3" (the "Picture").*  
       
     *B. A portion of the services of Artist to be provided under the Agreement may be rendered outside the United States.*  
- Schwarzenneger did not want to make the movie outside of CA or U.S. because he would have to be taxed in all of these different jurisdictions

- Protecting him from foreign tax liabilities with a **Tax Gross Up:**   
  * **After-tax position is as if he had worked in CA the whole time**

    *C. As a material inducement to Artist to enter into the Agreement, and in partial consideration of the performance of services thereunder, Contractor has agreed to reimburse Artist on a fully "grossed up" basis for the payment of any taxes (including, without limitation, income tax, social security tax, payroll taxes and other social charges, the value added tax and any penalties and interest on any of such taxes) to any foreign country or subdivision thereof imposed on Artist by reason of Artist's rendering of services to Contractor in any foreign country under the Agreement (the "Subject Taxes"), all on the terms and subject to the provisions more particularly set forth hereinbelow.*  
      
    *D. The parties' intention in entering into this Tax Reimbursement Agreement is to put Artist in the same economic after-tax position as Artist would have been in had all of Actor's services under the Agreement been performed solely in California through a U.S. "loanout" corporation, and this Tax Reimbursement Agreement shall be interpreted consistent with that intention.*  
      
  8. Assume that Schwarzenneger becomes subject to a foreign tax of $100,000. To achieve the purpose in ‚ÄúD‚Äù above, why is it not enough to pay Schwarzenner an additional $100,000?  
- It is not enough to just pay Schwarzenneger for the tax he owes because he will have to pay taxes on the additional money you give him to reimburse the tax 

  9. Assume the foreign tax is not deductible and that the combined federal and state tax rate is 50%. How much additional compensation must Schwarzenneger receive to make him whole for this tax?  
- $200K

  10. **To make a taxpayer whole for a tax of X, the taxpayer must receive a payment of X/(1-T), where T is the taxpayer‚Äôs marginal tax rate**.

  11. Schwarzenneger‚Äôs ‚Äúgross up‚Äù also included a payment for the following: ‚Äúthe amount of any professional fees and costs incurred by Artist to obtain foreign tax advice in connection with the Picture, if any.‚Äù If you were his lawyer, and you were trying to make the case for this term, what would you say?  
- He wants to make the movie in CA. You are making him fly everywhere to make the movie. **Because he is traveling and has to pay tax in these other jurisdictions, you have to compensate him for the advice he has to get to pay all the local taxes**

  12. What is the purpose of the following provision? Do you think it was included at the request of the studio or of Scharzenneger?:  
      *‚ÄúIn computing the Reimbursement Amount, all allowable exemptions, credits, deductions and offsets against the Subject Taxes shall be taken into account . . . . The Reimbursement Amount shall not include any amounts solely and directly resulting from actions or omissions on the part of Artist (except to the extent such actions or omissions are undertaken (or not undertaken, as the case may be) consistent with the Tax Reimbursement Agreement, with the Agreement or with the prior written approval of Contractor).‚Äù*  
- **The studio is including this to ensure that Schwarzenegger is on the hook for mistakes/errors in his tax planning**  
  * They will only gross him up for taxes that he actually owes after engaging in reasonable tax planning. If you overpay, that is on you

- 2 Types of Compensatory Revenue Sharing  
  * 1\) Net (bad)  
  * 2\) Gross (good)

3. Introducing Two Types of Participations: Net & Gross  
   1. Revenue is being divided‚Äîbut which revenue? To begin answering this question consider the following example: Art writes the script for *Big Hit*. In return, he got a fee of $50,000 plus a net participation. Assume for all the following questions that Art‚Äôs net participation is fairly standard. Ticket sales in theaters for *Big Hit* were $100 million.  
      1. To compute net participations, the first step is to start with ‚Äúadjusted gross receipts.‚Äù Whose receipts are we talking about? Will this be $100 million or something else?  
- **Gross receipts \= Studio‚Äôs Receipts after the cuts to the exhibitors**  
  * $50M of the $100M is the starting point (just revenue sharing with the studio‚Äôs cut of the total revenue)

    2. Home video revenue for *Big Hit* was $50 million. If Art‚Äôs contract is a standard one, will that be included in ‚Äúadjusted gross receipts‚Äù?  
- **Gross receipt percentage for home video is 20%**  
- Starting amount of actor‚Äôs cut is $60M  
  * 20%($50M) \= $10M  
  * 50%($100M) \= $50M

  2. Theater versus Home Viewing  
     1. Scarlett is the star of *Black Spider*, which was supposed to be released in theaters in May of 2020, but the release date was delayed for over a year because of the coronavirus pandemic. Eventually, Studio releases Black Spider simultaneously in domestic theaters (where it grosses $80 million in its first weekend) and also on its ‚ÄúStudio+‚Äù streaming channel (where it grosses $60 million in its first weekend). Scarlett sues Studio, arguing that her contract specified a release exclusively in theaters. Why do you think she cares about this? What is at stake for her?  
- This matters to Scarlet because the decision to release in other platforms, means that her theater revenue is lessened which she gets a bigger cut of (than home video revenue)  
  * **Actor does not share in the theater revenue the same amount as the home video revenue**

- **K‚Äôs are set to maximize the incentives of the people who are relevant at a given moment to make as much money as possible for the overall venture**  
  * **Actor can do a lot more to get people to see the theatrical release through their publicity than they can for a home video release**  
    * Motivate the talent to publicize/advertise the movie as part of the marketing plan for theatrical release (but doesn‚Äôt apply to home revenue) by giving her a cut of the theatrical release

    2. This is based on a real case, which was settled on very favorable terms for one of the parties. Which party do you think that was?  
- **By opening at home at the same time as theaters, studio took advantage of her marketing effort and diverted some of the theatrical revenue which she got a part of to the home video release which she did not**

  3. Gross versus Net  
     1. Let‚Äôs return to *Big Hit*. The star of this movie, Barbara, receives a ‚Äúgross participation.‚Äù How is this different from Art‚Äôs net participation?  
- Gross is better than net because it factors in more revenue and less deductions

  2. There are various costs in making *Big Hit*. Which of the following is subtracted before Barbara‚Äôs ‚Äúgross‚Äù is computed? What about Art‚Äôs ‚Äúnet‚Äù?  
     1. Studio pays $X to the director as fixed compensation.  
- Gross: Subtracted  
- Net: Subtracted

  2. Studio takes a distribution fee equal to 30% of receipts.  
- Gross: Subtracted  
- Net: Subtracted

  3. Advertising expenses.  
- Gross: Not subtracted  
- Net: Subtracted

  3. Barbara, the star of the movie, has a gross participation deal. Assume it‚Äôs a ‚Äúfirst dollar‚Äù gross deal, and that she‚Äôs supposed to get 10% of the adjusted gross and she receives a $5 million advance. How much must the adjusted gross be in order for her to get more than her advance?  
- Advance: Payment you get up-front  
  * Movie has not made any money yet  
  * This is guaranteed. If the movie does not make enough to generate the $5M, she still gets the advance  
- She can earn more than her $5M advance if her movie earns more than $50M  
  * $51M ü°™ She gets $5.1M total (an additional $100K on top of the advance)

  4. Accuracy  
     1. Can *Big Hit* be very profitable from the perspective of the studio‚Äôs shareholders, but still not generate a ‚Äúgross‚Äù profit participation for Barbara?  
- **Gross profit participation is meant to generate a lot of money regardless of how the movie does (even if the movie doesn‚Äôt turn out to be as profitable as everyone hoped it would)**

  2. True or false: ***Big Hit*** **can be very profitable from the perspective of the studio‚Äôs shareholders, but still not generate a ‚Äúnet‚Äù profit participation for Art**.  
- Net profit participations are not accurate, not generous to those who have them  
  * Income is understated, costs are overstated  
    * **If someone works on a movie for a net profit, the movie can do very well and generate little net profit (high expense counting, low revenue counting)**

- **Why are we doing different deals for different actors?**  
  * This is carefully calibrated  
  * **Who are the people that can make the biggest difference in making the movie and when?**  
    * **Screenplay writer: Not much to do after he writes the screenplay. There is nothing more the studio wants him to do**  
    * **Actor: There is a lot more the studio wants the actor to do after making the movie (marketing)**  
      * **Keep her motivated even as the movie comes out**  
  * **Therefore, there need to be different financial instruments that incentivize people in different ways at different times**  
    * **Art is not going to play a role in getting people into theaters so he should not be paid in ticket revenue**

# 20: Film (Profits Interest: Incentives or Risk Allocation)

- Incentives in entertainment industry align with 2 ways a movie can be profitable:  
  * 1\) Gross profit: Blockbuster bring stars in model  
  * 2\) Net profit: Disciplined cost control with relatively unknown cast  
    * These are the different ways you can motivate the relevant people making the profit-maximizing decisions in that moment

- Goldberg: Best way to explain film industry K‚Äôs is that they are nuanced ways to deal with information/incentive problems

3. Information and Incentives: Studios and Theater Owners  
   1. Studio, a film distributor, is negotiating a deal to distribute a movie. Tom, who owns a theater, contracts with Studio to show the movie. Under their standard contract, does Tom pay a fixed fee to Studio for the film? Or does Studio pay a fixed fee to rent the theater? Or something else? What is the usual arrangement? According to Goldberg, what is the reason for this?  
- Theater owner does not pay a fixed fee to studio. Theater owner and studio share revenue  
  * **Goldberg says that this reflects revenue sharing model where the revenue sharing percentage changes over time**  
- This arrangement responds to the uncertainty in the demand

- **Theater owners have some control over the size of the audience depending on their investments (concessions, seat size, theater size, cleanliness)**  
  * **Given that the audience size depends on the efforts/investment of the theater owner, you want the theater to get a contingent fee to have the incentives to invest in drawing a larger crowd**  
  * **The studio is also a critical player and must be motivated to make the best possible movie and market it properly**   
- **You want to motivate both parties to generate the maximum revenue**

- **Goldberg: Revenue sharing makes sense so that the amounts they are getting are contingent on effort**   
- **Theater recovers some costs and then it is a 50-50 revenue split from there**  
  * **You want theater owner to recover basic investment so that he can invest in basic components (staff, lights, etc.)**

  1. Like other standard deals, Tom‚Äôs contract with Studio has a provision that changes over the course of a movie‚Äôs ‚Äúrun‚Äù in his theater. What is this change? What explanation does Goldberg offer for it? Is his explanation about information, incentives, or something else?  
- **In the early weeks of the movie‚Äôs run, the studio takes a larger percentage and the then the theater slowly takes more and more share**  
  * **Goldberg: Whose contribution is more important at a particular moment in time (and then motivate that person)? This determines who gets most of the revenue**  
    * **In the opening days, the studio is more important because of the marketing they do and the film they put together**  
      * **After the initial burst of people go, the theater starts taking more revenue because more people are going just for the experience of being at a theater**  
  * Not a constant revenue split. Starts in favor of the studio and gradually the theater owner gets a bigger and bigger share

  2. Tina, who owns a small independent theater, strikes a different deal than Studio. She will get the movie late in its run. According to Goldberg, is she likely to receive a fixed fee? Or to pay a fixed fee? Why?  
- **In a late run, Theater is paying Studio a fixed fee because the studio‚Äôs efforts are not that relevant anymore**  
  * **The contribution that the studio made is over. At the late stage, the theater is contributing more (clean theater, nice concessions is why people are going). Therefore, theater should bear all the reward/risk in getting people to come because she is in a better position to make a difference with it**

  3. Talia owns a large and luxurious theater in Big City. The studio strikes a special deal to ‚Äúfour wall‚Äù it for the beginning of the movie‚Äôs run. According to Goldberg, is she likely to receive a fixed fee? Or to pay a fixed fee? Why?  
- **in the early days through ‚ÄúFour Walling,‚Äù Theater gets a fixed fee from Studio because when a movie is just opening, the ad campaign looms disproportionately large in getting an audience. Therefore, studio wants to bear all the risk/reward because they are in the best position to make a difference attracting people**  
  * The risk should lie with the studio to get the right ad campaign and make the right movie

- As the run continues, the contribution of the theater is larger and the studio is smaller  
  * At the beginning ‚Äì studio takes all of the risk/reward  
  * At the end ‚Äì theater takes all of the risk/reward

  4. Franco is a foreign subdistributor. According to Goldberg, what kind of deal is he likely to strike with the studio? What are the three components of the deal?  
- Rolling breakeven arrangement for foreign subdistributors: Subdistributor pays a fixed payment based on what he expects the film to generate. He keeps all the revenue until he recoups his costs. Then he splits revenue 50/50 with the studio

- **Who is important and at what point?**  
  * **The studio‚Äôs marketing at the beginning will not be as effective in foreign places so:**  
    * **Fixed payment from subdistributor to studio (up to subdistributor to attract audiences, bear the risk/reward)**  
    * **Cost recovery to subdistributor because studio wants him to spend on getting audience**  
    * **Sharing revenue thereafter so that studio is still incentivized to make movies that appeal to foreign jurisdictions**

- **At each moment in production process, who has the comparative advantage in maximizing movie revenue?**  
  * **Then give that person a larger share at that moment** to try to motivate the person to make the best decision

  5. Why does this structure serve the parties‚Äô interests? What effect does it have on their incentives?  
- Strike the incentives in the right way

  6. What information problem does it solve? Why does Goldberg compare it with an M\&A deal with an earnout?  
- **Blend of information and incentive problems:**  
  * **Incentive issues: Revenue sharing to make profit-maximizing decisions**  
  * **Asymmetric information: Revenue sharing signals that the studio believes in the movie**  
    * **Asymmetric information: Who knows better about this part of the market? That person should get the larger share because they know better to make the right decision**  
      

4. Incentives: Profits Interests, Writers, and Producers  
   1. Peter, a producer who works for Studio, negotiates a net profit interest with Wilma, a young screenwriter. You are a college friend of Wilma‚Äôs parents, and a Hollywood veteran. At her parents‚Äô suggestion, Wilma meets you for coffee to ask for your advice. She asks you why Studio recovers all its costs first, so that she shares in profits only after the breakeven point.  
- **Share the revenue in a way that creates an incentives that best motivate the party to make a profit-maximizing decision. It is the same analysis for the studios negotiating with the talent**  
  1. According to Goldberg, what is the rationale for this? What incentive does it create for Studio?  
- **Studio recovers costs before she gets net profit interest because it is in writer‚Äôs interest to let the Studio overmarket**  
  * She is worried that Studio will overspend because Wilma is subsidizing them through sharing in the profits (and not taking money if they spend more). However, Wilma can only write a good script and then her work is done  
    * Giving her something contingent doesn‚Äôt make sense because she cannot do anything to make the movie more successful  
  * **Studio‚Äôs underinvestment in marketing is bad for Wilma because her reputation relies on the movie being successful which at this point is out of her hands**  
    * **She wants to send the studio a signal: Spend what you want, you come first in recovering**  
    2. Wilma asks whether Peter also gets a net profit participation, and you confirm that he probably does, given the standard contract structure in Hollywood. You also mention that Peter is likely to have an ‚Äúoverbudget penalty clause.‚Äù What is that? According to Goldberg, what function does the clause and Peter‚Äôs net participation serve?  
- **Overbudget penalty clause \= Some of his fixed payment gets taken back from him if the movie goes over budget**  
  * **Writer doesn‚Äôt get this overbudget provision because she does not control the costs ‚Äì only producer does**

- **There are contingent amounts that depend on the person who is making the decision to influence the relevant factor** 

- **Net profits interest are rarely profitable, but there is one situation when they are more likely to pay: Cheap movie (controlled costs) that still generates a lot of revenue**  
  * **Producer controls costs so he decides whether they are going to choose a controlled costs movie or a blockbuster movie**  
    * **If he chooses the controlled costs model ü°™ His net profits interest is still in play, and he needs to be concerned about the budget (so that the budget penalty clause doesn‚Äôt come into play and his costs are limited to make money from the net profits interest)**  
    * **If he chooses the blockbuster model ü°™ His net profits interest is gone (because the movie is so expensive) and it is now the actor, receiving a gross profit interest, that is responsible for the movie‚Äôs success**  
    3. ‚ÄúThis is my second movie. My first did well, so I‚Äôm getting $50,000 for this script, which is a lot more than the $10,000 that I got for my first script. Obviously, that‚Äôs good news,‚Äù she says. ‚ÄúThey also are giving me a net participation, but my friends tell me that it‚Äôs unlikely to pay off in the end.‚Äù Is she right? What does Goldberg say about that?  
- **Net profits can pay her something in a low budget scenario, but it‚Äôs rare that it pays anything**  
  * **She is rewarded for success of movie through reputation and subsequent increases in future fixed fees**  
  * **For some people (writers) the incentive payment is in the form of making money in your fixed fee of your next movie**  
    * **Incentive still exists to do a good job but not on this movie anymore because there is nothing left to do**  
    4. ‚ÄúThe net profits formula is really unfavorable to me,‚Äù Wilma says. ‚ÄúMy friends say that this is just a sign that Peter drives a hard bargain. Studios will cheat us whenever they can, right?‚Äù Do you agree that bargaining power explains the net profits formula? (Hint: Goldberg is skeptical about that. Why?)  
- Not about Studios being abusive but rather   
- **Writer has the bargaining power when she negotiates the fixed fee so why are they using this net profits and not just giving her gross profits but paying her a much lower fixed fee. Not because she doesn‚Äôt have leverage but because she can‚Äôt do anything to maximize the movie‚Äôs revenue after she hands over the script**   
  * Therefore, incentivization process is through the change in fixed payment from movie to movie  
  * **Bargaining power can influence the total expected value of what you get but it does not impact the form of what you get necessarily**  
    5. In predicting whether Wilma‚Äôs net participation will yield a generous payment, you obviously need to make a judgment about whether the movie will be successful. Assume for the moment that it will be (a strong assumption, to be sure, but please make it anyway). For you to advise Wilma about her chances of getting paid, what question should you ask her? What else do you need to know? (Hint: This information is not about Wilma, but about other people.)  
- You need to know if there any major stars or directors who have joined the movie  
  * To evaluate a net profits interest value, you need to know if there are stars in the movie  
  * Once there is a star who takes a gross profits interest, your net profits interest is almost guaranteed to be nothing  
    * Two track aspect to this compensation:  
      * 1\) High budget route ‚Äì net profit means nothing  
      * 2\) Low budget route ‚Äì net profit could mean something

  2. Incentives: Interaction of Net and Gross Profit Participations  
     1. Three weeks later, Wilma calls you and says, ‚ÄúPeter has just signed a really big name‚ÄìAlicia Actress‚Äìas the star of the movie, but I‚Äôm not sure whether to be happy or sad about this.‚Äù What is the downside for Wilma?  
- **Downside: Wilma is going to make nothing from the net profits interest**  
- **Upside: The movie might do better and in her next script she will get a bigger fixed fee**  
  2. ‚ÄúKeep in mind that Peter is in the same boat as you,‚Äù you respond. ‚ÄúIn any event, I still think this is good news for you (and for Peter as well).‚Äù Please explain your statement. (Hint: Goldberg supplies the answer.)  
- **Downside: Peter makes nothing on the value of his net profits interest too**  
- **Upside: Compensated by higher fixed payment in his next movie**  
  * Like Wilma, producer will also be compensated in larger future fixed payment

- **Goldberg: Producer has two ways to make extra money so he will pick the way that will most likely make money for the movie**  
  * **Keep it low budget and we can still fill theaters (makes more on net profits interest)**  
  * **Make it big budget with a big star (get bigger fixed fee next time)**  
    * **Producer would not destroy his net profits interest unless he thinks that by giving a star a gross profits interest, he is significantly increasing the chance that the movie will be a big hit**  
    * Studio might even waive the overbudget penalty clause if the star is that big of a deal  
- **Because the producer makes the decision on getting a star, his compensation should be tailored to choosing the most profitable route for the movie**  
  3. Don‚Äôt Alicia‚Äôs gross profits interest reduce Peter‚Äôs incentive to control costs and do his best work? According to Goldberg, why is Studio willing to dilute Peter‚Äôs incentive in this way?  
- Yes actor reduces producer‚Äôs incentives to control costs  
  * **Once you go this route of getting the star, the revenue from the movie will be generated by the big star (not the producer). So move the incentive compensation to the big star and away from producer because the star is now the key decision maker**  
    * **Peter‚Äôs ability to shave a little costs here or there doesn‚Äôt matter**  
    4. After the production of the movie is complete, what additional steps can Alicia take to help it succeed? How, if at all, does a gross participation motivate her to take this step?  
- Only Alicia can do the talkshows, interviews, press tours  
  * She is busy on a new movie, but if you give her a cut of her the gross revenue in this movie she will do the advertising stunts  
    5. A film without a big star like Alicia can‚Äôt depend on the step in ‚Äúi‚Äù to generate interest in the movie. According to Goldberg, when this is the case, whose efforts become more important in marketing it? Does the typical deal structure for a deal without a big star create the right incentives for others to step in and do more?  
- **Without a big star, the producer‚Äôs efforts are more valuable to tailor costs and the studio‚Äôs efforts in advertising are key (without the star)**  
  * **So the deal is structured so that:**  
    * **1\) Producer has a chance to make money from net profits interest**  
    * **2\) Studio recoups its costs first before sharing with anyone**  
    6. Wilma calls you up a year later. ‚ÄúMy last movie was a big hit, but my net participation didn‚Äôt pay off. I‚Äôm pretty annoyed about that,‚Äù she says. ‚ÄúPeter (who produced that movie) just called me, asking me to do another script. He‚Äôs offering me $100,000 for it and another net participation.‚Äù After you congratulate her, she poses a question: ‚ÄúThis time, should I insist that the studio can‚Äôt grant anyone a gross participation?‚Äù What is your advice for Wilma? Why might this strategy backfire? (Hint: Again, Goldberg supplies the answer.)  
- No. Wilma should not do this because this ensures that big stars will not participate in the movie because they can‚Äôt get gross profit interest. This can prevent her from getting a higher fixed fee in the future  
  * The studio might just cut her outright because gross profits interest is part of the business model  
    7. Studio makes Wilma and Peter ‚Äúpay or play‚Äù on the movie. What does this mean? What kind of option does this give Studio? (Hint: we used this term in our discussions of venture capital.)  
- **Pay or Play \= Studio pays writer/producers even if the movie does not get made**  
  * **This is an option to abandon the movie**  
  * **This Staged financing \= In VC, you make a payment that gives you time to decide if you want to make another payment**

5. Monitoring and Incentives  
   1. Paula, a producer, is negotiating to sign Andrew, an up-and-coming actor, to work on a movie. The movie will be directed by Delia, a prominent and experienced director. Andrew receives a net participation. Does he need it to be properly motivated to do a good job? One potential rationale would be that his effort is not observable. If others can‚Äôt tell whether he is really trying, he needs to be self-motivated. But can‚Äôt Delia (the director) tell whether Andrew is making an effort and doing a good job? Also, doesn‚Äôt Andrew have other financial incentives to do his best work? What is Weinstein‚Äôs view on this issue?  
- Weinstein thinks that effort is very observable especially by directors who are highly skilled at identifying talent  
  * You don‚Äôt need the net profit participation because Hollywood is a small town and you get opportunities based off of reputation  
- **You do not need contingent compensation to motivate people when you can observe what they are doing**  
  * **Goldberg‚Äôs theory fails**  
  * You can ask the director, how is this actor? That will tell you if you are going to work with him again

  2. Ned, whose novel is being adapted for the screen to make this movie, is also receiving a net participation. Ned wrote the novel five years ago, and someone else is writing the screenplay. How important is it to motivate Ned to do good work? Is the rationale for his net participation an incentive-based rationale? What is Weinstein‚Äôs view?  
- **Rationale for net profits is better explained by budget constrained rationale**  
  * **For the actors you can observe them so they don‚Äôt need revenue sharing, and for the writers they are done so they don‚Äôt need revenue participation either (even through net)**  
- **Weinstein: This is about risk sharing**

  3. In the studio era, actors were permanent employees at studios. But since the studio era ended, they are hired only for a specific picture. According to Weinstein, what effect, if any, does this change have on an actor‚Äôs incentives to do her best work on a given movie?  
- **Frequent contracting:** This increases an actor‚Äôs incentives to do his or her best work on a given movie because they are constantly engaging in new contracts with new players. Frequent contracting increases incentives for actors to do well so that new producers/directors will want to hire them

6. Risk and Incentives  
   1. Do Goldberg and Weinstein have the same or different views about the function of net and gross participations?  
- Different

  2. Compared with Goldberg, is Weinstein more or less persuaded of the importance of motivating actors, directors, and others to do their best work? Does he have a different theory about how to motivate them?  
- Weinstein acknowledges the importance of incentives but does not think that the contracts are there to create incentives  
  * **Actors‚Äô incentives are already dealt with by direct monitoring (directors monitor actors)**

- **Weinstein: Net/gross participation is because of Risk-based allocation**  
  * Studio could‚Äôve paid the stars a fixed amount but instead they want to share the profit with them if the movie does well and cut their costs (allocating risk to the actor) if the movie does less well

  3. Hollywood studios generally are part of publicly traded companies. Their shareholders usually are diversified. So if studios are run to serve the interests of shareholders, shouldn‚Äôt they be risk neutral?  
- Diversification deals with the risk  
- **Goldberg critique of Weinstein:** Studio should not be run to allocate risk to talent because the studios are owned by diversified SH who do not care about risk

  4. According to Weinstein, why might studios behave as if they are risk averse? What kind of incentive problem (which we have encountered before) can cause this behavior?  
- **Weinstein Response:** Studio executives make the decisions, and they are not as diversified in the other companies  
  * Managers are much more sensitive to risk than the SH  
  * **Risk-spreading is for the benefit of the managers (not SH)**

- **Agency Cost: Managers want these profits interests to allocate risk to actors because they are making decisions based on their own risk tolerance without the risk sensitivity of SH in mind**

  5. What risk-related function does Weinstein attribute to net and gross participations?  
- Net and gross profit transfers risk from studio to the actors

  6. When Producer negotiates with Actors to hire them to work on the movie, what information might Producer have that they don‚Äôt have? Conversely, what information might they have that Paula doesn‚Äôt have? According to Weinstein, how (if at all) can net or gross profit participations help to bridge this information asymmetry?  
- Producer knows more about the other movie K‚Äôs and the movie‚Äôs market  
- Actors know more about their motivation and capabilities

- **Signaling Bridging Information Asymmetry: The willingness of talent to take this risk is a signal that they are confident they will do a good job**

  7. If the success of movies is hard to predict, one possible response is that producers and talent will invest time and effort in making better predictions. Alternatively, another response is to use profit-sharing contracts. According to Weinstein, how can net and gross participations help the parties respond to uncertainty?  
- It is not as important to predict the success because you are not as invested in each movie  
- If you don‚Äôt really know what is going to happen, **the uncertainty encourages sharing the risk because it‚Äôs not worth trying to draft a K to reflect accurate predictions of the movie ‚Äì just share the risk**  
  * Uncertainty in the industry is so hard to unpack that you are better off not trying to unpack it and just dividing it

- **3 Reasons for Profits Interests:**  
  * **1\) Risk sharing because of agency cost**  
  * **2\) Risk sharing to deal with uncertainty**  
  * **3\) Risk sharing as signal to bridge information asymmetry**

- Interprets the same K provisions differently than Goldberg (not incentive based)

7. Dan is a director, and he is negotiating with Paula, a producer who is the head of Studio, about whether he will direct *Hit Movie*.   
   1. A key question is how important Dan‚Äôs effort is in making the movie a success. If Dan just goes through the motions‚Äìnot motivating the actors, not keeping the filming on schedule, and not trying to be creative in the way the movie is filmed‚Äìwill his lack of effort impede the movie‚Äôs success?  
- Shirking will impede the success of the movie

  2. Assume that Dan is extremely motivated, tirelessly and energetically doing everything he can to improve the movie‚Äôs quality and to get it done on time and under budget. According to Arthur De Vany, do his efforts guarantee that the movie will be successful?  
- **A lack of effort can hurt the movie, but complete dedication won‚Äôt guarantee success because of uncertainty**

  3. Alan and Ann are actors who are the stars of *Hit Movie*. Let‚Äôs consider the same question. How important is their effort? If they don‚Äôt try, will their lack of effort scuttle the movie? According to De Vany, if they do try, will their efforts guarantee success?  
- Lack of effort will harm the movie, but extra effort won‚Äôt guarantee success because of uncertainty

  4. Paula is considering whether to pay Dan, Alan, and Ann with a fixed salary only, or to supplement their salary with pay based on the movie‚Äôs performance.   
     1. If Paula believes that Dan, Alan, and Ann‚Äôs efforts are important to the success of the movie, is that an argument to use incentive compensation?  
- Yes, incentive compensation is motivating (Goldberg)  
  2. True or false: **If the goal of contingent compensation is to motivate the talent to do better work, the condition that triggers the compensation need to be chosen carefully: the talent should be rewarded for effort and for adding value, and the reward should be withheld when they don‚Äôt do a good job**.  
- True.  
  3. Do ‚Äúnet profits‚Äù interests seem like they are set in the right way to reward the talent‚Äôs effort? Why or why not?  
- Net profits are not set properly to reward the talent‚Äôs efforts because even if the actors do a good job, net profits interest often doesn‚Äôt pay anything  
  * The only time net profits might pay is to producer (in a low budget movie with no gross profits interest hit)  
    4. What about ‚Äúgross participations‚Äù? Compared with net participations, do they seem better calibrated‚Äìor not as well calibrated‚Äìto reward Dan, Alan, and Ann‚Äôs efforts?  
- Gross participations are better calibrated to pay out for actor‚Äôs efforts

- **Gross profits interest is the real incentive compensation (only given to the people who need the incentive and whose efforts can impact the movie‚Äôs success)**  
  * Net profits interest only incentives in that low budget movie decision

8. Before Paula negotiates contracts with Dan, Alan, and Ann, and the rest of the team, she meets with Arthur De Vany.  
   1. In the course of the meeting, De Vany describes his thought experiment involving the weight of students in his class. What is the thought experiment? What is his point?  
- **Hollywood works on outliers:** Few outlier blockbusters dominate film industry success  
  * The difference between bad and average movies is small  
  * But the thing that drives revenue for the studio is the blockbuster movie. They are rare but important

  2. De Vany also repeats a line from today‚Äôs reading: ‚ÄúAbout 5 percent of movies earn close to 80 percent of motion picture profit.‚Äù Should this fact influence how Paula thinks about incentive compensation? To what extent does effort determine whether a movie ends up being in the top five percent of earners?  
- **In theory, the fact that the outlier blockbusters have so much influence could be an argument for incentive compensation being important because it will encourage hard work which will make it more likely for your film to be in that top 5 percent**

  3. Recognizing the extremely broad range of potential box office results, Paula says to De Vany, ‚ÄúThat‚Äôs why it‚Äôs so important to motivate the talent.‚Äù How do you think De Vany would respond?  
- **De Vany would be skeptical of the motivation theory because existence of motivation is not an indicator of success due to uncertainty**

  4. De Vany argues that it‚Äôs very hard to predict which movies will be the top 5% grossing movies. Part of the reason, he says, has to do with the demand for a specific movie. What shapes it? How predictable are these ‚Äúnonlinear‚Äù factors?  
- A lot has to do with the way the movie starts and the transmission of opinion from early audiences to later audiences

- **De Vany would critique Goldberg: It is random. Incentives are not important because of the uncertainty**

  5. According to De Vany, part of the reason why movie revenue is unpredictable has to do with supply. Once a movie has been made, how costly is it to show it to more people?  
- 

  6. During his meeting with Paula, De Vany repeats another line from the reading today: ‚ÄúIf you don‚Äôt know anything, then how do you pay a director or an actor?‚Äù What is De Vany‚Äôs answer?  
- 

  7. In light of your answer to ‚Äúf,‚Äù is De Vany recommending contingent compensation‚Äìsuch as net and gross participations‚Äìas a way to motivate talent?  
- 

  8. Is De Vany‚Äôs analysis more like Goldberg‚Äôs or Weinstein‚Äôs view?  
- **Weinstein‚Äôs explanation is better suited to De Vany‚Äôs analysis because De Vany thinks more about the uncertainty and risks uncertainty creates so dividing up these risks makes sense through the net/gross profits ‚Äì not guaranteeing the success of these movies through incentives**

- **Incentive compensation K‚Äôs:**  
  * **Why are they structured like this?**  
    * **Weinstein: There are other mechanisms that guarantee incentives (they will make more next time, they are monitored by others). These contingent K‚Äôs are about risk-spreading (because executives are not diversified)**  
    * **Goldberg: Incentives matter. You need to see how the incentives align with the behavior you want**

- Parallel to VC:  
  * Uncertainty as to what the successes will be, but a few success stories drive the entire industry  
  * **Incentives are critical to situations even when they do not guarantee you an outcome so act as if they do**  
    * **In the situations where you are right you will be glad you had the incentives (even if it is uncertain they will work in most situations)**

**Studio ROFR to Produce Sequel:**

- Did all the work to market the first one  
- Protects its investment in the franchise  
  * A studio invests significant resources in making a film, including development, production, marketing, and distribution.¬†A right of first refusal ensures the studio can continue the franchise and potentially recoup its investment and generate further profits.  
  * A studio wants to maintain control over its IP and brand.¬†A right of first refusal prevents another studio from potentially taking over the franchise, changing the creative direction, or affecting the franchise's overall success.  
  * A right of first refusal helps prevent potential disruptions and conflicts that might arise if a different studio were to suddenly take over a franchise.  
- Repeat Players: A right of first refusal allows the studio to make strategic decisions about the franchise, including casting, writing, and production.  
- Distributor has theater connections that worked  
- More certainty this time around that the sequel movie will be successful if the first one was so they want to lock it up

# 21: Earnouts (Solution and Source of Information/Incentive Problems)

- Earnouts: Another form of contingent payment

- 2 Components:  
  * 1\) Bridge Differences in Parties‚Äô Valuation of a Deal  
    * Without earnout parties might not have a ZOPA  
    * But the earnout can carve out the relevant contingency to reconstruct the ZOPA for the rest of the deal

  * 2\) Limited/hard to use because by solving the information problem, you create other information and incentive problems  
    * Need to define carefully what you are measuring in order to determine what the contingent purchase price will be  
    * What games can parties play to manipulate the number  
      * Buyer has ability to control/manipulate as the new owner of subsidiary  
        * Profit based number  
          * Costs are relevant but which costs (buyer has incentive to include additional costs)  
      * Buyer has ability to market/push relevant part of the earnout  
        * But seller‚Äôs ability to determine what the effort is undercuts the appeal of the earnout. They can do this for 2 reasons:  
          * 1\) Valid ‚Äì The other companies‚Äô products buyer bought were better. Can‚Äôt surrender this ability to make the judgments to seller management even if they stay on  
            * Either way valid or not, this is what buyer will say  
          * 2\) Self-interested ‚Äì Reduce the amount of the earnout by waiting to give it attention and when the earnout period is up, then engaging with seller‚Äôs company  
    * Therefore, earnouts are used less than you might expect

1. Seller has an established business selling software. The existing products generate a robust revenue stream, which the seller values at between $200 M & $250 M. Buyer is considering making a bid, valuing the business as between $175 M and $225 M.  
     
   Seller has a new version of its software coming out in six months. A key question is how well received it will be in the market. Skeptics think it is not as good as the new software soon to be released by a competitor, but Seller loyalists expect it is just what Seller needs to rejuvenate the brand. The key question is how well the new software will sell. Skeptics (including Buyer management) think there is a 20% chance that 300 million copies will sell in the first year, adding $100 M of value to the company, while loyalists (including Seller management) think there is an 80% chance that 300 million copies will sell and the new software will add $100 M of value.

     
   1. Without the new software, is there a ZOPA in this transaction?  
- Yes. ZOPA is $25M from $200M to $225M without the new software

  2. With the new software, does it seem (at least at first glance) that there is a ZOPA for this transaction?  
- No. ZOPA with new software is:  
  * Seller: 80%($100M) from new software \+ $200M reservation price of initial business \= $280M (least seller would accept)  
  * Buyer: 20%($100M) from new software \+ $225M reservation price of initial business \= $245M (most buyer would pay)  
- Contingency killed the deal/ZOPA

  3. Do you think predictions about the success of the new software pose issues of asymmetric information? Or uncertainty? Or both?  
- **New software poses uncertainty issues** because neither party is completely sure how it will play out especially in comparison to the competing product  
- **New software poses asymmetric information problem** because seller has more understanding than buyer of how the software was developed and if it will work well, but **seller also has incentive to misrepresent their information** to buyer to drive up the bidding price

  4. What contractual mechanism can potentially help to enable Buyer and Seller to find a mutually agreeable price?  
- **Earnout or contingent value right** depending on if the software company is public or private  
  * **This allows parties to go back to old ZOPA to figure out what the pricing would be without the new variable at a fixed price and then add in the varying/contingent price later**  
    * Let things play out and amount will depend on whose prediction was right  
      * Portion of the purchase price is resolved later instead

  5. What is the difference between an earnout and a contingent value right?  
- Earnout \= Contractual agreement where buyer makes a supplemental payment to seller‚Äôs shareholders if the post-acquisition company‚Äôs performance meets specific goals over a specified period of time  
  * Only works for privately held companies or subsidiaries of public companies because buyer must be able to locate the shareholders to pay them  
- Contingent Value Right \= Buyer issues a security to seller‚Äôs shareholders to compensate them if the post-acquisition company‚Äôs performance meets specific goals over a specified period of time  
  * Same as earnout but in a public company

- **Same thing economically but earnout is in a private deal and CVR is public (can be traded to someone else)**

  6. Do you think the relevant instrument here should be based on appraised value of the company in six months? Or on something else? Why?  
- The earnout should be based on the revenue generated (sales) from the copies of the software sold in the six months to a year after the closing  
  * It should not be based on the appraised value of the company because buyer might do other things to improve the company and its value other than have the software release (replace management)  
  * **Needs to be based on something objective, specific performance metrics (sales or approval of a drug) because valuations can shift over the time and parties may still not agree in six months on what the new value of the seller‚Äôs product was**


2. Ann is an up-and-coming tennis player. Nickel, a popular sports apparel company, is discussing an endorsement deal with her. Ann has charisma and an exciting playing style, as well as the potential to be a top 10 player or perhaps even a top 3 player. But she has a tendence to choke in big matches, and she also injured her knee last year. She is likely to recover fully, but there‚Äôs uncertainty about this. Bob, who is negotiating for Nickel, is trying to figure out what to offer Ann. A $100X annual payment is appropriate for a top-20 player (which is what she is now), but a $300X annual payment is appropriate for a top-10 player (which is what she could be). Nickel is also considering creating a special line of tennis sneakers named for Ann, which she would wear.  
     
   Obviously, Nickel is not the only game in town. Adonis is also speaking with Ann, Obviously, Nickel has many other prospects as well.   
   1. Why is the fixed payment hard to determine?  
- How good Ann is at tennis corresponds to how much Nickel will make from sponsoring her. Since there is uncertainty about how she will perform due to her game, characteristics, and injury, there is also uncertainty about how much Nickel should pay her

- 3 Variables make the player‚Äôs valuation difficult: Performance uncertainty (tendency to choke), injury risk, and comparison to other players are all uncertain  
  * Fixed payment is limiting because there is too much uncertainty in the expected valuation (how much she can help Nikel earn)

  2. What alternatives can Bob consider in structuring a package for Ann? Please offer some detail about the contract you have in mind.  
- Incentive based compensation will help Bob here:  
  * $100X annual fixed payment (lowest range of where she might be, the minimum)  
  * $300X if she is top 10 for a certain period of time  
  * More money if she becomes top 3 (highest range of where she thinks she can get)  
  * **Product line revenue sharing**  
  * Time limit on the deal subject to re-negotiation: 3-5 year deal  
  * Termination right if injury sidelines her for a long time


3. Christine, a scientist, is the CEO of a pharmaceutical startup called C-Pharma. She is hard at work on a new pain killer, Nopain, which would be effective and not addictive. She intends for this drug to be for patients who have had surgery or sustained serious injuries, and thus need pain relief for between 2 and 6 weeks. C-Pharma has no other drugs in development.  
     
   Dalia, who is the head of acquisitions for  Big-Pharma, is considering making a bid for C-Pharma. She recognizes that if Nopain actually works, it will be extremely marketable. Dalia predicts that the revenue stream‚Äôs discounted value will be worth $300 M or more.   
     
   But Dalia is struggling to figure out the likelihood that Nopain actually will succeed. There are several steps in the process. Will it actually be effective in treating pain? Will there be harmful or unpleasant side effects? Will it be finished before competitor drugs come to market? Will it get the necessary regulatory approvals?   
     
   1. Why might Christine and Dalia make different predictions about these questions? If they do, what effect do their differences have on the ability to price the deal?  
- Christine and Daila may make different predictions about these questions because of information and incentive imbalances  
  * Christine, drug owner, knows more about the drug and has more reason to believe in it because she is a founder. She also has incentive to misrepresent her confidence to Daila  
  * Daila can‚Äôt tell if Christine‚Äôs belief in the drug is really her confidence based on true information she knows or based on her incentive to make it up. Even if she has some expertise in evaluating companies in this field

  * **3 Rationales for Founder/Investor or Buyer/Seller Information Asymmetry:**  
    * **Founder knows more**  
    * **Founder is optimistic (has more reason to believe in it)**  
    * **Founder has incentive to misrepresent information (her confidence)**

  2. What alternative can you suggest for Dalia to propose? Why does it help bridge the differences between Christine and Dalia?  
- **Fixed price at the low end plus an earnout which is at the high end** helps to bridge the differences because it enables Christine to benefit from the drug regardless of if she owns the business when the drug‚Äôs successes are known  
  * This will bring her reserve price as to what the business is worth closer to Daila‚Äôs, creating a ZOPA, because the most uncertain factor of the drug‚Äôs effectiveness is out of the way

  3. Should your proposal in part ‚Äúb‚Äù be based on several milestones or just one or two? Which ones would you pick and why?  
- Several milestones will provide more accurate payment to Daila  
  * **Regulatory approval**  
    * Without this approval, the earnout price should be near zero. So fixed payment should be very low but provide a good bump if there is regulatory approval  
  * **Sales revenue in six months from drug**  
  * **Sales revenue in 12 months from drug**  
    * Need to prove that the drug is profitable (comparison to other competing drugs)  
  * Each milestone deducts lawsuit damages from patients with poor side effects

  4. What signal do Christine and Dalia send in crafting a term like the one in ‚Äúb‚Äù? Why might that be helpful?  
- **Earnout for Asymmetric Information: Seller knows more so buyer cannot commit to a high fixed price, and the earnout acts to bridge the information**

- Daila signals her confidence in the company outside of the drug because she is almost removing the drug from the company and treating as a separate revenue stream, making Christine a more willing seller  
- **Christine also signals her confidence** in the drug because she is willing to take a potentially lower purchase price in exchange for this after-closing payment. Therefore, she is signaling that it is going to work

  5. Is the term in ‚Äúb‚Äù more or less helpful if Christine would stay on to run C-Pharma as a subsidiary of Big-Pharma?  
- **Earnout for Incentive Problem: Helps incentive problem if seller is going to stay on to help launch the product because she is incentivized to make the product better to get the higher earnout price**  
  * Usually, seller would stay on to run this through regulatory approval, start the marketing campaign  
      
4. Whale-co, which makes widgets, is considering an acquisition of Minnow-co, which makes a specialized type of widget, which Whale-co management thinks could become a new source of revenue for Whale-co. Yet Whale-co is thinking of Minnow-co as just one possible new direction. It is acquiring 7 other companies that have similar prospects. There is significant uncertainty about which, if any, of these 8 companies will succeed. Meanwhile, Whale-co earns $1 billion per year, while Minnow-co earns $30 million per year.   
   1. If Whale-co wants to incorporate an earnout in the offer, would a revenue based earnout make sense? Or a profit-based earnout? What are the advantages and disadvantages of these two approaches?  
- **Profit-based earnout** \= Payment based on percentage of the revenue minus expenses  
  * Preferred by buyers  
  * Harder to measure/monitor  
  * Better aligned with actual value creation of the seller‚Äôs company  
- **Revenue-based earnou**t \= Payment based on percentage of the revenue  
  * Preferred by sellers  
  * Easier to measure/monitor  
  * Can incentivize overselling in the short-term

- **Profit-based earnouts are better because they are more accurate depictions of the value of the product. However, profit-based earnouts are harder because it is easier to manipulate profit numbers rather than revenue**  
  * **When Minnow becomes part of Whale, the revenue it generates is measured in sales, but its costs can include senior management time allocation, marketing campaign ‚Äì all of which Whale controls**  
    * **Whale has incentive to inflate the Minnow acquisition up with costs so that they pay less in the earnout**  
  * **So revenue-based earnout might be used even if it less accurate**

  2. Why might the Minnow-co management worry about depending too heavily on an earnout in this situation? How might Whale-co have a conflict of interest?  
- **The earnout might not benefit Minnow because Whale is acquiring other competitors who are in a similar line of business so they may find that another company‚Äôs product is better and focus on selling that one which de-values Minnow‚Äôs earnout or they may purposely favor the acquisition that they have to pay the least in earnout price to**  
  * **All 8 sellers compete for attention/resources from the buyer**

- **2 Incentive Issues with earnouts:**  
  * **1\)** **Cost Allocation** ‚Äì Buyer is inflating acquired company with costs  
    * Can be fixed with revenue based earnout  
  * **2\) Buyer Discretion** ‚Äì Buyer gets to decide how they want to market, advance, produce the product  
    * If buyer acquired multiple companies, they may choose to favor the company that has the most buyer-friendly earnout, lowering the purchase price

  3. Why is it challenging to pick a length of time for the earnout period? What are the risks if it is short? If it is long?  
- Because of the product or seller‚Äôs asset they want a piece of is uncertain, time for its success is also uncertain  
  * Too short and seller is robbed of revenue that they might be entitled to  
  * Too long and seller is taking additional revenue from buyer they are not entitled to

- **Uncertainty Issue:** On the merits, there is still a challenge of knowing what the right timing is  
  * Too short and buyer didn‚Äôt give the new subsidiary enough time to be successful  
  * Too long and earnout is starting to measure buyer‚Äôs contributions to the subsidiary more than the seller‚Äôs

- **Incentive Issue:** In addition, buyer is incentivized to manipulate the earnout by setting the period short and having no one works on the product for the earnout period and when it expires profits come in

- **Earnout: Information Problem ü°™ Earnout Solution ü°™ Information and Incentive Problems**

  4. Whale-co is buying a number of different companies. Why might this be a red flag to Minnow-co, as its management considers whether to agree to an earnout?  
- Buyer‚Äôs management has more control over the revenue that the product will earn than seller‚Äôs. They may make a strategic choice in good faith to focus on another product or they might intentionally divert revenue away from seller‚Äôs product to another product so they don‚Äôt have to pay so much in the earnout

  5. To what extent can the problems in ‚Äúd‚Äù be addressed by a reasonableness term in the contract? Or by specifying who makes management decisions?  
- Reasonableness can try to address the problem by demanding more from buyer management, but this can only go so far because reasonable efforts are hard to measure  
  * Can‚Äôt be specific about what choices will be made

- **Specifying who makes management decisions can help** because if it someone seller trusts or has influence over they will work to help seller‚Äôs product. Sometimes, it will even be the seller themselves staying on at the company  
- **But if you give full decision making authority to seller‚Äôs management, seller is reassured but buyer‚Äôs management will not be reassured because maybe buyer will make a good faith decision to not prioritize seller, but they are stuck with seller management making the decisions**

  6. What other ideas do K & S mention and what problems do they identify with them?  
- Agency costs of buyer management managing seller‚Äôs product


5. Did K & S recommend the use of earnouts or not? What position do you think they were taking? Did you agree with it?  
- K\&S recommend earnouts are used pretty rarely because they are difficult to design without creating new incentive and information problems

- Earnouts in the context of an identifiable event to which value can be tied to (product release, drug approval) is better

6. In what situations will earnouts be most effective? When will they be least effective? Why?  
- **Earnouts will be most effective where there is clear performance metrics of a freestanding event (regulatory approval)**  
  * **Both the acquirer and seller want approval (incentives aligned) and neither has complete control over whether it is granted because they are not making that decision**  
- **Earnouts will be least effective where there is high integration is necessary**. If the acquirer deeply integrates the seller‚Äôs company into their business, it will be hard to measure if it is buyer or seller generating the revenue and the buyer maintains more control over deciding whether or not to prioritize it

- Earnouts will be most effective when there is an identifiable event (freestanding thing) to which value will be linked like a regulatory approval of a drug  
  * Outside the control of both parties ‚Äì regulatory approval (both parties have incentive to get the approval even if there are further questions that come up while under buyer‚Äôs control like marketing)  
- Earnouts will be least effective when they are based on high integration is needed because it will be hard to determine how much the seller‚Äôs company actually contributed to buyer  
- financial metrics because they are harder to design and implement  
  * It is hard for the parties to agree on a set of financial data that expect will reflect the increase in value to the buyer after the acquisition  
  * It is also hard for the buyer and seller to agree on what value should be measured

# 22: Incentive Designs

1. Parties enter in transactions seeking value from each other. An important challenge arises when the parties have trouble determining whether their counterparty actually is doing what it promised to do.  
   1. What term do economists use to describe performance that is hard to observe?  
- **Moral Hazard \= When parties act in a self-interested way but their actions are not observable/verifiable**

  2. Even when performance is hard to observe, why might parties perform anyway? (Hint: the answer is not rooted in contract.)  
- Personal integrity, reputational reasons (repeat relationships)

  3. How is moral hazard different from adverse selection?  
- Moral Hazard \= When parties act in a self-interested way but their actions are not observable/verifiable  
  * **After Transaction:** Now that I have insurance, behave like go skydiving/riskier actions, undermining the deal

- **Adverse Selection \= Insurance or another industry attracts the types of people that are tempted to incur losses that insurance will pay**  
  * **Before Transaction:** People who are less healthy want health insurance more than those who do not  
  * The mere fact that insurance is offered will attract a group of people that will be more expensive to insure

- Timing difference: Moral hazard is happening after the transaction and adverse selection is happening before

  4. Adam is CEO of XYZ, a public company. CEO comp tends to rise with the size of the company. Adam has the opportunity for XYZ to acquire ABC. What is the potential for moral hazard here?  
- CEO may push for acquisition even if it is not in the company‚Äôs best interest to increase his compensation  
  * Agency cost  
  * Moral Hazard \= Hard to observe whether this is a good deal for company or more just a good deal for him

2. Betty is founder and CEO of a biotech startup, which is seeking to cure Disease. She approaches Big Pharma for financing. Big Pharma (‚ÄúBP‚Äù) provides $50 million. In return, if Betty develops a cure, Big Pharma will get a mandatory licence to commercialize the drug and will receive 15% the revenue from selling the cure.  
   1. Why didn‚Äôt BP simply buy the idea from Betty? What problems make this structure more appealing?  
- **2 Hurdles to Purchasing the Drug from Founder:**  
  * **1\) Asymmetric Information problem:** Team working on the drug knows more about how successful the drug will be than the buyer (and buyer will be skeptical)  
  * **2\) Incentive problem:** Structuring the deal this way instead of a fixed payment, encourages the Founder working on the drug to keep to working for regulatory approval (rather than in a fixed payment where she wouldn‚Äôt care if the drug was approved)

  2. Why didn‚Äôt BP offer a fixed price to Betty‚Äôs startup, instead of sharing royalties?  
- Information and incentive problems can be solved with contingent compensation

  3. Assume the annual revenue is $10 million. Assume also that BP has limited cash. Instead of investing in the development of Betty‚Äôs cure, BP could put money into a different drug, DD, which treats a different disease. BP developed DD itself, and expects it to generate $9.5 million of annual revenue. Which is BP likely to favor?  
- BP is likely to favor their own drug because they do not have to pay a royalty (acts as a tax). In the royalty, they only keep $8.5M

  4. To address this issue, is it better to have a larger up-front payment or a larger royalty?  
- Larger up front payment with small royalty is better to address the taxing issue of the royalty. So, making the contingent payment smaller is better because it distorts the buyer‚Äôs decision making less if the payment is smaller  
  * But the contingent payment is still necessary for solving the information/incentive problems that the buyer has when buying from Founder  
    * **Without asymmetric information and incentive issues, you would not need contingent payments**

- Solution in a deal creates new problems

  5. Are there other potential responses? (Hint: how can the visibility of BP‚Äôs actions be increased?)  
- Not enough visibility/observability here so maybe if you could monitor her behavior by getting audit rights, oversight committee that Founder is on, or paying out at certain milestones more money but less royalty

3. Christine is CEO of Debtor Co, and she owns all of its equity, which is worth $25 million. Emma has lent Debtor Co $50 million. Christine is considering a deal with a 40% chance of doubling Debtor Co‚Äôs total capitalized value (including debt and equity \= $75M so total assets are worth $75M) to $150, and a 60% chance of reducing its value to $0.  
   1. Is the expected value of this deal positive or negative overall?  
- Negative expected value  
  * $150M(40%) \= $60M  
  * $0(60%) \= $0  
  * Value of the business is $60M which is less than the current value

  2. For Christine in particular, is the expected value positive or negative?  
- Positive expected value for CEO  
  * $150M  
    * Debt gets $50M and equity gets $100M. **All of the increase goes to the equity holders**  
      * For equity:  
        * $100M(40%) \= $40M  
        * $0(60%) \= $0  
        * Expected Value for Equity \= $40M which is more than the $25M the equity holders have currently  
    * **Equity has incentive to take risks (even with risks with negative expected value) because the negative value will be passed to debt holders**  
      * **Another form of Moral Hazard/Agency Problem: Equity makes the business decisions but is incentivized to act in their self-interest over those of the entire company**  
  * $0  
    * Debt gets $0 and equity gets $0

  3. Generalizing from this example, how can leverage produce moral hazard for equity holders?  
- Equity holders have more incentive to take risks (even when risks have negative expected value) because they get the upside but debt holders are not able to share in the upside  
  * This is also moral hazard \= Keep gains for yourself and pass risks to debtors

- Why lend money at all then?  
  * There is a K. Debt covenants impose discipline/limits on the management, protecting debt holders from this incentive misalignment

4. In insurance contracts, how do insurance companies address moral hazard?  
- Insurance companies use:  
  * Exclusion/premium  
  * **Deductible/co-pays** ‚Äì making sure that the policy holder is responsible for some payment  
    * Undercuts misalignment that moral hazard creates  
    * **If policy holder bears some of the risk, this limits their interest in being completely indifferent to risk when being insured**

5. In principle, parties should try to introduce financial incentives to head off moral hazard. K\&S say this is challenging. Why?  
- You would create the perfect K if you could but imbalances in information//incentive problems make it hard to do. So instead, do something else. But these new tools create information and incentive problems

6. Frank is a salesman. He would rather earn a fixed salary, but his employer wants to pay him on commission.   
   1. What is the advantage of the commission structure?  
- Commission structure ties his compensation to making sales, motivating him. The salesman may not be monitored/observed like the talent in movies  
  * **Incentive/performance-based pay is a self-monitoring device**

  2. Why is Frank potentially unhappy with it? What might he demand in return?  
- Having pay conditioned on effort makes sense for monitoring but it is hard to measure effort precisely (doesn‚Äôt automatically translate to higher sales figures). So salesman will want higher commission pay or higher fixed pay if he can get it to offer some stability (in case external factors (tariffs) can harm him)

- If salesman wants fixed pay 2 Reasons why:  
  * 1\) Signals laziness  
  * 2\) Needs steady paycheck  
    * In order to take on this risk, he might ask for more commission payment. If I take this risk on, I want to be compensated more

  3. In principle, might it be worth it for Frank‚Äôs employer to pay him extra as a way to induce him to take more risk?  
- When you start introducing contingencies, expected value should go up because they are taking more risk

7. Georgia is managing partner of a law firm, which traditionally has lock-step compensation. She is considering switching to an ‚Äúeat what you kill‚Äù system.  
   1. What advantages do you see in this change?  
- **Eat What You Kill Advantages:**  
  * **1\) Motivates partners to bring in more business, increasing revenue**  
  * **2\) Firms will keep people who bring in the most business by paying them based on the amount of business they bring in** 

  2. What advantages do you see in the lock-step system?  
- **Lock-Step Advantages:**  
  * **1\) Teamwork/collaboration, less cut-throat culture by limiting internal competition**  
  * **2\) Not always clear on how to measure who brings in the business (constantly adjudicating disputes)**  
  * **3\) Eat What You Kill Shifts focus to bringing in new business from working for existing clients**  
    * **If the system is well-calibrated firm will cut your pay if you lose a client, but this suffers from the same fingerprint issues**

- When the issues are not fully observable, the issues become difficult

8. Incentivizing multiple tasks  
   1. Why is it so hard to design an incentive system when the goal is to motivate people to succeed at multiple tasks on multiple dimensions?  
- **You want people to make judgments about what is needed in each moment, but if the incentives skew in favor of one task rather than another, this will not be effective**

  2. Why is this a problem at a law firm?  
- Incentive system at a law firm encourages focus on getting new clients and could neglect the existing ones

  3. Why was it a problem to give a bonus for home runs? And a penalty for interceptions? And a bonus for completed passes?  
- Bonus for homeruns is problematic because players will try to get to this extreme result and not perform better on average which can be most helpful for the team

- **What you reward is not necessarily what needs to happen in a given moment. So if the contracts are not structured in the right way, you can end up with something that skews behavior instead of what is needed in that moment**

9. Elaine Entrepreneur launches a high tech startup. Six months later, she persuades Veronica, a venture capitalist, to invest $20 million for 40% of the company, which implies a valuation for the whole company of $50 million. Veronica received convertible preferred shares, which give her voting control unless and until there is an IPO, in which case Veronica‚Äôs shares mandatorily convert into common.  
1. Review: there is a potential tax reason why Veronica‚Äôs shares are convertible preferred. What is it?  
- People who get common stock want to be able to value this stock very low so that they pay ordinary rates when they make the 83(b) election at a low valuation. Then, when the stock appreciates they pay capital gain on the appreciation. They can only do this if they give investors a different kind of stock that is valued differently. So, they give them convertible preferred 

2. Assume that Veronica‚Äôs shares have a ‚Äú1X‚Äù liquidation preference. Veronica needs cash for another startup that she thinks is more interesting. Acquiror offers $21 million for the company, which obviously is $29M less than the implied valuation when Veronica invested. Does Veronica have an incentive to take this deal? Will Elaine think this is a good deal?  
- VC may take the deal because they can recoup their investmentq  
  * **Liquidation preference holder/common stock holder is similar to the debt/equity tension**  
    * **The preferred stock holder with the liquidation preference, is going to be okay with a deal where they recoup their investment, but the common stock holder will not**

- **But there is a reputational constraint on this: VC will not sell too cheaply because she will not get another deal next time**

3. In this situation, how much must Acquiror offer in order for Veronica to convert to common as part of the deal?  
- Offer must be above $50M

4. Assume that Veronica‚Äôs shares have a 2X liquidation preference, and Acquiror offers $41 million. How much does Veronica get? How much do Elaine and the other shareholders get?  
- Veronica gets $40M, leaving just $1M

5. Even though Veronica has voting control, how might Elaine and her team still try to block a sale?  
- When acquirer is buying the company, part of the reason that they are buying it is because the current management team is doing a good job. **If the Founder‚Äôs team threatens to leave, Founder can try to block the sale**  
  * There is leverage even outside of the K

10. SPAC  
    1. What is a SPAC?  
- **SPAC \= Special Purpose Acquisition Company**  
  * It exists to take another company public  
  * **SPAC Is public from the beginning, allowing public shareholders to buy into the company, but it is an empty shell. The public is buying stock in the leader that will find a deal/company to take public**

- **This exists to allow a company to circumvent securities laws (making them cheaper to comply with)** like the underwriting, disclosure forms, etc.  
  * **The SPAC is not a hard thing to take public by itself because you are disclosing an empty shell. Investors pay money which is spent on target. Once target is bought, there is not as much securities law disclosure necessary**  
    * This is a regulatory end-around

  2. SPACs have a ‚Äúsponsor.‚Äù What is their role? What expenses do they bear? What benefits do they get?  
- **SPAC sponsors have to put in money to IPO** (even though it‚Äôs an easier IPO, it is still expensive). **In return, the sponsor gets equity which matches the money they put in \+ equity beyond the % money they put in**  
  * Sponsor gets a disproportionately large amount of equity for taking the risk of IPO‚Äôing without a target  
    * This is how they are compensated before taking the risk  
      * If no deal ü°™ Sponsor loses its capital contribution for IPO  
      * If deal ü°™ Sponsor gets 10% cost for 30% ownership (extra equity)

- Example:  
  * $100M raised in SPAC  
  * Sponsor put in $10M, public financed $90M  
  * Sponsor gets 30% equity (beyond their 10% investment)  
  * Public gets 70% equity for 90% price

  3. What is ‚Äúdilution‚Äù? Who gets diluted in a SPAC?  
- **Dilution happens to the public investors in the SPAC (not the sponsor) because the sponsor gets a disproportionate amount of equity if a deal happens**. The extra shares that go to the sponsor come at the expense of the public shareholders

  4. Who has a strong incentive to do an acquisition within the prescribed time period? Why?  
- The sponsor has the incentive because they have a lot to gain from the deal and a lot to lose from the deal not happening  
  * If you don‚Äôt do a deal, sponsor loses 10% initial investment  
  * If you do a deal, sponsor gets 30% for 10% payment

- **Moral Hazard: Sponsor should be motivated to do a deal that is worth $100M or more, but instead they are overincentivized by diluted structure to do a deal so they will do one for less than the total investment (because their ownership percentage increases)**  
  * Over incentivizes a deal  
  * Example:  
    * Total investment \= $100M  
    * Deal \= $50M  
    * Sponsor keeps \= $15M (more than the $10M they put in)

- Defending SPACs: Shareholders always had the option to decline the deal by pulling their money out and selling  
  * Counter: SH didn‚Äôt know to ask for their money back because they didn‚Äôt know they were getting a bad deal

  5. Are the incentives of managers and shareholders aligned in a SPAC?  
- No. Incentives of managers and shareholders are not aligned ‚Äì management has more incentive to seek a deal
